Praise for Radical Transformational
Leadership
“We live in a world battling for peace, threatened with violence, where
inequality is justified as both means and ends. This self-destructive
worldview is dominated and backed by spurious arguments for a
leadership based on the idea of supremacy of a race, religion, or culture.
Monica Sharma’s counterargument is gentle and persuasive. Her voice,
based on experience and conviction, strengthens the case that leadership
can be shaped with equality, freedom, and justice, rooted in the
transformational power of human compassion.”
—ARUNA ROY, SOCIOPOLITICAL ACTIVIST, RECIPIENT OF
2000 RAMON MAGSAYSAY AWARD
“Monica Sharma is one of the elder statesmen of Sacred Activism. In
this passionate, subtle, brilliant book, distilled from a lifetime of
experience in the United Nations and elsewhere, she has given us all a
blueprint and map for radical systemic change and how best to enact it.
Everyone aspiring to affect authentic leadership in and for our time
should read this work. It is destined to become a classic.”
—ANDREW HARVEY, AUTHOR OF THE HOPE: A GUIDE TO
SACRED ACTIVISM
“We have the capacity to shift the paradigm; Monica Sharma tells us
how. With an expansive global vision, a compassionate heart, and
radical courage, she shares her practical experience in pioneering
transformative leadership. Monica is a rare soul who stands up to power
and demands results. We need to listen.”
—NANCY ROOF, PHD, EDITOR, KOSMOS JOURNAL
“This is a book that I have been waiting for all my life! Here is the
master document on human development and social change written by
one of the world’s leading social artists. Monica Sharma’s brilliant and
compassionate work and ingenious know-how has lifted, inspired and
helped to alleviate centuries-old problems. She offers a dynamic set of
approaches, templates and tools as profound as it is precise in the art
and science of transformation. To my mind, it is a crucial systemic
exploration of how to work with and transform the interlocking
circumstances contributing to the complex problems we are facing. To
achieve a world that offers peace, equity, and well-being for everyone,
this extraordinary work offers the reader creative ways and means ofcontributing to a world-changing agenda.”
—JEAN HOUSTON, PHD, AUTHOR OF JUMP TIME: SHAPING
YOUR FUTURE IN A WORLD OF RADICAL CHANGE
“Dr. Monica Sharma shares her deep experience through her work with
the United Nations and elsewhere in international human relations,
organizing for positive social change. We are informed by her vast
knowledge of global issues and her approaches to transition to more
sustainable, inclusive, knowledge-richer societies beyond today’s
dysfunctional economics and achieve the Sustainable Development
Goals. This book belongs in libraries and on the shelves of global
citizens all over our world.”
—DR. HAZEL HENDERSON, CEO, ETHICAL MARKETS MEDIA

“The world we have made as a result of the
level of thinking we have done thus far
creates problems that we cannot solve at the
same level at which we have created them...
We shall require a substantially new manner
of thinking if humankind is to survive.”
Albert Einstein
We live in turbulent times. Although the benefits of technological advances
are reaching many of us, at least two billion people on our planet are leftout completely. And whether we are rich or poor, we are all impacted in
some way by several disastrous decisions that humans have made in the
past. Some of these decisions were made unknowingly and often with good
intentions, but why do we continue to support them in the face of irrefutable
evidence that these decisions have resulted in environmental degradation,
species extinction, conflict, killing, the displacement of millions of
refugees, and appalling life conditions for one out of every seven human
beings? Trying to make sense of these issues, we ask questions that vary
tremendously based on who we are and our life’s purpose, and where we
are on our life’s journey. But these questions share a common theme: What
is missing? How do we engage meaningfully in life? How do we become
aligned and attuned in heart, head, and actions?
Looking back over my life, I think about what inspired me to act. There
is a quote by Swami Vivekananda that has great meaning for me: “Take up
one idea. Make that one idea your life—think of it, dream of it, live on that
idea. Let the brain, muscles, nerves, every part of your body, be full of that
idea, and just leave every other idea alone. This is the way to success.” For
me, that one idea is our innate oneness manifesting in our world. It is not an
obsession with an idea; it does not mean having others work on my idea
with zeal and force. Rather, the “one idea” is a rudder guiding me, making
me steadfast, clarifying who I am and the purpose of my life. I see it as a
mindset that expresses reverence for life, for all life—human, sentient, and
the life of our planet. It is not a mindset that commodifies our planet and
other human and sentient beings for personal benefit.
Throughout the many years of my life, I have recognized that our
humanity, irrespective of where we are born, thrives in the steadfast
presence of dignity, compassion, and fairness. This knowing of our heart’s
inner capacities has informed my professional and personal life. My father
and mother used to say I was born with the gift of empathy, and they
nurtured this in me instead of suppressing it, as many parents do. My
mother recounted numerous incidents. For example, at the age of three,
walking with my mother in the town of Shillong in India, where I grew up, I
ran to push a horse cart because the horse could not pull it and was being
whipped. My mother ran to rescue me, terrified, because the wheels were
moving backward despite my efforts! This empathy comes naturally to me;
it is effortless, and feels like a deep energetic exchange. It is what has
inspired me and keeps me alive.
My universal heart of compassion has broken open many, many times in
my life. I remember sixteen-year-old Champa, writhing in pain, who was
brought by her family late in the evening to the clinic in a remote village in
Haryana, India. She had an ectopic pregnancy; her life was in danger, and
she needed urgent surgery. This was in 1974; there was no ambulance for
miles, no buses, no telephone connection, no one in the village with a car or
tractor. Her family members insisted on waiting till the morning. It became
the night Champa died. I was devastated. It was my first month serving as a
physician in rural India.
When I was serving in UNICEF Bangladesh, I met girls whose faces and
bodies were disfigured by vengeful men who threw acid on them because
they would not acquiesce to sexual advances. We organized comprehensive
services for them with our personal resources, but that does not take away
the horror and existential pain of facing debased human behavior!
I witnessed another view of humanity too, many times. In Hyderabad,
India, children with physical disabilities were participating in a race, and
one child fell down. The children running ahead noticed, and all of them
came back to lift her. The crowd in the stadium gave them a standing
ovation, a tearful honoring of the children’s immense compassion.Our suffering and our empathy moves us to respond, knowing that
dignity, compassion, and justice are the universal values for humanity and a
thriving planet. Human beings in every part of the world have a sense of
self-worth and fairness, and to thrive, we must be able to anchor ourselves
and others in this space of self-esteem, dignity, and equity.
Early on, I learned that opening our hearts sources our compassion, but it
does not necessarily solve problems. It is equally important to address the
cultural and systemic causes of problems. I learned this from my parents.
They supported the education of many young students, always discussing
the ways in which the system and culture excluded many children because
of their poverty or their caste. My parents’ hearts and minds were open, and
their actions followed accordingly.
In 1955, the gramophone came to our town, Shillong, for the first time,
and my father bought one. He got music from all parts of the world; Paul
Robeson’s songs were among his favorites. My father explained to me that
Paul’s heartfelt songs about inequity applied not only to the United States
but to India too. Racism was America’s issue, and caste was India’s. Our
parents have such a profound role in shaping our thinking and our values.
My first lessons about systems thinking, norms, and structures came from
this set of songs by Paul Robeson. Inequity bothered my father, and it
bothers me.
I have had the opportunity to observe inequity in countries around the
world through working in the United Nations, partnering with multiple
sectors and at different levels ranging from grassroots organizations to
executive leadership. Aware of the importance of interdependencies, I
worked hard in every assignment to integrate technical solutions with
systems and normative change based on universal values.
We can always generate deeper impacts, depending upon the decisions
we make, strategies and programs we design, policies we formulate.
Reducing maternal mortality is about establishing emergency care for
pregnant women; it is also about a woman’s dignity, her voice, her rights,
irrespective of her capacity to pay for services. Child survival and
protection is about immunization and care for acute childhood illnesses as
well as ending child labor and child trafficking; it is also about children
reaching their full potential and thriving. Reversing the HIV/AIDS
epidemic is about reducing new infections and mortality; it is also about
moving from stigma and discrimination to inclusion, dignity, and respect.
Reducing crime rates is about safety; it is also about transforming
exclusionary social norms and creating equal opportunities.
If we do not integrate solutions with radical systems and cultural change,
we will not have equitable and sustainable results. In this work, everyone is
a player, bringing in radical transformation as we solve problems, sourcing
our compassionate hearts and integrating it with systems and cultural
change. Citizens must actively engage in changing unworkable norms, as
must governments, businesses, corporations, nonprofits, and civil society.
But why are there so many compassionate people in the world whose
hearts don’t yet burn for dignity, justice, and fairness? A compassionate
heart is an innate state in human beings, and when it is open, it cannot walk
past suffering and inequalities without responding.
I invite you to journey around the world and meet the global citizens in
this book. This book will take readers through value-based strategic actions
and results using nontechnical language.
Part One explores the approaches, principles, and practices of this new
field of radical systems and cultural transformation. We will ponder: What
are our challenges? What myths and fallacies keep our stories unexamined
and maintain the status quo? How can we reverse inequity in a world ofabundance that is socially rooted in separation and exclusion, with
economic and financial systems that benefit only a few? How do we see and
seize the opportunities presenting themselves in seemingly chaotic
situations? What are the features of the unique radical response that can
make a positive impact in the changing and complex world of today?
This work is about emergence—unpredictable, but with direction.
Emergence as we engage in transforming our global and local community
with open arms embracing a paradigm shift—one arm is steady in who we
are BEING, what we stand for, our universal values; the other arm opens to
making the changes in the world, the world we dream about and want to
live in. These two bookends of our emergence—who we are BEING and
the results we deliver—are foundational and always present. We need both
—an orientation toward results as well as the inner knowing and confidence
in what we stand for—to manifest our greatness, and to challenge the false
dichotomy between being and doing.
Part Two of this book takes us through the intertwined aspects of being,
designing, and doing that are potential capacities inherent in everyone, and
explores their manifestation through action in the world. When we draw
upon our wisdom and inner capacities, we respond with courage and
compassion; we embody universal values. Our courageous heart is called to
respond to humanity; our discerning eye sees with pristine clarity.
We will look at inspiring stories of practitioners of heart, mind, and
action to see how these global citizens generate results and contribute to
transforming the world. These people are contemporary pioneers, unifying
architects, mindful pro-activists, and radical transformers—all at once!
As contemporary pioneers, we manifest our inner capacities to generate a
new, equitable reality. We will see what happens when moment-to-moment
we work from our inner space of oneness, transcend the ways in which
society stereotypes us, and embrace all our identities. We explore practical
ways of bridging our nondual and dual worlds. What does it mean to be a
paradigm shifter? There is a huge difference between talking about values
and embodying values. We find ways to transcend rhetoric and move into
being the change we wish to see.
Through activating the unifying architect within each one of us, we will
explore r-evolution—a movement that is radical and evolutionary, a way to
design our life, work, projects, and initiatives. We create breakthroughs by
designing differently to make a real difference. We tap into the pattern mind
innate in us and reinforce the link between our everyday life and work.
How do we become this fearless architect? What attributes do we
demonstrate? How do we recognize the invisible multiple patterns, norms,
and systems that shape society? How do we become principled game
changers with the ability to transform the invisible rules of the game and
create a new narrative of civilization? How do we design for principled
action based on embodying values?
We work with templates to respond to these questions, creating new
designs with the potential of a paradigm shift. We are then able to redesign
our work to generate enduring and positive change throughout the many
movements and initiatives that are occurring in the world. And when the
question arises, “Can I really bring about transformative results?” we can
answer with a loud and resounding “Yes!”
How are we mindful pro-activists? We discuss how to implement our
actions and projects differently. There is little point in having a
transformative design unless we implement it. Investing to realize our full
potential through action is a choice and vital to our success. BEING a
leader, stewarding change while actively supporting others to lead, is a new
pattern emerging worldwide. How do we engage at home, in community,and in our workplace in this manner? Through a combination of listening
deeply, speaking responsibly, and generating new societal conversations
drawn from our inner capacities and universal values, we become principled
game changers who also inspire others to commit to action. This is what
generates results.
In Part Three, we explore what it means to be a radical systems and
cultural transformer and the strategic elements of equitable and sustainable
results when we operate at scale. Scale for many simply means more—
more numbers, more countries, more groups, more of everything. But scale
for equitable and sustainable results is much more than larger numbers. It
requires us to design and implement value-based strategies to solve
problems while shifting unworkable systems and norms.
Everywhere I engage, inevitably someone asks: the problem is so large,
and my project is small, how can I ever make the difference needed? Too
often we think we are small players unable to address anything meaningful
because the problem is so big. Here is the good news. Here is the great
news. We are all pattern-seers; we are born as pattern-seers, and if we
cultivate this dimension in ourselves and look into culture, systems,
structures, patterns within and outside, we can design in a radically different
manner and create something stupendous, transforming what exists.
We explore how to scale up, designing a fractal that is the essence of a
radical paradigm shift. A fractal is a pattern that can be generated by
everyone—citizens, governments, and business. Every initiative, every
activity is a space for transforming the whole.
We find ways to respond to problems people face, such as lack of basic
needs for survival, and at the same time, we find ways to realize the full
potential of people.
With radical transformative approaches, we can interrupt exclusionary
and socially constructed prejudices, or “isms,” such as classism, racism,
casteism, tribalism, sexism, hateful religiosity, and homophobia.
We want to be radical systems and cultural transformers—so, how do we
enhance our own capacities to be this radical transformer? What we
measure is what we move—what could we measure to track the difference
we make? What we “see” is what we write and say, which informs our
societal narratives—what new narratives do we see that inspire a paradigm
shift? Social media connects people around the world and has the potential
to mobilize equitable and sustainable action; but it is also being used to
manipulate people and their opinions. How can society and business
regulate unethical actions? Generating results requires us to create resonant,
interdependent constellations, each entity in the constellation solving
problems and changing norms and systems based on our shared unity, inner
wisdom, and universal values—what does this imply?
A radical transformer asks questions that require new ways of thinking
and acting. As technology advances, more specialization is inevitable. Do
we know how to transcend disciplinary boundaries in this information and
knowledge era in order to generate new learning and action? How can we
design and act to further whole-systems transformation in order to presence
equity and sustainability? CEOs from different sectors want to break open
silos in organizations. Do we have simple and effective ways to align,
attune, and overcome fragmentation in the workplace? Although we have
many challenges today, we have new opportunities, such as the Sustainable
Development Goals (the first-ever global consensus on what must be done
to address inequity, climate change, conflict, and poverty), technological
advances, deeper understanding of human potential, and increased
connectivity. How can we leverage these for transforming economic and
financial systems and exclusionary norms?Part Four highlights practical steps all of us can take to generate a
paradigm shift—how current social movements, businesses, governments,
media, academia, and citizens can be aligned and synergistic to make
significant positive impact in today’s complex and ever-changing world.
Everyone can contribute to shift the paradigm and address complexity with
sophisticated simplicity—source universal values for strategic action,
manifest our full potential, interrupt divisive isms, put in place what is
missing, and urgently complete the unfinished business of equity and
sustainability in our world.
Practices and their applications are listed in some chapters and may
inspire you. This book is not only about the theory of change; it is mainly
about the praxis of change. Practice and application are core aspects of
learning-in-action programs. The numerous extraordinary examples in the
book are the result of these learning-in-action programs, which include
three dimensions: the ability to source universal values for designing and
implementing transformative programs; the capacity to design projects that
result in solving problems while simultaneously shifting systems and
norms; and practices to implement change and generate transformative
results. These programs are meant for anyone who wishes to make change
and generate results, from the grass roots to executive leadership—people
from different sectors, governments, civil society, academia, scientists,
social workers, concerned citizens, nonprofits, politicians, businesses, and
development agencies.
Participants are drawn to these learning-in-action programs because they
care about people and our planet, are committed to making a difference, and
have questions about what can be done differently to solve current
problems. They begin by articulating the results they want to generate,
engage in strategic design, learn new tools and praxis in workshops, design
and implement breakthrough initiatives, and transform their everyday work
and projects. I designed these learning-in-action programs to accomplish
results in the projects I directed and in the areas of my interest, mentored
people around the world, engaged in implementation with partners, invited
experts of specific topics, and I learned hugely in this process. And as
participants developed their capacity, they started designing and
implementing transformative programs and mobilized their teams and
partners.
Radical transformation is easy because it sources our innate attributes
and potential. And this work is difficult because it requires self-discipline,
reflection, practice, and systematic application. We have busy schedules. On
top of that, we are often distracted by our fancies, flooded with information,
driven by the usual work pressures. Practice is essential—just like
practicing to be a champion in any sport.
You will meet people in this book from every continent, of all genders,
practicing different faiths and religions, speaking various languages, with
diverse social and political backgrounds and engaging in distinct roles at
home, in society, and at work. You are likely to see yourself in them, as I
do. I know the people in the book well because we worked together closely,
designing and implementing transformative projects, facing challenges and
generating results. And as you read their stories, you are likely to see the
threads that weave all of us together in an interdependent fabric of
humanity—to see our universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion
transforming unworkable situations—and resonate with our intrinsic
yearning for radical evolution. I hope you enjoy seeing how each person is
shifting the current paradigm toward an equitable and sustainable planet. It
is reassuring and energizing to know that there are so many people
committed to making dignity and equity a reality for a thriving humanityand planet.
PART 1
ARE WE WILLING AND ABLE TO
FACE TODAY’S CHALLENGES?
Chapter 1
The New Field
Radical Systems and Cultural
Transformation
This book will not be useful for those who are satisfied with the world as it
is today.
This book is for people who have questions.
Radical Evolution Inside Out
How is it that the richest 1 percent of people in the world have more money
than the rest of the world combined?1 Why do we have so much suffering
when technology and science have invented services and products to make
our lives so much better? Why, with so many people working for change,
do we find that the results are scattered, incremental, and unsustainable?
We are living in a time of paradox. More people than ever before live
with their basic needs comfortably met. At the same time, more people live
in abject poverty. This huge disparity and the appalling conditions in which
more than one billion people live are not always due to malicious intent.2
They are a consequence—both unintended and otherwise—of the norms
promoted by societal forces, culture, as well as the political and economic
systems and structures we put in place.
Amid this disparity and suffering, something unusual is surfacing: a new
opening for ethical and courageous action. People around the world are
asking deeper questions. People have asked questions about the inequalities
of their times for centuries, but now, through the internet, we are connected
and have access to information as never before. Invisible patterns are
revealed with this information. People are asking questions about
exclusionary and divisive cultural norms and systems that were designed
and articulated by a few, and benefit the few at the expense of many. All
around the world, people are assessing and questioning banking systems,
the justice system, energy sources, the systems we use to grow and
distribute food and other goods—the norms that marginalize people and
push them to the brink of survival despite our bountiful planet. Asking
pertinent questions about the way the world works is the role of responsible
citizenship, governance, and business. They are challenging the
inconsistencies between policies and reality—the polarizing and
exclusionary cultural norms, unethical behaviors, and broken promises.
As a global community, we are deepening, reflecting. True, there are
setbacks, but that is the nature of foundational change—five steps forward,
two steps back!
Do you see what is happening? We are in a profound space
asking new questions about norms, systems, and patterns;
thinking in terms of systems and cultural norms;
creating platforms for value-based action through social and
environmental initiatives, projects, and movements to address what is
unjust and unsustainable;
deepening our own awareness through the human potential movement
and recent advances in neuroscience.
Radical Systems and Cultural
TransformationThis work is about radical transformational leadership based on universal
values. The word radical means “root.” The Oxford English Dictionary
defines radical as “relating to or affecting the fundamental nature of
something.” I am using the word radical in this sense—the root of our
being, our innate self, which manifests when our ego takes a back seat. In
common parlance, people associate the word radical with politically
extreme positions and actions—that is not what I mean. If we are to address
exclusionary fundamentalism in political or social phenomena, such as
divisive and domineering extremisms in any religion, culture, system,
economy, or politics, we need powerful radical responses sourced in
universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion for all—in other words,
radical wisdom. This wise radical response spans across religion, culture,
political ideology, and economic theories.
The three universal values that anchor equitable and sustainable
transformation in our work are dignity, equity, and compassion—all
emanating from our inherent oneness. By universal, we mean that these
values apply to all human beings, with no one left out anywhere. These
universal values are not culturally determined, and they transcend religious
tenets, norms, and other social diktats. Dignity, equity, compassion are
universal values.
Dignity is based on self-esteem and a sound sense of self-worth; all our
actions must create spaces and circumstances for a person’s dignity to be
present and unfold into their fullness, without prejudice.
Equity is the quality of being fair and impartial—just and fair inclusion
of people into a society in which all can participate, prosper, and reach their
full potential. Fairness is based on our inner sense of equity—the quality of
making decisions that are free from discrimination. In this book, fairness
and equity are used interchangeably.
Compassion is our love-in-action. When our universal heart breaks open,
we are called to respond to suffering in others and act to alleviate it.
Karuna, a Sanskrit word, describes this compassion. Without compassion,
our endeavors to manifest dignity and equity are likely to become a
polarized space, a fight. Transcending polarization through compassion
along with universal dignity and equity will generate enduring
transformational results, where the actions that diminish and exploit people
and our planet have no place.
There are several reasons why I single out “radical systems and cultural
transformation.” First, it is almost always the missing piece in the
discourses, books, and programs on leadership. Many leadership programs
include personal transformation, and some suggest that participants apply
their learning to solve a problem, but the programs rarely include ways to
transform the “rules of the game” or to address vested interests. Second,
there are committed people who work on changing exploitative systems in
order to find solutions, but they rarely explore the most powerful and
enduring asset of human beings—our radical inner capacities and universal
values. Third, the space of most potential for a paradigm shift occurs when
systems and cultures are transformed based on the root of our being—our
oneness.
What is emerging is extraordinary and unfolding, not yet at its fullest
expression, not yet widely spread. We have begun to transcend the silos and
vested interests, and we are generating new pathways by using our innate
wisdom to deliver specific meaningful results; we are shifting the systems
and cultures that sustain inequity and injustice. This is radical systems and
cultural transformation. Sourcing our unique BEING with a deep knowing
of the whole, we design and shape a new reality, new norms and systems
that benefit all. Transformation is not simply transcending differences orfinding common ground or new formations; it is new formations, new
patterns and systems grounded in what we stand for, our actions emanating
from embodying universal values such as compassion, equity, dignity for
everyone, everywhere.
In this work, system is a term that all of us can relate to. A system is a set
of connected, interacting, interrelated, interdependent elements forming a
complex whole—and/or a set of principles or processes according to which
something is accomplished or a function is performed. For example, our
human circulatory system includes our heart, arteries, capillaries, and veins,
all moving blood throughout our body.
How do we generate the outcomes and impacts we wish to see?
Architecture for Equitable and Sustainable
Results
Working for the United Nations and other organizations in more than sixty
countries over the past thirty years, I have learned which strategies, tactics,
and tools generate transformative results, and I have designed and
implemented these tools to radically transform systems and culture. I named
this transdisciplinary frame for responding to the challenges “conscious
full-spectrum response.” This response takes today’s complexity into
account. Most issues that affect our daily lives are complex—for example,
climate change, poverty, conflict, and the lack of clean drinking water,
nutritious food, and quality education. There are many interventions and
strategies in place that produce some results—they are partial responses that
tackle some aspects of the problem, but they limit possibilities and the
potential for radical transformation.
To generate equitable and sustainable results, we have to design our
responses in such a way that we address the whole culture and system—a
conscious full-spectrum response. For example, to deal with a problem such
as unavailability of potable water or nutritious food in a particular
community, technological solutions must be coupled with transforming the
rules of the game. If limited amounts of potable water or nutritious foods
are available, certain groups who have money, influence, and social status
usually benefit at the expense of others. When we source universal values
such as equity, we can transform systems and cultural norms so that
resources are made available to solve the problem, and it becomes possible
for everyone to have potable water and nutritious food. These three
components—solving the problem, shifting unworkable norms and systems,
and sourcing our innate wisdom—together form the conscious full-
spectrum response. This is illustrated in Figure 6-2.
A system delivers according to its intrinsic features. Most of the time, we
engage partially: we focus only on the overt expression of a problem, or
only on understanding the system, or only on enhancing our inner capacity.
Using the example of potable water, we could have partial responses: One
group may attempt to solve the problem by providing piped water to a
community. Another group may be working on policy, setting the rules for
equitable access to water. A third group may be working on enhancing
ethical leadership in both community members and those who are providing
water. There is nothing wrong with engaging partially, but it is limited and
limiting. We can produce much greater and enduring results if we design
our projects so that all the components work simultaneously—a conscious
full-spectrum response—solving problems, shifting systems and culture,
and sourcing our inner power and universal values simultaneously. This is
radical transformative power.
In other words, we need to design our projects differently to get the
results we want.Emerging Paradigm Shifters
Using the conscious full-spectrum response approach generates radical
systems and cultural transformation, not incremental changes. It does not
require us to be systems analysts or classical systems thinkers. We are
required to activate our pattern mind, our critical thinking mind, to see what
is working or not, see the invisible rules of the game, and connect the dots
from our whole BEING, always grounded in universal values of dignity,
equity, and compassion. Everyone who chooses to engage in radically
transforming systems and culture can do so.
The world as we know it is not working for humanity, nor for our planet.
To begin with, we must recreate our context. We need to rewrite our cultural
stories and rethink the assumptions we collectively hold that form the
foundation of our social norms. We have a fallacy that being ethical or
principled is idealistic and that it is pragmatism that is real and required in
order to succeed.
Anchoring ourselves in universal values of dignity, equity, and
compassion is normal—it is grounding ourselves in what is innate in us.
Fallacy: Ethical or principled action is idealistic, and what we
require is pragmatism in order to succeed. We should “get the job
done at any cost,” and we are wired to be independent and look
out primarily for ourselves and our families and kinship groups.
New Learning: Being ethical and principled at home and at work
is not only realistic, but normal. Being principled is simply the
alignment of who we are, what we wish for ourselves and others,
and the words we speak and the actions we take, knowing we
live in a world of abundance. When principled realism becomes
our reality, we create new contexts and redefine success, and we
move beyond money and social status as proxies of success. We
create the space and circumstances for our full potential to
manifest.
What do we need to cultivate in order to change the world? As radical
systems transformers, we are unwilling to let the status quo remain, and we
take the risks necessary to make a difference that matters.
The words “activist” and “activism” are loaded with ideological baggage
and repeatedly exclude certain people. Activists are all too often dismissed
as angry, resentful, and oppositional. The spaces of potential change are
polarized, each group blaming the other, creating an “us versus them”
atmosphere. But activists often think beyond the box, beyond narrow self-
interest, and act to transform the environment in which they work and the
society in which they live. And when activists burn for dignity and equity
for all from a knowing of our innate oneness, they are radical systems and
cultural transformers—they are what I term “pro-activists.” I have known
pro-activists in every sector—business, politics, academia, media,
medicine, civil society, government, social services. Very few people want
the status quo to remain—the vast majority of people want change.
As you read, you may reflect on new pathways for generating results
through the work you do every day. This book invites you to magnify your
own greatness and synergize different aspects of yourself to create a vibrant
world through your everyday activities.
Is This Book for You? Are You Ready?
This book is for everyone who has a courageous, compassionate heart.
Courage is not the absence of fear; it is the ability to act in spite of fear.
Courage is the beauty of human consciousness where our compassionate
heart breaks open and can relate to every human being, every sentient
being, every aspect of earth as one, with our inner capacities, our wisdom asthe source of our action. With our ability to generate new patterns, we bring
the inner power of oneness and compassion to the outer world. We no
longer must compartmentalize these aspects of ourselves.
This book is for everyone who is committed to act to solve problems, be
they local or global. It is about radical design evolution based on practical
experience and results that are being generated worldwide, on every
continent and in every sector of life. I learned and revised strategies locally,
nationally, and globally while in action, reshaping initiatives that generated
results over the past three decades. There are many examples in the book.
I consider myself to be primarily a practitioner—I learn and apply my
new learning to real life situations, transforming them to generate results at
home and at work. And practitioners from all walks of life who want to
influence the big picture of change with concrete actions and results will
find the examples in this book educational and inspirational. Every person
who wants to participate in solving societal problems, particularly complex
problems, will find this book valuable. Solving problems not just for the
moment, not just for today, and not just in the short term, but solving
problems in a way that creates alternative patterns and transforms systems
and culture to truly allow everyone to prosper, truly allow our earth to
thrive.
This book is meant for those who want to create alternatives by working
in multidisciplinary teams to shift the very context that gives rise to the
problem. It deals with both short-term and long-term change. This book is
about a new type of stewardship and leadership dealing with systems and
culture; it is for leaders choosing to create new futures, leaders who are
principled game changers.
This book is about embarking on an adventure—an adventure to discover
one’s self and create new realities. There is no prescription or set of rules,
and this book does not provide the answers. It does provide ways to
discover answers in such a way that we simultaneously combine our
deepest wise self with our intelligent mind and contribute significantly by
solving both immediate and long-term problems.
This book is for paradigm creators, for those who choose to enhance their
innate stewardship, their deep sense of responsibility, and their contribution
in the world. It is for people who want to create a new narrative while being
in action.
We will explore exactly what it takes to live as if transformation really
matters and tap into the courage to create real and abiding change. When we
ask the right questions based on our shared universal values we can
manifest our creativity beyond measure, unfolding a narrative and a reality
in which our world works for everyone.
Chapter 2
Challenges and Opportunities
Humanity and Our Planet
For the first time in history, our collective decisions will determine the
trajectory of our earth and civilization—whether we destroy ourselves and
the planet or survive and thrive.
We face grave issues on our planet, as I will discuss in this chapter. I will
also address possible methods for redressing them by exploring “creative
opportunities.” Helen Keller says, “Although the world is full of suffering,
it is also full of the overcoming of it.”
Thanks to a combination of advances in technology, infrastructure, and
access to resources, we have more abundant opportunities to fulfill people’s
basic needs worldwide than ever before. More than two billion people
globally live more comfortably than did the Tudor kings in the 1500s. For
the most part, we no longer need to organize labor and walk miles to fetchwater. We can simply push a button to have light in our homes. For these
two billion people—including people in developing countries such as
Brazil, China, India, and South Africa as well as in Europe and America—
there is ample, easily accessible food and shelter. Services for health and
education are available. This quarter of our global population is mobile and
connected, and they have their basic needs met with ease.
A massive amount of information is generated and moves constantly
through cyberspace, connecting people around the world. Our capacity to
communicate and exchange information across time and space is enhanced
beyond imagination. Through this connectivity, a new global culture is
taking shape, particularly among the younger generation, redefining beliefs,
perspectives, and tastes. However, technology cannot resolve deep-seated
prejudice, hatred, and violence, nor can it address the never-ending spiral of
material consumption, greed, and domination.
In spite of advances in living standards for two billion people, four
billion people on our planet barely manage to eke out an existence. Income
poverty, malnutrition, lack of employment, and inadequate shelter,
combined with an ever-widening gap between the rich and the poor, have
resulted in human suffering and violence on an unprecedented scale.
Another one billion people live in extreme poverty and each day is a life-
and-death struggle. These people do not have the means to stay alive, much
less thrive in the face of chronic hunger, illness, and environmental hazards.
Yet we live in a world that has enough food to feed everyone, the ability to
tackle disease, and the power to make decisions to create a pollution-free
environment.
Although our child mortality rates are half of what they were in 1990,
around six million of the world’s children die every year before their fifth
birthday—that is sixteen thousand children every day; and every day, eight
hundred women from around the world, including many adolescents, die of
complications of pregnancy and childbirth.3 As we now have all the
technology and resources to prevent most of these deaths and problems, we
must ask ourselves: Why we are unwilling or unable to harness the
necessary resources for the less privileged on our planet? How can we shift
societal norms toward equity and put in place systems that serve everyone?
Violence and conflict are all-pervasive. It is shocking to learn that one in
three women worldwide will be subjected to sexual or physical violence in
her lifetime.4 Although we always profess to love our children beyond all
else, many still live in deplorable conditions. Globally, approximately 168
million children are forced into child labor.5
It is estimated that violence costs 13.3 percent of the world GDP, which
is equivalent to $13.6 trillion.6 How can we radically transform our
worldviews and universal values in ways that make violence and war events
of the past? Do we have the courage to transform these military-based
economic systems that are so destructive and archaic? How do we commit
to the changes that are possible and within reach? What is stopping us?
Fallacy: It is impractical and/or not economically sound to stop
war.
New Learning: The cessation of violence worldwide would result
in economic gain and new business created each year.
It’s not just humans who are affected by our business-as-usual responses
and destructive decisions, but also our earth. The concentration of carbon
dioxide in the earth’s atmosphere crossed the brink of four hundred parts
per million for the first time, and we have known for decades that carbon
emission threatens our very survival.7 Climate change is one of the greatest
threats humanity has ever faced. We are actually on the verge of destroyinga thriving planet, including the abundant and diverse plants and animals
upon which our lives and livelihoods depend. Indeed, we may be destroying
the possibility of our children and grandchildren to thrive, perhaps even to
survive. Yet, subsidy to fossil fuels continues—estimated in 2012 to be
between $775 billion and $1 trillion globally. In comparison, total subsidies
to renewable energy in 2010 were $66 billion.8
There are some positive initiatives surfacing. The development of solar
energy and wind power is showing record growth around the world. But
although there are currently as many as 6.5 million direct and indirect jobs
in renewable energy, the rate and type of change is far from adequate to
make the shifts that are needed.9 For decades we have had scientific
evidence showing that the increasing carbon emission level is threatening
the very future of humanity.
The ultimate life-compelling questions are: How can we move beyond
incremental changes in the system and in society? How do we challenge
vested interests of the enormously wealthy and political powerbrokers in
this world and transform the financial rules and laws that concentrate
wealth in the hands of a few? How do we address unwillingness to change
worldviews and decisions in spite of irrefutable evidence of impending
destruction, and deal with our fears of losing external power, money, social
stature, and reputation? The answers to all these questions are in our
discovery of human greatness and our full potential and courage to act. This
book is about discovering who we are and taking action that serves
everyone on earth and this earth itself.
When we access our inner capacities for compassion, equity, and dignity,
spaciousness opens up. Suddenly we have the courage to create, to review
our assumptions, to be willing to say, “It was a mistake,” to listen, to alter
our views, renew our positions and strategies. We discover our capacity and
act to create a healthy, vibrant future for all of humanity and for our planet.
Contributing Factors and Creative
Opportunities
There are many intertwined circumstances contributing to the complex
problems we are facing. What are they and how do we start transforming
them? There are three key and interrelated factors—inequity, identity, and
the underlying economic and financial system—that underpin the
challenges we face worldwide. Inequity on a planet with abundance is
essentially the consequence of our individual and collective mindset and of
the worldviews that determine the policies and programs we put in place.
Social identities are often paramount in the minds of people of different
cultures; this creates major rifts in the midst of our immense common
global heritage. People around the world are willing to wage wars and
destroy families and communities in order to acquire resources for personal
gain, for themselves, their families, kinship groups, and/or colluding
partners. The rules that govern fiscal and economic policies as well as the
free market are formulated by only a very few people but they impact the
lives of all of us; these rules and policies are inextricably linked with every
sector of human development and business. But they are not a given—they
are rules, systems, and norms that can be changed. And we can change
them. Exploring existing opportunities and generating new ones are
essential for the future we wish to create—a world that offers peace, equity,
and well-being for everyone.
Inequity
Inequity is a critical issue at the very core of many of the problems and
challenges we face. In the past, we assumed that the circumstances under
which people lived or their propensity toward having problems explainedwhy people “at the bottom of the social ladder” were poverty-stricken and
disenfranchised. But extensive research reveals a different pattern.10 Health
and social problems are a result of the wide imbalance in the scale of
materialistic difference between people living in that particular society. This
inequity is also a driving force behind conflict and destabilization in
society. We have all heard people say that poverty is a major cause of
violence, and that poor people are angry and violent. In actual fact,
scientific studies show that people living in poverty are no more violent
than the wealthy. It is major discrepancies in income and ever-increasing
deprivation in the midst of plenty that leads to conflict and violence.
Fallacy: Poverty breeds violence.
New Learning: Disparity leads to conflict and violence.
And, as if the miserable conditions of poverty are not bad enough, those
who are kept marginalized pay the biggest price during times of social
unrest.
Ever since the 1980s, when the dominance of the financial sector started,
policies for full employment have been neglected in most countries around
the world due to financial pressures to cut back, have smaller governments,
or implement austerity measures. More workers and their families are living
on the brink in ever-more precarious situations. During this time, incomes
have soared for a few individuals worldwide, primarily in the finance
sector. According to Paul Krugman, an eminent economist, in 2013 the top
twenty-five hedge fund managers each earned an average of $1 billion per
year.11 Inequity is derailing positive economic growth and increasing
poverty. It is stalling progress in education, health, and nutrition,
undermining the very human needs and capabilities required to achieve a
good life.
The Creative Opportunity
There is nothing inevitable about inequity. Extremes of inequalities can be
reduced quickly by expanding employment opportunities, creating fair and
sound taxation policies, sustaining health and education services, reducing
income disparity, eliminating discrimination and prejudice, and establishing
fair and equitable wages.
Fallacy: Inequalities are inevitable, and have always been there.
New Learning: Inequalities can be, and have been, reduced
quickly through appropriate policy measures.
Discrimination, prejudice, and social exclusion are deeply entrenched in
the social, economic, and political processes of every society in varying
degrees. They reinforce the normative and systemic inequalities that are
transmitted from generation to generation. Dominant and dominating
groups benefit by gaining more and more access to resources and
opportunities, perpetuating inequities that are self-benefiting to a few while
marginalizing the many. But we can tackle collective prejudice though a
radical shift—a deep knowing of our innate oneness, our responsible
worldviews, living from abundance rather than scarcity.
Who benefits from the status quo? What stalls creative and life-affirming
action, thereby fueling the inevitable poor health and limited education of
so many and their exploitation through violence, unrest, and conflict?
Inadequately regulated financial systems, globalized trade where the “rules
of the game” create an unequal playing field for developing countries,
unconcerned governments, corporations who put profit before people—allfuel poverty and inequity.
How can globalization become an economic force for good? What needs
to happen for business to have foresight, operate ethically, and play a role in
changing the rules of the game? Does business fully understand the
discrimination and decimation connected to the current rules of the game?
What will it take for shareholders to see beyond quarterly returns? Do
markets have to crash for shareholders to wake up and notice how current
practices are contributing to such pervasive inequity? Must municipalities
and states be continually pressured to cut down their investments in critical
services like health, education, and social protection, while those who
barely eke out an existence have to pay the price again? Many people are
increasingly asking these critical questions and feeling the urgency to create
new conversations in society. Advances in technology have impacted our
lives in a positive way, and in today’s context, the rich do not have to be
obsessed with accumulating massive wealth in order to feel secure. When
we establish equity and fairness, peace and security are present.
The basic question for us all is, How do we put in place ways to use our
innate sense of equality, compassion, and courage to design and implement
new policies and strategic action to create a new reality, not from our
ideology and rationality alone, but from our humanity, embodying universal
values? Until we add this foundational question to our formulation and
implementation of every policy and strategy for action, we will not reduce
inequity nor will we uplift humanity, save this earth, and offer a future to
our children.
Identity
Our families, the society we live in, our education, religion, and culture all
influence our perception of who we are and the assumptions we make about
the world and people around us. Our families and kinship groups may teach
us reverence for nature and appreciation of other people and other cultures.
We may celebrate together as social groups, rejoicing in our diversity
through poetry, art, music, theater, and cuisine. Sometimes we organize
ourselves around social groups, solving problems together or collectively
managing resources like the ecological commons. Our identity—who we
are and our social affiliations—can contribute to our world and our
community—but only when it is fully inclusive. Our inclusivity allows us
to celebrate, create social and cultural bridges, and generate beneficial
results for us all.
Too often, however, identity and its expression become rigid, defining,
and exclusionary. Groups with external power and influence often draw
sharp boundaries, teaching their young they are superior, seizing
opportunities only for themselves, and using their clout to access the
benefits offered in their society. Therefore, they have an incentive to
maintain the conditions of inequity that afford them great privilege.
Families reinforce these sharp boundaries. A student from Nepal shared that
he was beaten when he was four years old for playing with children whose
parents were from “a low caste.”
Discrimination and social exclusion are embedded in all societies in
varying degrees. Rigid and exclusionary identity fans the fire of anger, often
driving young people, particularly young men, into radical groups. Maajid
Nawaz, now engaged in reconciliation and conflict prevention, explains that
as a young person he decided to join an extremist group for two reasons: he
felt at a loss growing up in the United Kingdom, asking himself whether he
was British or Pakistani; and he was discriminated against by the police for
being a Muslim, and attacked by a gang of white teenagers as an adolescent.
He related to members of the extremist group more than anyone else and
felt as if he belonged. Later, when he decided to leave the group, he wasostracized by his community, and his close relatives and friends cut off
connections with him. It was a very high price to pay.12
Honor implies respect and esteem; it is based on integrity. “Honor
killing,” however, is nothing but an expression of domination and control in
families and society. The ultimate price for this type of “honorable” identity
is the loss of life. More than five thousand women are put to death
worldwide every year due to “honor killing.”13 Women’s groups estimate
the number to be at least twenty thousand. It is not simply about the
numbers. The fact that a father, a brother, or a mother can actually murder
their own daughter or sister is testimony to how outrageous and cruel the
sense of honor, reputation, and identity can be.
Most honor killing is in the Middle East and South Asia. Instead of
families being nurturing, accepting, and compassionate, they become
sources of fear and oppression. Based on depraved notions of masculinity
or “male identity,” acts of vengeance and murder are committed by male
members of the family against female members for supposedly bringing
“dishonor” to the family. A woman can be targeted for any reason, including
her refusal to enter into an arranged marriage, being victim of a sexual
assault and rape, seeking divorce from an abusive husband, or simply
alleged to have committed adultery. The societal perception is that a
woman’s behavior is sufficient to trigger an attack on her life by her own
family members. It is a means of controlling women. Homosexual men are
also targeted.
Fallacy: I am dishonorable and a coward if I do not fight or kill for
the “honor” of my family and social identity.
New Learning: The emerging heroes can, and are, stopping the
cycle of hatred and killing, for example, Nobel Laureate Malala
Yousafzai and her father Ziauddin Yousafzai.
A characteristic of honor killings is that perpetrators don’t face negative
stigma within their communities because society justifies their behavior. In
India, so-called upper caste groups have murdered many couples for daring
to love and marry outside their caste. Ethnic cleansing and genocidal rape
are other brutal expressions of perpetuating and preserving identity in
barbaric ways.
Looking deeper and beyond the political rhetoric of equity and
acceptance, globally we see widespread prejudice, discrimination, political
manipulation, and even hatred of others, including in wealthy nations. It is
hatred based on race, class, caste, ethnicity, religion, gender, or sexual
preference. For example, Freddie Gray, an African American man from
Baltimore, Maryland, in the United States, died in April 2015 in police
custody because of injuries to his neck and spine, which he sustained while
being transported in a police vehicle. The massacre in Charleston, South
Carolina, on June 17, 2015, where nine people were shot dead by a gunman
at a black Church, is another recent incident of extreme racial prejudice.
These events sparked nationwide anger and inquiry into racial
discrimination in the United States. Discrimination and hate crimes based
on race, religion, and ethnicity continue in Europe too.
The Creative Opportunity
Most of us learn to speak a language, practice a religion, and play. In this
way, we are all the same. The language we speak, the religion we practice,
the games we play are usually determined by where we are born—what
country and culture we are born into. Where we are born is a matter of
chance, not choice. We also all feel love, belonging, abandonment, betrayal,
fear, grief, and a plethora of emotions that are part of our human experienceregardless of where we are born.
Our most powerful identity is much more than the social forms of
nationality, race, religion, gender, class, caste, tribe, language. We must
embrace all these social aspects in ways that are inclusive, but our most
powerful identity lies in our unique inner capacities, our essential oneness.
Discovering this, knowing who we are, sourcing our wisdom constantly for
courageous strategy and compassionate action is what will bring enduring
results, ensuing dignity for all human beings. When we loosen the
stranglehold of exclusive and divisive social identities, we create a vibrant
and peaceful future for ourselves, for one another, and for generations to
come.
The basic question for all of us is, Are humans more than a compilation
of social characteristics? Or are we profound conscious human beings? We
need to put in place ways to transcend and include our social identities,
anchoring ourselves in our inner greatness in all our endeavors, initiatives,
and programs—personal, professional, and institutional. We will explore
this question further in Chapter Five.
Finance and Our Economy
As citizens wanting to make a difference in this increasingly globalized
world, we must understand the way our economies work. We can and must
understand the rules that govern global money matters, financial systems,
and fiscal policies, as well as the theories that form the basis of economic
policies. Money is our current global medium of exchange; financial
systems deal with the management, creation, and study of money, banking,
credit, investments, assets, and liabilities; fiscal policies are the means by
which a government adjusts its spending levels and tax rates to monitor and
influence a nation’s economy; and economic policies refer to the wealth and
resources of a country or region, especially in terms of production and
consumption of goods and services. Since finance and our economies
interact with every part of our lives—business, health, law, environment,
education, energy, food, housing, infrastructure, technology, corporations,
transportation, mining, water, natural resources—understanding the basics
is crucial.
Over the last few decades, market fundamentalism has fueled massive
inequalities because of reduced government interventions and unregulated
markets. “One of the flaws of market fundamentalism is that it paid no
attention to distribution of incomes or the notion of a good and fair society,”
writes economist Joseph Stiglitz.14
When people notice that enormous wealth is amassed by some and their
own lives are getting harder and they are left out, they are angry. But
instead of making the effort to understand the financial and corporate
policies that lead to this inequity, they tend to blame the scapegoats and
myths that have been intentionally constructed—for example, regulation
curbs freedom, immigrants are taking our jobs away, governments don’t
function and must be reduced to a minimum, privatization is the answer.
Consequently, they make erroneous decisions, back the wrong people and
policies, and live in frustration, fear, and even hatred. We can shift our
perspectives by informing ourselves and understanding the system and
culture we live in.
We are often told that topics like economics and finance are too complex
for us to understand, and that responsible citizens should leave it to experts
to determine the rules and functioning of finance and our economies. Like
any field of knowledge—medicine, law, environment, education, energy,
food, technology—finance and economics can and must be demystified in
order for people to actively participate.
Expertise has a crucial role to play, but expertise alone does notdetermine how a system is designed. Systems or enterprises can be
designed and cultural norms established based on universal values
articulated by people, both informed citizens and thoughtful experts who
then help determine the results produced by that system or culture. For
example, the success criteria of a system or enterprise may be limited to the
bottom line of profit at any cost; or the success criteria of responsible
citizens and farsighted businesses may include profit, fair wages, employee
benefits, and replenishment of the environment.
Fallacy: Citizens cannot work on complex issues—we cannot
understand all the complexities related to finance and our
economies, and we have no power.
New Learning: Demystifying complex issues leads to change in
policies through citizens’ perspectives and demand.
Global, national, and local economies need to be reformed to distribute
benefits fairly. As citizens, we need to understand that the price of our
purchase does not take into account all the costs of producing goods. For
example, the sale price may not reflect the human and environmental costs
of producing inexpensive clothing or electronic goods. Consequently, the
workers are paying the price by laboring in spite of unfair wages, and we all
pay the price when the production process causes damage to the
environment.
The broader purpose of the financial sector is to contribute to shared
prosperity. However, instead of serving the economy, the financial sector is
currently the driver of economic policies and political power. Since 1980,
the new economic and fiscal policies have been destroying the foundational
principles that have in the past resulted in productivity, growth,
employment, and greater demand. For example, the financial sector in the
so-called “First World” countries such as the United States and European
countries have used political power to lobby for deregulation, which has led
to further inequities. Politically endorsed economic and financial policies
have systematically disempowered unions and workers, and instead
supported labor laws that favor corporations over workers trying to form
unions and obtain fair wages and working conditions. The financial sector
supported globalization policies through trade agreements like NAFTA
(North American Free Trade Agreement), which lack fair labor and
environmental standards. Most deftly, policy-makers and leaders in the
financial sector have been apportioning to themselves growing amounts of
scarce resources by transferring income from the real economy to the
financial sector, dramatically increasing shares of its profits and personal
incomes at the top management level, and at the same time fueling unequal
distribution.
There are other dimensions to finance and our economies. Trade
agreements between countries rarely create a level playing field. Fair taxes
are fundamental for prosperity in society. What, and whom, we subsidize is
equally important. Corporate money in tax havens does not create
prosperous communities or nations.
The Creative Opportunity
Transforming finance away from the current ethos of making outrageous
profits and paying unacceptably huge salaries to CEOs to playing the role
of serving the real economy and the public good is being openly discussed
all over the world. This shift is urgent and possible, although difficult
because of the vested interests of a small group unwilling to give up money
or power. In public policy circles, conversations are addressing the need for
smaller, simpler, more transparent, and accountable financial sectors, both
nationally and globally. For example, financial instruments that obfuscateinformation in order to pay minimum taxes to the government while
presenting high incomes to investors can be radically changed. Another
imperative is the urgent need for a “tax revolution” in which corporations
and wealthy individuals who have more pay more taxes. Taxing the world’s
billionaires 1.5 percent could raise $74 billion per year, enough to finance
the gap in in the world’s forty-nine poorest countries, so that every child
could go to school and health services could be delivered to everyone.15
Stopping tax havens would ensure that corporations pay taxes based on
their actual income. The link between political campaigns and finance has
to be reformed or else the urgently needed fiscal and economic policy shifts
will not happen. Real democracy will not have a chance.
Sufficient sustainable finance is vital for infrastructure development,
green investments that address climate change, and investment finance for
business enterprises that create jobs and enable people to participate in their
economy. There are growing possibilities and openings.
Rapid innovation in technology can be a creative opportunity when
visionary policy-makers and business leaders shape win-win pathways.
Increasingly, certain jobs are becoming redundant because of innovations in
technology. “The direction of technological change should be an explicit
concern of policy-makers, encouraging innovation in a form that increases
the employability of workers and emphasizes the human dimension of
service provision,” stated Anthony Atkinson, an economist.16
As responsible citizens, we need to participate actively in our economy,
promoting policies and programs that result in prosperity for all. This is
feasible.
Fallacy: Investing in climate action is a drain on the economy.
New Learning: Investments in climate action are economically
sound at global, national, and local levels.
The basic question for all of us is, Do we have the common sense and
courage to move away from the lure of materialism and unbridled money
and examine the assumptions and theories that justify and underpin the
“market” to reform finance? Will we step up and review the fundamental
assumptions of market economies in order to generate prosperity for all in
our world of abundance? Can we move away from the primary
preoccupation with private interests and ownership and short-term gains—
for example, our focus on quarterly shareholder returns—and our
competitive, scarcity-oriented mindset?
Chapter 3
Commitment to Transformation with
Results
Our Unique Radical Response
We must radically shift our strategies for global change to manifest. The
wealth gap is escalating exponentially worldwide; massive inequalities and
misguided exclusionary identities as well as shortsighted economic and
financial systems are eroding the very foundation of global prosperity.
The unique radical response draws upon our ability to see patterns and
opportunities for systems change.
Disciplines that explore underlying dynamics, systems thinking for
solutions, and integrated analysis have evolved over the past seventy-five
years, helping us to understand patterns and structures that underpin
complex problems with a view to solve them. The pioneers and innovators
in these disciplines, however, recognized that there is another dimension
beyond classical systems theory—the innate ability of human beings to see
patterns and respond. For example, farmers who understand the weather
and seasons time their planting and harvest accordingly; parents whounderstand the body as a system provide nutritious food to their children for
growth and health.
Donella Meadows, a pioneer in systems thinking, notes that before
humans were educated in rational analysis, we had already developed a
practical understanding of how systems work, and how to work with
them.17 Some experts refer to this ability as intuition, but this approach is
far beyond intuition. Intuition, according to the Oxford English Dictionary,
means “the ability to understand something immediately without the need
for conscious reasoning,” and intuitive is defined as “based on what one
feels to be true even without conscious reasoning; instinctive.”
Our unique and radical response to systems and cultural transformation is
far deeper than intuition. Even though a few people recognize this innate
ability of human beings to see patterns and respond creatively and
constructively, a systematic way of harnessing this capacity by people
themselves is largely absent. The inherent strength in collective humanity is
the immense untapped resource that could vitalize global transformation.
Seven characteristics of this unique radical response:
Action and results are sourced from universal values of dignity, equity,
and compassion for all, with no one left out.
It transcends social biases and personal positions.
It draws upon an innate ability in human beings to understand patterns,
systems, and cultural norms and work with them, without formal
expertise in systems theory.
It enables everyone who chooses to participate in social and planetary
transformation to deal with complexity.
It enhances the ability to design responses, take action, and see results
as a comprehensive, evolutionary process—not as fragmented,
piecemeal reactions to problems.
Because of the design principles and methods, each person’s
contribution affects the whole and is a fractal; thereby, each person
moves the agenda forward, irrespective of how small or large the idea or
project.
A set of templates, tools, and techniques now exists to support us in
discovering and activating our inner greatness and compassion;
transform culture and systems; and act to create change and generate
results toward a paradigm shift.
This is perhaps the first time that a set of tools, techniques, and practices
used by ordinary people can systematically create pathways for generating
extraordinary, far-reaching systems and normative transformation for the
common good. Some of these practices for the alchemy of a paradigm shift
are described in the following chapters. They relate to knowing one’s
authentic deeper self and learning ways to source our oneness for strategic
action and developing the ability to use transformational design templates
for a paradigm shift. These techniques offer a praxis for mindful
implementation and ways to scale our actions.
Fallacy: The thought of shifting systems and creating new
patterns and norms is complicated—I am, and we are,
overwhelmed.
New Learning: Everyone can create new patterns and shift
systems and cultural norms when they choose to do so. Our
response can be simple, though not simplistic, and impactful!
Sourcing inner capacities—learning and using coherent
transformative design and operating principles, tools, and praxes—is the way.
Most people are overwhelmed when they think of shifting systems and
creating new patterns—the multiple, nonlinear, interdependent factors
appear abstract and complex. Sourcing our inner capacities along with
coherent design and operating principles, tools, and practices, we can
respond efficaciously, and anyone can learn them. Once applied in everyday
life, they become part of our moment-to-moment way of being at home,
with our friends and community, at work, and wherever we wish to
transform societal paradigms.
Buckminster Fuller said, “I am pitting a world around bloodless,
constructive, design transformation revolution against a world around
destructive bloody revolution. The Design Science revolution can be won
by all. The bloody revolution can be won by none.”18 The unique radical
response draws on our innate capacities and is based on activating the
unifying architect within us to design differently and make a difference. To
transcend the seeming paradoxes, with all that we know and can act upon,
gives us a choice other than bloody revolution. We can design our responses
through integrating technology—the application of scientific knowledge for
practical purposes—with our pattern mind, rooted in our being, and imbued
by universal values. A pristine, informed clarity from this space shifts the
paradigm from a separate, independent perspective to interdependent global
and local ways of living. We use technologies, information, and our
connectedness in service of well-being for everyone, everywhere. Our
limitless selves and our abundant planet generate transformation through
measurable results and immeasurable compassion.
The unique radical response is “r-evolution”—a radical evolution in
design.
Emergence, Results, Impact
The technological and material progress we have made over the past few
decades is unprecedented in the history of humanity, but the benefits of this
growth are increasingly skewed in favor of a small minority. We are at a
crossroads, and the only viable direction is “inside out”—the one that is
rooted in our way of being in oneness with each other and with the earth. It
is about generating results instead of talking and analyzing ad nauseam
about results!
“Bookends” for Emergence
This work is about the emergence of human potential to generate equitable
and sustainable change with direction; it’s not a prescription. We do not
come with ready-made solutions, and we do have a high regard for the
science behind the solutions for problems we face. But science in itself
cannot solve problems. For example, vaccines are used for disease
prevention, but, still, many die of vaccine-preventable diseases. Technology
is only as valuable as the way it is used. It can enhance life, but used
inappropriately it can destroy life. It can also be used to either exclude or
include people from different echelons of society.
Emergence doesn’t mean that the unfolding is haphazard. Two steady
components hold emergence like bookends:
What I stand for, the universal values I embody and manifest through
my actions.
The impact I wish to generate—well-being for all and a thriving planet.Emergence is the unfolding and materialization of new patterns,
understandings, and solutions that are not always predictable as we design
and implement programs, initiatives, and ideas. For example, as our team
from the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP) engaged with
government, media, and civil society to reverse HIV/AIDS in Papua New
Guinea, major changes were made in the budget allocation for treatment,
and journalists started to shift the negative societal conversations that
stigmatized people living with HIV/AIDS. Unpredictably, beyond our
formal project mandate, a campaign to stop rape emerged through the
initiative of four thoughtful male Papua New Guinean journalists.
Fallacy: Emergence is a conceptual phenomenon that scientists
and thinkers study and talk about, often chaotic and nothing to do
with me or my everyday reality.
New Learning: When I give myself permission to source my
universal values and oneness for action and results, I will
manifest my greatness and contribute in unpredictable ways, and
I am the unfolding new reality and emergence in this world.
Results Orientation
If we want something we have never had, we must do something we have
never done before to produce results. As a child, I heard my father say it is
important to produce results, or else the situation will remain the same. As a
youth, he worked for India’s freedom; in independent India he set up the
first pension scheme for tea plantation workers. He stood for dignity and
equity. Yet, my father always said that my effort was more important than
the grades I scored in class, and I should not be attached to the results; so I
don’t define who I am through results. His advice and this principle, which
is very resonant with Eastern philosophy, have stayed with me throughout
my life. I remember scoring a poor grade in pathology in medical school.
My father then flew more than a thousand miles from Shillong to New
Delhi to give me a hug and to say he knew what I was going through; I had
done my best in my situation, which was all that mattered. It’s about
accomplishment, not just achievement. In this work, we distinguish
accomplishment as the inner space of knowing I am contributing and
making a difference, even though I may not yet have “succeeded.”
While we should not be attached to results in an egoic way, our work is
entirely results oriented. For example, in Papua New Guinea, our goal was
to increase treatment for those living with HIV/AIDS and to have zero
tolerance for stigma and discrimination. We oriented our actions to generate
results as an expression of who we were; we were nonattached to results.
We distinguish nonattachment from detachment. Nonattachment is an
ability to engage fully, give our best, spare no effort, commit to change, and
be totally immersed and present to what we wish to accomplish.
Detachment is withdrawal or passive engagement, not fully committed. We
engage passionately in a nonattached way in order to make a measurable
difference.
Most human beings want to see measurable change because it is
concrete. However, in common parlance, we use words such as impact to
mean marked effect or influence, and outcome to mean consequence. In
order to move the hands on the clock of results, these terms need to be more
specific so that people are able to gauge whether the change they wish to
see is actually happening, or whether they are merely engaging in quick
fixes to maintain status quo. We must demystify policy jargon.
I use the United Nations definitions for results and have adapted them to
imbue universal values: “Impacts” are profound paradigm shifts resulting in
equitable and enduring well-being and dignity for all human beings and athriving planet; “outcomes” are efforts that measurably change the
conditions in which humans, all sentient beings, and our earth, thrive;
“outputs” are specific products delivered or services provided; and “inputs”
