are resources, such as money, materials, or time used for activity and
effort.19
For example, for our children we may wish impacts such as enduring
well-being, dignity, and happiness, which means we must go far beyond the
traditional, materialistic way we look at success—the trappings of money,
fame, status, and luxury goods. For these impacts, parents will make the
effort to generate outcomes, such as children who are healthy and intelligent
(distinguished from smart), who value themselves and others, who are
responsible, fun-loving, and educated, who have compassion and empathy,
who are competent to make a living, and who thrive. The related outputs
could be quality school education, a healthy lifestyle, and outdoor
recreation; and the inputs may be the attention and time of nurturing
parents, money for schooling, and opportunities for wholesome life-
enhancing recreation.
The desire for change exists in every community worldwide, even when
individuals say they are helpless or unable to make a difference. This work
is about transformational leadership tapping into that urge for change and
having people discover what it means to significantly contribute and
generate results.
Starting the Journey with Partners for Results
Since there is no specific prescription for how to engage to generate results,
whenever we decide to work for change, we begin with an inquiry to
understand what stakeholders want to change and the results they wish to
produce. However, the technical standards and procedures based on science
and expertise are established in programs based on the best available
knowledge; for example, standardized testing procedures or treatment
regimens for HIV/AIDS established by the World Health Organization.
When our team at the United Nations Development Programme worked
to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic globally in sixty countries, we began by
asking stakeholders: What are the major issues related to HIV/AIDS in your
community and country? To solve these problems, what needs to shift in the
systems or cultural norms of society? Which sectors? What are the
universal values I live by without question? What results will I produce
through my project? What shifts will I create in the system? Which cultural
perspectives and norms will I shift? Asking the right questions activates our
systems thinking and opens our pattern mind to cultural shifts, priming
ourselves for meaningful concrete action.
In Ethiopia, over 90 percent of the stakeholders identified four major
issues related to HIV/AIDS in their country: stigma and discrimination,
finances that did not reach the clinical and social services providers, gender-
based violence, and competition for resources among nongovernmental
organizations. Through the learning-in-action program, stakeholders
designed and implemented breakthrough initiatives in each of these four
areas. A few chose to work on additional topics related to the HIV/AIDS
epidemic, for example, expanding testing facilities.
Stakeholders say they find this process of inquiry useful and anchoring. It
is essential to guide ourselves up front by the changes we wish to see and
the results we wish to produce. If we don’t engage intentionally with a view
to generate results, the transformative practices we learn—tools, techniques,
and distinctions—are used primarily for personal transformation, without
engaging in workplace, community, society, institutions, or projects. Since
our work is about results leading to social and planetary transformation, we
continuously inquire and reflect on the transformations and concrete resultswe want to bring about as a dynamic process of engagement and discovery.
The purpose is to focus on action while discovering our innate greatness,
firmly grounded in universal values and informed by pattern and systems
thinking.
Fallacy: If I embark on a journey of personal transformation, I will
automatically generate social and planetary transformation.
New Learning: Personal transformation is a necessary but not
sufficient condition for social or planetary transformation. When I
simultaneously ache to see tangible results and act to manifest
my greatness, I will generate social and planetary transformation.
Participants in the learning-in-action programs learn effective leadership
and stewardship skills, but they also learn to be leaders themselves. An
analogy for this shift in participation can be found in athletics. People with
leadership skills are like spectators sitting on the sidelines enjoying a game.
Leaders are out on the field playing their hearts out. The transformation we
need in this world now requires every one of us to be out on the field, fully
committed to playing our best game.
To generate results, we must act. We often create a false dichotomy
between doing and being, as if they were two mutually exclusive spaces.
I’ve experienced this throughout the many presentations I have given about
this approach to solving problems through sourcing inner capacities and
activating our pattern mind for creating alternatives. On one occasion, a
colleague at the United Nations said, “This is really about spirituality, not
action.” When I presented the same talk at a gathering on Spirituality in
Action in Europe, a gentleman commented, “This is about doing, not
being.” Generating results through doing and using all that we know and
understand is a way of being. To do and to be at the same time in the world
is what makes today’s global pioneer.
Our Innate Capacities to Shift the Paradigm
As change agents, we are committed to the well-being of people and a
thriving planet. Who do I need to be, how do I need to think, and what do I
need to do to create paradigm shifts? Based on our innate capacities as
human beings, I see four inseparable, essential, and interrelated ways of
being through action necessary to produce transformational results:
I am the contemporary pioneer grounded in universal values
manifesting my greatness through compassionate and courageous
action. Do I embrace confusion as a space for allowing emergence and
new possibilities? How can I be courageous, listen deeply, be willing to
alter my perspectives based on universal values, and be strategic at the
same time? Can I, and will I, apply this learning to shift the current
paradigm of economics and finance?
I am also the unifying architect designing differently to make a
difference. Do I choose to be a principled game changer and change
those norms and systems that exploit people and/or the planet? Do I
generate worldviews emanating from who I am, what I stand for, and
for our oneness? Designing differently to make a difference, tapping
into my pattern mind innate in us, and making the link with our
everyday life and work results in breakthroughs. The question no longer
is, Am I a small player or a big player? Indeed, it may be better to limit
the engagement of big players who, while solving problems with quick
fixes, perpetuate the systems and norms that gave rise to those very
problems. Everyone can learn to design initiatives with the potential tomake a significant and enduring change, whether they are small or big.
They all matter.
I am also the mindful pro-activist responding through my inner fire,
fully present and fully aware in every strategic action. I navigate and act
with the flow, like a river yearning to reach a destination through
obstacles and gradients, gravitating to and merging with the sea. It is
about flowing intentionally, not haphazardly. That intention comes from
the courageous commitment to make a difference, mindfully.
I am the radical systems and cultural transformer noting the impacts,
outcomes, and outputs being made through my mindful actions, and I
am inspired to take this to deeper foundations and greater heights
throughout society. I foster stewardship in others, co-creating equitable
and enduring results, while continuing to practice and generate
transformational results.
These four inseparable, essential, and interrelated ways of being through
action are emerging. We explore these in Parts Two and Three.
While raising a family, we (a) abide by norms and values, (b) plan and
design how to shape careers for ourselves and other family members, (c) act
on the plans, and (d) do more of what is successful. The invitation here is to
be grounded in our oneness and universal values, design principled game-
changing initiatives for a thriving humanity and planet, implement action
mindfully, and bring this to scale for greater and greater manifestation.
PART 2
A NEW WAY OF BEING THROUGH
ACTION FOR EVERYONE
Chapter 4
The Three Innate Intertwined Attributes
Three innate, life-enhancing elements within each one of us nurture us on
our path and sustain the results we produce: first, our universal heart of
compassion; second, an empathetic burning for equity or fairness; and
third, our discerning eye—seeing both invisible and visible patterns for
social transformation with light and pristine clarity. By universal we mean
“including everyone, with no one left out anywhere.”
Our Universal Heart of Compassion
Our universal heart may be broken open by an everyday occurrence or a
dramatic incident. When it breaks open, our universal heart of compassion
is an opening for our greatness to manifest. I am not referring to our
feelings and emotions, which have an important place in our lives. Nor am I
referring to the inner changes that unfold when life grinds us down or we
face a personal tragedy. We often reconnect with our personal tragedies, like
the death of our loved ones, to relieve our pain and serve similar, if not
identical, causes. For example, if a loved one has died of cancer, we will
often begin supporting ways to prevent or cure cancer. This may be when
our unstoppable courageous heart breaks open and engages in
compassionate action. But too often we get stuck in our emotions and go
about doing things to ease that pain as an extension of our own suffering.
Our universal heart beats in a different space—a space where our heart
unpredictably breaks open and we embrace humanity in its totality as we
journey through our lives. It is spacious, nonjudgmental, creative, and
compassionate—a quiet but determined space, where our will rises to action
in universal love and compassion.
In this work, when we use the word love, we mean a strong, courageous
love for humanity, not only those close to us. When we use the word
compassion, we mean karuna (Sanskrit)—any action that is taken todiminish the suffering of others. Karuna could also be translated as
“compassionate action.”
Hemlata Kansotia was a young volunteer in India working with the
children of landless construction workers who live in dire circumstances. A
construction worker’s four-year-old son fell into an unprotected ditch
surrounding the mansion they were constructing and died on the spot.
Hemlata recalls seeing the child’s mother and father weeping inconsolably.
Then the contractor arrived, gave the parents twenty-five rupees, and told
them to leave the place immediately and return to the village with the body
of their dead son. The contractor said these things happen, he did not want
the media to arrive, and there was no need for so much emotional drama!
Hemlata left the place awake and changed within. Subsequently, she
educated herself, achieved a master’s degree in spite of opposition and
obstruction from her father, and now devotes her life to working for labor
rights. Hemlata is called to address inequity.
Fallacy: A few extraordinary, saintly people can care for others
and humanity.
New Learning: All human beings have the innate capacity to be
empathic and compassionate. Nurturing environments in
childhood stimulate this potential. Adults who have had difficult
childhoods can choose to access our humanity with appropriate
support and can also act to support others.
Not only does practical experience bear testimony to the compassionate
action of millions of human beings, but recent neuroscience research shows
humans are wired to connect and can “feel into” another person. Empathy,
activated by our mirror neuron system in tandem with recently evolved
parts of the cortex, is fundamental to compassionate action. The three
sequential aspects of empathy are noticing another person’s feelings, feeling
what the person feels, and responding compassionately to another’s distress,
states Daniel Goleman, a well-known author and psychologist.20
My universal heart has broken open many times in my life. Shanti was a
twenty-four-year-old woman admitted to the All India Institute of Medical
Sciences in New Delhi with terminal cancer, and I was the medical intern
caring for her. She was a laborer who worked building roads, and she
brought her two-year-old daughter with her since she did not have family or
friends to help. Twice a day, I brought her little daughter over from the
nursery to be with Shanti. Every day, morning and evening, Shanti implored
me to ensure her recovery, “at least until my girl can look after herself.” To
date, including in this moment as I share this incident with you, I get teary
recalling her passage, vividly seeing Shanti’s baby trying to “wake up” her
still mother.
Somehow, these incidents live within us, awaken us, and lead us to
compassionate action, without our having to think about them. If we look
within, we notice this space. Most of us have touched and been touched by
our universal heart of love and compassion more than once. In our cluttered
lives, without moments of stillness, it is hard to reconnect with this aspect
of ourselves.
When did you touch your universal heart for compassionate action?
An Empathetic Burning for Fairness
The prevailing assumption is that people inherently pursue their self-
interest. But there is an innate sense of what is universally just and fair
present in us. Recent research in several countries reveals that fairness is
very important to people. People know there is something wrong whenthere are high levels of inequity—“the equity instinct,” notes Oxfam, was
evident in 92 percent of those surveyed in the United States who indicated a
greater preference for equity.21 Even other mammals care about each
other’s well-being. For example, rhesus monkeys give up their food when
getting their portion causes another monkey pain; rats display similar
behavior.22
This empathetic burning for fairness is a knowing within that we
ourselves are the kernel for an equitable, just, and humane world to
manifest and that we can manifest it, through reaching deep and reaching
out. Too often, however, our socialization process or education dims, and
may even obliterate, this altruistic and kind aspect in ourselves. It is
possible to teach and talk about injustice, but the yearning for a just and fair
world cannot be taught—when our universal compassionate heart breaks
open, it is the inner fire within that ignites us.
Fallacy: Human beings are driven primarily by self-interest.
New Learning: Human beings inherently care about others;
compassion is an integral component of our social intelligence
that has enabled us to survive for thousands of years.
It is not an obsession. It is not a cause. It is not dogmatic. It is not
fundamentalism. It seems like a paradox—no agenda and yet a profound
inner agenda to manifest a humane, equitable, and just world. There is a
deep sensitivity to the intense suffering in the world, moment to moment,
because of what we notice and experience magnified by this knowing
within that I can make a difference and will not stop until I do. It is a fire
with the flow of water, the energy of a secular, global Bodhisattva, the heart
of compassionate action that heals itself and others, with a constant eye out
for inequity and injustice. When we listen to ourselves, we locate the inner
fire and can let it shine its light to show us the way, even when it is dark and
difficult to see. Listening to ourselves fuels the fire so that it generates light
rather than burning us internally, which creates arid landscapes incapable of
nourishing human life. To listen is to heal, and as we do this for ourselves
we create new possibilities, designing and establishing different patterns—
patterns of equity and justice—for ourselves and for others.
Paola Babos works with the United Nations. She started as a volunteer
for the United Nations Refugee Programme in postwar Serbia and Kosovo,
where she worked on reconciliation between Serb and Kosovar
communities, opening spaces for voluntary returns. At the same time, she
sees her agency trapped in the power politics between nationalist forces,
crushing dialogue between individuals in the name of national identity.
These are the same patterns and politics she experienced growing up in a
contested World War II territory characterized by issues of refugees and
minority politics between Italy and the former Yugoslavia. Growing up in a
divided society of “us versus them,” she had a knowing within her that
something else was possible, and that she would not stand for divisiveness.
So, she chose to engage differently. She left her home in her late teens to
engage in an alternative education that would take her on the journey of
cross-cultural understanding. When she arrived in the Balkans, she
immediately recognized the realpolitik that had trapped socialization in her
own experience as a young girl in Trieste.
Initially, Paola felt crushed, unable to be just another UN officer falling
prey to the larger political game. Then she realized that this time she had
the chance to be part of the solution by actively participating in the creation
of new patterns—those of dialogue, of opening to each other’s suffering
over war, destruction, and human blindness. She started supporting youthadvocating and acting for justice and dialogue between individuals
recognizing each other’s humanity and diversity. She connected with
Kosovar youth running an alternative radio station and then met Andrej
Nosov, a Serb journalist in his teens who was the first to dare to inquire and
write openly and truthfully about human rights violations committed by
Serbs against Albanians in Kosovo. Paola provided support to Andrej to
visit Kosovo while he was giving life to Youth Initiative for Human Rights
(YIHR)—the first youth-led organization striving for open dialogue and
movement between Serbs and Kosovar youth.
Andrej is compassionate and courageous, breaking boundaries of state-
run media; he is a burning fire bringing light to the darkest spaces of
nationalism, division, and identity politics. He is apolitical and yet
completely political. He does not side with nationalists or progressives in
the political debate—he reports independently about human rights
violations. Andrej creates platforms for people whose rights have been
violated to be heard, regardless of the arrogant, paternalistic discourse of
the powerful, or of their manipulation of facts to create mistrust and hatred.
He listens and is ready to speak truthfully with anyone.
When he met Paola and me at the United Nations, Andrej shared what he
felt during those times. Neighbors, friends, and family had come over to
meet his parents, asking them to stop Andrej or else there would be dire
consequences. A few even threatened to kill him. Andrej recalls how afraid
he felt the night before he was going to speak at the tribunal to address war
crimes. And yet, he went ahead and spoke up with courage and a profound
knowing within. He promotes youth exchanges between Serbia and
Kosovo, and then organizes Serb youth to stand for and manifest their
values during national elections.
When were you moved beyond self-interest to speak up for fairness for
others?
Our Discerning Eye Seeing Patterns
Our discerning eye sees both invisible and visible patterns for social
transformation with light and pristine clarity. When our universal heart is
wide open and vulnerable and we locate our inner fire, we tap into our
guiding light, even when it seems on the surface difficult to “see.” This
inner guidance and light clarifies and generates insights, revealing much
more than our mind’s eye is able to see. It allows us to see things as they
really are, transcending our fears, separateness, ideologies, and anxieties; it
gives us courage to see and face what is present and to create new patterns
for ourselves and others based on universal values that apply to each and
every one, such as compassion, equity, dignity.
Cultivating discernment is essential for our discerning eye to “see.”
Discernment is our ability to hold multiple perspectives without
compromising the universal values we stand for. We are able to transcend
our usual reactivity and impulsiveness and interpret what is going on
without prejudice or bias. We make decisions without being judgmental,
anchored in universal values, willing to alter our point of view in the
interest of humanity as a whole.
Paola stands for dignity, compassion, equity. Paola and Andrej’s
engagement in this work is not a one-time or sporadic response. It is a way
of being in action in every opportunity that comes their way, and in the
opportunities they create based on this discerning eye energized by their
inner fire and compassionate healing heart.
Fallacy: To understand the complex challenges we face, I must
equip myself with structured ideological perspectives, current
research, and knowledge. Only then will I see what is going onand decide what to do.
New Learning: We now know that decisions are informed by our
learned perspectives and ideological biases, not primarily by
knowledge and information presented to us. We have a choice to
“see” what is invisible in different ways based on universal values
—dignity, equity, compassion.
In her different capacities, Paola learned that equity requires systems
change and new patterns, not just isolated “good” work. She is currently
leading regional gender work in West and Central Africa to enhance girls’
sexual and reproductive health and girls’ secondary education and to
address gender-based violence and child marriage. Paola understands that
beyond providing opportunities for girls we need to open new spaces for
men and boys to engage differently, reinventing themselves alongside
women and girls, unlocking the underlying feminine and masculine ideals
that affect all of us and creating new, win-win patterns of gender equity.
Paola sees from her discerning eye unique opportunities for paradigm
shifts in all her engagements. Wherever Paola works, the inner fire is
inextinguishable. And she is in action.
When did you see invisible patterns and act from this new space,
activating your innate ability to see with your discerning eye?
As we cultivate these three attributes inherent in us—our universal heart
of compassion, an empathetic burning for equity or fairness, and our
discerning eye that sees patterns, we manifest phenomenal transformation
with extraordinary results.
Chapter 5
The Contemporary Pioneer
Sourcing Inner Power for Radical Systems
and Cultural Transformation
When you know who you are; when your mission is clear
and you burn with the inner fire of unbreakable will;
no cold can touch your heart;
no deluge can dampen your purpose.
CHIEF SEATTLE
The contemporary pioneer is simultaneously a unifying architect, a mindful
pro-activist, and a radical transformer.
The contemporary pioneer’s success emanates from foundational ways of
being, thinking, and acting to manifest transformative results throughout
life’s journey. Contemporary pioneers embrace their identities as assets
without creating divisions or excluding others. They embody and live
universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion beyond the rhetoric of
values. These pioneers are stillness in action as they generate transformative
results.
Knowing Who I Am for Strategic Action
Our extraordinary innate capacity for compassion and clarity seems
paradoxical when juxtaposed with the enormous man-made inequities and
exploitation of our earth and its people. It is precisely because of this state
of the world that accessing our capacity as human beings is imperative.
An example of how we sourced inner capacities to address the global
HIV/AIDS epidemic is the Leadership for Results program through the
United Nations that reached 4.5 million people directly and an estimated
130 million people indirectly in sixty countries on every continent.23 As the
global director of HIV/AIDS in UNDP, I designed this program with
practitioners and partners in leadership positions from countries around the
world, putting in place synergistic operational strategies for conscious full-spectrum responses and transformative results. This can be applied to any
challenge or endeavor.
Knowing who I am and sourcing my inner power for transformation are
foundational steps to unleash my human potential.
Standing for universal dignity, people in places around the globe
generated results. Youth in Ukraine established places for people dying of
AIDS; religious leaders of both Islam and Christianity in the Arab States
endorsed treatment for those living with AIDS in prisons and elsewhere;
villagers in Cambodia accepted people living with HIV/AIDS and
advocated for their rights in hospitals. These different approaches were all
based on dignity and equity.
Committed colleagues in UNDP made it possible to engage meaningfully
and generate results in the sixty countries—I am introducing you to two of
them. Nileema Noble, the deputy resident coordinator of the United Nations
in Ethiopia, was the driving force behind the response to reverse the
HIV/AIDS epidemic in Ethiopia. Nileema took principled risks to challenge
the system and cultural norms. She enrolled the political, executive, NGO,
and media leadership in Ethiopia for policy shifts, to generate urgency for
action, and to build local capacity.
Douglas Gardner as the resident coordinator in Ukraine, involved leaders
from all levels and sectors of society, and created a deeper understanding of
the factors influencing the spread of HIV/AIDS in Ukraine. Disc jockeys
from discotheques, where sharing needles and intravenous drug use led to
the spread of HIV/AIDS, participated in the learning-in-action programs,
and decided to stop using intravenous drugs themselves and also in their
dance clubs.
The HIV/AIDS epidemic expresses power dynamics in many ways—
from the bedroom, with our personal responsibility for safe sex, to the
boardroom, with our responsibility to provide access to affordable
treatment. In the short period of four years, we documented 728 initiatives
and their results, and this was only about one-fifth of the total number of
initiatives implemented.24 This work continues to this day. The HIV/AIDS
epidemic is waning around the world because of our partnership efforts.25
Around the world, 21 million people are trafficked every year, and 5.5
million of them are children.26 Estimates reveal that every day over six
thousand children around the world are trafficked for sex.27 This abhorrent,
perverse practice is perpetrated by the clandestine demand of “ordinary
citizens” everywhere. In Jaipur and Chennai, India, Sonam Yanchen Rana,
Ravi Pradhan, and I designed and implemented a program to address child
trafficking. Sonam works for the United Nations, continually deepening the
engagement to redress unacceptable societal expressions of inequity. Ravi
Pradhan has engaged with several social movements in Nepal and is a
world-class coach. Together, we worked with pimps, sex workers, police,
members of nongovernmental organizations, and staff from the United
Nations. Our focus was on discovering each person’s inner power and
sourcing this for strategic action to stop child trafficking.
It is easy to see this inner power in our friends or in people working on
issues we care about such as reducing poverty or hunger. It is more
challenging, and yet soul-fulfilling, to see this discovery in people whose
actions we strongly disagree with, such as people selling children for sex.
As a result of our work, fewer children were trafficked, and many pimps
stopped trafficking children altogether. This inference is based on reports
from the locations where we worked; the degree of change is difficult to
measure.
This inner capacity in each and every one is awe-inspiring.
Six Steps to Knowing Who I AmDiverse stakeholders anchored in universal values work together through
the following six steps.
One: Discovering Who I Am and What I Stand For—Knowing My Innate
Greatness
Each one of us has inner power. Because of our socialization process and
perhaps traumatic childhood, we are often unable to tap into this robust
space. Different people and scholars refer to this space with varying
expressions, such as self-awareness, inner capacity, inner power, oneness,
wisdom, full potential. Essentially, this space is wordless. It is a way of
being.
Fallacy: Some of us are good people who have positive inner
capacities, and there are bad people who are born with negative
inner capacities.
New Learning: The space of oneness and inner potential exists
in all human beings and can be accessed by anyone who
chooses to do so, even if their actions are inhuman and
unacceptable. And by accessing this inner capacity, we transform
our actions and reality.
Leadership and management courses usually focus on personal
development and teamwork to improve productivity, efficiency, cost-
effectiveness, and sometimes enhanced quality. However, these courses are
integrated firmly in current notions of growth, economic thinking, market
forces, and/or corporate policies. These courses may be important in a
particular setting, but in the larger picture, they generally do not generate
the societal and environmental outcomes and impacts we wish to see for
thriving organizations, communities, humanity, and our planet. In our work,
exploring and grounding ourselves in inner capacities and universal values
for action is the doorway to our own happiness and simultaneously to the
prosperity, productivity, efficiency, cost-effectiveness, quality, and well-
being of all people.
Let me share an example that has a profound impact on well-being.
Khadija Moalla, her team, and I were in Djibouti, where the most influential
religious leaders, both Muslims and Christians, gathered from twenty Arab
states to address female genital mutilation (FGM). Khadija, of the United
Nations, and her exceptional colleagues worked tirelessly with religious
leaders in creating new strategies to tackle HIV and AIDS. Most imams
resonate deeply with the mercy of Allah, and priests with the compassion of
Christ; they articulated what they stood for and the actions they would take
to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic. During this work, female genital
mutilation surfaced as a major issue.
Female genital mutilation has been practiced for thousands of years by
both Muslims and Christians living around the river Nile. But religious
scholars have confirmed that FGM has never been prescribed by any
religion. Unfortunately, uninformed imams and priests have been told, and
consequently believed, that it is mandated by religion.
Others, including medical practitioners, are confused and aggressively
promote FGM, thinking that it is similar to male circumcision. Girls and
women are told they are “unclean” if they do not go through FGM, which
is, at the most fundamental level, a disempowering patriarchal practice. It
was against this backdrop that results were generated.
Most interfaith initiatives strive toward creating common ground and
enhance understanding for collaboration. Our approach is different. As a
religious leader, I discover who I am, my inner capacities, and groundmyself in what I stand for, in order to transform the situation and generate
results. I resonate with others from this space, the ground of my being.
A pivotal moment in Djibouti was when a graceful twenty-seven-year-old
Somali woman stood up and described her pain, bleeding, and near-death
situation when she was five years old. She spoke without blaming anyone.
It was difficult to stand there, holding back my tears, simultaneously
holding a space of authentic reflection, without knowing what would
emerge, trusting in the inner wisdom present, and internally not allowing
social or cultural biases or justifications to cast yet another shadow on
human well-being. Grace has an unpredictable way of showing up. These
men and women, mostly religious leaders, discarded their defensive armor
and became present with their empathy, compassion, and commitment. One
influential imam stood up and had the courage to say that he had promoted
female genital mutilation in the past because he did not know the medical
and religious facts, and that he would issue a fatwa against this practice
when he returned to Cairo. And he did. (A fatwa is a ruling on a point of
Islamic law given by a religious authority.)
Then a priest from Jordan stood up and said he was committed to
stopping this practice. He revisited the orientation program for young
priests and changed it. And ever since, his sermons have described the harm
done to girls, women, and society, emphasizing each person’s power to stop
female genital mutilation. One after another, Muslim and Christian religious
leaders, male and female, stood up and committed themselves to the
prevention of female genital mutilation.
Khadija Moalla played a significant role in the program to protect women
and girls from FGM. The secretary-general of the United Nations conferred
the 2010 South-South Collaboration award to Khadija, her team, and the
network of religious organizations for partnering and addressing
HIV/AIDS.
Khadija’s passion for equity and the roots of her pro-activism started in
her early years. She was raised in a big family in Tunisia, nurtured in a
strong culture of respect, love, and support, and, above all, in a family that
valued freedom. She says her father was the strongest influence in her life,
an exceptional figure who embraced others with genuine, unconditional
love.
Fallacy: During my work, in community engagements and in my
family, I do not have the time or energy to focus on my inner self.
New Learning: Knowing who I am and sourcing my inner
capacities is a time-saver and energizer at work, in my
community engagements, and in my family interactions. I can do
more, and I am more aligned and attuned within myself and
resonant with others.
I stand for compassion, dignity, equity, and courage. Connecting
continuously with what I stand for in life through action is joyful and
purposeful. I find this way of engaging deeply aligning within me,
grounding, stilling, and generative.
Two: Articulating What I Stand for and Saying It
Speaking what we stand for seems hard for many adults. They say they are
shy, even when they are otherwise talkative. Perhaps we don’t want to own
our greatness, or do not want to expose ourselves, or we fear manipulation.
Whatever the justifications, we know that compassion that leads to action
requires us to be vulnerable and courageous.
Young children can say with ease what they stand for and what they willdo, connecting deep within. But do we nurture our children and create the
conditions for them to realize their vision and manifest their greatness
throughout their lives?
Ecologist Kirsten Gallo and I were in Las Cruces, New Mexico,
implementing a program at a day-care center for children whose families
lived in shelters because they had no homes. We engaged with fifteen
children, ages nine to twelve. These children knew each other well and
welcomed my granddaughters Anusha and Maya.
We invented ourselves as new types of superheroes, tapping into our
inner superpowers. The children drew their own superhero names and
figures on chart paper, tied the paper around their shoulders, acted out their
superhero, and then stated their inner power. The next morning, they shared
their inner power. “Shining Sun” skipped to each table; she is light and will
create a world where everyone is happy. “Wild Wind” rushed around; he is
strength, and will see who needs help and go there. “Catwoman” is caring;
she connected with a high-five to each of the other children, and will care
for all animals and children who are ill. “Batman” is courage; she spread
her wings and flew around the room, and will stand up for people when
they are right. “Bravo” is the bear who represents hugs and includes
everyone so no one is lonely.
It is amazing how accurate children are about systems and cultural norms
that impinge upon their lives and their families. Kirsten recounts: “I was
deeply touched in Las Cruces seeing how easily the kids connected with
their stand; and when Arturo stood up to say that he could now use his
superhero strength to say ‘no’ to drugs and to the people pushing him
toward ‘bad’ behavior.”
Kirsten Gallo is the chief of the Inventory and Monitoring Division of the
US National Park Service. You may wonder why a scientist who enjoys her
work is engaging with children in a day-care center. Because sourcing inner
capacity is not related to specific areas of expertise, it is a way of being.
I am a proud grandmother. And I am fortunate that our children want me
to share what I do with my four delightful grandchildren. Maya, at nine
years, was insightful: “I stand for caring,” she said in Las Cruces. “I know
what I fear the most—friends and others leaving me. I will practice
listening, and not be so dependent on other people.” Four years after Las
Cruces, she says that the experience left her knowing within that she can be
anybody she really wants. I feel Maya’s empathy, love, and caring.
Recently, during a rough period in my life, I stayed in her home. Every
night, I had flowers beside my bed, poems composed by her on love, joy,
and beauty, and a chocolate!
Anusha, at eleven years, thoughtfully declared in Las Cruces: “I am
courage; I will do what is right, no matter what people think of me, and I
will believe in myself. I will pick myself up when I fall down, stand up for
people, and do what is right. Now I know who I am and who I will always
be.” Four years later, she says, “I envisioned who I wanted to be in Las
Cruces; I was and continue to be inspired.” Recently, I was irritable and
complaining about waiting for our car outside a restaurant. Anusha hugged
me on the pavement and whispered in my ear, “I love you. Do you know I
said in my class at school that you are one of my role models? And you are
my role model even when you are down!” Anusha picked me up!
Sourcing our inner power is a way of being in life, not just in work.
Fallacy: Children and youth cannot engage in “complex” themes
such as discovering what they stand for or connect their values to
action they will take—especially children from families facing
disadvantageous situations.New Learning: Children and youth from every culture connect
powerfully to their inner capacities and their dreams, irrespective
of where they are born or where they live.
Three: Knowing My Fears
All of us have fears. Fear is an unpleasant emotion that arises when we
perceive danger or threat and move into action to protect ourselves.
However, as a consequence of our socialization process, we may grow up
with certain fears that limit us rather than protect us, such as the fear of
making a mistake, or fear of isolation, or fear of not being good enough. We
generally do not know, notice, or name our fears; therefore, our fears
control us.
Too often, we cope with our fears by suppressing them. Our body gives
us many signals—for example, our mouth may become dry, our heart beats
faster, or we feel an empty ache in the pit of our stomach. We can learn to
notice our fears instead of suppressing them. Then, we can name the fears
and release them by turning our attention to our inner capacities. However,
we should not take the real life fears of assault and molestation lightly. We
are not talking about mental illness or phobias—such as fear of heights or
claustrophobia, which require professional support and care. The fears we
are talking about are related to the socialization that limit us—for example,
fear of failure or rejection—and we can transcend them for powerful action.
Auroville is located in Tamil Nadu, India, and is a universal township
with residents from fifty countries. Let me take you to Upasana, a fashion
design studio founded by Uma Haimavati in Auroville. Uma is a trailblazer.
She grew up in a remote village in Bihar and, according to tradition, was
married off as a very young girl. But she insisted on completing middle
school and high school, pushed ahead to earn her college and post-graduate
degrees in fashion design, and created opportunities for her four younger
sisters to obtain post-graduate degrees.
Uma created the space for a learning-in-action program for the entire
staff of Upasana—around fifty people, including three women who brought
their teenage children. This was one of several programs I designed and
delivered in Auroville. After the participants and I grounded ourselves in
our inner capacities and what each person stood for, each one of us explored
our fears. My deepest fear is abandonment, which I discovered at thirty-
eight years of age in one of several programs for my personal growth. Uma
shared her fear of isolation.
There is something liberating about saying aloud what we fear; and even
more so when we see we are not alone. Fears surfaced in this learning-in-
action program: fear of being dominated, misunderstood; fear of failure, of
not being in control, of being rejected, abandoned, isolated; fear of making
a mistake, of being insulted in front of people, of not being good enough.
Our ten-year-old granddaughter, Naina, who was a participant in Upasana,
said, “I did not know adults have the same fears as children.”
Fallacy: Fear is a sign of weakness, and those who are weak
have fears. If I am brave, I have no fears.
New Learning: All of us have fears as a consequence of our
socialization process. Becoming aware of them allows us to have
more options to make decisions and choices.
Our fears are like default settings—just like the screen that appears when
we open a computer. It comes up first. Similarly, our fears show up first,
whether we are aware of them or not; they come up as a limiting voice,
especially when we venture into unfamiliar territory. Being mindful—
noticing and naming our fears—allows us to move toward transcendingthem.
Four: Transcending My Fears
Courage is not the absence of fear but the ability to act in spite of fear. We
notice our fear, name it, let go, and release it by sourcing our inner capacity
and taking action.
Figure 5-1 illustrates moving from our fears being strong to our inner
capacities being strong. When Andrej, whom you met in Chapter Four,
spent a sleepless night facing death threats, he was afraid. Nevertheless, he
went to the tribunal the next morning and reported grave violations and
killings committed against Albanian Kosovars by people from within
Andrej’s own community; he was courageous, sourcing his inner capacities
of compassion and fairness.
Whatever we give our attention to grows. When we give attention to our
inner power for action, our inner power grows. If we give attention to fears,
fears loom large in our thoughts, feelings, and emotions. What we give
attention to is our choice. Transcending fears requires us to move from
habitual patterns informed by our deepest fears to a creative space sourced
in our inner capacity and greatness.
Figure 5-1 . Transcending Fears: BEING in Action.
Around the world, bullying is a major issue in most schools and on
college campuses. And often, bullying is an expression of divisive social
identities—gender, caste, class, race, age. Through the Tata Institute of
Social Sciences (TISS) in Mumbai, the Youth Leadership and People Skills
program (YLPS) was delivered to over ten thousand students from thirty
colleges by two hundred master trainers and voluntary teachers. Nasreen
Rustomfram, professor at the Centre for Lifelong Learning at TISS, and I
designed this course. The foundation was self-awareness, agency, and the
embodiment of values of dignity and fairness in action. Students applied
their learning by clarifying their purpose in life, facing the inordinate
pressure from parents to achieve high scores, and confronting bullying.
Our message is, Our inner capacities and what each one of us stands for
is the place of our real strength—when someone dominates, bullies, or
ridicules someone else, it is not real strength—they are weak inside. No one
can take our inner strength away from us. Obstacles may come, and we may
be “down” sometimes, but we bounce back soon. Our inner strength is
invincible. This work is an example of sourcing inner capacities to
transcend fears and be in action.
Nasreen and I enjoyed developing the course together. She says, “I bring
deeper insights into training teachers and students through the potential of
sourcing inner power and knowing and transcending fears. I integrate
universal values into any content that I take up for training or teaching.”
In the Upasana workshop, Vikram, our eleven-year-old grandson, shared
how he felt when he transcended “a very uncomfortable feeling” when he
was in fourth grade. Vikram saw his four best friends ridiculing another
boy, Pronoy, calling the boy names and saying he was fat, weak, and bad in
sports. Vikram decided to risk their friendship. He intervened and stopped
the bullying and badgering. Vikram is still friends with the four of them and
continues to stand up for fairness and for what he believes is right, “even if
I stand alone in my views.”
Vikram, now a fifteen-year-old, recently shared, “Today I was out with a
bunch of my friends, and they all ganged up on one boy who is pretty naive.
This boy doesn’t really understand adult humor—which is very popular
among young people my age, including myself—and the nuances of
teenage interactions, and hence, many take advantage of him and tease orbully him. He is a really good, nice guy once you get to know him and
understand that he grew up in a secluded part of Kazakhstan in a school
with some twenty children.
“I am his friend, and it irritates me when others, even my good friends,
mercilessly tease him. While one of my favorite activities is cracking silly
jokes about my friends, I understand that there is a line between humor and
ridicule. I see nothing wrong with simple banter, because I understand that
nobody means anything nasty by it, and people should just learn to relax
and not be so thin-skinned. However, when a bunch of my friends gang up
on him, make fun of everything about him, and treat him in a very
condescending manner, then I have a problem. As a result, in that moment, I
stood up for him, despite everybody, even some of my good friends, being
on the other side.
“This was a pretty small action, and you have to understand that my
friends are not bad people and don’t mean what they say.”
It is the everyday small actions, and speaking up for what is not okay,
that creates a culture of inclusion and respect. How often do we pass by
when something hurtful is happening to another person, as though we don’t
notice, even though it bothers us?
Recently, when I was recovering from a knee surgery, I was touched by
Vikram’s kindness and thoughtfulness; he gave up his free time to care for
me when he came home from school.
Naina, our granddaughter, who was ten years old in the Upasana
workshop, stands for equity, and she shared that her greatest fear was being
considered unimportant. I was inspired when she reflected upon a
participant’s inquiry, “What are some possible responses to unacceptable
behaviors by family members?” Naina stood up and shared her insights
based on how she deals with people. Naina said, “The best way is to say
what action is not acceptable, instead of calling the person bad; then
children (and adults) will not feel they are bad and become afraid; and I
learned this from my teacher at school and practice this most of the time; I
am aware when I forget to practice it.” Naina’s awareness extends lovingly
to me. She notices when I need something, and always lent a supportive
hand when I found it hard to walk after my surgery. Four years after the
Upasana workshop, Naina recalls how moved she was hearing women share
about poverty and violence in their lives—their pain and their courage. Her
learning in the stewardship program inspires her to work through service
clubs on issues that women and girls face.
During a stewardship program in New Delhi, a participant who served in
the Indian Air Force and subsequently worked as head of human resources
in a social enterprise stated that courage actually means bravery. In our
conversation, we distinguished courage from bravery, seeing that both have
found a place in today’s world. Most military personnel are trained to be
brave, to risk their lives for their country and its people and endure difficult
conditions. The superheroes in movies and books are brave, risking their
lives to save someone in danger or protecting people from disastrous
situations, inspiring people to go beyond their own personal interests. My
hunch is that suicide bombers are considered brave by those who trained
them and the groups they represent. Bravery is often based on ideological
perspectives. In our work, courage has a different connotation. It is beyond
ideology, where humanity is simply present for everyone, everywhere.
Courage is derived from the Latin word cor, which means “heart.”
Courage is the quality of our inner spirit informed by our wide-open heart
of compassion that enables us to be in action and face danger and fear. It
makes us commit to what we deeply care about and be resolute to take
appropriate action.Josselyne Herman-Saccio, an exceptional coach, refers to this ability to
transcend fear while standing in one’s universal values as being
“unmessable with.” It is not defensively saying “don’t mess with me.”
Josselyne says that working in a conscious full-spectrum way connected her
to a deeper level. “I learned that I had a level of compassion and a level of
intolerance for injustice that I did not think I had prior to doing this work,”
she says. “I also was inspired by people in surprising circumstances. I found
people in the Caribbean to be most powerful and able to see systems in a
way that we Westerners were not. I gained the ability to identify systems
instantaneously, whereas when we started I could not see them at all. I am
no longer able to participate in Band-Aid solution projects blindly. I never
thought I could make a difference at scale in my life.”
Fallacy: There is nothing I can do about my fears, and I should
not expect myself to be courageous. In fact, it may be harmful
and/or distressful if I expect myself or others to be courageous
and act.
New Learning: Courage is our will rising through our wide-open
heart and being in compassionate action. Courage is not about
being forced into action through coercion. Courage is not about
bravado or macho behaviors or performing dangerous actions.
Five: Commitment through Action
Personal transformation in our work is the source of generating results, the
foundation of social transformation. As contemporary pioneers, we ask
ourselves, How can we be of significance and make a difference, not from
our egos, but from our oneness, our very BEING? Commitment is an innate
aspect of our deepest self.
Knowing my purpose is asking simple questions: Why am I here? What
is the source of my action? When I clarify and articulate my purpose based
on my wisdom, I have renewed energy for action.
Contribution is what I do based on my purpose, the specific change I
want to make in the world. Contribution can be measured. When people
articulate what their contribution looks like, they share their aspirations and
commit to reach beyond their comfort zone. We follow our bliss, the hero’s
journey! Helen Keller said, “One cannot consent to creep when one feels
the impulse to soar!”
There are many examples of committed people making unique
contributions to this world. Megan Joseph, the executive director of the
nonprofit Rise Together, says she learned that when she embodies her stand
of equity and dignity and connects with people from that inner place, she
discovers commonalities and possibilities that were not there before.
When Megan was the director of community organizing for United Way
of Santa Cruz County, California, she worked on preventing violence,
reducing recidivism among formerly incarcerated individuals, and taking a
public health approach to criminal justice issues. Often, when working on
criminal justice system reforms, she was faced with addressing
controversial issues and interacting with adversarial sectors.
Using the conscious full-spectrum stewardship practices and staying
steadfast in her commitment to creating possibilities for transformational
projects in her workplace, Megan has garnered support for doing things
differently. Because of Megan’s work, over three hundred other community
leaders have engaged in transformative learning-in-action programs,
generating results described throughout this book. Megan has been
recognized nationally and locally for her outstanding work and received the
national Community Indicators Consortium Emerging Leaders Award,Santa Cruz’s NEXTie Award for innovative leaders under forty, and a Game
Changer Award for creating change with those who are living in poverty.
Megan Joseph’s interest in activism and the criminal justice system
began very early while she was in school. In college, she began to
understand the impact of injustices on the human condition and felt amazed
that there were others who felt the same way she did about various social
issues.
Sometimes we are drawn toward particular themes when we are young,
and they inform our path later in life. In fourth grade in India, I was in awe
reading about the life of Albert Schweitzer, a physician from Germany who
served communities in Africa by raising funds and providing health care.
When I told my father that I wanted to be like Albert Schweitzer, he said,
“You can be who you want to be, do what you want to do, and I will support
you.” Although I forgot about this incident for several decades, I trained as
a physician and worked extensively in Africa while serving the global
advisor for child survival at UNICEF and the director of HIV/AIDS at
UNDP.
Sometimes we may commit to action from our own wounds to prevent
such suffering for others. Megan recalls that for her one of the most
poignant moments in this work was the day she was able to release herself
from the pain and suffering of her father having been in prison for six years
while she was a teenager. She had been practicing the conscious full-
spectrum stewardship work for nearly two years when she experienced a
shift in how she sourced her passion and her drive for creating change. It
was then when she realized that she could not source her most powerful self
through the wound of her father’s experience. She could let this experience
be in the past, and source her present and future from a different place. She
felt a new expansiveness with this release, one that allowed her to source a
universal compassion for the suffering of all human beings and one that
held a much deeper well of power and passion for creating change. She
knew she could continue to work on criminal justice system issues, but it
would now come from a different place.
Today, Megan continues to work on criminal justice issues, and yet she
also works on inequity, poverty, reducing youth access to alcohol and other
drugs, homelessness, and other social issues with as much vigor and
commitment, sourcing her power for generating transformation from the
same deep well of equity and dignity for all.
I recall a similar situation. I served as the chief of health at UNICEF
India, where our mission was to dramatically reduce vaccine-preventable
deaths and reach twenty-five million infants and twenty-five million
pregnant women five times every year. Six one-minute advertisements were
publicized for the six health conditions of concern—tuberculosis,
poliomyelitis, diphtheria, pertussis, measles, and tetanus. The advertisement
on tetanus showed a mother rocking her baby in a hammock and singing a
lullaby; suddenly the hammock was still and the mother was sobbing. I left
the room every time because I simply could not hold back my tears,
whether I was in the room in the prime minister’s office with the council of
ministers in India, or with international donors, state government officials,
or our partner Rotarians. Though our infant son had died more than twenty
years ago, I carried him in me, and somewhere, I was working to reduce
child mortality from my pain of his death. Transcending this wounded space
through awareness, even though he still lives in me, offers limitless
possibilities with humanity fully present.
When we commit to action in the areas where we suffered offering our
talents and serving with the intention that others do not suffer the same
thing, in some way this is an extension of us, our suffering, and is limiting.It is when we transcend our own wounds and suffering that we embrace and
serve humanity in its totality.
Fallacy: Committing to action is not for everyone—extraordinary
people and certain personality types are more likely to do so. I
am just an ordinary person. Circumstances largely determine
what happens.
New Learning: As human beings, we have an innate ability to
commit to action when we get out of our own way. Committing to
action is what makes ordinary people do extraordinary things.
Six: Creating Platforms for Others to Source Inner Capacities for
Action and Results
We must stand alone, but not go it alone. This may seem like a paradox, but
it is not. To stand alone means to stand in the power of my being, my
agency, anchoring it in what I deeply care about, BEING “unmessable
with.” Not to go it alone is about collective action and partnerships that
generate results. As we journey through life, we bring our unique gifts for
humanity, and sometimes, we must be able to stand alone. For when we are
pushing the envelope in the face of no agreement, we must have the
courage to act even if we are alone. I am inspired by Rabindranath Tagore’s
song in Bengali “Ekla Cholo Re,” scripted over a century ago during India’s
freedom movement. He wrote:
If they answer not to your call, walk alone;
If they are afraid and cower mutely facing the wall,
Open your mind and speak out alone.
This was one of the ten songs my mother sang regularly when I was
child. Rabindranath Tagore, a Nobel Laureate, implores us to have the
courage to walk alone, even as we call others and they turn their faces away.
It is the courage to speak one’s truth. Yet we can never truly go it alone—
we will always have to work with others.
In these learning-in-action platforms, we individually develop our
agency, and then we learn to journey together in resonance with others,
generating synergistic results. These platforms are spaces for enhancing
shared leadership—the ability to both lead transformation and support
others to lead transformation.
Sudarshan Rodriguez, an environmentalist, leads the Social and
Ecological Stewardship Program at the Tata Institute of Social Sciences
(TISS). He is passionate about demystifying knowledge, science, and
technology and making it work for planetary and people’s well-being. He is
working in coastal areas for restoration, fostering grassroots leadership in
Ladakh, and working on water resources at the national level. He stands for
inclusion and equity and says he learned to acknowledge and celebrate his
contribution through his work with the conscious full-spectrum approach.
Sudarshan tirelessly creates transdisciplinary learning-in-action platforms
for fostering the leadership of others. He opens spaces to generate shifts in
current detrimental practices. For example, social media connects people
around the world as never before; but it is also plagued with propaganda
that manipulates public opinion. What strategies and action could we design
and implement to address this?
Sudarshan champions and supports leadership of fisherwomen’s unions
and generates a new level of trust between formal leaders and communities
in the coastal areas of Tamil Nadu. He also nurtures every member of his
team, encouraging them to act from their inner capacities and make unique
contributions. He rejoices in their successes, and says that this is everydayleadership based on universal values.
He encourages them to work directly with directors and CEOs of partner
organizations, thereby shifting the hierarchical work culture in the
development sector. He says, “Who I am is BEING, and so is each member
of our team. We are shifting and reinforcing the meaning and ways of
fostering agency in the community.”
In turn, the members of his team foster leadership development in others.
For example, Srinivas Vatturi, who leads the TISS partnership with the
Water Mission of India, recognizes that the intrinsic power within people
will manifest the radical systems and cultural shifts in order to redress
inequities. He consistently invests in the personal and professional growth
of his team, designing breakthrough initiatives on transformative templates
for radical responses in the water sector.
Sudarshan says, “The most challenging part is engaging near and dear
ones!” He enrolled his mother, founder and chairperson of the governing
board of a school for children with autism, to come to a learning-in-action
program. “It has transformed my relationship with her.” Sudarshan finds the
stewardship tools simple, universally applicable, and accessible, enabling
ordinary people to make extraordinary contributions. He uses these tools
and practices in a personal family setting as well as at work.
It is not easy to follow this innovative pathway and create platforms for
emergence and transformative results. During my years working at the
United Nations, I faced widely divergent views and responses—some
extremely supportive, a few indifferent, and a couple subversive.
Yohannes Mengesha, the assistant secretary-general managing the largest
department at the United Nations Secretariat, and I created platforms for the
entire staff of eight hundred people who translate documents, speeches, and
deliberations at the United Nations Secretariat in the six official languages
—Arabic, Chinese, English, French, Russian, and Spanish. Staff separated
by “language silos” came together. It opened new unexplored avenues for
them to relate to each other and appreciate their own contribution. Yohannes
shared ideas with his colleagues without being stuck in the normal
hierarchical constraints. Staff appreciated developing themselves—it was
their first such experience ever. It also provided them the space to interact
with Yohannes as their manager in informal but meaningful ways. Opening
up to new transformational approaches generated both respect and greater
productivity.
Recently, two colleagues wrote about Yohannes on Facebook, “I and
many others definitely respect [your] authority, fairness, humaneness.” And
“You brought the concept of being a boss combining warmth and achieving
results at the UN, and now the Management Development Courses are
steering participants in that direction. I never forgot it and was so proud to
reinforce it.”
Another example of a learning platform follows. The Dalai Lama
encouraged several higher education institutions in India, including TISS, to
design courses to inculcate universal values as a foundation of learning for
students. S. Parasuraman, the director of TISS, invited me to design and
deliver this course on Secular Ethics. The course focuses on generating new
ways to address the root factors of poverty and depravation through action
based on universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion. It also enables
students to grapple with issues of identity and to transcend divisive social
forces. In the current context of religious fervor in different parts of the
world, including India, Secular Ethics, which is based on universal values,
is crucial for our youth. Students design campus- and community-based
initiatives, building their own agency for responsible action. Srilatha Juvva,
Sudarshan Rodriguez, and I piloted this course at TISS. The feedback wasexcellent, and students find the course useful. It will be implemented in
several other universities in India. We are honored that the Dalai Lama
inaugurated this course on Secular Ethics.
Fallacy: Through well-designed workshops, events, and
platforms, participants will automatically generate results and
create change.
New Learning: When platforms, workshops, and events are
designed and implemented as part of a larger strategy to
accomplish goals—and when there are committed individuals
who create a supportive environment that fosters transformation
and encourages initiative—results are generated and
transformation occurs.
While serving at the United Nations I worked in more than sixty
countries. Initially, some potential partners and stakeholders may be
skeptical. “Is this spirituality?” I was asked in China during my presentation
to the Ministry of Health. “Successful climate change strategies are mainly
about applying scientific knowledge,” observed a few scientists in Oslo.
“Structural analysis is the foundation of sustainable change,” noted some
professors at a left-oriented university. But as we engaged together, their
perceptions changed.
Our work in China on HIV/AIDS was highly appreciated because people
started to take initiative to change the situation. Climate change scientists
learned the importance of inspiring and engaging people to generate
change, rather than convincing them to change; this was a shift from the
conventional approach within the environmental movements. Professors
can see that structural change will not happen without building agency—the
power within people to make that change. Knowing who I am, sourcing
inner capacities for strategic action—this is the magnificence of our innate
agency fused with illumined humility at the core of dedication. You may
already be manifesting your full potential at home, at work, and in society.
Discovering who I am and what I stand for, my universal values—
knowing my greatness
Articulating what I stand for and saying it
Knowing my fears
Transcending my fears
Commitment through action
Creating platforms for others to source inner capacities for action and
results
Embracing “Identities” as Assets
Embracing our “identities” as assets for humanity, instead of letting them
become disruptive forces, is one of the essentials for peace.
In today’s troubled world with its numerous human-made crises,
everyone’s capacity to embrace our many identities without excluding
anyone anywhere requires a foundational shift in our exclusive, defensive,
exclusionary mindset. Our social, professional, and personality profiles can
be either nurturing or disempowering, unifying or divisive, assets or
liabilities for society, depending on whether or not we choose to source
universal values.
I am using the words “identity” and “profiles” synonymously, to mean
“who we are, both intrinsic and extrinsic, and the characteristics that
determine and describe each individual person.”
We socialize our children to develop life skills and social affiliations thatmay support them to thrive in their lives. Our intentions are to nurture and
foster development of our children to be happy and realize their full
potential, but we often become rigid. We impart exclusionary perspectives
with our scarcity and competitive mindsets, unaware of our own biases and
their consequences, and/or unwilling to examine the assumptions behind
our opinions and actions. Unintentionally, we do the opposite of what we
really want for our children.
Inner Capacity or Wisdom Profile
Earlier in this chapter, we explored our inner capacities and wisdom.
Wisdom has a broad reach of meanings, including beneficial traditional
knowledge and practices we can learn. I am using the word wisdom to mean
something very specific—our inner capacity for compassionate, courageous
action in the world.
This is a space of renewal that leaves people inspired, empowered right
now. It is mindful action in the now—not a someday or a one day
phenomenon. We are impacted and we impact the world right now.
Fallacy: There are many problems in the world—I have to
concentrate on finding the solutions. I do not have the time to
focus on my inner capacities and wisdom.
New Learning: My inner capacity or wisdom leads me to
compassionate action in the world, giving me inner guidance,
insight, and light that clarifies universal, life-giving principles. My
wisdom and inner capacity resolve and release fear, anger,
separation, and anxiety. I know what I care for deeply, and I am
anchored in my universal values in a way that sets me free. I
engage in compassionate action in the world.
Social Profile
Our social profile is created from the assumptions and conditioning that
influence our perception of who we are and what the world is. We receive
this comprehension from our family and our social institutions, and they
shape our understanding of race, gender, sexuality, religion, politics,
education, culture, and nationality.
Most of us are traditionally socialized to believe that as human beings we
are defined and bound by where we are born, who our parents are, whether
we are born a girl or a boy. We identify with these features and say I am my
family, my religion, my politics, my race, my culture, my country, my caste.
We frequently introduce ourselves according to what is socially or
politically correct, and then we find out more about each other out of
curiosity, common interests, or the wish to make acquaintances. Often,
beneath the social façade, we find out more to gauge social class—such as:
Where do you live? Where do you work? The intentions behind these
questions are other questions. Do we belong to the same privileged class for
me to continue this acquaintance? Or, What can I get out of this person?
Wars are fought over religious difference. Youth feel excluded on the
basis of their ethnicity or religion and turn to fundamentalist groups to have
a sense of belonging. Families perpetuate gender and caste discrimination in
the name of “honor and respect.” Racial, caste, gender, and class prejudice
play out in the discriminatory application of laws or exploitative labor
practices.
However, when we embrace the different aspects of our social profile in
an inclusive way and ground ourselves in our inner capacities or wisdom
profile, we create new realities that work for everyone.
These new realities are concrete. An inspiring example of embracing
one’s religion while simultaneously embracing others’ religion emerged
during the four years we worked with religious leaders from twenty-twoArab states, using the conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR) approach.
Sheikh A, an influential imam from Syria, said, “I know who I am, my
essence and quality of my BEING. There is no separation between being
and doing. I now know how to honor and source this inner space for action
in everyone—and I do so routinely now.”
He redesigned a large program supporting widows and their children,
providing opportunities for their inner growth along with support for their
basic needs. Prior to working with the CFSR approach, he distributed
money, food, clothing, and medications. “They came before with their
hands stretched out to get what they needed, their heads bent down, feeling
obligated for the support given to them. They came from poor families. I
started working with them with what I had learned in the conscious full-
spectrum response approach. The change was miraculous. They viewed
themselves differently, with dignity, no longer hiding their face in a kind of
shame. I asked them what they were interested in doing—some wanted to
cook in restaurants, others to provide care for children, others wanted to
work in accounting. I organized training for developing their skills, along
with enhancing their inner capacities. Now they are proud to give and
receive.”
Sheikh A says, “I state what I stand for in life and stand firmly in the
ground of my being. I proactively engage with Father X, a Catholic priest
from Syria, and I have been openly threatened for collaborating with him. I
have attended many interfaith dialogues and meetings, and I appreciated
what is common in our religions—our common ground. This work that we
are now engaged in creates a very different space for conversations with
action. Father X and I deeply respect each other as human beings and work
together to address social issues, such as the stigma related to HIV/AIDS,
from the ground of our being, far beyond the common ground of our
respective religious tenets.”
These two men from Syria have the courage to create a new way of
BEING in action for themselves and others, transcending their traditional
cultural divides.
We moved several steps forward and generated a shift in the culture.
Looking at present times in Syria and other places, where the political,
social, and economic norms and “rules of the game” are far from conducive
for people to work together in peace, it is easy to think we have moved
backward. But paradigm shifts and evolutionary processes are never
straight, linear changes. They are spiral—five steps forward, two steps back
—because of pushback, a repeating pattern as we evolve.
What keeps us going? First of all, when as partners we connect and act
from our entire BEING we experience the joy of working with like-spirited
people—an alignment in our purpose and a resonance with our universal
values. Second, knowing that there are other people like us all over the
world, even if we do not know them personally, inspires hope, energy. They
are ordinary people like you and me, generating extraordinary results,
sourcing their wisdom for transformation. Third, we know that
impermanence is a way of life and the universe. Difficult conditions that
obstruct our full potential will change; people will progress again. And
fourth, we know that this work works. This knowing is essential in order to
persevere and change the prevailing conditions of war, deprivation, and
hatred.
Here is another example of transcending socially constructed exclusion,
this time based on nationality and race. Samantha Chuula, a citizen of the
United Kingdom, directed a pan-African program for leadership
development through a global organization. Her approach was a
fundamental shift. She redesigned their leadership program, and Samanthaand I trained coaches from Africa to deliver the program. When she
generated results, institutional forces rallied to replace the core strategies
for authentic self-expression with a more “token,” superficial, business-as-
usual partnership, thereby maintaining the status quo. Samantha, however,
remained highly effective in subsequent assignments.
Fallacy: I am who I am because of my family, my religion, my
nationality, my race, my tribe, my caste—this is what defines me,
this is my identity.
New Learning: Where I am born is a matter of chance, not
choice, determining the language I speak, the religion I follow, the
name of the country on my passport. As I embrace my social
profile—nationality, race, religion, gender, class, tribe, language
—I do so transcending all divisive perspectives and actions,
embracing diversity, authentically accepting others in an inclusive
way. Our most powerful identity for a paradigm shift lies in our
unique inner capacities, our essential oneness.
Personally, I am fortunate. From my father, Subodh, I learned what it
means to uphold fairness, dignity, equity, integrity; from my mother, Sujata,
I absorbed a sense of inclusion through celebrating many religions without
religiosity; and a celebration of the natural world—animals, trees, plants. It
was not always easy for my mother to see the joy in life, because of her
sorrow at the death of my five younger siblings. The death my youngest
brother, Shankar, when he was only seventeen years old, was the hardest for
our family to bear.
When I was six years old, my mother asked me to accompany her to a
talk by the revered philosopher Jiddu Krishnamurti. I was the only child in
the room, and Krishnamurti asked me to get up and sit in front near him. I
still remember what he looked like—calm, as tall as my father, silver hair,
smiling. Then, when I was in medical school, I started reading all the books
he wrote. His teachings on choiceless awareness continue to have a
profound influence. Another fortunate event I remember clearly was when
my mother took me to hear a discourse from the Dalai Lama when I was
seven years old.
My mother celebrated festivals of all religions with her friends. My
friends and I were always part of them. This was unusual in the 1950s.
There was animosity due to the partition of the British Indian Empire into
the sovereign states of India and Pakistan in 1947. Approximately 500,000
people were killed in retributive genocide, and 14 million Hindus, Sikhs,
and Muslims were displaced, losing their homes, friends, and possessions.
It was the largest mass migration in human history. My mother saw the
pain, hatred, and killing firsthand and decided to live as inclusion, as
oneness. We rejoiced during Eid with her friend Miraja; lit lamps and
candles in Diwali celebrations with our neighbors; celebrated Buddha
Purnima in the monastery, feeding monks and chanting; and celebrated
Christmas with Santa Claus at the Shillong Golf Club. I liked the
storytelling sessions at Aurobindo Centre and Ramakrishna Mission. My
mother’s engagement kindled in me a lifelong reverence for the sacred.
During my work with different echelons of society, with people from
different walks of life in various countries, I have noticed patterns in the
major stumbling blocks to people working to build community, provide
equitable services, and promote the presence of fairness and joy. People
usually speak in the corridors of their workplace and in social gatherings
about “us” and “them,” formulating rules and applying norms that do not
necessarily produce the stated results. Unwittingly, and sometimes covertly,
strategies are designed for “us” without including “them.” These exclusionsare usually based on religion, race, caste, nationality, tribe, political
ideology, gender, class—all social identities. Often these biases are not
conscious and remain unexamined.
The economic policies and financial systems established today by a few
people contribute to amassing enormous wealth for “us” at the cost of
“them.”
Embodying universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion can
transform social norms and systems that alienate and diminish people and
groups and disenfranchise them. It is also the only way we can interrupt the
social divides and inequities of classism, racism, casteism, sexism,
tribalism, homophobia, patriarchy, identity politics, and hate-filled
religiosity. In Chapter Nine, we explore what it means for everybody to
participate as a radical systems and cultural transformer and disrupt these
negative patterns.
Professional Profile
Some people identify totally with their work and profession. Worldwide,
such workers, professionals, and experts may be well-meaning, but they
solve problems by implementing technical solutions. They feel strongly
about their knowledge and skills and may be unwilling to hear others. They
dominate conversations, often without being aware of it. Consequently, staff
are not able to contribute to the solution. Professionals, experts, and
workers, like anyone else, make decisions that are colored by their own
