subconscious, by unexamined prejudices based on their own socialization.
The insatiable desire to have more has led to distortions in our perception
of what is really needed, compromising what is most important for society.
For example, the salaries of primary school teachers are low in almost every
country. Primary school teachers are professionals requiring profound
understanding and skills to educate and nurture our most precious resource
for the future of humanity. The wealthy who can afford the best education
for their own children often have no problem ignoring massively
underfinanced public schools. This perpetuates social and economic divide
and inequity.
The growing inequities we see in society today are intertwined with the
way we “commodify” professional talent. For example, the purpose of
clinical professions is healing, and that of professionals in health insurance
companies is to support human beings to heal and remain healthy. Yet, in
the current financial paradigm, CEOs of hospitals are expected to make
profit; and some of them get bonuses while economizing on staff salaries or
retrenching clinical staff, reflecting the limited industrial mindset of
efficiency and cost saving.
Many professionals, experts, and workers collaborate beyond disciplinary
boundaries and serve for the well-being of people and our planet. Our skills
and professions are assets, our offering to humanity, and our way to earn a
decent life. When we embrace our work and profession grounded in our
universal values and inner capacity, with a clear purpose, our work becomes
part of unfolding our full potential through our contribution. Albert
Schweitzer said, “Success is not the key to happiness. Happiness is the key
to success. If you love what you are doing, you will be successful.”
Fallacy: What I can solve in the world is based on my profession
and expertise. That is all that matters to produce changes
needed in my workplace and the world.
New Learning: The way we express our professional talents and
abilities, as well as the skills we have for managing, creating, and
manifesting professionally, contribute to success, prosperity, and
service in the world as we transcend our social biases andsource our inner capacities and wisdom for action.
Personality Profile
Our personality profile is our personal style of expression. It is the
particular way we move in the world and how we wish to be seen in the
world. It includes our particular strengths, as well as limitations and defense
mechanisms. Our personality is a contributing factor to the unique way we
can create and act from our inner capacity, our essence.
Some people mistakenly think that our personalities determine who we
are. During a learning-in-action session with business executives I asked
who they really were. More than half answered promptly: “I am INTJ” or “I
am ESFP” or “…”—stating one of the sixteen distinctive types in MBTI
(Myers-Briggs Type Indicator). The Enneagram distinguishes nine
personality types, encouraging us to transcend our egos and understand
ourselves with greater depth. No one personality type is better than another,
whether it is assessed by MBTI, the Enneagram, or other methods.
Our inner essence, our oneness, the universal values we stand for, is
different from our personalities. No one is excluded from contributing to the
changes we need in this world—and any personality type is fine.
Fallacy: There are some personality types that are very good,
and if I had that personality type, I would be more successful.
New Learning: All personality types are adequate—none is
better than any other. My personality is my style of expression. I
contribute in a unique way based on my personality type when I
source my inner capacities and wisdom for action, accept
diversity and embrace others, and use my professional abilities
skillfully.
Here is another example of transcending national, ethnic, and cultural
boundaries. Helena Becker works for health and well-being in a clinic,
leverages change through study groups, and organizes the Stewardship for
New Emergence learning-in-action program with three others in Auroville,
India. Auroville works to realize human unity through inner growth and its
manifestation in the outer world through action.
For Helena, all service to others is “conscious service” emanating from
compassion. She says, “I ask myself: First of all, how does my inner being
relate to the issue or project? How does it want to express itself? What
drives me to participate and commit? As soon as I am clear, it is easy to
connect, and new ideas emerge on what needs to change and how. I learned
to do whatever I do with love and passion. I admire the simplicity in the
complexity of our work using the conscious full-spectrum response, and it
is a design that satisfies logic but allows and invites our deepest knowing,
beyond logic. It inspires me to manifest my intention, fully focused on the
goal and universal values. After the first stewardship program my
commitment toward working on supportive, flexible, and progressive
organizational structure became clear.”
Helena was born into an ethnic German family in the former Soviet
Union. She says, “In my childhood I dreamed about a thriving society. Soon
I observed many controversies and hypocrisies in the system; at the same
time I could imagine society based on the universal values of equity and
unity. In 1977 our family was allowed to immigrate to West Germany.
Rejecting the overall capitalist approach, I acknowledged the social aspect
of the German society that provides for people’s basic needs and safety
nets. But the consumption pressure fueling greed for more and more was
driving people into modern slavery. The ideals of Auroville resonatedcompletely with my inner dream, and I moved. I steadily follow my dream
and search for ways to implement changes toward ‘what life is not and yet
must be’ wherever I go.”
Helena expresses her potential, open to living beyond conventional
cultural boundaries.
Fallacy: I must follow my profession and social tradition—it is the
only way I will get respect from my family and society and be
happy.
New Learning: Happiness is a profound state of BEING that
comes from sourcing my inner capacity or wisdom to inform my
actions and my creativity. When I am able to embrace all the
parts of myself—my inner wisdom, personality, professional skills,
and social aspects—without excluding others, I can be happy,
productive, peaceful, and prosperous.
I was traveling by train from New York to Philadelphia when an elderly
lady inquired whether she could sit near me. I was curious why she asked
me because the seat was empty. “You know, sometimes people don’t like to
sit down near very old people, and I am ninety-two years old,” Irene said.
We had a great conversation. She was a housewife and a concerned citizen,
passionate about the state of the world’s peace, and her late husband was an
eminent scientist. In 1955, Irene Goldman met the secretary-general of the
United Nations, Dag Hammarskjöld, along with Paul Robeson (whose
songs touched me deeply as a child). She recalled saying to the secretary-
general, “My children cannot play in peace in Brooklyn as long as the war
in Korea is on, and children cannot play in peace in their streets in Korea.”
She engaged through community organizations to promote local and global
peace. I was inspired by Irene and invited her to lunch at the delegates’
lounge at the United Nations to honor her.
When Irene arrived, she was surprised and said the United Nations
Secretariat looked pretty awful compared to fifty years ago. “There are so
many gates! We didn’t have any security at that time. We just walked in,
and it was so easy.” Later she sent me a thank-you note with the stamp of
Paul Robeson on the envelope.
Creative and courageous leaders and stewards generated results in
difficult situations. What was common among them? They loosened the
stranglehold of exclusive social identities or dominating professional
stances. As stewards and leaders, they cultivated the ability to source their
own and others’ inner wisdom and the universal values for strategic action,
and fostered this as the most important determinant of equitable and
sustainable change.
We can practice and apply our new insights and learning. We can be
mindful, notice our changes and growth, and break ineffective habits.
We can be aware of our socially constructed biases and notice when
they come up in conversations or actions at home, in our workplace, and
in society; and we are able to shift our perspectives by sourcing our
inner capacities.
When our friends or family members ridicule or stereotype others on the
basis of their social profile, we can speak up and generate conversations
that allow all of us to see how valuable every human being is.
We notice exclusion and labeling based on prejudice in the workplace,
in society, and at home, and speak up to change the situation.
We intentionally expand our circle of friends and colleagues to include
diversity.Bridging Our Nondual and Dual Worlds
The nondual universe is where you and I are one—as Rumi says, “where
there is no each or other.” The dual world is the outer world we live in,
where there are differences, and our experiences are named, defined, and
measurable. When the dual world is informed by our oneness—the nondual
self and universe—a different set of realities and entities emerges, creating
a thriving interdependent world among humans and between humans and
our planet.
Phenomena in the dual world are interdependent and related through
cause and effect. The world operates largely in a dualistic manner with
many polarities. The complex problems we currently deal with, such as the
inequities of the financial system or the unending conflicts, are based upon
exclusionary cultural human behaviors. They cannot be resolved by the
linear reductionist thinking that created them in the first place.
How do we engage at the cusp of the nondual universe and dual world
and connect who we are with what we do? Our entire work is designed to
bridge the nondual universe and the dual world. Here is a sample of
practical tools and templates that bring about the integration of these two
seemingly divergent worlds.
Bridge: The Conscious Full-Spectrum Response
To unleash our full potential we need to picture the whole, then bridge the
nondual “picture-less” universe and the dual world of cultural norms,
systems, and action. In this whole picture, three spaces can be activated
simultaneously through inquiry, insight, and specific results orientation.
Figure 6-2 illustrates the conscious full-spectrum response, which is used to
bridge the dual and nondual worlds in project design.
The first space is the nondual universe, where we are one, and we stand
in our unique universal values, such as dignity, compassion, and
fairness, and commit to action.
At the cusp of the nondual universe and the dual world stakeholders
articulate the core values for the project, for example: equity, integrity.
The second space is the dual world of invisible, multiple patterns and
systems, cultural norms, and “rules of the game” that require us to
create alternative strategies for equitable and sustainable results.
The third space is the dual world of solutions and action, including
technical know-how to solve problems.
When we keep walking on the bridges connecting our inner wisdom
located in the nondual universe and our manifestation in the dual world, our
work becomes sheer joy! It is an expression of the beauty of our BEING.
Fallacy: There is no clear way to connect my essence, my inner
capacity, with action in the world. These are two entirely different
spaces.
New Learning: Knowing what I stand for, my inner capacity, and
articulating the core universal values applicable to everyone and
then making these universal values the basis for strategic actions
provides clear pathways for generating results in our projects
with stakeholders.
Bridge: Compassion—Universal Heart Responses
Leading to Action
A compassionate open heart is a space of endless possibilities—a nondual
space of higher consciousness where spacious awareness is in unison with
mind, emotions, and body. Emotions felt in the moment as well as feelingsprocessed over time are an intrinsic part of us too. When we distinguish
these two spaces with awareness and act from our courageous
compassionate heart, we bridge the nondual and dual spaces by BEING
through action.
In one learning-in-action program, participants discussed what happens
when they walk on the bridge connecting their compassionate self in the
nondual universe with their dual world of commitment-in-action. They
reflected and said:
I am able to source my inner power and feel abundance and
spaciousness.
Standing in a space of discernment I feel my power and clarity for
transforming the space.
We are in a resonant space, tuned in with others, when we recognize our
inner power and act—we are attuned.
I have the ability to notice and transcend the emotional hurt when I am
typecast and act from my inner power.
When I am focused in my purpose, I let go of control and engage from a
place of flow and co-creation.
Gregg Osofsky shared, “I found many of the people engaged with
Occupy to be difficult personalities—rightfully angry about a system
driving our world to the brink, but unable or unwilling to channel their
anger in productive ways. One incident where I was arrested along with
many other people in Zuccotti Park helped me to see how powerful
empathy and compassion can be when you not only cultivate it in yourself,
but seek it out in others as well. I sat next to a friend in the paddy wagon,
and her handcuffs were cutting off her circulation. She was in a tremendous
amount of pain. Several others on the bus who were already furious about
their arrest were outraged by my friend’s circumstance and were expressing
their anger rather than their concern to the police officers outside the
vehicle. As a result, the intensity of the situation grew and the officers
ignored my pleas for help.
“While I recognized that screaming at a cop was the least likely way to
get what you want, I didn’t know how to distract them from the yelling and
screaming and engage their humanity. Fortunately, a woman who was with
us in the back of the paddy wagon had the presence of mind to start singing
a song we all knew well. We all joined in and the energy in the wagon
shifted completely. From this calmer, quieter space, my effort to reach out
to the officer and engage his compassion for someone in pain worked. We
still ended up in the tombs of the NYPD that night, but he loosened her
cuffs, making the experience a little less painful. I had connected with the
police officer and his humanity.”
Gregg recounted how the space that we created through the Occupy
Manifest learning-in-action programs was something deeply personal for
everyone involved. For some, this was the first time that they engaged in
their personal development in the context of politics and activism. For
Gregg, the most significant aspect of this work in his life was the tools he
received to ground what he does within the political sphere in his deepest
personal wisdom.
Since we so often suppress our feelings, many self-development
programs rightly focus on getting in touch with our feelings in order to
notice and release “blockages.” This does not mean that our compassionate
heart is automatically broken open and we suddenly act courageously
simply because our emotional baggage is lighter. We must expose ourselves
to real situations, be willing to be vulnerable and uncomfortable, and allow
our universal, compassionate heart to break open.Sometimes, life presents these opportunities for us. At other times, we
may choose to put ourselves in situations where we allow our
compassionate hearts to be touched.
Fallacy: Human beings often react to other people’s suffering
because they have “bleeding hearts” and are not being rational;
they are being emotional!
New Learning: My courageous, wide-open heart is a space of
endless possibilities. It is nondual space where I am spacious
awareness, in unison with my mind, emotions, and body; I am
moved to compassionate action in the dual world.
So often, when we speak up from our compassionate, courageous heart
against exploitation, discrimination, or unworkable systems and norms, we
are told that we are being emotional, and our points of view are dismissed
—sometimes intentionally. It is important for us to acknowledge this
heartfelt space of compassionate action as a strong and profound
commitment in service of humanity, say it, and not let misinterpretation or
ridicule negate progress or maintain the status quo.
Bridge: Listening with Our Still Mind and Wide-Open
Compassionate Heart
As human BEINGS, we can learn to still our minds and open our hearts to
listen deeply to people, simultaneously aware that we constantly mentally
filter what people say and do. When we are aware of our mental filters, we
are able to notice when they surface; and this is the beginning of releasing
our biases. When we listen deeply from the nondual universe of our
compassionate heart, we walk on a creative bridge because we are moved to
act in an entirely different manner in the dual world. This way of listening
can be cultivated.
There are many types of listening exercises, and each has a purpose and
utility. Most listening exercises deal with attention, focus, encouragement,
negotiations. This way of listening has a different purpose. It is to listen in a
way that sources our oneness in the nondual universe in order to act in the
dual world. When I learn to still my mind, I am able to listen with my heart
wide open without judging the person or jumping to conclusions.
Jonathan Bartels, an emergency room and palliative care nurse at the
University of Virginia, built a bridge from the nondual universe to the dual
world by listening with an open heart to people in their pain and suffering.
Jonathan says, “With fierce compassion as my source I saw an opportunity
to act working as a trauma nurse, while I was participating in the leadership
program. I witnessed the health care team walk away from the trauma bay
full of disappointment and frustration when a patient died. Chaplaincy was
often present to offer a Judeo-Christian prayer. This prayer meant
something to some, and less to others. I felt we were missing a universality
that could capture the unspoken shared caring. It did not need a policy. It
did not need a mandate. It needed someone from among the health care
team to have a fearlessness to do what is right.
“One day after the death of a trauma patient I spoke up and stopped the
team from leaving the room. I asked them all to take a moment to pause and
in silence together offer recognition of this person in the bed. This was
someone’s child, friend, or family. This was someone who loved and was
loved. Each in our own way and in silence should just take a moment to
recognize this person and to also honor the efforts we have made to attempt
to save this life.
“This one- or two-minute act caught on and is now called ‘The Pause.’
Others started doing it in their own practice. It provides space to heal andtime to invite stillness. It is a simple, reproducible, and universal
intervention. In an organic way it has spread beyond our emergency
department and into areas across our health system.”
Listening with a still and aware mind and an open compassionate heart
creates a willingness to alter our point of view, because we don’t identify
our point of view as “the truth.” As a politician, policy-maker,
businessperson, and citizen, knowing I care deeply for humanity, I listen
from my inner capacity and wisdom, and I am willing to alter my attitudes
that are simply not working!
Fallacy: This touchy-feely way of listening with a still mind and
compassionate heart is not practical, denies what is rational, and
does not produce change.
New Learning: Listening from our deepest source allows
creativity to unfold, informs our thinking, and opens up numerous
possibilities for measurable transformation.
Bridges: Worldviews Emanating from “Who I Am BEING
and What I Stand For”
Having a worldview based on who I am being and what I stand for is
different from perspectives based on logic and ideology. I am using
worldviews to mean views based on universal values and perspectives to
mean views emanating from ideological stances.
The complex world today requires us to be aware of how our
perspectives, ideologies, and worldviews have been formed—from our
education and culture, what we learned from our parents, and what our
parents learned from their families and culture. We don’t need to
necessarily negate our perspectives. But we do need to inquire into the
assumptions behind these perspectives so as to embrace them differently,
based on who we are, what we stand for, and universal values.
We can embrace the culture we are born into and the religion and faith
we have as something that enhances life. Or we can embrace culture in a
way that it becomes a chain that leads us to dogmatic ideologies.
Our understanding determines the decisions we make, and it is too often
fueled by the fear of not complying with traditions lest we be ostracized. It
becomes the justification for divisive propaganda, the prescription for
“moral actions,” instead of a way of discovering the most beautiful, most
powerful, most compassionate space of our being: our oneness. Out of this
discovery, we know that we can listen to each other differently and deeply;
we can listen to each other and be willing to change our point of view
without compromising on any of the universal values.
When we choose to listen deeply, standing in our universal values of
dignity, equity, and compassion, we are willing to alter the limiting
economic policies and disempowering financial systems and norms.
Fallacy: Every group puts together frameworks to understand
how things work and which ideological perspective is the best.
This leads to disagreements and polarization—unfortunately,
nothing can be done!
New Learning: When we are able to stand in our oneness and
are willing to alter our ideological thinking, we can transcend
differences and create a new synthesis resulting in a paradigm
shift.
BEING a Paradigm Shifter
There is a foundational difference between focusing solely on achieving agoal and engaging in order to accomplish a principled game-changing
paradigm shift. I asked “principled game changers” how they describe
themselves when they are aware of both who they are BEING as well as the
results they generate. Here are a few of their reflections on BEING a
paradigm shifter:
Creating a future based on who I am and what I stand for and a clear
knowing of the steps required to generate impact, outcomes, or goals
and outputs or activities—I know where I am going.
Unwavering commitment to results—commitment that goes beyond
intention only and includes strategic action based on universal values.
Unstoppable even when I see obstacles; when I see the results clearly,
the obstacles appear as part of the journey.
Clarity that comes from knowing my stand; an ability to relate to others
through my stand; being “unmessable with”; being courageous.
Ability to hold multiple perspectives without compromising universal
values.
Spacious—expansive; where there is no “me” or “I”—my thoughts
dissolve.
Nonattached, yet engaging passionately—this is different from
withdrawing or detachment.
Using my full influence, not just my authority.
Standing in my power and light and creating opportunity for others and
fostering their growth.
Yearning for a just world—the next step of our evolution.
Do you see yourself as a paradigm shifter?
Fallacy: We need to show results—we need to achieve our
stated goals at any cost.
New Learning: For paradigm shifts and creating a new future,
we need to engage to accomplish goals in a manner that
changes the norms and systems that gave rise to the problems
we are trying to solve; this is simultaneously based on our
oneness, our humanity, and our universal values.
Embodying Values Instead of Talking about Values
BEING a paradigm shifter requires us to embody values instead of only
talking about values. It requires us to avoid fundamentalism or any other
isms or dogmatic perspectives. Our courage to create is grounded firmly in
our universal values, our oneness—the inner capacity, wisdom, full
potential—and our stillness in urgent action.
An example: the United Nations, along with other agencies, has made a
significant contribution toward a just and fair world for humanity and our
planet through the Declaration of Human Rights, the Sustainable
Development Goals, and other agreements. The human rights declaration, in
which each and every human being can aspire to live with dignity and
thrive in a planet where there is no discrimination, is a big step forward in
realizing our unity. The human rights agenda is based on universal values,
applicable to everyone, everywhere.
We designed various instruments to address violations of human rights
and claim our rights. But there’s a big difference between talking about
human rights and universal values and embodying universal values.
When I stand in this place and when there are human rights violations, I
can speak up, speak out, and act to redress them. I speak to what is
incongruent with our humanity, but I do so not with destructive anger or
revenge, not with hatred, but with knowing that human beings are limited
simply because we are human, and our actions are unacceptable when thereare violations.
Cynda Rushton, a professor of bioethics at Johns Hopkins University, is
clear that moving from rhetoric to reality and embodying the values we
wish to see manifest is at the core of sustained transformation. She
integrates mindfulness and compassionate care in clinical practice and
teaching. She says, “One of the most profound aspects of the conscious full-
spectrum response (CFSR) is the grounding in who am I being in this
moment. This orientation has engaged a deep space of personal inquiry with
mindfulness as the foundation for engaging wisdom and compassionate
action. Instead of beginning with what I will do, the CFSR approach invites
inquiry into who I am. What do I stand for? For me, this required
relinquishing a long-standing fear of not being enough to confidently
embrace the idea that BEING who I really am is enough. Clarity in who I
am and what I stand for has provided me with an anchor through many
leadership challenges. For example, while coleading an initiative at a
university, an influential member of the community questioned my
intentions and actions about the design and focus of a large-scale initiative
focusing on compassion. At many junctures, I was called to inquire over
and over again, What do I stand for? Who am I being in this moment? I
found that grounding this inquiry in a space of mindful awareness,
embodying the values I speak about, allowed me to quiet my fear, anger,
and disappointment to respond with clarity, stability, and an unwavering
commitment to integrity, compassion, and equanimity.”
Fallacy: These human rights instruments and conversations on
ethics are about pointing fingers and making others wrong. Let’s
focus on getting the job done!
New Learning: Human rights and ethics are primarily about
embodying the values underpinning these instruments by all,
leading to the highest quality of life for humanity, where we all live
with dignity, thrive without discrimination, and are whole with
integrity.
Stillness
Too often we are given advice to “slow down” instead of “learn to be still.”
During his discourses, my teacher Tsoknyi Rinpoche taught us to practice
stillness and encouraged us to allow our wisdom, our inner capacity to
surface—to create the space between our thoughts.
When I served at the United Nations Secretariat, meditating often in the
meditation room infused in me a sense of peace within. The room, named A
Room of Quiet, was created by Dag Hammarskjöld. He was a profound
secretary-general of the United Nations, an inspiration to many of us.
He wrote a piece that is placed at the entrance to the meditation room in
the United Nations building.
We all have within us a center of stillness surrounded by silence.
This house, dedicated to the work and debate of peace, should have one
room dedicated to silence in the outward sense and stillness in the inner
sense.
It has been the aim to create in this small room a place where the doors
may be open to the infinite lands of thought and prayer.
People of many faiths will meet here, and for that reason none of the
symbols to which we are accustomed in our meditation could be used.
However, there are simple things which speak to all of us with the same
language. We have sought for such things and we believe that we have
found them in a shaft of light striking the shimmering surface of solid
rock.So, in the middle of the room we see a symbol of how, daily, the light of
the skies gives life to the earth on which we stand, a symbol to many of
us of how the light of spirit gives life to matter.
But the stone in the middle of the room has more to tell us. We may see
it as an altar, empty not because there is no God, not because it is an
altar to an unknown god, but because it is dedicated to the God whom
man worships under many names and in many forms.
The stone in the middle of the room reminds us also of the firm and
permanent in a world of movement and change. The block of firm iron
ore has the weight and solidity of the everlasting. It is a reminder of that
cornerstone of endurance and faith on which all human endeavour must
be based.
The material of the stone leads our thoughts to the necessity of choice
between destruction and construction, between war and peace. Of iron,
man has forged his swords, of iron he has also made his ploughshares.
Of iron, he has constructed tanks, but of iron he has likewise built
homes for man. The block of iron ore is part of the wealth we have
inherited on this earth of ours. How can we use it?
The shaft of light strikes the stone in a room of utter simplicity. There
are no other symbols, there is nothing to distract our attention or to
break in on the stillness within ourselves. When our eyes travel from
these symbols to the front wall they meet a simple pattern opening up
the room to harmony, freedom and balance of space.
There is an ancient saying that the sense of a vessel is not in its shell,
but in the void. So it is with this room. It is for those who come here to
fill the void with what they find in their centre of stillness.
Dag Hammarskjöld, 1957
There is a beautiful painting outside the meditation room. In 1964, Marc
Chagall created a stained-glass window titled Peace and gave it to the
United Nations to honor Secretary-General Dag Hammarskjöld and fifteen
others who died in a plane crash on September 17, 1961, while on their way
to broker a ceasefire in the Congo. Hammarskjöld was posthumously
awarded the Nobel Peace Prize for his humanitarian efforts. The 11.7 x
17.7-foot window can be seen in the visitors’ lobby of the United Nations
building in New York. Music and text inspired Chagall in creating the
window’s scheme. Symbols evoke Beethoven’s Ninth Symphony, a favorite
piece of Hammarskjöld’s.
Every act of courage, compassion, and service for all awakens a new
story, a new possibility for healing on the planet.
Fallacy: Working hard and fast is bad for health. It is obsessive!
We must slow down to be effective.
New Learning: It is not about slowing down. It is learning to be
still in the moment, BEING still; and in that stillness, there is
profuse and profound energy.
Contemporary pioneers are impactful because they
Know who they are and what they stand for and transcend their fears.
Are anchored in universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion;
commit and act.
Create platforms for others to source their inner capacity for action.
Hold their social and professional identities without rigidity; ground
themselves in their inner capacities for strategic action in order to
generate equity and sustainability.Notice their own background conversations to release prejudice and
bias.
Distinguish universal heart responses from emotional reactions; move
with compassion.
Generate worldviews sourced from universal values while
understanding ideological perspectives.
Embody the universal values underpinning strategic action, instead of
only talking about values.
Are able to be still in order to respond by sourcing their inner capacity,
rather than reacting.
They use the CFSR and similar transformative design templates to solve
problems and be principled game changers. This is discussed in the next
chapter.
Chapter 6
The Unifying Architect
The Radical Unique Response: Design
Differently to Make a Difference!
The world we have made as a result of the level of thinking we have done
thus far creates problems that we cannot solve at the same level at which
we have created them.… We shall require a substantially new manner of
thinking if humankind is to survive.
ALBERT EINSTEIN
A designer is an emerging synthesis of artist, inventor, mechanic,
objective economist and evolutionary strategist.
BUCKMINSTER FULLER
And every citizen is a designer!
Our Design Capability
The unifying architect is simultaneously the contemporary pioneer, mindful
pro-activist, and radical transformer.
The unifying architect is fearless, has reverence for life in all its
manifestations, and sees the beauty of uniqueness and diversity in our
inherent oneness, in the interdependence of humans, all sentient beings, and
our earth. The unifying architect is a radical designer who is not afraid to
see human reality with discernment for what it has become—a reality
currently drenched with inequity and identity barriers, even identity
fortresses. Unifying architects identify systems and cultural opportunities as
well as dysfunctionality and engage in crafting a new architecture based on
universal values of compassion, fairness, and dignity. They do this even in
the face of ridicule, opposition, obstruction, and slander.
The unifying architect
Has the compassionate courage to act.
Is a principled game changer, sourcing inner capacities and creativity.
Sees and takes into account the invisible patterns, norms, and systems as
well as root factors that shape global and local situations and actions.
Crafts policies, projects, and processes for enduring equitable and
sustainable change.
Is proficient in using transformative design templates such as the
conscious full-spectrum response, synergistic operational strategies, and
transformational results chain.
Generates transformational results, steadily contributing to paradigm
shifts.How Does a Radical Fearless Architect Design
Differently to Make a Difference?
Ehab El Kharrat is an example of a radical fearless architect. We worked
together in the United Nations program to address HIV/AIDS in the Arab
States. In a world where wars are being fought on religiosity and religious
affiliation, it was inspiring to see Khadija, a Muslim woman born in
Tunisia, work powerfully and seamlessly side by side with Ehab, a
Christian man born in Egypt. Individually, they stood for dignity and equity
for everyone, beyond social divisions. As team members, they created
unique spaces for staff and partners to transform cultural norms and systems
while addressing HIV/AIDS. You first met Khadija in Chapter Five. Ehab
too is a remarkable professional committed to making a concrete difference
and generating transformational results wherever he works. Even when
there is ridicule, disagreement, opposition, obstruction, and slander, Ehab is
unstoppable.
There were other architects of equitable and sustainable change on
Khadija’s team. Nabil Elkot, a physician from Egypt, engaged to reverse the
HIV/AIDS epidemic. He also worked in several other fields, including with
civilian and peace activists in Egypt and Syria and in his clinic with his
clients and colleagues to address trauma. His worldviews and actions that
embrace everyone inspire people to transcend the exclusionary religiosity
that is pervasive. Olfat Allam is another example. In addition to her
contribution to the HIV/AIDS program, she supports women and girls to
heal from trauma and manifest their full potential. She says the learning-in-
action programs refined her skills and competencies: “I treat people with
my heart, not only my mind. I understand the deep meaning of empathy. I
support people in greater depth using the new tools which are
comprehensive and useful. I have a new vision which makes me persist,
hopeful and free, working toward a vibrant society in Egypt.”
As the executive director of Freedom, a nongovernmental organization
working on substance abuse and HIV, Ehab applied the transformational
leadership tools and approaches with his team of 190 staff members, most
of them recovering drug users. Moving beyond stigma to manifest the full
potential of people is not easy. Ehab says, “As the founder and leader of a
growing organization, I do things differently. I recognize ‘emotional
intelligence competencies’ in my team and foster them. I use the conscious
full-spectrum response (CFSR) in designing and planning our work. I have
been working to shift the stigma against addicts since 1989. Using these
universal value-based approaches, generative conversations and tools made
a huge difference and rapidly accelerated change. Now I can safely say that
the culture in Egypt is shifting. Recovering addicts are being recognized as
full-fledged citizens and professionals and current drug users are given their
rights to health and respect. I proposed some laws to consolidate these
gains, but the second wave of revolutionary protests in June 2013 led to our
resignation as opposition parliamentarians from the legislative body. I have
not given up.”
This brings us to a foundational aspect of this work: standing steady with
compassion and courage, “giving up” does not exist in one’s BEING. It is
really impossible to give up when our compassionate universal heart is
open; we burn with an inner fire of will and fairness and “see” with
discernment and clarity what needs to be done.
Ehab says, “This whole array of tools, techniques, and approaches, which
was the foundation of the results I accomplished, made me think in a clearer
way, opened my consciousness to the true roots of my commitment. Now
whenever I face a challenge or examine a dilemma, I am able to use a
number of leadership lenses. But I always, almost automatically, look at it
through the lens of the conscious full-spectrum response template, whichhelps me to design the response. This has helped me during the years of the
Arab Spring revolutions; I was able to better communicate with other
leaders, even with political ‘rivals’ or ‘competitors,’ and with the media.”
Ehab was one of the main campaign leaders of the presidential candidate
who lost to President Abdel Fattah el-Sisi. He notes, “I am still well
respected as an opposition leader, appearing on national, regional, and
international TV at least two or three times a week, on some days two or
three times a day. I believe I have contributed to a shift in the political and
democratic consciousness of the people in Egypt. I guess people like to host
me as a TV speaker because of this unique transformational approach that
makes complex principles and ideas simple, accessible, meaningful, and
comprehensive to the average audience.” In Chapter Eleven, you will meet
Ehab again in his political role.
It is people like Ehab who bring the most important missing piece for
enduring, equitable results—our universal values, our humanity, our
compassionate courage-in-action; in short, our inner capacities.
How do we develop these abilities? Through choosing to be an alchemist
and manifest our full potential, generate equitable and sustainable change,
and learn and practice the transformative tools, techniques, and templates
necessary for a paradigm shift.
Praxis of a unifying architect:
1. Explores ways to continuously deepen personal transformation and
practices consistently.
2. Steadfastly commits to equitable and sustainable results; learns new
tools and techniques to craft the alternative architecture that shifts
paradigms.
3. Embraces confusion as a creative space for emergence.
4. Cultivates the ability to see connections and patterns with discernment
and pristine clarity.
5. Fearlessly examines and redefines the prevailing notions of underlying
or root causes/factors of today’s major challenges (e.g., people are poor
because they are lazy, or it is their “karma”) and does not blindly agree
to unexamined assumptions and superficial explanations that
camouflage reality.
These aspects of the praxis are elaborated in the paragraphs that follow.
1. Explores Ways to Continuously Deepen Personal Transformation
First, the fearless architect explores ways to continuously deepen personal
transformation and practices constantly. In the previous chapter, we
discussed some of these ways and practices. We touched our essence,
articulated what we stand for and the universal values we embody. We
moved past our fears, anchoring ourselves in our inner capacities for action.
We chose to release our prejudices and biases. We recommitted to
compassionate, courageous action when we transcended norms and biases
to stop female genital mutilation. We created platforms to foster
stewardship and leadership in others.
Fallacy: If everyone works on their personal transformation,
everything else will fall into place, and the current problems in the
world will be resolved.
New Learning: Personal transformation is a necessary but not
sufficient condition for social transformation. We need to have the
courage to design and implement the appropriate architecture for
systems and culture to result in a thriving humanity and planet.2. Commits to Equitable and Sustainable Results
Second, the unifying architect commits to making a concrete difference by
generating transformational results based on universal values; and learns
new tools, techniques, and templates to craft alternative architecture that
shifts paradigms for equitable and sustainable results.
Self-discovery alone will not transform the planet unless we also commit
to strategic action. Self-discovery may pave the way for different
perspectives and actions to emerge; but only if we choose to source our
inner capacities, manifest them through our actions, and put in place the
architecture and actions for equitable and sustainable change. Contrary to
what several consciousness gurus and human potential experts say,
equitable and sustainable transformation does not “automatically” flow
from self-discovery. Andrew Harvey, an author and scholar, coined the
phrase “sacred activism” in his writing, teaching, and speaking to describe
the absolute necessity of embarking on our personal journeys of self-
awareness while at the same time manifesting our inner capacities to
transform the norms and systems that create the major problems of today.
3. Embraces Confusion as a Creative Space for Emergence
Third, the fearless architect embraces confusion. They know that radically
different approaches are necessary, that they do not know all the pathways
to bring about the necessary transformation. They are willing to go beyond
rationales and logic and allow their minds to be confused before new
clarities emerge for strategic action.
Embracing confusion as a creative space instead of fretting and trying to
understand everything before actually engaging allows us to use our
experience and expertise differently, opening up new pathways for strategic
action. It does not mean procrastination, which is delaying action. It means
constantly renewing oneself and having a “beginner’s mind.”
Throughout our lives, we are taught that confusion is a negative space;
we are trained to be clear and are praised for knowing the answer. The
unifying architect, however, is a person who doesn’t have all the answers
but has some very profound questions. Einstein said, “to raise new
questions, new possibilities, to regard old problems from a new angle,
requires creative imagination and marks real advance in science.” If we
cannot ask the right questions and we constantly avoid talking about what
we don’t want to see, we reinforce the same unacceptable reality.
Embracing confusion simply means I do not know all the answers. It does
not fit into my current way of thinking, but I am willing to remain in this
space. I remain in confusion because I burn for a just and dignified reality
for all beings; I am willing to explore new design alternatives, and I trust I
will find the pathways.
Fallacy: It is important to have certainty if I want to be effective.
Confusion is a sign that I am ignorant; I must show my
colleagues that I know and that I am right.
New Learning: Confusion simply means I am unlearning and
relearning, so my mind cannot figure it out with my old
frameworks and methods. I embrace confusion and am patient,
knowing that when I stand in my universal values and commit to
equitable and sustainable results, I will learn to design differently
to make a difference. I am comfortable and able to “hold
questions or ask new questions, even if I don’t have the answer”
as an essential part of progress.
4. Sees Connections and Patterns with Discernment and Pristine
Clarity
Fourth, the unifying fearless architect sees connections and patterns withdiscernment and pristine clarity. They understand the invisible, multiple
patterns and systems that shape our lives and shape society and the world,
recognizing interdependence as a core principle in generating equitable and
sustainable change. An enhanced ability to exercise critical thinking about
self and society leads unifying architects to be more effective and impactful
by shifting the systems and norms that maintain the status quo.
Currently, in every part of the world, the thinking on policies, rules, and
regulations is done by a few on behalf of the many—a pattern—with the
assumption and justification that citizens do not have the expertise to
understand and influence policy nor the ability to think critically. Humans,
regardless of education, have the innate ability to see patterns and to create
new systems and forms.
Because critical strategic thinking is possible for all, not just the experts,
we need to demystify technical jargon and create ways to stimulate critical
and pattern thinking along with authentic processes for everyone to have
their voice heard. This is critical for shifting the current paradigm. Too
many platforms intentionally have “token” participation to superficially
show diversity in decision-making processes without creating spaces for
genuine, informed participation.
Uma Haimavati, whom you first met in Chapter Five, sees connections
and patterns with pristine clarity. As the founder of Upasana design studio
in Auroville, India, she uses her expertise to create beauty through fashion
and fabric, while simultaneously responding to cultural and social issues.
Uma created Upasana to also provide opportunities for work, skills
development, and growth for women and men from local villages, and as a
platform where students and volunteers from different parts of India and
abroad come to learn and contribute to socially responsible design. Uma
created modern designs and textiles from India’s disappearing traditional
fabrics while simultaneously revitalizing the economic opportunities for
weavers.
Uma says, “After the stewardship program, my relationship with my
team has improved. I am more approachable. I have learned to listen,
stopped taking things personally and saw people’s commitment for action
behind their complaints. I see a bigger and wider picture behind my actions.
And every member of my team sees the same. As an organization that uses
organic materials, has zero waste in production, fair wages, we collectively
saw our contribution to the world and to sustainable change and became
more conscious of our responsibilities. Because almost all my team
members went through the same process, Upasana’s organizational
language changed.”
Uma transformed her workplace; Caitlin Wiesen worked to shift cultural
norms related to social issues, for example, women’s inheritance rights.
Caitlin Wiesen was a member of our HIV/AIDS team at UNDP and
continues to work with transformative practices in various positions she
holds at the United Nations. Worldwide, women are disproportionately
excluded from owning or inheriting property. Even where the country’s
laws indicate equal inheritance rights for women and men, customary
practice continues to exclude women. Ethiopia is no exception. In the
leadership program in Ethiopia that Caitlin and Allan Henderson
implemented, participants noticed the incongruence between their universal
values and the cultural norms in their community. They took action with
their families to ensure that widows inherited property, shifting the cultural
norm.
Fallacy: In any organization, systems, norms, and structures
need to be designed primarily to increase performance.
Employees need to be motivated to complete tasks toward thatend. They do not need to think about the bigger and deeper
questions. This leads to chaos, and it is not practical or profitable
to do so.
New Learning: When formal leadership chooses to establish a
work culture that sources people’s inner capacities for creativity
and work processes and connects them to the bigger picture,
every organization can have a thriving work culture with systems,
norms, and structures based on authentic information and
universal values. This is both profitable for the organization and
wholesome for employees.
5. Fearlessly Speaks about Underlying Factors of Major Challenges
Fifth, the unifying architect recognizes and fearlessly speaks about the
underlying or root factors of today’s major challenges, instead of blindly
agreeing to unexamined assumptions and camouflaging them. In explaining
our global and local crises we generally focus on the importance of
economic, social, and political forces and factors as causes for the
persistence of the world’s problems, or the problems in a local town or
village. We say the underlying cause for lack of education, or food or health
care, is poverty. But poverty in our abundant world is a result of individual
unbridled greed manifested through financial and economic policies that
serve a few and leave out many, combined with a culture that perpetuates
inequity and discrimination.
The mindset with which we collect, analyze, interpret, and present data
and opinions and positions on any subject directs our response. Currently,
when we study problems and causality, we identify the immediate and
systems causes; and inevitably, we think of particular conditions such as
poverty, illiteracy, and disparity, as the “underlying or root causes” of
problems. This must be redefined to shift the current paradigm.
Let’s take the example of childhood mortality. The immediate cause may
be death due to pneumonia; systems causes may be lack of access to
treatment or overcrowding in an urban slum. When we find that the family
does not have money, analysts will pin the underlying cause on poverty. But
poverty is not an underlying or root cause; it is a result of the systems and
cultural norms we have put in place. Poverty is a systems cause in a world
of abundance. Poverty ensues from unequal opportunity, inequities,
disproportionately high resources taken by those with influence, and a
belief that some people are better and more deserving than others. Yet we
continue to tell ourselves we can do nothing until those who are poor step
up and earn a decent living! The root cause or factor, the underlying cause,
is our apathetic, unconcerned mindset emanating from human beings who
have not touched their inner compassionate core. As Nelson Mandela said,
“Poverty is not an accident. Like slavery and apartheid, it is man-made and
can be removed by the actions of human beings.”
The underlying or root cause rests in an acausal domain—the domain
where there is no cause or effect, only emergence, resonance, light, oneness.
To address the challenges we face today, we must recognize the root factors
as an integral part of strategic design and response. The same principles that
apply at work apply to what I do in my family, community, or society.
In our daily lives, too often we find superficial reasons for what seems to
be going awry. The root factors of a child not getting good grades in school
may be beyond inadequate learning methods or the child not working hard
enough. It may be that the measures of success need to be redefined to
include the joy of mastery and learning itself! The root factors of aggressive
behaviors at work, at home, or in school lie beyond socially acceptable
manners: they are sourced from our inability to transcend our fears, orjudgment, or the defense mechanisms we may use to protect our ego or
image. BEING a radical, fearless architect of our lives and of society
requires us to see the problems we face in the context of our systems and
culture and dive deeper into the root factors the give rise to these problems!
Conscious Full-Spectrum Responses
What is an appropriate architecture for equitable and sustainable results?
What is missing foundationally in the way we respond?
Fallacy: In today’s world, the most important elements for
projects to produce the intended results are a knowledge-based,
robust design and good management skills related to inputs,
outputs, and relationship management.
New Learning: For enduring results, the design and
implementation of projects or ideas need to harness knowledge
and skills and address the systems and cultural causes as well
as root factors of the problem. The key is to source inner
capacities and universal values for both design and
implementation. This work is about BEING through action,
embodying universal values for personal and planetary
transformation—at home, in our workplace, in society, and for the
planet.
CFSR: A Design Template
The conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR) framework is a tool to design
projects that make a difference. This design template intertwines three
threads of the triple helix of a paradigm shift: (a) source our wisdom/inner
capacities and universal values for action; (b) shift cultural norms, systems,
and structures that maintain the status quo and become principled game
changers; and (c) solve problems in order to generate specific equitable and
sustainable results.
Special features of the conscious full-spectrum response design
framework:
1. Personal transformation leads to societal and planetary transformation.
2. The CFSR template seamlessly integrates people’s inner power and
universal values—the most vital assets of humanity worldwide—to
shift systems and cultural norms and solve problems.
3. It is a principled game-changing architecture for equitable and
sustainable change.
4. It is broad-based enough to take diverse conditions into account, and at
the same time to design specific innovations, generate breakthroughs,
and sustain the changes.
5. It provides a direction without prescription for emergence of
appropriate responses to generate specific and measurable results.
6. It integrates the “what” (results), the “how” (transformative
methodologies), and the “why” (the root factors) into one effective
response through projects or initiatives.
7. Everyone can participate meaningfully. It demystifies the complexity of
strategy and planning to shift from token participation of people to
informed decision-making. The CFSR template addresses complexity
without being complicated—it is an art to simplify without being
simplistic.
8. The CFSR is subject-agnostic and can therefore can be used for any
topic or problem.
9. It is a transdisciplinary template for strategic action, robust enough to
hold the frameworks of different disciplines and various schools of
thought, retaining the needed rigor of excellence.Feature 1: Personal to Societal to Planetary Transformation
The CFSR template is designed to manifest personal transformation that
leads to societal and planetary transformation. Personal transformation
bridges the nondual universe with strategic action in the dual world,
manifesting in transformative results (outer, middle, inner circles of the
template, Figure 6-2), as one seamless whole.
Most frameworks that refer to the inner dimension of human beings
enhance our understanding of human nature, and they have their place and
purpose. However, simply understanding ourselves and the parameters of
the challenge is not enough; we need to respond to critical challenges with
urgency. The conscious full-spectrum response template requires us to
engage in a specific set of inquiries and questions to examine the context of
the problem in order to solve it sustainably; the template is designed to
generate responses connecting our personal transformation to social and
planetary transformation.
Feature 2: Sourcing Wisdom/Inner Power/Universal Values
To source wisdom, inner capacity, inner power, what I stand for, is to
embody the universal values of dignity, fairness, and compassion in order to
take action. I use these four words/phrases interchangeably as expressions
of our full potential. When we manifest wisdom in action, we address the
underlying causes or root factors of an issue. We operate from a passionate,
enthusiastic place, which is essential for empowering others and inspiring
them to take action.
The conscious full-spectrum response template requires us to constantly
ask ourselves “What is the source of my action?” It is a profound inquiry to
shift mindset and our current paradigm.
Feature 3: Principled Game-Changing Architecture for Equitable and
Sustainable Change
The CFSR template creates the architecture for an emerging paradigm
necessary for equitable and sustainable change. For the paradigm shift to
occur, we must design and generate responses that integrate the different
domains that are related to the entangled hierarchies of any given situation:
we must source our individual inner capacities, wisdom, and universal
values for collective action. In solving problems, we must have the
compassionate courage to address systems and cultural causes as well as
root factors of the problem.
Most social, political, economic, and business forums were set up with
altruistic or pragmatic intentions; and then human limits and egos entered
unintentionally and imperceptibly. Embodying and sourcing the universal
values of dignity, fairness, and compassion is essential for real
transformation, not the rhetoric.
Laws, policies, and procedures create systems and structures with defined
rules. We need to constantly review these and the “rules of the game” to
ensure integrity with the outcomes, goals, and impacts we wish to make in
support of a thriving people and planet. When we source our inner
capacities for shifting systems, structures, and policies, we are not rigid or
attached to our point of view; we are vibrant, dynamic, ethical; able to
renew ourselves, our strategic direction and actions.
Cultural norms are societies’ and citizens’ expression of the “rules of the
game” and must be constantly reviewed too, based on universal values. This
is what transforms exclusionary and exploitative ways that diminish
individuals and groups, embraces change, and creates new ways for all of
us to unfold into our full potential.
When we do not make the rules underpinning cultural norms and systems
visible, do not intentionally examine them and publicly articulate what wesee, we maintain the status quo and the present inequities, divisive cultural
identities, and financial systems that concentrate money for few at the
expense of many.
Fallacy: I feel powerless to shift norms, systems, and structures
—I am just a worker, or just a housewife.
New Learning: Game-changing solutions and perspectives
based on universal values manifest in every situation: from the
interpretations parents and family members give to their children
to the way a consultant, worker, community member, or policy-
maker sees and responds in their work or life.
Feature 4: Broad-Based, Context-Specific Breakthroughs and
Results
The CFSR template can be used in diverse settings and conditions, as we
design and implement specific initiatives and ideas. When we shift systems,
structures, and cultural norms without sourcing our inner capacities and
universal values, we become dogmatic, considering our own social,
political, or economic ideology to be the truth and the only way.
Consequently, we polarize the situation and miss many opportunities.
It does not matter whether we support a particular party and identify
ourselves as Republicans, Democrats, Libertarians, or Independents in the
United States or with the Congress Party or BJP in India; it does not matter
what faith we follow, or how famous we are as academics, politicians,
movie stars, innovators, artists, billionaires, philanthropists. What matters is
whether we can manifest our full potential in every context, through every
political group, every religion, every profession, in order to heal our aching
humanity and earth, and thrive. The conscious full-spectrum response
template requires us to stand in the inner power of dignity, equity, and
compassion to foundationally disrupt the patterns of exclusion and
domination and mindfully establish principled game-changing solutions.
Feature 5: Provides Direction without Prescription for Generating
Results
Through CFSR, we design responses to generate specific and measurable
results. When we connect universal value-based actions to changes in
policy, cultural norms, systems, and structures, we establish new rules of
the game, changing what gives rise to the problems in the first place.
When we do not source inner capacities for action in order to generate
systems and normative shifts, we move ahead with a “fix-it” mindset; then
we wonder why we have a never-ending list of problems. It is because we
keep in place unworkable patterns.
For example, to address climate change, we need action based on
technically sound solutions or outputs, like establishing sources of
renewable energy; we also need to change the limiting norms and systems
that determine how fossil fuels are connected to our economies, individual
wealth, and production processes. We must inquire within to see how each
one of us individually and collectively relates to our planet and act from
what we see. Can we see that our very existence is nurtured by our planet,
or do we view our planet as a commodity to exploit and use for the never-
ending list of things we think we must have?
Fallacy: If we can identify the problem and its causes, and we
have the right technologies, we will be able to solve the problem.
We are not solving the problems because we are not investing
enough time and money in technical solutions.
New Learning: The complex problems of today are the result ofmultiple interdependent causes. As we invest in technologies to
solve problems, we must simultaneously address the cultural
norms and systems that maintain the conditions giving rise to
these problems. To address the root factors of these problems,
the universal values unpinning our humanity need to be the
foundation of our dynamic and impactful engagement.
Feature 6: Integrates the “What,” the “How,” and the “Why” into a
Whole-Systems Response
The conscious full-spectrum response is based on skills, competencies, and
inner capacities that simultaneously include the what, how, and why of
design, strategy, and implementation—a whole-systems response.
Application and manifestation of our inner capacity toward a particular
issue that addresses the root factors of a problem, and the systems and
norms we establish, corresponds to the why of that issue. When we do not
have the ability to address root factors based on universal values and what
we stand for, we are unable to generate enduring transformation and create
systems and cultures that deliver equity, dignity, and prosperity to everyone.
Over the last several decades, we have demarcated the skills and
competencies as well as the roles of experts. We have separated the “subject
matter” experts who shape strategies and tactics of what needs to be done
from “process” experts who guide initiatives and organizations on how
things need to get done.
When we separate the why, the what, and the how, “process” experts
become neutral to the policies and goals of organizations, seldom
questioning whether their goals have intended or unintended consequences.
Integrating the why, what, and how in the fabric of universal values lays
the foundation for the emergence of paradigm shifts, combining purpose,
goals, and processes that generate equitable and sustainable results. The
CFSR template enables this integration.
Feature 7: Sophisticated Simplicity to Address Complexity—Strategy
and Planning Demystified
We need to demystify complex strategy and planning processes in order to
shift from token participation to informed decision-making that creates
openings for everyone to participate.
As more countries and people accept democracy, everyone’s informed
and wise participation is essential. However, people with influence and
extrinsic power, including policy analysts and experts in every part of the
world, formulate policies with only the token participation of nonexpert
people. The thinking on policies, rules, and regulations done by a few
people impacts all of us. Experts do have valuable knowledge in particular
areas, which must be used. The assumption that citizens do not have the
ability to think through complex issues is not valid.
When we do not demystify the complexity of strategy and planning, we
close down opportunities for citizens to participate meaningfully.
Demystification is crucial for the new paradigm to emerge.
The CFSR template addresses complexity without being complicated—it
is an art to simplify without being simplistic! This work is about
sophisticated simplicity.
Feature 8: A Subject-Agnostic Template
The CFSR approach is subject-agnostic and therefore can be used for any
topic or problem. Universal values apply in all settings, irrespective of what
we choose to work on.For example, colleagues at the United Nations who used the CFSR
approach to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic also used this approach when
engaging citizens in fair and responsible elections, and when working for
women’s rights and inheritance of property, for human rights, and for
quality education.
Feature 9: A Transdisciplinary Template for Strategic Action
The CFSR is a transdisciplinary template for strategic action, robust enough
to hold the frameworks of different disciplines and various schools of
thought. To address complex problems, we need to partner with people who
are from different disciplines, cross sectorial boundaries, and take a whole-
systems approach. Citizens, policy-makers, and experts, from various
disciplines and professions, create new knowledge and understanding for
strategic action, always anchored in a resonant space of oneness and
universal values. They transcend ideology and work together to create
value-based alternatives.
Distinguishing Conscious Full-Spectrum Responses
from Partial Responses
Most issues that affect our daily lives are complex. And most of the time,
we have partial responses—we don’t include the three key elements of the
CFSR framework. There is nothing wrong with partial engagement, but it
limits the results we can potentially generate. We can produce far greater
results if we design our projects in a way that solves problems, shifts
systems and cultural norms, and sources our inner power based on universal
values. This may sound difficult, but in practice, it is easy and aligns
different aspects of ourselves within.
Let’s consider the global “food crisis.” One billion people most often go
to bed hungry in an abundant world. What can we do? Speaking about
hunger and malnutrition in countries in Africa and Asia, where large
populations are poor and do not have access to food, is not the same as
speaking about hunger and malnutrition in the United States and countries
in Europe. In these countries, where market forces play a big role in
determining access to high-quality foods, it is obesity that is a major
problem. The patterns, however, are similar. Partial responses are only
partially effective. It is the conscious full-spectrum response that makes the
change that will be most impactful and that will last.
When a person in the United States or Europe is hungry, does not have
food or the money to buy food, what can be done?
Here are six partial ways we can address the problem of hunger,
producing some results. These partial responses are illustrated in Figure 6-1.
1. Offer immediate relief to a hungry person by providing food at soup
kitchens and food banks (inner circle).
2. Shift policies, systems, structures, and norms to produce more
nutritious foods, stop promoting and subsidizing unhealthy food
production, adopt healthy food habits, have nourishing food for all,
abolish food deserts, create jobs and income (middle circle).
3. Combine the two strategies above, without developing inner capacities
(inner and middle circles).
4. Develop our inner capacities only without action—work on personal
transformation (outer circle).
5. Offer immediate relief to a hungry person and develop inner capacities
without addressing any of the systems causes and cultural norms (inner
and outer circles).
6. Shift policies, systems, structures, and norms and develop inner
capacities without having strategies for immediate relief from hunger
(middle and outer circles).
Figure 6-1 . Partial Responses to the Food Crisis.Instead of these partial responses, we have far greater impact if we
address all these dimensions together—a conscious full-spectrum response
(CFSR).
I was born in India; I love India and love serving in India. I recognize the
depth of spirituality, celebrate the diversity in culture; I am inspired by the
presence of committed people making a difference. I also recognize the
unbridled materialism, widespread corruption, hideous exploitative
practices, and pervasive exclusionary practices based on gender, caste, and
class. I wonder what India would look like today if we had achieved our
independence from Great Britain with conscious full-spectrum responses,
rather than partial responses.
It is true that India is a sovereign nation and progress has been achieved
in some fields, such as information technology; there is infrastructure, such
as roads and hospitals, for those who can afford it. However, the plight of so
many Indians is fraught with uncertainty and deprivation because of
extreme poverty and social prejudice. Mahatma Gandhi had a strategy of
nonviolence—he called the Indian freedom movement satyagraha, the
force which is born of truth (satya) and love or nonviolence. Sarvodaya is a
Sanskrit term meaning “universal uplift” or “progress of all.” To ensure that
self-determination and equity reached all strata of Indian society, Gandhians
embraced this term for India’s post-independence social movement.
The freedom movement in India, however, did not provide platforms for
everyone to source action from deep within, from universal values. As a
result, over time actions have fallen very short of the potential for every
Indian to generate an equitable society, in which everyone thrives with
dignity, where there is “progress for all.” We are not BEING the change we
wished to see during the independence movement. Discrimination based on
caste, class, and gender is pervasive, affecting the lives of millions of
people. Now the richest 1 percent of Indians own 58 percent of the
country’s wealth,28 and the trend in inequity is accelerating.
What would be different now if we had embarked on policies, programs,
and implementation strategies in which ethical citizenship and leadership
prevailed? Could we have had economic and financial rules that did not
concentrate wealth in the hands of a few people? Could we create wealth
without liabilities and debt? What could be different now, and in the future,
if we decide to invest in conscious full-spectrum responses instead of partial
responses?
Fallacy: It takes too much time for people to work on their
personal transformation and also design and implement policies
and programs based on universal values. It is faster to focus on a
few strategies.
New Learning: Using a conscious full-spectrum approach,
moving beyond partial responses, is actually a time-saver. We
now have new praxes to source our inner capacities for action,
principled game-changing approaches, and technical innovations
that generate results simultaneously in time and at an
accelerated pace.
Conscious Full-Spectrum Responses
Knowing how to design the architecture of a conscious full-spectrum
response is at the core of being a unifying architect, a principled game
changer. Using the same example we used to illustrate partial responses, we
will now design conscious full-spectrum responses in which all three
domains are connected.Figure 6-2 . Conscious Full-Spectrum Responses.
The conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR) framework is for
designing responses that generate enduring results based on universal
values. In this response, the three essential components are addressed
simultaneously:
Solve problems with appropriate technologies—the inner circle
Shift systems and cultural norms as we solve problems so that our
solutions are game-changing—the middle circle
Source our inner capacities and universal values to solve problems as
we shift culture and systems—BEING a transformational steward and
leader—the outer circle
A conscious full-spectrum response to the food crisis has all three of the
strategic components mentioned above. I am hungry, do not have food, and
I do not have money to buy food. What can be done? The best, most
equitable and sustainable response is to act in all the three dimensions at
once: (1) give food or volunteer in a soup kitchen and solve the immediate
problem of hunger—inner circle; (2) shift the current policies on food
subsidies for more production of nutritious foods, increase access to
nutritious food for all, end food deserts for people with limited resources,
implement policies and programs that generate income—middle circle; and
(3) embody universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion as the
foundation for strategic action and policy change, so that our responses are
enduring and equitable—outer circle.
CFSR Projects Worldwide
Several decades ago, I made a decision to design and implement every
activity, project, program, or policy through the conscious full-spectrum
approach—at home, in my workplace, and in society. Depending on the
theme and on policy agreements both with governments and partners and
within the United Nations, I titled the initiatives differently. For example,
Leadership for Results was the program for addressing the HIV/AIDS
epidemic; Women’s Rights in Action was the program to reduce maternal
mortality; We Care was the program for our colleagues affected by
HIV/AIDS in our workplace. Hundreds of initiatives have been designed
and implemented with the CFSR approach, generating results, through the
United Nations and beyond. The practitioners, whose contributions are cited
throughout this book, all designed and implemented their ideas and projects
using the conscious full-spectrum approach.
Some gave unique names to the CFSR framework they used in their
projects, to reflect what they wanted to manifest—for example, GAIA—
Generative Action for Impact through Awareness for responding to
environmental issues, designed by Kirsten Gallo; 3H—Hands, Head and
Heart, for ecologically friendly architecture, by Martin Scherfler; ACT-GAP
—Advancing Common Transformational Good for All People, for
addressing social issues, by Megan Joseph and Kymberly Lacrosse; SFAB
—Spectral Framework for Agency Building, for demystifying science and
technology for social justice, designed by Muthu Kumaran, Adya Siddarth,
Vivek Coelho, and Sudarshan Rodriguez; Liberation through Landmines,
related to banning landmines in Israel, by Jerry White; HTR—Holistic
Transformational Response, for addressing violence, by Vernon Williams;
ACT—Awareness, Creating Shifts, Transforming Responses to the Climate
Change Crisis, designed by Karen O’Brien; framework for resilience in the
workplace by Dorrie Fontaine; the Equity Framework by Megan Joseph.
We will learn more about these projects later in this book.
To reverse the global HIV/AIDS epidemic through the United NationsDevelopment Programme, we focused on reducing stigma and
discrimination and increasing treatment and preventive measures through
building leadership for results in multiple sectors and levels. In all the sixty
countries where we worked, the design and implementation of programs
and projects had a few core elements.
Praxis to source universal values and enhance self-awareness, to know
who I am and what I stand for, and the ability to source inner capacity for
strategic action, was foundational. Stigma and discrimination cannot be
present in a space of our oneness, a space of our human unity. But stigma
runs even deeper when there is the hint of connection with sex, making the
HIV/AIDS program even more challenging to implement because of the
self-righteous morality we hide behind. This is why the universal values of
dignity, fairness, and compassion are so vital as the foundation of our work.
Universal values disrupt the discriminatory, exclusionary, exploitative
patterns we see in classism, racism, casteism, and patriarchy.
Explicit shifts in culture, norms, and systems were addressed by people
themselves. For example, people living with HIV/AIDS in villages of
Cambodia were sent away—they were thrown out. After going through
their own personal journey of self-awareness, seeing the incongruence
between what they stood for as human beings and what they were doing to
others, community members articulated the norms they wanted to shift in
the villages. Thereafter, people living with HIV/AIDS were fully accepted
in their villages and even welcomed to worship along with others in the
temples.
In Ethiopia, women would not go for testing out of the fear that they
would be labeled promiscuous. But after engaging with our program,
testing became a normal procedure, free of fear or stigma.
In the Arab States, HIV/AIDS treatment was denied to people in prisons
because they were labeled as “promiscuous” by society and religious
leaders, who thought these prisoners “deserved to suffer.” But these same
religious leaders transcended their judgments and committed to treatment
for prisoners based on universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion;
and they publicly declared their decision.
Problems faced by people were solved. In Botswana, the money
designated for local health institutions was held up at the national level
because of bureaucratic red tape; within six months, over 90 percent of this
money reached local institutions because of the breakthrough initiatives
designed and implemented by the practitioners of the learning-in-action
program. In Ukraine, there was no place for people dying of AIDS to go;
youth and disc jockeys from discotheques organized a campaign to establish
care and physical spaces for people dying of AIDS.
As a result of explicitly designing initiatives with elements of the
conscious full-spectrum response approach, we generated new
conversations in society that embraced people living with HIV/AIDS and
