increased prevention, testing, and treatment.
Karen O’Brien is a professor of human geography at the University of
Oslo and the cofounder of cCHANGE, which engages with “transformation
in a changing climate.” She adapted the conscious full-spectrum response in
a transdisciplinary way, using results-oriented research on global
environmental change and focusing on both commitment and results. Karen
designed the “three spheres of transformation” in relation to adaptation,
mitigation, and sustainability. This approach has been included in the
chapter on climate-resilient pathways in the Intergovernmental Panel on
Climate Change (IPCC) Fifth Assessment Report, and it is central to a new
initiative on transformation in a changing climate (cchange.no). Karen’s
point of departure is “that climate change is not simply a technical problemthat can be addressed by managing existing systems better, by improving
technology, by pricing emissions, by allocating special funds for adaptation
and mitigation, or by reforming institutions and governance. It may require
these changes, but it is also a challenge that is intricately related to other
ecological, social, and economic issues, which call for deeper
transformations in current systems and structures that promote undesirable
outcomes—pollution, biodiversity loss, poverty and inequity, insecurity.
Moreover, climate change can be considered a transformative challenge for
human beings that requires individuals and groups to ground themselves in
universal values and examine their worldviews and assumptions about
change itself.” By integrating the practical, political, and personal spheres
of transformation, she is using the conscious full-spectrum response
framework to generate adaptive and sustainable responses to the climate
change crisis.
Karen O’Brien was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 2007 along with
other scientists in the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC)
and Albert Arnold (Al) Gore Jr. “for their efforts to build up and
disseminate greater knowledge about man-made climate change, and to lay
the foundations for the measures that are needed to counteract such
change.” Karen realizes the need for an actionable approach for
transforming at the rate, scale, and depth that is called for by the IPCC and
the 2015 Paris Agreement on climate change, and she finds the three
spheres framework based on the CFSR approach to be a promising way of
connecting personal and planetary transformations.29
Hemlata Kansotia, whom you met in Chapter Four, designed and
implemented her work through the conscious full-spectrum response
approach. One of her breakthrough initiatives was to design and implement
learning-in-action programs in India to shift the way in which sewage
workers and street cleaners, all Dalits, are treated and supported. For their
entire lives, many Dalits are told they are “untouchable”; they are excluded
from worshipping in temples, and other castes do not drink or eat with
them.
The participants of the learning-in-action program held in Dehra Dun,
India, were workers, union leaders, and members of community-based
organizations, joining hands and hearts from all castes. They generated
results standing in their oneness, working together beyond socially
constructed caste labels with a shared commitment to transform the
unacceptable conditions in which Dalits are forced to work. They produced
documentary films revealing the horrific labor conditions of sewage
workers and what was needed for their safety. Safe working gear was
obtained, and labor unions articulated new demands.
One of the most profound and moving moments for me was when several
participants stood up and shared their insights: they discovered who they
are and their oneness with all human BEINGS—they are not “untouchable.”
For most of them, this was a pathbreaking and life-changing experience.
These are timeless moments—when time stands still in the presence of
grace, pristine awareness, and unity.
In one of Hemlata’s learning-in-action programs, two participants out of
seventy-five could not read or write. Since the CFSR worksheet puts the
message across through a diagram, with a little help from their friends and
colleagues, they could understand the issues and their interconnections, and
were able to fully express themselves. They said this was the first time they
could articulate a future for themselves and their children. Hemlata and her
colleague Janaki Ragai said, “This CFSR framework is extremely helpful.
We can write up and submit our proposals to donors for funding ourselves
now. Previously, we paid NGOs (nongovernmental organizations) to writefor us. The key elements of a comprehensive proposal and what donors ask
for are all covered in the questions on the CFSR worksheet. We felt so
proud that my idea for educating children of street cleaners received funds
from a donor.”
Fallacy: The issues are complex. As ordinary workers and
citizens, we cannot plan what we need for our future and the
future of our children. The methods are too complicated. Only
“highly educated” people with formal degrees can do this.
New Learning: All concerned citizens can actively participate
and plan for their future. They are capable of seeing the big
picture and know what patterns need to change in cultural and
formal systems in order to solve problems. Everyone has inner
capacities that they can get in touch with and source for action.
Anita, Hemlata’s colleague, traveled overnight with her fourteen-year-old
son, Raju, on a bus from a village in Uttarakhand to Dehra Dun for the
program. She could not leave her son behind. We invited Raju to participate
in the sessions with us. He presented his breakthrough initiative, referring to
the CFSR worksheet (Figure 6-3). Raju stands for dignity and courage for
all. He said he would transform the culture of systematic discrimination in
his school, where as a Dalit child, he had to drink from a separate water
fountain. Sometimes there was no water for him and the other Dalit
children. The Dalit students also had to stand in a separate line to get their
midday meal, after all the “upper caste” children were given their meal.
They had to sit separately to eat—they were labeled as “untouchables.”
Raju committed to take action. He would take his uncle and go to see the
district magistrate, an Indian Administrative Service (IAS) officer, who
Raju had heard was a man who worked with fairness. Raju would tell him
what needed to be changed, and said he was not afraid of repercussions—
what the authorities might do to him in school. “The worst thing that could
happen to me is that I am kicked out of school,” Raju said. Six months later,
we learned that Raju went ahead with his plan, met the district magistrate,
and was successful in changing the norms around water availability for all
and sitting together to eat—a great beginning.
Fallacy: Young people do not have the experience needed to
make a difference, even if they care. Planning and strategy
require “maturity,” and adults need to take the lead.
New Learning: Young people, like adults, can get in touch with
their inner capacities if they are provided opportunities to do so;
and like adults, they have the choice to manifest their inner
greatness through action.
Applying the CFSR Frame
The prerequisite to using the CFSR approach for generating results is that
the person cares deeply about humanity and the planet and is willing to take
courageous action. Without this predisposition, the individual will not ask
the right questions or generate transformational responses.
How do committed individuals design? They begin by identifying an
idea, project, or initiative related to an issue they are currently engaged with
and passionately concerned about. This is not about new ideas or different
ideas—it is about doing the same thing differently.
Design on the conscious full-spectrum response template begins with
inquiry, starting with these six questions. Please refer to the CFSR
worksheet, Figure 6-3.
My inner capacities (outer circle):1. What universal values do I stand for and embody as I make this
happen?
Universal values and principles of the project (at the cusp of the outer
and middle circles):
2. What (a) universal values and (b) principles underpin the project?
What will I do? In other words, what visible, measurable actions will we
deliver to generate results, outputs (inner circle)?
3. How do the problems show up (give one or two examples)?
4. What will change visibly because of my actions?
5. What will I do?
Systems and cultural shifts (middle circle):
6. What systems and cultural norms must be changed in order to create
equitable and sustainable outcomes? (Examples: changes in policies,
rules, organizational structures and their functioning; changes in
cultural norms in my community, society, governance, or business.)
As practitioners inquire, they map their responses on the CFSR
worksheet (Figure 6-3, CFSR Worksheet: Architecture for Equitable and
Sustainable Change). The conscious full-spectrum worksheet represents a
map for transformation with interdependent components. It is like a fabric,
with the different interconnected threads and elements intricately
interwoven. I can pull up the whole fabric from any point.
The CFSR approach can be applied to specific projects or large
programs, or to the different components of any project. The first couple of
times may feel awkward confusing, because we are not used to addressing
complexity in this simple yet powerful way.
For the next paradigm to emerge—indeed, for the next civilization to
emerge—it is vital that each of us accesses the unifying fearless architect
within us.
This work is about people everywhere engaging in creating a paradigm
shift because they care deeply about people and the planet. The CFSR
framework is not only for citizens’ engagement, it is also for professionals,
program managers, business people, journalists, academics, and policy-
makers from different disciplines and sectors.
Figure 6-3 . CFSR Worksheet: Architecture for Equitable and Sustainable Change. Solving
Problems, Shifting Systems and Culture, Sourcing Universal Values.
Martin Scherfler, a sociologist who was born in Austria and now lives in
Auroville in India, is a unifying architect using different frameworks
including the CFSR to generate results. He designed and implements a
summer school for students of architecture, engineering, and design.
Martin’s version of the conscious full-spectrum response is a “3H approach:
hands (skills), head (systems thinking, pattern recognizing), and heart (inner
capacity and universal values).” The objective of the Auroville Green
Practices Summer School program is to shift higher education from a
fragmented approach to an approach that nurtures hands, head, and heart by
offering a new generation of environmental stewards the space to explore
and express their full potential. The workshop is an experimental,
transdisciplinary educational laboratory, connecting theory and application
with core human values such as justice, dignity, and beauty, around aselected theme. The themes include low-cost house design, water
harvesting, passive cooling, ecological sanitation, and urban agriculture.
The 3H summer school program is designed as a fractal in which each
program activity contains elements of all the 3H components. It aims at
fostering a new generation of leaders who are capable of connecting their
hearts, heads, and hands for a more equitable and sustainable future.
Martin stands for integrity as wholeness, compassion, and courage. He
says, “I never considered myself to be a traditional activist who merely
focuses on deconstruction and criticism of the existing, and not, or only
little, on the creation and upkeep of alternatives. My quest has been shaped
by the values my parents attributed to simplicity and hard work; by a
confrontation with Marxist ideas and their lack of respect for the inner
space; a quest to combine an aspiration to unending inner growth with a
sense of responsibility for the well-being of mankind and planet; an
ongoing inquiry into my real needs—material, emotional, intellectual, and
spiritual—combined with the urge to action and lifestyle change.”
Srilatha Juvva is a professor at the Tata Institute of Social Sciences in
Mumbai with expertise in disability studies and mental health. Srilatha
stands for full potential, compassion, and dignity. For Srilatha, well-being is
fundamental to human existence.
Since childhood, Srilatha has faced numerous adversities. She sources
her inner capacities and stretches to manifest her full potential. Her work in
mental health and disability focuses on each individual realizing their full
potential, maintaining well-being, and meeting challenges as effectively as
possible. She says, “I have learned to source my inner wisdom to put forth
my views and act. I design courses as well as projects using the CFSR
framework. It is easy to use this framework to design any activity, big or
small. I synthesize with clarity and purpose, explicitly sourcing my inner
wisdom along with modeling my behavior as a teacher; I connect universal
values that students, project staff, and I stand for to the systems and cultural
issues related to mental health and disability which we must address, as we
develop skills, competencies, and inner capacities in order to provide care.
“As a teacher, I apply the CFSR approach to organizing my theoretical
courses as well as fieldwork. The responses of students and trainees have
been significant: they shifted from being indifferent and ‘difficult’ to being
engaged and active. Using mutually reinforcing frameworks and tools, I see
that teaching-learning for me has been transformed not only by how
students respond to classroom teaching but also by how their thinking has
shifted about their inner capacities and universal values, their socialized
fears, background conversations and listening, and their commitment to
learning. I see them apply this to their fieldwork and design strategies to
make the invisible and subtle patterns of stigma and discrimination visible,
making complex systems simple for the service users and enrolling them to
design initiatives for change. There are measurable results—I am working
to make this way of learning the norm.”
Fallacy: Planning templates cannot be used for the many
challenges we face in complex settings. These methods (e.g., the
CFSR approach) are for projects. Social norms leading to stigma
and discrimination in society, difficult issues in our workplaces,
and academic institutions for higher education require very
different approaches.
New Learning: The conscious full-spectrum response is robust
and profound. The practice of sourcing universal values for action
to shift disempowering patterns needs to be constantly applied in
various settings to solve different problems.Srilatha reflects: “Currently, in academia, we let differences rule us rather
than our individual and collective greatness. Such divisions have reached an
undeserved place of importance only because we did not operate from a
place of abundance, strength, and wisdom, but from one of fear and
competition. With a renewed focus, we are averting further and future
breakdowns and setbacks, as we reclaim ourselves, ground ourselves in our
wisdom, and work together. I have learned that universal values truly bind
us so deeply, that this never fails; our inner capacities and universal values
exist in one place where all can connect; this increases respect for diversity
in thinking and doing.”
There is a big difference between teaching universal values and
embodying them. This work is about the profound humility of BEING open,
self-aware, in practice, on a path of lifelong learning for academicians and
others who excel in the subject matter of what they teach.
Dorrie Fontaine, the dean of the School of Nursing at the University of
Virginia (UVA), launched the Compassionate Care and Empathic
Leadership Initiative, collaborating between the Schools of Medicine and
Nursing. Her commitment was to create a healthy work environment in
which all could flourish and to increase interprofessional education,
especially for nursing and medical students to learn together and gain
respect for each other. The goal was to improve the lives of those with life-
threatening illnesses by transforming practice, education, research, and
community partnerships.
Dorrie used the CFSR approach to design her own framework for
resilience in the workplace and opened up an immense, deep
interprofessional learning space through the Compassionate Care Initiative.
Dorrie says, “Over one hundred health care providers from across multiple
disciplines in our health system at UVA engaged, putting into practice forty
leadership capacities in a little over three years. We fostered a multitude of
nurses, chaplains, and physicians who were no longer content with business
as usual. Numerous initiatives for caring and ways of building resilience
were put in place. We changed the culture at UVA. This made a name for
UVA School of Nursing throughout the health system, so we are known for
the Compassionate Care Initiative, now and hopefully forever.”
Dorrie combined her professional excellence with the humility to learn.
She says, “Every single workshop empowered me a little more, as we
always went back to this core of who we are, and the ability to know myself
and stand in my power. I listen more deeply, engage in more mindful
behaviors, get to know people differently. I loved hearing folks say, ‘I stand
for.…’ I use these ideas in all my work now with various groups.”
I met two nuns, Reverend Guo Chan and Reverend Chang Ji, in the
Spiritual Caucus at the United Nations. They follow the teachings of
Chinese Chan Buddhism and wanted to learn ways to enhance their abilities
for social action. I invited them to participate in learning-in-action programs
with others, and was curious to know what these programs meant for them.
Reverend Guo Chan was born on Taiwan. She said, “We stand alone but
we walk together—the choice of words helps me to see my work
differently. This is a very special systematic and powerful process that has a
universal language and universal context, which allows me to resonate with
the teachings of Chinese Chan Buddhism and to live in a larger scale.
“Using the conscious full-spectrum approach increases my ability to
recognize where others and I source our projects. I am able to identify what
is missing and also to express myself in a language that encourages others
to be in the space of their potential instead of fear, and reduces conflict.”
Reverend Chang Ji was born and educated in the United States. She
shared the following: “I realized, working with participants outside myspiritual order, that I am not alone in my suffering. In many ways, although
the situation and context may be very diverse, the basic sufferings are very
similar and sourced from our fears. I developed more empathy and
compassion toward my fellow earthlings and became less judgmental.
“When applying the conscious full-spectrum methodology, I find that I
can be in my unmessable with stand and design projects to have outcomes
and impact instead of just inputs and outputs. They would have deeper,
immediate, and longer-term impacts within the organization, with the
project partners and stakeholders. With this clarity, I am able to more
effectively enroll people to support my project through the creation of the
space of our potential and universal values.
“After working through the stewardship program, and having spent time
in solitary contemplation since then, I have had many ‘aha’ moments in
which I saw my fears under my background conversations and stories. I saw
how they prevented me from being able to be completely present and
nonjudgmental. I have been able to practice discernment and found great
power in that.”
Synergistic Operational Strategies
The architecture for generating a paradigm shift with equitable and
sustainable results needs to be operationalized and put into practice.
Synergistic operational strategies (SOS), when put in place, generate
exponentially greater impact. They operate in tandem with the different
components of the conscious full-spectrum response framework, expanding
our ability to respond to complex challenges.
Synergy occurs when the interaction or cooperative efforts of two or
more entities, organizations, groups, substances, or other agents produce a
combined effect greater than the sum of their separate effects. When we
design for synergy, we achieve more with less. In fact we do this in our
daily lives by combining several measures for well-being such as healthy
diet, exercise, satisfying work, adequate sleep, and meditation. Each one of
these practices produces positive effects; done together, the effect is far
greater than what each one offers individually.
Figure 6-4 . Synergistic Operational Strategies (SOS).
Before we explore and establish the operational strategies for our idea,
initiative, or project, we need to be clear about (a) the universal values we
embody and the purpose of our engagement, (b) core universal values at the
foundation of the project, (c) results we wish to generate and our goals, (d)
principles we agree to follow to operationalize the program (such as
informed participation, transparency), and (e) the strategic direction of what
we do and how we intend to do it—are we going to use conventional ways,
or transformative approaches.
After designing an idea, initiative, or project using the conscious full-
spectrum response approach, we use the four synergistic operational
strategies (SOS):
Developing transformational leadership or stewardship
Providing information for decision-making
Creating an enabling environment for change
Supporting principled change-makers and risk-takers
Transformational Leadership and Stewardship
A steward is a leader who is also a paradigm shifter. Transformational
stewards manifest paradigm shifts, holding themselves responsible simply
because they care deeply. They develop the following skills, competencies,and inner capacities:
Source intrinsic power, universal values, creativity, for inspiring change
and strategic action.
Move from resignation in the face of negative circumstances to
commitment, initiative, and action.
Bridge the nondual universe of oneness with that of the dual world.
Exercise critical thinking about self and society.
See root factors of challenges and issues, and address them effectively.
Design strategic initiatives that transform unworkable systems and
cultural norms and build alternative architectures for equitable and
sustainable results.
Are BEING principled game changers while solving problems.
Continuously practice in order to presence equity, dignity, compassion.
Create transdisciplinary strategies dedicated to synergetic solutions
based on universal values.
Generate transformational outputs, outcomes, and impacts for
immediate and long-term results as well as large-scale equitable and
sustainable impact.
Forge results-oriented, aligned synergistic partnerships based on
universal values across sectors and issues.
Build capacity in a significant number of practitioners to coach others to
generate results.
I designed an integrated approach to enhance both leadership and design
capabilities and have used this approach in learning-in-action sessions
focused on issues such as reducing maternal mortality, reducing recidivism,
reversing the HIV/AIDS epidemic, ending female genital mutilation,
fostering resilience and transdisciplinary work in clinicians, preventing
climate change, and promoting conservation in coastal areas of India as
well as in the US National Park Service. This learning-in-action modality
effectively weaves personal transformation with social transformation based
on universal values during the same learning sessions.
In our response to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic in around sixty
countries, twelve external facilitators designed and delivered the leadership
development component in fourteen locations, and the essential
architectural skills for social transformation were designed and delivered by
us at UNDP. They included the CFSR approach, synergistic operational
strategies (SOS), the transformational results chain (TRC), information,
policy, community engagement, governance, and communication. I am
highlighting the imperative of knowing how to design programs for
leadership development and results for social transformation.
Information for Decision-Making
Relevant information must be widely available for design, decision-making,
educating the public, and tracking progress. Otherwise, as our stewardship
capacities unfold, we will make well-meaning decisions based on hunches
and positions rather than sound information. I have noticed that most final
decisions made by leaders related to money and policies are based on
political pressures and other influences. Rationality, science, data, and
information did not prevail. This is why transformational stewardship based
on ethical values is so vital for informed decision-making.
Fallacy: Leadership programs are the key to change. As long as
the tools in the workshops are powerful, the strategies and
actions will follow and change will occur.
New Learning: Transformational leadership programs are
necessary. Even more important is each person’s praxis, based
on universal values, continuous engagement in strategic action,and working in synergy with other essential components for
transformation—information for decision-making, effective policy
and media environments, and support for principled risk-takers.
Transparency is essential because people are increasingly demanding
openness as they participate in democratic processes. Information on the
“invisible” rules of the game allows us to change what is not working. For
example, what we buy, and the information we seek when we buy,
determines our collective future and the state of our planet. Did the workers
who manufactured the items we purchased receive fair and living wages?
Were they treated with dignity? Are the products in any way harmful to our
health? Does the production and distribution process have harmful effects
on our planet? Most people conscious of animal rights choose to be vegan;
those who remain vegetarian or omnivorous are beginning to seek
information about whether animals were treated and fed well, allowed to
move freely and killed humanely. Informed citizens who care will
encourage market transparency, and some scholars opine that the businesses
that will thrive in the future will be those who are “radically transparent.”30
Today, we are flooded with information—and misinformation—related to
marketing, sales, and stock markets, all with various interpretations about
this global casino. We receive so much information from so many sources
that it is difficult to figure out what is going on. It can even be difficult to
access accurate information about important issues that affect our daily
lives, such as the basics of banking and investing, the harmful effects of
medications, who benefits from various policies or transactions and where
the loopholes are, how products that had disastrous consequences are now
being repackaged under new labels. For example, financial derivatives that
created economic havoc in 2008 are now repackaged as safe financial
instruments. Our lack of awareness about products dangerous for health,
such as pesticides, does not serve us. Ninety-five percent of adults in the
United States have organophosphates in their blood, and the levels are twice
as high in children. Exposure to organophosphate pesticides is linked to
hyperactivity, behavior disabilities, developmental delays, and motor
dysfunction; half the insecticides in the United States have
organophosphates.31
As citizens, we need to be aware of the facts about policies and products
that affect our lives. We need to learn how to know which data sources we
can trust, in order to decipher what is best for us and for our children’s
future. We must transcend ideologically motivated information by inquiring
into what is really going on based on universal values of equity and dignity.
I had the privilege of meeting Aruna Roy, whose work I admire, to start
designing a community leadership program. Aruna invited women and men
from villages in Rajasthan to participate, and I learned how appropriately
and assertively they used information for decision-making and change.
Aruna Roy’s seminal work in India’s Right to Information movement
emerged from the struggle for livelihood of the rural poor and the blatant
corruption of the government schemes in their villages. This work was the
foundation of the Right to Information Act in India. Roy was a cofounder of
the Mazdoor Kisan Shakti Sangathan (MKSS), an organization based on the
strength of labor-workers and farmers, which demanded authentic
information to counter embezzlement, fake payments, and false bills issued
by the establishment. Aruna says there is a difference between transparency
and a people’s demand for the right to information. When people demand
information, it indicates a fundamental shift in who asks the questions. It is
more than asking a question—they break the silence and fiercely oppose
behaviors that do not uphold integrity.For rural poor people in India, demanding information is a matter of
survival. Thousands of people demanding information have made a
measurable difference in their own daily lives—for example, obtaining
information on disbursements for village work; having accurate information
about teachers and schools.
The Government of India states that “the basic object of the Right to
Information Act is to empower the citizens, promote transparency and
accountability in the working of the Government, contain corruption, and
make our democracy work for the people in real sense. It goes without
saying that an informed citizen is better equipped to keep necessary vigil on
the instruments of governance and make the government more accountable
to the governed.”32
Aruna Roy received the Ramon Magsaysay Award for Community
Leadership and the Lal Bahadur Shastri National Award for Excellence in
Public Service, Academia and Management and was named as one of the
hundred most influential people in the world by Time magazine in 2011.
A few farsighted businesses are starting to change, going beyond the
limiting confines of intellectual property rights and focusing primarily on a
thriving humanity and planet. For example, Tesla, an automobile company
that aims to further sustainable transportation, shares information related to
the technology they use, relies on customers to spread the word, and
provides excellent services—and their sales and profits are on track.33
Fallacy: Information is needed primarily for policy-makers and
executives. Citizens are likely to misinterpret data and
information because they do not have the full picture, and they
may cause unnecessary conflicts. Further, if information is
shared, our competitive edge will be lost in our field, and we will
not be successful.
New Learning: Citizens, academics, and businessmen who rise
above their narrow personal interests use data skillfully to shift
disempowering cultural norms and systems, generating results
for equitable and sustainable change. Further, sharing
information enhances creativity, increases knowledge, and
contributes to progress.
An Enabling Environment for Change
An enabling environment for change requires responsible media, legal
provisions that support people to make change, and empowering social
norms. We need ethical conversations that support people and our planet to
thrive.
During the 2008 financial crisis in the United States, inequitable fiscal
policies rendered eight million people homeless. The rules of the game, the
policy environment, and lack of information for people led to this. Although
the cause of the crisis is widely accepted to be the sale of “derivative”
products, financial giants in the United States and Europe are now
repackaging those same products for their personal continued monetary
gain.34 Globally, laissez-faire financial deregulation has hurt many people,
particularly those living in poverty.
However, as we move ahead, there is hope. The Sustainable
Development Goals and the broad strategic direction they represent have
been signed off on by the 193 countries of the United Nations General
Assembly.35 In creating an enabling environment, all the stakeholders need
to be proactive. As citizens in any country, particularly in democracies, we
too can choose to inform ourselves and make these goals and agreements a
reality; we can demand that our “market states” are held to account. Thenotion of sovereign countries has been eroded not only by the rich countries
with military might but also by some multinational market forces who
lobby and control a country’s leadership.
Creating a safe environment for teams and organizations to step up and
transform dysfunctional systems and norms is essential; and we can do this
by encouraging generative, value-based conversations and actions for
policy change. Shebreh Kalantari Johnson, a public health consultant, and
Brenda Armstrong, a public health professional with the local government
in Santa Cruz County in California, were engaged in a seven-month long
process to address the complex issue of health and substance abuse. They
used the conscious full-spectrum response template.
Standing in unity and equity, Shebreh and Brenda approached the
cultivators of marijuana whose profits would be affected by prohibition or
increased regulation. They held conversations from this generative space
with large number of cultivators, spending time and listening deeply to their
point of view without judgment or mental filters.
The initial response by members of the cannabis industry was what is
usually said in such situations: “The research is biased and funded by
government agencies that are anti-cannabis. Therefore, we don’t really
know if there are negative impacts to young people and their growth and
development”; “You have a prohibitionist perspective that will never allow
for sound regulations to be put in place”; “Problems will be gone once
prohibition is gone”; “This is a medicine, but you are treating it like other
harmful drugs. Why don’t you address the evils of pharmaceutical drugs?”
Shebreh and Brenda shared their universal values and opened up the
space to everyone to articulate the shifts that are necessary for well-being in
their communities: moving from prohibition to regulation; shifting from
mistrust to trust; and shifting from a narrow definition of community to a
broader definition of community, where the representatives of the cannabis
industry offered solutions based on universal values for the well-being of all
segments of the community, including their own families.
On the day of the Santa Cruz County Board of Supervisors meeting,
where some former participants of the stewardship program were also
present, new conversations were generated. Essential regulations were put
forth committing to a medical cannabis program that would have the least
negative impact while providing access to medicine for those who need it.
Brenda notes, “Local officials, industry representatives, law enforcement,
and community members advocate for our voice and inclusion in policy
development and community issues. Instead of soliciting invitations to the
decision-making table, we are identified as key stakeholders with a valued
perspective.” Shebreh adds, “We have reached an unprecedented
partnership and collaboration with ‘untraditional partners.’ Industry
members are coming to us for our expertise with the spirit of mutual
support. They are asking us for brochures that they can give others on how
to safeguard against youth access to medical cannabis. When youth access
their products, they want to strategize with us on how this can be avoided.
Just today I was meeting with a cannabis industry person, and he said, ‘Two
years ago when I first encountered your group, I thought you were just
another just say no group. Now I understand and know that we can’t do this
work right without you guys at the table.’”
Some media professionals say they have to be sensational to keep us
interested. We say we are tired of what the media projects, but continue to
consume gossip and allow media to distract us beyond rational thought and
ethical conversations. Who is going to break these circles of consumerism
and commodification? The media often perpetuates the commodification of
human beings, instead of reverence for life.Fortunately, there are responsible citizens and media. Community
conversations and the media played an important role in reducing stigma
against people living with HIV/AIDS; several media networks are focusing
on inequity—for example, Al Jazeera reported on “The World’s Inequality
Countdown”; and reporting on racism has opened the doors to new policies
and actions for inclusion and change.
Although democratic policies and norms are for everyone, unfortunately
some of the richest and most influential people consider themselves to be
above the rule of law—as if following the law is an option for them. People
who are not in that category are often afraid to speak up against what is not
working, against corrupt officials and business practices, against
exploitation or discrimination. They are afraid that what they have will be
taken away; or worse, that their loved ones and family will be threatened
and hurt. This brings us to the next synergistic operational strategy: support
principled risk-takers.
Support for Principled Risk-Takers
This is a missing piece in most programs. It is essential to support the
principled risk-takers whose actions move us toward equitable and
sustainable change. Principled risk-takers are there in every establishment,
every society. They challenge what is not working not because they have a
personal agenda or complaint but because they are grounded firmly in the
space of oneness, of universal values that apply to everyone, everywhere.
They are ethical and cannot close their compassionate hearts to what is
unfair or undignified. Unfortunately, others all too often label them as
disgruntled, rebellious reactionaries, or simply dismiss them as emotional.
Courageous, politically conscious individuals with bold ideas that support
the common good are often branded as idealists and are told that the system
will stall their ideas. But they continue. And they need support so that they
are not rendered ineffective.
As individuals, societies, and communities, we have yet to develop the
language that differentiates emotional reactions from courageous,
principled, practical, heart responses. We are too often afraid to break the
silence, imagining that dire consequences will follow when we speak up
and speak out. Supporting principled risk-takers openly, not just among
friends, is essential in order to make a paradigm shift.
Fallacy: Governments curb our freedom by having policies
based on constitutional values of equity and dignity for all. In this
modern era and globalized world, control should be given to
people who are in leadership positions in multinational
companies, financial institutions, and think tanks, because they
have the knowledge and experience, create jobs, and make
money for the country. Media should be silent on the big issues
even when it compromises people’s well-being because this may
cause instability. The Constitution guarantees freedom of speech;
we are entitled to write whatever we feel like, including
expressions of hate.
New Learning: In the globalized world with its invisible “rules of
the game,” a few people in multinational companies, financial
institutions, think tanks, and governments largely control
operations; this gives rise to intended and unintended negative
consequences. Responsible policy-makers and stakeholders
from every sector and citizens must inform themselves and
actively participate to assure equitable humanity and a
sustainable planet for us and our future generations. Responsible
media needs our support to create the culture of inclusion anddignity. Freedom is a precious, profound, responsible, and
creative space!
In our work around the world, we use three approaches to support
principled risk-takers: connect and engage with ethical people to become
champions; work with responsible journalists and individuals from the
media; encourage constant principled conversations in society.
Champions Support Principled Risk-Takers
When principled risk-takers are threatened and attacked, champions with
local or even global influence openly and publicly support their actions. I
often wonder how much would rapidly change if every ethical and
respected person with influence (whether a global figure like Desmond Tutu
or local leaders) proactively supported five thousand or even one thousand
principled persons who took the risk to lead transformational change and
move ahead despite opposition. It works—people with vested interests
often back off.
The following is an example of champions supporting principled risk-
takers. The mandate of a police department is to ensure the safety and
security of citizens. There are also instances of unwarranted political
interference around the world, particularly at local levels, where police
officers are expected to compromise integrity and give in to undue pressure
from politically powerful persons. Everywhere, there are some policemen
and policewomen who are courageous, who uphold universal values rather
than giving in to this pressure. They are usually punished by the authorities
—fired, or sent off to very difficult jobs or remote posts. Typically, the
police excesses involving unacceptable forms of violence, including killing,
are given socially acceptable labels, like “encounters with terrorists or
criminals.”
However, there is a police department in one state of India where
principled police officers are supported. Informal coalitions of retired police
officers, lawyers, and civil rights activists volunteer to help police officers
who, based on their own ethical principles, dare not to follow orders to kill
or arrest a person based on false charges.
In April 2007, “criminals” or “terrorists” were “encountered” and killed
by the police. A principled and competent police officer in integrity took the
risk to stand up against false accusations; after a lengthy process, the
involved senior police officers were penalized. There have been no such
police “encounters” in this state since. It is remarkable to note the major
significance of this one act of courage.36
When the imam from Cairo whom you met in Chapter Five issued a
fatwa against female genital mutilation as he had committed to do, his life
was threatened by fundamentalists. The Grand Mufti openly stood by the
decision of the imam from Cairo, saving his life.
Throughout my own career, almost every time I took risks, I was
supported by people who cared about my work both within and outside the
United Nations. One time, I handed in my resignation after unsuccessfully
challengingly the false accusations made about two members of the team I
supervised, who successfully mobilized leaders in Washington, DC, to
support child survival initiatives. James Grant, the former executive director
of UNICEF, gave us his full support and got the petty UN bureaucrats off
our backs.
James Grant always supported principled risk-takers. My colleague was
UNICEF representative in a country office, where he reported that child
soldiers were being recruited. He was declared persona non grata (an
unacceptable and unwelcome person) by the government the same day andasked to leave the country. It was James Grant who acknowledged him for
his courage and compassion for the plight of these children and assigned
him to another post with a promotion.
Once, a competitive colleague from another UN agency openly
complained to experts and government officials that our team was “moving
too fast” to reduce childhood mortality and made it difficult for us to
operate effectively. The director of program division at UNICEF mobilized
key stakeholders in different countries to support our work.
When I worked on HIV/AIDS with strategies based on universal values
of dignity, equity, and compassion, I was accused by two public health
experts of having religious intentions to convert people in Africa from Islam
and Christianity to Buddhism. My approach to generating results—solving
problems, shifting norms and systems while sourcing universal values—
was limiting their technical solution–based consultancies. This may sound
like a minor issue and gossip, but when such points are made publicly and
misinterpreted in professional, expert forums, they undermine the
foundation of ethical leadership and generate opposition from leaders and
stakeholders. The experts said they had “more evidence” because I worked
on emotional intelligence and systems change—and the eminent experts
related to these topics meditated with Buddhist teachers. Such baseless
rumors, which morphed into ridicule and slander, do not deter me; but they
do lead to delays and temporarily hinder progress and results. The director
of the UNDP’s Bureau for Development Policy at that time, Eimi Watanabe,
along with the deputy director, Carlos Lopes, openly supported me and
encouraged me to continue working with the strategies I designed and
implemented. Conversations with each of them enhanced my understanding
of broader human development issues.
On this and other times, Eimi stood with me, asking the right questions
but with a confidence in me that opened spaces for further development.
She is deeply concerned about inequity and discrimination—gender, caste,
class. Eimi relentlessly championed girls’ education and women’s rights and
inspired us to focus on gender dimensions in all our projects or initiatives.
Carlos said he trusted my approach was worth supporting. Carlos created
the space for people’s agency—our self-determination based on universal
values—to thrive in our workplace as well as through the programs we in
designed and implemented. Through his steadfast support to countries in
Africa and other developing countries around the world, he created many
learning opportunities for us to contribute meaningfully. On another
occasion, when my work was being plagiarized by an academic from a
university in the United States, Nancy Roof, the founder of the award-
winning Kosmos Journal, supported me unconditionally and offered to
publish my work and results in the journal. I was surprised and deeply
touched that she reached out to me, because she had seen me only once in a
one-hour presentation at the United Nations. Nancy Roof cofounded the
Values Caucus and the Spiritual Caucus at the United Nations, advocating
for deeper responses to global challenges.
On only four out of dozens of instances in my entire career did I have to
“pay a price” for speaking up and speaking out when there were
irregularities, and for advocating for ethical and principled actions rather
than giving in to vested interests. I have no regrets, because I was fully
attuned and aligned within me and with my inner values.
I have stood by others who take principled risks. An example: Khadija
Moalla, whom you met in Chapter Five, secured funding for a series of
crucial events in the Arab States to ensure access to treatment for those
living with AIDS irrespective of their sexual preference. It was opposed
from within our institution. The head of one multilateral agency, from acountry where homosexuals are sentenced to death, objected to the wording
of the Cairo Declaration of religious leaders in the Arab States in response
to the HIV/AIDS epidemic, which was to be signed in the meeting in
December 2004, on the basis that it encouraged homosexuality by offering
treatment to gays. She withdrew funding abruptly a week before the event,
saying that the Grand Mufti would never endorse a declaration mentioning
condoms and guaranteeing treatment for everyone.
Khadija was successful in her efforts to advocate for universal treatment,
and the Grand Mufti signed the Cairo Declaration. Khadija says that
without my open support, she could not have made this happen.
Allan Henderson, one of our world-class coaches who worked with our
team and stakeholders in countries in UNDP’s HIV/AIDS program,
acknowledged me for my support to staff when unnecessary bureaucratic
pressures came their way. “You absorbed all the nonsense, shouldered all
that bureaucratic stuff, created an umbrella, a positive space within which
your staff could work!”
Journalists and Media Professionals Support Principled Risk-Takers
There are individuals from the media who care and yearn to see a more
equitable and sustainable world. They participate in the stewardship
program. In the program to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic, the director of
the ministry of health handed in his resignation to the minister, because he
refused to be part of corruption related to the Global Fund to Fight AIDS,
Tuberculosis and Malaria (GFATM). Normally, his resignation would have
been accepted, and the incident would have created no ripples. Because
individuals from the media were witnesses to this incident, the minister
refused to accept the director’s resignation and put a stop to the corruption.
Encourage Constant Conversations That Support Principled Risk-
Takers
Whatever we give attention to grows. When we see and note exciting,
everyday ethical acts of courage and compassion, we foster a culture of
ethical behaviors and principled risk-taking.
Megan Joseph, whom you met earlier, recalls that when she was working
as the director of community organizing, the leader of the local homeless
services agency became the target of public outcry and was scapegoated for
her risk-taking and forward-thinking approach. Megan and her colleagues
made the current narrative about people who are homeless visible and
articulated the necessary shifts in numerous meetings by naming it and
talking about it with stakeholders and offering sustainable solutions that
benefited everyone.
In the months following, the focus of media coverage and public
attention and in city council meetings shifted toward smart solutions rather
than the problems. This shift paved the way for a growing cohort of
principled risk-takers to promote sustainable solutions to homelessness and
create opportunities for a community that works for everyone.
Fallacy: There are people who object to unethical practices. It is
human nature to think about self-interest, and sometimes there
are excesses—this is part of life, and it has always been this way.
There are others who challenge the disempowering norms and
systems we have, and they sometimes fight for equity and
sustainability. They are generally angry people with a chip on
their shoulder. We should not be so emotional!
New Learning: Human beings have an innate “equity impulse,”
and we are wired to connect. We are endowed with bothcompassion and courage, which we can discover, cultivate, and
celebrate. When we are called to action from our universal heart
of compassion, we act from a space of inner peace and
principled outrage (not destructive anger). It is being ethical, not
being emotional, although it may appear otherwise to others!
Another example: S. Parasuraman, director of the Tata Institute of Social
Sciences (TISS), is a scholar, pro-activist, and institution builder. “You
never need to guess whose side I am on—I always side with people,” he
says. I am inspired by Parasuraman’s continuous effort to support principled
risk-takers. He hires courageous people like Shanaaz (pseudonym for her
protection). Shanaaz had moved to a village to support tribal people in
Madhya Pradesh when their land was being forcibly grabbed by local
vested interest groups. They hired a gang of hoodlums to kill Shanaaz who
gang-raped her, beat her up, and left her for dead. The villagers and her
family nursed her back to health, and legal action was initiated.
Parasuraman plays a pivotal role in addressing inequities in society by
supporting principled risk-takers and puts in place the four synergistic
operational strategies. Parasuraman encourages students, fellows, and
faculty to engage in national- and state-level policy formulation to obtain
new information through field research on social issues for strategic action.
He creates opportunities for students and faculty to enhance their ethical
leadership.
Typically, most programs have one or two of the synergistic operational
strategies in place. Few programs systematically develop transformational
stewardship or leadership in people from all the concerned constituencies,
including stakeholders; and even fewer have strategies in place to support
those who take the risk to rattle the status quo and be principled game
changers. To generate results, it is essential to have all four strategies in
place at the same time.
This may seem complicated, but it is not so. In our work and everyday
lives, we put similar strategies in place so that we achieve what we set out
to do. For example, an effective financial chief in an organization develops
personal leadership, does not have personal biases, works with information,
implements appropriate policies, and has accountability structures and
mechanisms in place to support those who take the risk to point out
mismanagement or fraud. Similarly, as parents, we develop parenting
capacities, find out relevant information and keep track of what is going on
with our child, intentionally create a nurturing environment and support our
child’s principled risk-taking behavior even when society ridicules or
frowns upon our child or even threatens to ostracize the family.
Applying Synergistic Operational Strategies for Results
in My Breakthrough Initiative
In the stewardship and leadership programs, participants take the project
they have designed on the CFSR worksheet and respond to four questions
related to the four synergistic operational strategies:
Do I have an explicit strategy in my project to develop transformational
stewardship and leadership capacities in a large number of
stakeholders? If not, this needs to be put in place.
Do I have information on the topic I am addressing (e.g., on HIV/AIDS,
youth violence, conservation and biodiversity)? How will I disseminate
this information to stakeholders?
Do I have an enabling environment for my project to succeed? Are the
necessary policies and laws in place? If not, connections must be made
with people who are working on these laws and policies and they mustbe proactively supported. In some instances, I may be able to engage in
policy reform myself. Have I involved the media in order to shift
societal conversations?
Do I have a strategy for support for myself and my team when I take
principled action and risk? Champions who value our work?
Responsible media? Have I worked along with stakeholders, co-creating
the strategy? Will they step up and speak up?
These synergistic operational strategies have been fundamental to
generating measurable and effective results—outputs and outcomes.
Transformational Results Chain
The Iterative Continuum for Transforming Reality
The transformational results chain (TRC) is the third design frame we use
to generate principled game-changing results. This template is useful for
those who work on projects and programs in all sectors—government,
nongovernmental organizations, aid agencies, philanthropy, academia,
media, business, political institutions, and international organizations,
including the United Nations.
In every process and every step of the continuum, the transformational
results chain designs the interplay between:
explicitly stated universal values;
different results (what) on a continuum (inputs-outputs-outcomes-
impacts), and the necessary shifts in systems and culture;
transformative processes (how) and breakthrough initiatives;
while sourcing my inner capacities, what I stand for through strategic
action (“who” I am BEING in action)—my agency.
Our praxis is alchemy in every process and every step, inextricably
linked to generating a new culture and systems, transforming our mindset to
support an ethical paradigm shift for humanity and planet. We must be able
to articulate the shifts necessary to generate outcomes and achieve goals;
and when these shifts become the norm, we have the building blocks of a
paradigm shift.
The transformational results chain is a planning and implementation
framework that responds to key questions with clear pathways for
implementing projects to achieve principled game-changing results.
What is different in the way we design, plan, and implement using the
transformational results chain? There are radical differences:
It builds on conventional frameworks, tools, and techniques and goes
beyond to deal with the complexity of problems we face today.
It takes interdependencies into account in simple and accessible ways so
that citizens can participate meaningfully. Instead of a “waterfall”
unidirectional process, it is a dynamic, generative, iterative process.
It explicitly and specifically sources the most important parameter for
paradigm shifts—the inner capacities of people in order to manifest
planetary transformation. Tools and techniques that source our inner
capacity are used in every step of designing, planning, and
implementation of our projects or ideas in action.
It requires us to embody the universal values that underpin the vision,
mission, and results we articulate in our initiatives, not only talk about
values as rhetoric.
It helps us to make the normative and systems shifts necessary for
generating an equitable and sustainable paradigm.
It enables us to co-create programs with a wide spectrum of citizens andexperts. It demystifies planning processes, allowing for informed
participation with an openness to transform.
It helps us to co-create new patterns, develop new rules of the game,
new norms, and new systems to solve problems for equitable and
sustainable change.
It is a planning template geared to transforming the reality of today and
creating a new future, without extrapolating from the past. Our vision
and mission statements are often grand and inspiring. But the planning
processes and tools are mostly based on our past experience, on unmet
goals and targets that were set in a previous context.
It enables us to build a stable foundation in the face of accelerating
change.
The transformational results chain enhances the conventional results
based management approach by anchoring every process in universal
values of dignity, equity, and compassion, and generating equitable and
sustainable results.
We need to engage in the entire transformational results chain in order to
generate equitable and sustainable results. In the human development
sector, people relate to the entire results chain; interestingly, some sectors
relate to the results chain partially and differently. For example, in the
corporate and business world, plans and their implementation include
outcomes, outputs, and inputs, but not impacts. Very few corporations and
businesses relate to impacts, which are results based on equitable and
sustainable outcomes. This phenomenon limits the purpose, contribution,
scope, and horizon of business endeavors. Profit is considered an outcome
by most businesses and corporations. But profit is an output; outcomes are
“measurable changes in human development.”
Other examples of sectors relating partially to the results chain include
donors and civil society organizations. The intention of people in these
organizations may be to generate outcomes and impacts, but they usually
fund and implement inputs to generate outputs. Outputs are essential, but
unless they generate normative and systems shifts in the context of
equitable and sustainable outcomes, they will not produce the intended
results. Their intentions remain unfulfilled, and they wonder why. For a
paradigm shift to occur, we must dance on the entire transformational
results chain with universal values.
The Existing Blueprints for “Change” Need to Change!
An enormous amount of time is spent developing plans to translate policies
into programs and projects in order to achieve the stated goals of
governments, businesses, nongovernmental organizations, international and
national aid agencies, and the United Nations.
The linear approaches have produced partial responses to problems; they
have solved a specific problem, for example, small pox eradication. But
they do not address more complex issues like health care; they do not alter
the cultural norms and systems for equitable and sustainable impact. The
transformational results chain is a design template to solve problems in the
midst of complexity, address interdependence, and shift patterns in order to
generate equitable and sustainable results.
Fallacy: Today’s advances in science and technology are so
rapid and specific that we will inevitably create silos when we use
new technologies for our well-being. There are no simple ways to
address this.
New Learning: There are simple and powerful ways to create
spaces for dynamic integration of new technological solutionsthrough transformative planning and implementation processes.
Goals, outcomes, and impacts provide direction. In the midst of
rapid change and seemingly overwhelming external conditions,
the universal values of equity, dignity, and compassion are the
only constant stabilizers of our endeavors.
Figure 6-5 is an illustration of a conventional results chain cited from the
United Nations Development Programme.37 Similar processes are used
worldwide for many other topics. The figure shows the continuum of results
—outputs, outcomes, impact—and inputs; the sequence for planning (from
impact to outcome, output, inputs); and the reverse sequence while
implementing.
Impacts are equitable and sustainable changes; outcomes are changes in
development conditions; outputs are specific products or services, activities,
and tasks completed; inputs are the resources we need to get activities
completed. Some agencies, for example, the United Nations Educational,
Scientific and Cultural Organization (UNESCO) add an additional
component—interim outcomes, described in Table 6-1.
Figure 6-6 is an illustration of a transformational results chain, for
increasing employment. Can you imagine what would become possible and
present if we designed programs for employment and ending joblessness
using the transformational results chain? We would stand firmly in our
universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion, and base our economic
and financial policies and strategies on these values. We would not be afraid
to change existing disempowering policies. We would create decent
working conditions for all workers, including in the United States, and not
promote worker insecurity by the constant threat of workers being laid off.
We would not manufacture goods in sweatshops or factories where the
human labor conditions are appalling. Responsible corporate leaders and
citizens (who are shareholders and consumers of cheap goods) would move
beyond the profit-by-any-means and the money motive, and design and
support strategies that create more decent jobs and labor conditions
globally. Responsible wealthy individuals, for whom a hundred thousand
dollars is pocket change, would step up and think about what is necessary
for the next generations, prosperous communities, and a thriving planet. We
would consider new policies that shift global systems and norms without
fear and illogical justifications that maintain the status quo—for example,
equity could manifest if labor mobility matched capital flows around the
world; or closing down tax havens. And this needs courageous, visionary
corporate leadership and citizen engagement.
Figure 6-5 . A Conventional Results Chain.
The transformational results chain is about doing the same things
differently. It has the same continuum of results as the conventional results
chain—outputs, outcomes, impact—and inputs; the same sequence for
planning—from impact to outcome, output, inputs; and the same sequence
while implementing. But it has vital new components: (1) transformational
processes and breakthrough initiatives that generate results at different
interfaces: input-output; output-outcome; outcome-impact; (2) foundation
of universal values for every step, every process, all breakthrough
initiatives, sourcing our inner capacities and wisdom; and (3) who I am
BEING in action, in every process and every step of the continuum—a
transformational manager, a transformational leader, a transformational
steward.

*Interim outcome: Another result added as a precursor to outcomes or change in development
conditions. Several organizations (e.g., UNESCO) add “interim outcomes” in their results chain,
because their results noted as “outputs” may not adequately reflect the degree of efforts and
achievements, and the goals and outcomes are yet to be fully achieved. Some programs include
interim outcomes in their results chain, for example, in the project Saving Women’s Lives through
UNICEF in South Asia.
Fallacy: Advances in technology require us to have step-by-step
logical analytic and planning tools to ensure that we do not miss
details to address problems and produce results in complex
situations.
New Learning: When we anchor our logical thinking, strategies,
and action in the universal values of dignity and equity, we are
open to seeing interdependent factors and can address complex
problems in order to generate transformational results; and we
can integrate rapid scientific and technological advances.
Interim outcomes are changes and results that must be in place for the
outcomes and goals to be achieved. For example, the purpose of the project
Saving Women’s Lives through UNICEF in the late 1990s was to reduce
maternal mortality in South Asia.
The impact was to realize the self-determination, dignity, and well-
being of all women.
The outcomes were to have a reduction in maternal mortality and
related disabilities that arise during complications of pregnancy.
One of the interim outcomes was to establish a fully functional hospital
with emergency obstetric care for a population of five hundredthousand; another interim outcome was to have 100 percent of women
with obstetric complications treated in the facilities.
A sample of outputs include: value-based leadership learning-in-action
programs for social services providers and community members
designed and delivered; local plans for maternal mortality reduction and
disability prevention, including indicators developed and implemented;
technical guidelines formulated and made accessible; clinicians trained
and capable of delivering adequate care; and health care facilities
outfitted with essential equipment and supplies.
Three universal values underpinned the design and implementation at
every step based on the Human Rights agenda—dignity, equity (non-
discrimination), and self-determination as “agency.” “All beings are
born free and equal in dignity and rights” is Article 1 of the Declaration
of Human Rights.38
Table 6-2 compares conventional business-as-usual ways of working with
transformative approaches. Transformative approaches lead to equitable and
sustainable change.

Fallacy: We will not be efficient and our productivity will suffer if
we base our strategies and actions on universal values in
institutions. In addition, our economy will have setbacks—there is
no time! We must enhance labor productivity!
New Learning: Creating a culture of responsibility based on
universal values is a time-saver; it enhances productivity and
efficiency, fosters creativity, and generates results. It transcends
the industrial mindset of finishing more tasks in as short a time as
possible (efficiency) and values enduring results, well-being, our
earth, and thriving and prosperity for all!
Reversing the HIV/AIDS epidemic globally through the United Nations
involved several agencies focusing on specific aspects of the response, with
the Joint United Nations Programme on HIV/AIDS (UNAIDS) as the
coordinating body to avoid duplication of efforts. Our role through UNDP
was to address governance and leadership challenges within countries. Our
unique contributions were based on using transformational templates and
techniques for design, planning, and implementation: a conscious full-
spectrum response, synergistic operational strategies, and the
transformational results chain as well as a mindful implementation praxis.
Dignity, equity, and inclusion were the universal values upon which all
processes, strategies, and actions were based. And each step was an
opportunity for both personal and social transformation.
We were clear about the normative and systems shifts needed to reversethe HIV/AIDS epidemic, and explicitly articulated them together with
partners. We worked on shifting from stigmatizing people as promiscuous
and irresponsible to inquiring into our own behaviors, sourcing universal
values to embrace people living with HIV/AIDS and others who are
stigmatized; shifting from unprotected sex to respectful and responsible
preventive measures during sex, such as using condoms to prevent the
spread of the virus; shifting from discrimination in the workplace and health
facilities to dignity, respect, inclusion, and compassionate care; shifting
from lack of testing and treatment facilities to increased availability, access,
and utilization of testing and treatment services; and shifting from
prohibitive treatment costs and enormous profit margins on life-saving
medications to affordable treatment.
Shifting the norms related to sex and sexuality, our tendency to be
judgmental, exclusionary, and “holier than thou,” requires that we source
our universal values to disrupt the patterns. It requires profound personal
transformation.
Figure 6-7 illustrates the interplay of our transformative roles and actions
as managers, leaders, and stewards generating a continuum of results in the
HIV/AIDS program.
Figure 6-7 . Transformational Results Chain for Reversing the HIV/AIDS Epidemic.
Considering how our personal transformation profoundly influences the
results we generate, each interface where actions and breakthrough
initiatives occur—the dark vertical arrows in the diagram—must (1) be
sourced from our inner capacities and universal values, (2) shift
disempowering norms and systems, and (3) solve specific problems.
Throughout this book, there are examples of transformational initiatives and
results related to reversing HIV/AIDS in the locations where we worked.
Fallacy: Since every culture is so different, it is not possible to
operate from universal values in global programs. We must
respect differences and should not impose our values on others!
New Learning: Different cultural expressions celebrate diversity
in various ways. “Culture” must not be the cover-up of
disempowering, diminishing, and exploitative behaviors in
different parts of the world. Human beings deep down are the
same. We love, grieve, are wired to connect, need to belong and
be embraced. In every culture worldwide we express
compassion, fairness, dignity, empathy, and sympathy. Global
programs based on universal values work as long as they are not
prescriptive and do not impose our opinions on how things must
be done. Instead global programs unfold inner capacities of
people and uphold universal values through action, while
celebrating different wholesome expressions.
Another example: The Youth Violence Prevention Task Force of Santa
Cruz County, Megan, whom you met, and Sarah Emmert, the director of
community organizing at United Way of Santa Cruz County, California,
launched an initiative. The universal values of dignity and equity
underpinned the initiative, its processes, and its results. The impact sought
was a thriving community where all youth and adults have well-being and
are reaching their full potential. The outcomes and goals were an increase
in young people ages sixteen to twenty-four obtaining and retaining quality
jobs; an increase in young people graduating high school; and a decrease in
violence among young people.
Many outputs were listed—for example, programs for youth employment
training, employer engagement, academic tutoring, and parent/familyengagement; programs for gang prevention and intervention; law
enforcement training in youth development and trauma-informed policing.
Inputs included staff, meetings, funds, data, and reports.
Working together on the transformational results chain, Megan and Sarah
recognized that the education, public safety, government, social service, and
community-based sectors all had to collaborate based on universal values to
reach these impacts, outcomes, goals, and outputs. Sarah says, “If we
continue to state the issue as ‘we are against police brutality’ only, we will
not create change. We are focusing on what we want to see in the
community: ‘strengthened law enforcement–community relations.’ Law
enforcement, youth, families, and grassroots organizations are all able to
see that they have a role to play in improving law enforcement–community
relations, based on dignity and fairness for all. Our county’s Law
Enforcement Chiefs Association has been funding the Youth Violence
Prevention Task Force and its efforts for the past three years.”
Fallacy: Government, business, and civil society in developing
countries are poor, corrupt, and inefficient, with inadequate
infrastructure and services—they need to use templates like the
conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR), synergistic
operational strategies (SOS) and transformational results chain
(TRC). In developed countries like the United States, Japan, and
countries in Europe, we have the technology and know what to
do, so we do not need these templates.
New Learning: All countries need transformational templates in
government, business, and civil society for designing and
implementing equitable, sustainable change. The conventional
planning methods used in all countries are based on linear,
reductionist templates.
The scarcity and dominator mindsets are common in all countries, rich
and poor, and influence strategic design and action. “Otherness,”
corruption, exploitation, and inefficiencies are present in different forms in
all countries. Transformation templates such as CFSR, SOS, and TRC are
essential to manifest paradigm shifts based on universal values in every part
of the world for peace and prosperity.
Applying the Transformational Results Chain to Our
Breakthrough Initiatives
It is our choice: do we simply want to do a job because we have to, or are
we choosing to make a significant impact? If we cannot or do not choose to
transform the way we design, plan, and implement large-scale or small
initiatives, we will not produce the results needed for the future we want to
create.
Every program and institution makes vision statements; most articulate
impact and outcomes they wish to produce. They state the core values that
form the basis of strategy and action. Results are crucial, they say. Yet most
conventional initiatives ultimately center on outputs only—production of
goods and services, profit, investments in infrastructure, training, studies.
Conventional initiatives include selected social and economic outcomes as
their purpose, such as increased income, health, education, healthy food,
nutrition.
Unifying architects know that although outputs and outcomes are
important, they do not provide a true measure of human potential and well-
being. They know that for equitable and sustainable impacts, the outcomes
and outputs must be firmly based on universal values and results must be
generated through personal and social transformational processes. Thesestewards and leaders also hold with ease the seeming paradox of insisting
on accountability and results while allowing for the unpredictability of
emergence.
Practitioners and participants of the stewardship program source
universal values for design. They refer to the project already designed on
the CFSR worksheet (Figure 6-3), including the four synergistic operational
strategies (Figure 6-4). They now expand their design on the
transformational results chain through three sets of design questions or
inquiries (Figure 6-6), which are given hereafter.
Exploring inside out:
I am clear about what I stand for; do I embody the universal values that
underpin my commitment and initiative?
Have I designed forums where I partner with others to generate results,
each person standing in their inner capacities and wisdom, embodying
values that support the project, with the commitment to manifest our
greatness for a new future?
Did we articulate together the universal values that underpin our
breakthrough initiative or project?
Did we articulate the principles for implementing our project?
What results do I intend to generate? I begin with impact.
What impact do we intend to make? Equitable and sustainable change in
human well-being, for all species, the environment, the planet?
What paradigm shifts are essential for impact?
What outcomes and goals do we intend to achieve aligned with the
impact? What are the changes in development conditions that we wish
to see, based on equity and dignity for all?
What shifts must we have in cultural norms and systems in order for
these outcomes and goals to be generated?
What outputs do I intend to achieve? What specific products,
technological solutions, or services will be delivered, based on universal
values and aligned with the impact and outcomes? What activities and
tasks must we complete?
What inputs, based on universal values and ethical operations, will I use
to generate these outputs?
How will I work to generate the results? I begin with the input-output
interface.
Input-output interface: How do we generate transformation through
routine activities to achieve outputs? How do we source our inner
capacities through daily activities such as routine meetings, replying to
e-mails, report writing, workshops, planning sessions, and organizing
events? Which techniques and praxes are to be used? Are the necessary
resources procured according to universal values and ethical principles?
Does the funding come from people and organizations who want similar
outcomes and impacts and source universal values? Are we getting
value-based expertise from people who care deeply about dignity and
equity for all as well as a thriving planet? Are we procuring equipment
and supplies manufactured under fair labor standards in ways that are
ecologically sound?
Output-outcome interface: How do we create the necessary shifts in
norms, systems, and structures while achieving the goals and outcomes?
How do we source our inner capacities in order to have the courage tocreate? Which transformational tools, techniques, and praxes will we
use?
Outcome-impact interface: How will we make our initiative equitable
and sustainable? How do we source our inner capacities to create a
paradigm shift and move equity and sustainability from rhetoric to
reality? Which transformational tools, techniques, and praxes will we
use?
Einstein said, “Insanity is doing the same thing over and over again and
expecting different results.” This is not about introducing some new tools or
tinkering in the margin. It is about fundamentally generating the radical
culture of transformation, in which people give themselves permission to
act from a new way of BEING, embracing change that leads to results,
instead of fearing change. Organizations have their procedures, processes,
