tools, and techniques, and cultures have their norms and rituals. Both are
often unwilling to embark on vital, radical transformation. Embarking on
such programs to generate transformational results requires citizens,
leaders, managers, and everyone to step out of their comfort zone and take a
principled risk in order to create courageous new pathways.
Most importantly, how do we begin our work by looking within—to our
attitudes, our behaviors, our worldviews, and the inner indomitable spirit
that informs our decisions even in the face of opposition? We ask ourselves:
who am I, and do I embody the values that underpin an equitable and
sustainable world with thriving humanity and planet? What is the source of
my actions?
BEING a Principled Game Changer
Typically, we think of game changers as people who innovate and, through
that innovation, disrupt or change a pattern. For example, before the 1970s,
computers were far too big and expensive to be used by ordinary people in
their own homes; since then, innovators have created the opportunity for
people to use computers at home; we can even be mobile on laptops and
smart phones. This is referred to as a game-changing solution. I have
redefined game-changing solutions to mean “innovations and solutions that
shift patterns based on universal values.”
BEING game changers requires us to weave technological solutions with
shifts in systems and cultural norms and embody universal values for
strategic action, simultaneously and synergistically, into patterns that
generate powerful transformational results. While we practice and grow, we
walk more joyfully and buoyantly in life. A participant-practitioner’s
insight: “I am the spirit of interdependence—I do not just understand
interdependence intellectually.”
A principled game changer requires criteria for the ideas and initiatives
to be game-changing. When our intention is to shift disempowering norms
and systems to generate new patterns as we solve problems, we sometimes
assume that our efforts are game-changing. But we must have explicit,
simple criteria to know whether our intentions are actually changing the
rules and norms of the game.
In “The Story of Solutions,” Annie Leonard posits the fundamental
question, is the solution game-changing?39 The problems that require game-
changing solutions are both global and local: extrinsic power and decisions
are concentrated in the hands of very few people; our purpose in life is lost
in the midst of an unending spiral of wants; we have established an
accounting system that externalizes costs, thereby perpetuating exploitation
of people and our planet; and we face huge, escalating inequity. Leonard
uses an easy acronym to define her criteria to assess game-changingsolutions: GOAL.
G: gives more power to people
O: opens our eyes—once our basic needs are met, happiness comes
from community and purpose
A: accounts for all costs
L: lessens the wealth gap
The essential point is to have explicit interdependent criteria that allow us
to distinguish a “game-changing solution” from one that is a “solution that
keeps the old game in place.” I add two additional criteria for a solution to
be principled and game-changing:
It must be based on universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion
which form the foundation of systems and cultural shifts.
It must generate transformational results, where the “what” and “how”
are integrated—transformative outputs, outcomes, and impacts.
We need to have criteria. Otherwise, we may assume that our
“intentions” will automatically result in the change we wish to see.
These criteria can be applied in any situation—in our everyday lives and
at home, as we volunteer in community, in our workplace, in policies,
programs, and initiatives. If we choose to change our consumption patterns,
stark materialism, and the unending spiral of opulent wants present in every
country in the world, we must be principled game changers! How we
remunerate and treat our maids and domestic help in our homes in
Bangladesh, India, Nepal, or Pakistan can be game-changing instead of
exploitative. Similar patterns of exploitation exist in other Asian, African,
Middle Eastern, and Latin American countries.
Fallacy: As an ordinary citizen, there is nothing I can do to
change the unworkable patterns. At best, I can take small
actions, which do not matter in the big picture. I am not a game
changer.
New Learning: Anyone can be a principled game changer. This
is about sourcing our universal values and being willing to
change what is not working. We need to think about what matters
to us deeply and articulate the shifts we wish to see. It is not
important how big or small the actions are; what is important for a
paradigm shift and transformational results is that each one
chooses to BE a principled game changer and acts accordingly.
BEING a principled game changer implies having the courage to create
new patterns. Courage is not the absence of fear or despair. As sensitive
human beings, we will often be faced with despair and also face our fears as
they come up—the fears that are the result of our socialization process.
Courage is our capacity to speak up and move ahead in spite of despair or
fear.
An example of someone who uses his profession as an entry point for
changing the game is Priyadarshan, my husband, an econometrician who
designs programs for decision-making at the national level in collaboration
with governments. Using numerous simultaneous equations representing
interdependent factors affecting different sectors of the economy, he has
created new and original templates. They are unique because he connects
financial policies to investment policies, making explicit the
interdependent, interconnected factors and consequences of particulardecisions.
Priyadarshan’s work is potentially game-changing because he makes the
invisible factors visible and transparent through concrete analysis and
predictions. Decision-making for equitable and sustainable change becomes
possible at the highest national level. He ensures continuity by sharing his
work as open source and building capacity in economists and policy-makers
to use the related tools and techniques. Priyadarshan’s contributions have
the possibility of creating game-changing policies and strategies within
countries through farsighted leadership, connecting the abstract to concrete
decisions.
BEING a principled game changer implies that we stop sitting on the
fence. We engage passionately in a joyful pursuit of a world that works for
everyone. We criticize what does not work by creating alternatives. We
engage courageously. This courage requires centeredness within our own
being and being continuously in touch with who we are and what we stand
for.
Sometimes, our selective engagement with a cause we identify with
personally and strongly can become a limiting, transient response. We often
“fight” for justice, but do not embrace everyone as we engage. Do we not
see that our behaviors may be fueling another form of injustice? For
example, in villages in India, women from so-called higher castes working
for gender equity in India refuse to sit together and have a meal with other
women who are from a so-labeled low caste. Equity is not rhetoric or self-
serving—it is a way of BEING. In the United States, African Americans
and those of Hispanic origin are working to end racism, but some of them
maintain a class hierarchy and elitism within their communities.
We will not be able to end discrimination by engaging with a single issue
only; we must go deeper and end discrimination in all its forms and
expressions. The root factors of gender discrimination or racism or the caste
system are the same—they are related to our inability to stand in universal
values of dignity, equity, and compassion.
Courage is not bravado. Bravado is a bold manner, or a show of boldness
intended to impress or intimidate. Courage is not about fighting for or
against something—it is the ability to transcend what does not work with
strategic and concrete action, beyond “us versus them.” Principled game
changers do not walk by injustice or exclusion apathetically, because their
compassionate hearts do not allow it. They are called to respond and act.
Chapter 7
The Mindful Pro-Activist
Being and Implementing the Change I
Wish to See
I slept and dreamt that life was joy.
I awoke and saw that life was service.
I acted and behold, service was joy.
RABINDRANATH TAGORE
The Joy of BEING in Action
The mindful pro-activist is simultaneously the contemporary pioneer, the
unifying architect, and the radical transformer.
We manifest who we are BEING through action based on radical design
and universal values. This work is “mindfulness and heartfulness in action”
in every step. “Mindfulness is awareness that arises through paying
attention, on purpose, in the present moment, nonjudgmentally. It’s about
knowing what is on your mind,” says Jon Kabat-Zinn, an eminent scholar
and expert on mindfulness and its application for stress reduction.40 In our
work, I expand Kabat-Zinn’s definition to include “awareness that arisesthrough paying attention, on purpose, in the present moment,
nonjudgmentally, as I source my oneness, what I stand for, and my universal
values through BEING in action.”
When we implement whatever we do mindfully, moment to moment, the
process of implementation itself becomes a space for nonjudgmental, wise,
ethical action. Juxtaposing “nonjudgmental” with “ethical action” may
seem like a paradox; but in our work ethical action is not about judging a
person as bad or wrong, but recognizing what actions are incongruent with
our universal values and taking the necessary steps to change. This
mindfulness is imbued with the energy and equanimity of universal values
of compassion, dignity, equity. This is the way we generate results,
transform conflicts, provide services, organize in communities, and conduct
business at home, in our workplace, and in society.
It is the work of BEING a mindful pro-activist. Activism is action to
bring about political or social change based on values that benefit society.
However, activism is not always perceived in that spirit, because direct
action can be interpreted as disruptive or even aggressive, especially when
it is in support of or opposition to a controversial issue. I coined the word
“pro-activism” to mean the “proactive actions” we take when our universal
heart of compassion is wide open to everyone—to those whose actions we
oppose and those whose actions are in sync with our universal values. Pro-
activism is mindful. When equanimity and our innate sense of equity are
present we find the courage to speak up and speak out. Breaking the silence
about issues that are inconsistent with our universal values is the beginning
of systems and normative change.
Each one of us can recall moments in our life when we knew that what
we saw was unfair, and we either did something about it or wanted to do
something about it. Mindful pro-activism is the next evolutionary stage of
activism, in which we have the possibility of transcending polarities, where
there is no winner or loser. From this space of compassion and humanity it
is possible to create systems and cultures that work for everyone, with no
one left out.
The three threads of our abilities—BEING, designing, and taking action
—are woven together to manifest a radical paradigm shift. In Chapter Five,
as contemporary pioneers, we discovered how our inner capacities are the
most powerful source of transformational results; in Chapter Six, as
unifying architects, we worked with transformative design templates. In this
chapter, we explore ways to source our inner capacities for implementing
our ideas and projects as well as our everyday actions.
When we explicitly align each value-based design template with our
actions, these templates become a guided continuum for mindful
implementation. They create cognitive coherence, so that our mental
processes of perception, insight, memory, discernment, and reasoning are
congruent with universal values. The ways in which the transformative
design templates are used for mindful implementation are described in this
chapter, and in Part Three.
What Is Not Considered
This book does not discuss any technical implementation strategies for
specific topics, not because they are not important, but because each
complex problem needs a wide array of expertise and talent. The
implementation of immunization services, reversing the HIV/AIDS
epidemic and providing affordable treatment, reducing maternal mortality,
addressing climate change, ending violence, reducing poverty, and creating
thriving communities all require strategies with technical expertise for
dealing with problems. Expertise is essential. However, expertise alone will
not solve problems equitably and sustainably.Everyday Actions of Mindful Stewards,
Leaders, and Managers
No matter what we are called to do at work, at home, and in the world, we
express ourselves as mindful pro-activists in all our roles. As mindful
stewards, our approach of working with all the resources—people, sentient
beings, and planet—is one of caring, compassion, and courage to create
new patterns and generate new societal conversations, with reverence for
life. As ethical leaders, we design plans aligned with universal values for
our families, in our projects, our work lives, and our communities. We make
pragmatic decisions, have generative conversations, and take principled
action. We engage in routine activities mindfully, completing activities and
tasks not as chores or a burden, but as a contribution: “I care for myself and
others; I engage in the same way in my community and at work.” Even
when someone in an organization has a specific role, transformational
managing, leading, and stewarding are all an integral part of the way the
person thinks and acts.
Choose Appropriate Implementation “Lenses” or
Frameworks for Transformation
I explored seven templates created by different experts or scholars on the
ground in various locations to analyze what works best for generating
results through various programs, such as enhancing child survival,
reducing maternal mortality, addressing the HIV/AIDS epidemic,
strengthening human resources within UN agencies. Most of these
templates heightened understanding. I found the Likert-Emberling
framework most suitable for responding to our professional and
programmatic challenges and for achieving results.41 Allan Henderson and
Sharon Knoll introduced this framework in the UNDP HIV/AIDS program
in several countries. People from different walks of life—government,
business, civil society, UN agencies—could see how they functioned as
leaders, and what they could do differently to be more effective.
Here are some of the features of the modified Likert-Emberling
framework that are important:
It is simple, clear, relevant in different settings, and easily
comprehended at all levels of bureaucracies and in different sectors and
cultures;
It is aligned with how we are expected to function within the United
Nations and in the countries in which we work. It focuses on timely
decisions during a crisis, sound management, a results orientation,
principled (ethical) action, holding multiple perspectives without
compromising universal values.
It creates common understanding between both “program” and
“operations” divisions. In most large and medium-sized organizations,
people working in the administrative, financial, and human resources
units have little interaction on a daily basis with people working on
different projects in the same organization. They work in silos. This
framework speaks to staff with different functions in the various
departments, transcending silos.
At each stage, the process is languaged in a way that encourages us to
notice, name, and be aware of our behavior. It is not about judging
ourselves—it is about being mindful and noticing that our own behavior
is sometimes healthy and sometimes unhealthy; that our well-being
depends on moving toward BEING healthy at each stage.
It relates to everyone who participates and practices, knowing that each
person is at a different stage of personal growth and evolution. No one
is placed in a category. The underlying assumption is anyone cantransform profoundly at any time. This is acceptable for all present. As
we hold each other with respect and acceptance, each person is
stimulated by the pull of our evolving self to strive to BE healthy at
every stage.
It is cognitively coherent with the conscious full-spectrum approach and
other transformative practices, making learning easier and practice
smoother.
The stages of personal leadership development follow the same pattern
as the stages of organizational and team development, and are therefore
easier to apply to individual, team, and organizational development.
The stages of leadership in the modified Likert-Emberling frame are:
Stage 1. Autocratic, making decisions by oneself and forcing others to
comply—“Do what I say right now”—without explanation. Under certain
circumstances, this may be appropriate—“healthy.” For example: when a
parent pulls a toddler away from putting their hands into a fire; firefighters
instruct people to get out of a burning building; when there is an accident
and someone’s life must be saved.
It is unhealthy when it is coercive, when it demands that others do
something with no context or relatedness present.
Stage 2. Management and managers, relating to rules and roles.
Following the rules and acting out specific roles is required in any
organization, whether it is a university, a company, a government
department, an NGO, a restaurant, or a sports team. What is healthy is to
follow the rules when necessary: in a basketball game when the referee
calls someone out for a foul or double-dribbling; or when a teacher pulls up
a student for lack of attendance or an assignment not completed on time; or
when the accounting group notices that bills submitted are not in line with
the rules.
What is unhealthy is when the rules become the all-important thing,
instead of a mechanism to accomplish something; for example, a manager’s
obstruction of the signing of an important paper, even when all rules are
followed. Many organizations get stuck at this stage, where the rules
become an end in themselves. Another example can be found in families
and communities: “Children should be seen and not heard” is a rule that can
result in suppressed human beings.
Stage 3. Pragmatic leader, focusing on practicality and results. This is an
action- and results-oriented person. When healthy, such a leader proactively
involves people; for example, leading a campaign for renewable energy.
It is unhealthy when the ends justify the means, when the leader demands
results at any cost, even at the cost of the well-being of employees, or when
personal principles are abandoned in the pursuit of profit. Another example
is the unrelenting pressure put on children to produce high grades at the cost
of their physical, emotional, mental, and social well-being.
Stage 4. Principled leader, steward, mentor, relying on ethical principles
of conduct. Individuals embody core universal values of dignity, equity, and
compassion in all the stages of leadership, and act accordingly. In this stage,
every action is explicitly reviewed in the context of universal values and
integrity. A healthy example is when consideration is given before accepting
funds from an organization that does not adhere to universal values. Ethical
dilemmas are bound to present themselves as we engage—at work or at
home—and learning how to transcend them is vital.
It can become unhealthy when principles become the “truth” and
individuals become self-righteous, making other people or groups wrong;
we become rigid and inflexible, judging other people. At work, at home,
and in our community, we often put each other down through self-righteousbehavior, stifling the manifestation of principled action.
Stage 5. Perspectivist leader and steward, holding multiple perspectives
without compromising universal values. This does not mean an “anything
goes” attitude. It means this leader can discern and distinguish worldviews
based on universal values from opinions and points of view based solely on
rational arguments and justifications. They are not stuck in a particular
perspective or in any particular point of view. This is a leader who
recognizes interdependence and values diversity, and has the ability to see
different perspectives and provides a space for creating alternatives and
innovation. BEING healthy at this stage implies BEING healthy at all the
other stages. There are people like Andrej Nosov, whom you met in Chapter
Four, and Irene Goldman, in Chapter Five, who are stage-five leaders in
their communities.
These stages of leadership generated conversations for staff in
government, civil society organizations, and UN agencies to move ahead
because each progressive, healthy stage helps to overcome the unhealthy
expression of the preceding stage; and yet we need to work in all stages.
Depending upon the function we perform at a particular time, we locate
ourselves in that stage. With common language and distinctions, individuals
and teams are stimulated to notice (without judgment) unhealthy patterns of
behavior and delays at work; and then strive toward healthier ways. For
example, while we were reviewing plans and achievements on the integrity
lens (Figure 7-1) related to financing and decentralization, a few staff
members from accounting, after some reflection, pointed out how their
team was unhealthy because they had not processed payments on time. And
they took immediate steps to remedy the situation. This was a dramatic
change from usual defensive behaviors.
Do you have a framework that allows you to track where you are, and
how you are functioning? If not, find one that fulfills the needs of your
work and that is based on universal values.
BEING Mindful Stewards, Leaders, and Managers
Transformational Managers
In conventional settings, a manager is expected to be efficient and
productive and to get the job done. How the job gets done is less important.
Managers are not expected to either question or contribute to the larger
policies, goals, and outcomes of the organization.
Transformational managers in the workplace use every routine activity—
meetings, e-mails, communication, planning, reporting, supervising—to
generate new conversations, results, and outputs based on universal values.
They practice various ways to source inner capacities for action. They are
attuned with others and create spaces for the full potential of people to
manifest in the workplace. They and their teams contribute to the “big
picture”—the transformational results, or outputs, outcomes, and impact—
of the organization. Staff are inspired to align and encourage equitable and
sustainable communities, society, and workplace, and they grow personally
and professionally as they see how their contribution helps create this
change.
Transformational management also applies at home. Family members
create spaces for everyone’s love and views to be expressed, for everyone to
have fun, listen deeply, speak responsibly, and not feel the need to be
perfect.
Transforming Routine Activities
When we choose to use transformational processes, the manner in which
routine activities are performed and business is transacted becomes acontinuous opportunity for extraordinary change. We have many
opportunities to influence change in our daily activities through the
conversations we have and the way we are BEING. At home, we can be
conscious about how we do routine activities such as taking children to
school, doing chores, preparing and eating meals, cleaning, sharing what
happened during our day, and playing games together. At work we can
transform routine meetings, replying to e-mails, report writing, planning
sessions, monitoring, or organizing events. Are we implementing these
everyday activities in a manner that allows transformation to naturally
emerge? These opportunities present themselves frequently and regularly in
all human endeavors, in every organization, at home, in community, and at
work, and remain largely underutilized for a paradigm shift to occur. The
pace of generating sustainable and equitable results would accelerate
exponentially if we chose to harness the transformative potential of the
plethora of routine activities in everyday life.
Let’s take the example of routine meetings to illustrate how we generate
new conversations and transform results. There are many useful
management exercises with different objectives to change the way meetings
are held—to make meetings more efficient, to reduce defensiveness in
meetings, to negotiate in conflict situations. These exercises have their
place. Here, the purpose is to transform meetings into spaces that focus on
generating results based on universal values.
In Chapter Five, you met Irene Goldman, a global citizen from Brooklyn
who was deeply concerned about children and civilians affected by the
turmoil in Korea during the Korean War. Irene spoke up at community
meetings, shifting the conversation to action based on universal values.
When other community members were arguing about “teaching the country
a lesson,” oblivious to Koreans’ suffering, Irene challenged the prevailing
philosophy of needing war “in order to create peace.”
Societal conversations shift when we have the courage to break the
silence. Hemlata from New Delhi, whom you met in Chapter Four, engages
with labor unions and also supports people living in shelters. She recalls
changing neighborhood conversations where citizens stereotyped “those
people in shelters” as disreputable, nasty, rough—to be avoided at any cost.
By speaking about what she cares about deeply, the universal values she
stands for, the world with fairness and dignity children could grow up in,
Hemlata drew her neighbors into conversations that included homeless
people as human beings who have dignity.
Megan Joseph noted how the trajectory of a community project shifted
when she spoke up about the lack of representation of people who had
experienced incarceration at a meeting intended to plan reentry activities
specifically for them. Bringing attention to the fact that the voice of those
impacted by the planning was absent ruffled some feathers but ultimately
led to including the leadership of those who had been formerly incarcerated.
Nestor Arias, while working to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic in Latin
America, transformed routine bureaucratic meetings into spaces for new
breakthrough actions to emerge. This may not strike you as an important
example, but anyone who has worked in bureaucracy anywhere in the world
knows the significance of his contribution. These bureaucratic forums are
most often uninspiring, unimaginative, and procedure-driven; they have a
high transaction cost and are time-consuming. But Nestor used this routine
activity as an opportunity to create results-oriented partnerships—
negotiating, deepening, and aligning with participants’ inner capacities,
connecting through action with the mandate of truly serving and truly
caring for people living with HIV or AIDS in Latin America and the
Caribbean. The results were significant. Through the transformationalleadership programs, seventy-five key leaders generated record-setting
results.
Based on my experience in the UN and elsewhere, I designed several
templates for transformative processes, and I am sharing one of them—
making meetings transformational spaces for generating results based on
universal values. It does not matter whether we are the chair or moderator
of meetings—what matters is our ability to generate new conversations for
the changes and results we are committed to. Practitioners find this template
for transformational meetings very useful and are able to move agendas that
are “stuck.”
Ten Steps for a Transformational Meeting: Generating Conversations
for Results
Before the meeting, we need to prepare ourselves, sourced in our stand,
with information related to the people in the room, the topic discussed. Too
often, we attend meetings unprepared and uninformed.
In the meeting:
1. Ground yourself in your stand—constantly remind yourself of the
universal values you stand for, the qualities that manifest in this world
through you to make a better place. Source the values that are uniquely
yours.
2. Create the space for everyone to access their own power and go
beyond the mundane. Ask them: what do you care about? Acknowledge
them for their presence and the deeper commitment behind their
presence. There are several ways to do this, including short, powerful,
value-based videos.
3. Set up the conversation for co-creation. Invite participants to be part of
the larger change you want to see. For example, if the meeting is on
equitable services or ending violence in schools, invite them to have a
stake in the accomplishment of the goals.
4. Engage in conversations that invite in the possibility of seeing the
value of a conscious full-spectrum approach. Participants need to see
that typical responses to problems are partial. To do that, acknowledge
that they have been working and yet the results they wish to see are not
there.
5. Reposition the problem that the group would like to solve, as a
breakdown (because of their/our commitment), as a possibility for a
breakthrough.
6. Notice the commitment for action behind complaints and shift the
conversation. Avoid the normal way of responding: arguing, agreeing,
disagreeing.
7. Skillfully transform output-based conversations to outcomes and
impact. Focus on what really matters to people—the outcomes and
impacts they want to produce. In most instances, we get “stuck” in the
small stuff, the nitty-gritty. Draw people to the bigger picture and
context.
8. Offer a concrete strategy for sustainable results through the conscious
full-spectrum approach. Speak succinctly—don’t get complicated. For
example, “Our work is to (i) solve problems, (ii) shift patterns, (iii)
source inner capacities.”
9. Create an agenda for action with two timelines: (i) next three months,
(ii) two years. In too many meetings, we continue to discuss
challenges, but not about the actions to be taken.
10. Close meeting with acknowledgment.Transformational Leaders
The conventional leader is expected to deliver results related to stated goals
of the organization. In some organizations, the leader may be expected to
deliver results at any cost; in others, there may be limited authority to put
plans into action.
Transformational leaders, however, work “inside out,” sourcing their
inner capacities to generate results—at home or in the workplace. They
operate by embodying universal values, not just talking about them—they
are wise, ethical leaders in action. Wherever they engage, they are attuned
to others, and create a resonant space for transformation and
communication.
In their workplace, transformational leaders foster and proactively
support transformational managers and staff, actively engaging in
transformational processes in all activities and encouraging staff to do the
same. Transformational leaders design and implement strategy,
transcending linear fragmented methods, and establish iterative, dynamic,
and transformational approaches and methods. They are steadfast and able
to navigate the system, uphold dignity and fair wages for labor, and
constantly review processes to ensure sustainability for our planet. It is a
way of BEING. The achievement of the goals and outcomes are as
important as the transformational approaches and methods used to achieve
them—it is not about producing results at any cost. Attuned with others,
achieving results is a vehicle for self-expression, accomplishment, and
realizing the full potential of people engaged in these endeavors. These
transformational leaders are adept at making necessary systems and cultural
shifts for achieving goals, outcomes, and outputs both within and outside
the organization, often in the face of little agreement or outright opposition.
Transformational leadership manifests at home as well as in the
workplace. The conversations we have at home, the responses we have
when children ask questions, our interpretation of what we consider
“successful” for ourselves and our family members, expectations we have
of each other, all determine whether we choose to live our lives to our
fullest potential or decide to join the never-ending rat race! As consumers in
today’s world, we all determine the fate of other human and sentient beings
and the planet through our awareness and the choices we make.
Transforming Systems and Cultural Norms
Transformational leadership is about BEING principled game changers, not
simply problem solvers. These leaders deal with what is not working by
creating alternatives. Transformational leaders not only solve complex
societal problems at a surface level but also proactively address the deeper
dimensions of the problem. They show that it is possible to design, plan,
and implement programs differently to generate transformational results.
Jerry White shares the 1997 Nobel Peace Prize awarded to the
International Campaign to Ban Landmines. Jerry lost his leg to a landmine
while hiking through an unmarked minefield in the Golan Heights, Israel,
when he was twenty. He says he never thought much about landmines until
he stepped on one in 1984. “Suddenly the earth exploded around me. I
looked down at my shredded bloody legs in confused horror, wanting to
know where my right foot had gone.” Since then he has dedicated his life to
building resilience in individuals and communities affected by violent
conflict.
Landmines made and planted in the thousands by humans continue to kill
innocent people every day, 80 percent of whom are civilians. It is estimated
that ten people still die every day from landmine explosions; in 1999 it was
twenty-five deaths a day.42
Jerry wanted to launch the Mine-Free Israel Campaign in 2004. He nowreflects that this was a personal crusade to make Israel mine-free, and
“crusader” energy, when it produces action, whether medieval or modern in
its manifestation, never ends well. Advocating for banning landmines, he
recounted his personal experiences, spoke regularly about how Israel was a
decade behind in banning landmines, and said he was ready to help.
Princess Diana was a champion for this cause. With his connections,
credentials as a Nobel Laureate, personal experiences, and knowledge to get
the job done, Jerry surmised that Israel would get to action. It did not work!
Jerry shares that until he participated in the leadership workshop at the
University of Notre Dame’s Mendoza College of Business, he did not have
the vocabulary or tools to know how to “tap into the power of wisdom.” I
designed and delivered this course with Leo Burke, a professor at the
Mendoza College of Business. We invited the executives to delve deeper:
Who are you and what do you stand for as a leader? What does large-scale
systems change and global interdependence imply? Finding grace in a
competitive world and understanding interdependence is vital. Most
executive education programs render leadership as a skill set with a
toolbox. But Leo and I brought in new ways of addressing complexity,
simply—and with a foundation of universal values, weaving these into new
ways to address practical issues faced by the executives.
Jerry says, “Wisdom, though essential, is too often missing in modern
leadership. Leading from the soul of our deepest awareness and core values
will tap into transformative power to deliver lasting results. Reaching
deeper to know my own leadership DNA—my wisdom—was extremely
helpful during the leadership program.” He returned to Israel in 2009 to
relaunch the Mine-Free Israel Campaign; this time, with a growing capacity
to stand firmly in his wisdom, he designed the campaign differently.
“The difference was the presence of wisdom, something indivisible that
can never be owned by one individual or any one group, let alone
trademarked or branded,” recounts Jerry. “The essence of wisdom is
transcendent, dynamic, spiritual, without being the exclusive franchise of
any one person, religion, or tradition. Wisdom cuts across borders,
boundaries, and social, religious, and national identities. That is why it is a
great unifier and liberator. Wisdom, born of a deep reverence for life, is
anchored in universal values, unleashes our inherent resilience, potential,
and power, and helps replace fragmentation with unity, shortsightedness
with vision, and fear with courage.”
This newfound realization became especially critical for his political
outreach in the Israeli Knesset (the national legislature of Israel). As he
engaged, attuned to people around him, he accessed others’ universal values
in boardrooms and in numerous conversations in cafes and living rooms.
Jerry inquired: What qualities of leadership are needed to create the space
for transformation to manifest? He believed leaders of transformation must
align three core elements in order to manifest a system-level change that
unlocks previously intractable problems. “Successful leaders must learn to
(1) source the power of their inner wisdom, anchoring themselves in their
values, not just personal interests; (2) seek the understanding of the
underlying complexities and behavior patterns that contribute to harmful
spirals, and discern and work toward healthier alternatives; and (3) use
knowledge and know-how to deliver measurable results, offering everyone a
way to contribute, to pick up a shovel and dig. Wisdom, Understanding,
Knowledge.” This “Liberation Leadership: W.U.K.” model is Jerry’s
expression of the conscious full-spectrum approach.
Jerry and his team were clear about the results they wanted and the shifts
necessary to make them happen: the possibility of a mine-free Israel within
ten years; liberation of fertile land for farming; families living in safety, freeof fear; and healing of people and the environment.
Jerry and his team urged the prime minister, the minister of defense, the
minister of foreign affairs, and the head of the opposition to fulfill their
promises and called on them to vote in favor of the proposed mine
clearance bill. That day, the bill passed its first reading by a unanimous vote
across all party lines (sixty to zero). This was unheard of. The proposed
legislation was brought back to the full Knesset for a final historic vote on
March 14, 2011. Once again, the vote was unanimous. Israel had
unanimously agreed to clear its nonoperational minefields for the first time.
Wisdom, Understanding, and Knowledge (W.U.K.) formed an undeniable
platform for liberation.
How did the Mine-Free Israel Campaign make this historic shift? Why
did this campaign succeed where others (including Jerry’s earlier effort) had
failed? A major factor was the ability of wise leadership to transform
contentious issues into unifying opportunities, activating other leaders to
transcend their personal identity and social profiles in order to serve
humanity for the well-being of future generations.
Jerry says, “Working this way, I breathe more and wait for diverse
leadership to emerge more organically. I invite people to engage their gifts.
I don’t have to burn out on activist anger, but can tap into sources of hope
and wisdom and strength to sustain the work beyond me.”
Not everyone operates with a national legislature and prime ministers as
Jerry did. Each action and conversation in any situation has the possibility
of generating transformational results—it is about leadership creating new
futures wherever we are—at home, at work, in society. Andrej’s mother, a
housewife in Serbia, stood for equity and dignity for everyone. When
Andrej, whom you met in Chapter Four, came back from school in eighth
grade and shared how some boys in his class ridiculed and teased a new
child from Latvia, his mother responded by saying, “How would you feel if
you went to the United States, and everyone ridiculed you?” Andrej thought
about it that night and recalls that his mother’s compassionate statement
changed his perspective for life. We must not underestimate the powerful
and life-changing influence our conversations may have. Andrej challenged
his classmates the next day. Years later, when he challenged the injustices in
Kosovo against Albanians, Andrej’s mother stood by him in the face of
social disapproval and threats.
You also met Raju, in Chapter Six—the young student who went to the
district magistrate in Uttarakhand in India to take steps to reverse caste-
based stigma and discrimination in his school.
Fallacy: Leaders who have the knowledge to get the job done—
necessary resources, the right connections—are the ones who
are most likely to succeed and achieve goals.
New Learning: In addition to knowledge and resources, leading
from our inner capacities, our wisdom, taps into the power to
deliver sustainable results. It requires that we operate from
universal values such as dignity, equity, compassion. Our inner
capacity, our wisdom, is fundamentally about who we are at our
core, beyond title, social identity, personal passion, skill set, or
curriculum vitae.
Transformational Stewards
In conventional settings, a steward may be responsible along with others for
natural assets like land, the “commons.” The motivation is usually the
shared interest of the community. A steward may also work according to
mandates and agreements and may also be entrusted to oversee resources in
the best interest of certain people or groups.Transformational stewards manifest a paradigm shift through holding
themselves responsible simply because they care deeply, at home, in
community, and in the workplace. As consumers, clients, providers,
workers, staff, and family members, they are not observers without concern
—they choose to take on responsibilities to generate enduring results
because they care deeply; their strategic actions establish dignity, equity,
and compassion as norms.
Transforming Paradigms
Many unique individuals live in oneness in every part of the world, their
stewardship transforming divisive patterns. An example from Syria always
stands out for me; and particularly now, with the immense pain, needless
killing, mass exodus, hatred, and fierce rivalry for control in the country.
When Khadija and I worked with the UN in the Arab States to address
HIV/AIDS, we designed our work with the conscious full-spectrum
approach and used transformational praxes with religious leaders grounded
in universal values of dignity, fairness, and compassion.
Khadija, whom you met in Chapter Five, recalls an unforgettable moment
when both the imam and the Catholic priest chanted to Mary in a church.
She said, “It is customary to have Christian hymns in church; what was
extraordinary was that it was followed by chanting of verses from the
Quran. Subsequently, in a mosque, prayers were said based on the Quran as
expected, followed by prayers from the Bible. What admirable courage in
the midst of divisive religious politics! I can only imagine how these events
must have deeply inspired those who were present, touching their minds
and hearts, activating a space that those present can never forget. If only we
were able to have all Muslims and Christians from all denominations sing
together, we wouldn’t see the massacre we are seeing every day in Gaza,
Syria, Iraq, Libya, Yemen, Sudan, Somalia.…”
The Sunni imam and the Catholic priest are transformational leaders and
stewards. They are the torchbearers of radical paradigm shifts. True, there is
tragic and catastrophic turmoil in the world today. We move a few steps
forward and then may have to take a few steps back, only to find
opportunities to move ahead again. When we touch the space of oneness
and resonance, life is never the same again.
Kirsten Gallo, whom you met in Chapter Five, is a transformational
manager, leader, and steward. She cares deeply about people, all sentient
beings, and the planet. She focuses on natural resources and people,
including the staff of the National Park Service and visitors to the parks.
Work is not just a job for Kirsten. It is a place for her to manifest who she is
at her deepest core. Kirsten says, “The National Park Service (NPS) is
known around the world for the spectacular landscapes and treasured
cultural resources we protect and for top-notch environmental education
programs. I endeavor to build on that foundation. NPS staff convey a
powerful message to visitors by showing them by example what is possible
when we take action sourced from our commitment to what matters most to
us, thereby demonstrating the power that one person, one family, one
community has to make a difference.”
Kirsten and her team formulated the impacts they would generate based
on the universal values of respect and integrity in the context of their
present vision and the mission of the NPS:
Thriving humanity—healthy, prosperous, and sourcing full potential
Healthy earthUsing transformative processes, they inquired into what paradigms,
norms, and systems must shift for sustainable and equitable results to be
generated and sustained. A paradigm shift occurs when transformational
shifts that are necessary to generate equitable and sustainable results
become the norm.
The first paradigm shift: from a culture sourced from fear and scarcity to
a culture sourced from contentment and abundance.
Their teams’ commitments are to have a thriving workforce whose
actions demonstrate interdependence; to establish the rights of nature, with
community, equity, and dignity present; and to provide opportunities for
NPS visitors to demonstrate interdependence and be part of an active and
engaged citizenry that is a social and political force for a thriving people
and planet.
The second paradigm shift: from a culture of separation and otherness to
a culture of unity, inclusion, and interdependence. They are generating a
new paradigm for wholesome recreation in nature. We know that nature
inspires and heals us.
They plan to contribute to humanity and well-being by transforming the
culture of what is recreation in today’s world. They will foster primary
recreation activities enjoyed in the United States that are life-affirming,
such as outdoor activities, volunteerism, service to others, dance, yoga, and
art.
The third paradigm shift: from a culture of domination and
commodification of people and the planet to a culture of deep regard for the
intrinsic value of all people and the planet. Kirsten and her team members
see a time when stewardship becomes normative practice for society; when
species and habitat conservation is just the way we live; when there is a
dramatic decrease in consumerism and materialism; when all destructive
polluting, mining, and harvesting practices have ended; when materials for
manufacturing are derived from landfills; when all energy is generated from
renewable, nonpolluting sources.
Right now, they are focusing on the following results: increased
populations of rare and sensitive species; improved habitat conditions;
increased biodiversity.
Although Kirsten and her team articulated foundational shifts for their
work at the National Park Service, these shifts apply everywhere—in our
homes, communities, workplaces, and institutions. Can you see how these
shifts apply to the ideas and initiatives you and I work with?
Kirsten says, “The NPS is learning how to truly steward lands in a
manner that honors the rights of nature and humanity and supports others in
doing the same.” The NPS cannot do it alone—citizen stewardship needs to
emerge strongly. As science is demystified, citizens become skilled in the
use and interpretation of data for learning and decision-making. Projects are
designed on the Generative Action for Impact and Awareness (GAIA)
framework, a conscious full-spectrum approach.
During the learning-in-action programs, Kirsten and NPS staff identified
the individual attitudes that need to shift in order to generate results—and,
here again, these attitude shifts apply to everyone who wants to make a
change in the world.
They noted that we need to:
Shift from an attitude of “I’m not part of the problem” and a belief that
we are victims of external circumstances to an expression of full
potential, personal responsibility and action, and BEING part of the
solution.
Shift from a culture of competition to synergistic partnership and
resonance; shift from “I” to “we”; and, in the NPS context, shift focusfrom inside park boundaries to BEING a global leader in conservation
and transformation through alternative strategies and action.
Shift from making decisions based on politics, the past, and/or fear to
making decisions based on sound information and attainment of desired
outcomes and impacts.
Shift from resignation in the workplace and in community to self-
organization and self-correction based on integrity, accountability, and
responsibility.
Fallacy: Climate change and environmental issues can be
addressed adequately through strategies such as carbon
trading, adaptation, and mitigation. We need to be practical
and make incremental changes. The same applies to complex
issues like peace—we need to be practical. Strategies based
on universal values are primarily meant for addressing social
issues.
New Learning: The mindset that commodifies other human
beings and treats them as utility objects also commodifies our
earth and her resources. Our oneness includes human
beings, all sentient beings (animals and plants), and the earth.
Incremental changes such as adaptation and carbon trading
do not address the current magnitude of the crisis we face;
they create a false sense of being in action that leads to
complacency. At best, they are strategies for surviving, not
thriving.
The universal values, and the mindset or worldview
emanating from these universal values, underpin peace and
equitable change for humanity and also underpin sustainable
change for our planet.
Kirsten reflects: “What comes alive for me as a scientist? Most of us
want to make a mark in the world in some way, to know we made a
difference. Scientists want the results of our work to contribute to
something … whether it’s a cure to a disease, alleviating hunger, or
conservation of a species. Even those of us who just want to know how the
world works want society to use that knowledge in a meaningful way. What
comes alive for me using conscious full-spectrum response is 1) I embody
my stand and ensure that my work is aligned with my deepest values and
commitments. This has created a deeper level of conviction and aliveness
within me. 2) I’m much more strategic so I have more confidence that my
work will be meaningful and useful for my audience. 3) I can enroll and
empower others to contribute to my vision, so there are many of us now
working toward the same outcomes. 4) Instead of designing individual
experiments or studies, I’m designing larger projects that have much
broader implications, moving from ‘single parks’ to ‘landscapes’ to the
globe.”
Fallacy: We need to inform people about what is happening to
our environment and the science behind it. Then it is up to them
to find out more information and take action.
New Learning: The manner in which scientists demystify
knowledge, inspire wonder and discovery, connect human beings
to their own interdependence with all life, and generate reverence
and respect for all forms of nature determines action for a thriving
people and planet.Our daughter Aeshna, an accomplished practicing endodontist, finds
nature deeply nurturing, and consistently shares her passion for all forms of
life with her children. Looking with wonder at every creature in the tide
pools, connecting the dots in the web of interdependent life in parks and at
natural history museums, Aeshna has ignited in our grandchildren the thrill
of discovery and an interest in science. Science is for citizens, not only for
scientists.
As a child, Aeshna rescued wounded birds and cats and brought them
home to heal. The streets in India were full of stray dogs, and Aeshna fed
and played with them every day. Half a dozen dogs waited every day to
receive her at the school bus stop and escort her home. Whenever she
walked out of the house, street dogs happily followed her. Childhood
attributes live on through life, giving expression to what we deeply care
about. Aeshna cared for me through my recent life-threatening accident; in
the past, she has made time to travel and celebrate my work-related
accomplishments.
Caring for the planet is inextricably linked to peace on the planet.
Millions of people are suffering and displaced because of the effects of
climate change. Unfortunately, those who have the least suffer the most.
The shift from separation to ubuntu (“oneness” in Zulu) is imperative.
Stewardship is about caring for both people and the planet. Kirsten Gallo
and Paola Babos are transformational managers, leaders, and stewards. You
met Paola earlier, in Chapter Four. Paola leads the work on gender in
UNICEF along with other United Nations agencies in West and Central
Africa.
Manifesting human rights related to gender issues regionally in West and
Central Africa through the United Nations is a challenge. In the twenty-four
countries of West and Central Africa, women provide most of the
agricultural labor; nonetheless, they have limited access to productive
assets. Specific legislation and policies have been ratified for women’s
rights related to inheriting property, marital status, stopping violence against
women. Culturally defined interpretations of the place of girls and women
combined with a consistently poor record of implementation of human
rights instruments for women and girls are major obstacles to progress.43
Paola created country and regional profiles on gender equity, using data
to make the “invisible” patterns visible by uncovering structural gender
determinants of outcomes (health, education, safety) for children. Staff and
partners have started viewing the gender problem through deeper systems
lenses, going beyond simple sex disaggregation of data on gender
differentials. Paola is changing the way gender work is being done: shifting
from the conventional narratives that describe what is being done through
different UN or other organizations to gender programing with tangible
results for girls at scale. She is supporting country programs to go beyond
small-scale, unsustainable projects by identifying entry points for systems
and cultural change. She is designing programs with potential for
generating transformational results in the areas of girls’ sexual and
reproductive health, girls’ secondary education, and gender-based violence
and child marriage. Her engagement using transformational approaches for
gender is recent—this is work in progress.
Paola and her partners used the transformational results chain to
formulate a program based on the universal values of dignity and gender
equity. They are committed to a clear impact: that girls’ dignity and
autonomy is recognized by institutions, parents, and the adolescents
themselves, resulting in well-being. To accomplish this impact, they defined
three specific goals or outcomes: they specified the activities and services
they provide.
First: Paola and her partners are working for expanded girls’ learningopportunities (formal and informal) that provide the skills and competencies
needed to transform economic, social, and political patterns of gender
inequity. They work on gender-equitable education policies and budgets;
they ensure schools are a safe and protective environment for girls; they
respond to specific needs of adolescents, including life skills,
comprehensive sexuality education, and entrepreneurship skills. All of us
can relate to these activities—we want the same for our children.
Second: Paola and her partners are working to support vulnerable girls
(adolescent mothers, victims of violence, HIV-positive girls, girls living in
poverty) to have access to and utilize quality, age- and gender-responsive
social services and livelihood opportunities. Local government officials,
social sector service providers—health workers, teachers, social workers—
as well as communities and parents are trained to skillfully identify
vulnerable girls and refer them to appropriate protection and support
services.
Third: They are fostering social innovation and citizen action that
transforms the social, cultural, and economic manifestations of gender
inequity across different sectors of society—specific topics are identified
and acted upon in each country. Child marriage rates are to be reduced in
the region. Influential organizations are being invited to take action and
champion initiatives on gender equity, challenging and transforming
harmful norms and constructs around masculinity, and challenging the
custom of child marriage. Worldwide, we need men and women to stand up
for gender rights.
Stewardship is about people and planet; it is also about other sentient
beings. We have not explicitly spoken about how animals and other
creatures have the capacity to feel, perceive, or experience subjectively
through their senses.
Many millions of animals are killed unnecessarily each year in
vivisection laboratories. Human beings continue to fancy their fur coats
even when we have beautiful, elegant alternatives. Animals are killed when
they are held captive in the animal slavery enterprises of circuses, rodeos,
zoos, and marine parks; and animals are killed while pressed into blood
sports such as bullfighting, cockfighting, dogfighting, and bearbaiting.
Horses and greyhounds are exterminated after they are no longer deemed
suitable for racing. How can citizens be unconcerned about this cruelty and
even be entertained?
Fallacy: Gender, class, caste, and racial inequalities have been
present for several thousands of years. It is bound to take a long
time to address these issues!
New Learning: Transformational tools, techniques, and praxes
based on universal values generate results in a short period of
time even when problems have been there for a long time,
because of people who yearn to make an equitable and
sustainable difference for humanity and our planet. These people
choose to learn, practice, and act to transform today’s
unworkable realities.
Intentional cruelty to animals is strongly correlated with other crimes,
including violence against people.44
Thoughtful citizens and policy-makers around the world are asking
pertinent questions about how we treat animals—the commodification and
exploitation and the cruelty human beings subject them to.Manifesting Wholeness: Integrity
Earlier in this chapter we discussed the joy of being in action and noted that
transformative design templates and mindful response praxes are in sync.
We use the conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR) template for
implementation—the integrity lens is one example.
Integrity means “BEING whole.” It is based on our evolutionary impulse
to be whole, undiminished. The terms morality, ethics, and integrity are
often used synonymously, but in our work we distinguish them. Peter
Singer, a bioethical philosopher, uses ethics and morality interchangeably.45
In common parlance, morality relates to individual, value-based conduct in
society. But it can sometimes become a space of judging and ostracizing
people, promoted by exclusionary religiosity. Ethics is understood as a set
of value-based principles operating in society. In our work, ethical
leadership specifically refers to the ability of a leader to act from the
universal values of dignity, fairness, and compassion for all, and transcend
cultural expressions and differences.
The integrity lens is a process of inquiry into what it means to be whole,
rather than a process to assess, monitor, or evaluate whether we are on
track. Such processes have their place, and we will explore them in Chapter
Ten.
I have found it essential for me and the teams I work with to have
nonjudgmental spaces to inquire, reflect, notice, be mindful as we are in
action, neither elated nor defensive. I have formulated six questions for
inquiry coherent with the architecture of the conscious full-spectrum
response and have used them at varying levels in different cultures and
settings. It is an inquiry to notice gaps, without blaming or feeling
inadequate or guilty because of not completing activities. This reduces the
defensiveness we often face at home and at work. And since we are pulled
to be whole, it works! It always generates new perspectives, strategies, and
actions, in both personal and professional lives.
The six inquiries illustrated in Figure 7-1 are an indivisible value-based
set, and are articulated below.
Figure 7-1 . The Integrity Lens and Six Inquiries.
Two inquiries relate to the inner circle of CFSR. These inquiries initiate
action toward equitable and enduring results.
1. I do what I say. I am my word, and I take my word seriously, in the
numerous minor and major decisions I make day-to-day. Not “you do
what I say”! Family, friends, and colleagues know that they can count
on me to honor my agreements with them. When I engage with others,
each person notices when there are gaps between what I do and what I
say. This allows me to move forward with clarity. There are articles on
how crucial it is for leaders and managers to “honor their word.”46
However, most of these articles position “I am my word” as a stand-
alone point. As crucial as “I am my word” is, without universal values
underpinning my word and without inquiring into the equitable
strategic implications of “I am my word,” I may fall into a totally
misguided trap. Take an extreme example, just to make this point—was
Hitler not his word? He delivered on what he planned, inflicting
horrific, excruciating torture and death upon Jews and other people he
considered inferior.
2. I deliver on my action plans. When I deliver on what I plan, I renew
myself in spaces of completion with quiet joy, feeling accomplished. It
is not about resolution to finish tasks—it is about inquiry so that I
notice the gaps in my progress and choose to narrow them. Are there
gaps in some areas of my engagements? What am I doing or not doingthat derails my intentions and actions? Has the context of my family
life, community, or work changed? Did I make assumptions that need
to be changed? Members of our team found it increasingly easy to
notice and simply state “I was out of integrity,” without feeling guilty,
judging or blaming themselves and others, or being defensive. They
simply noticed mindfully.
Two inquiries relate to the middle circle of CFSR regarding what we
need to shift in norms and systems to make our actions and results equitable
and enduring.
3. I establish congruence between strategies and actions and universal
values and principles. It is practical and essential to reflect on this as
we design and implement our programs or ideas. We can shift norms
and systems to generate equitable and enduring futures if we choose to
—after all, we humans created these structures and patterns. For this
future to emerge, strategies and actions must emanate from the
universal values of dignity, fairness, compassion for all. This is perhaps
the weakest component of human endeavors in our world today, in
which vested interests and personal agendas are at the core,
maintaining the status quo and unworkable systems. Why strain every
day on life’s treadmill, running tirelessly in the rat race, when it is
possible to change the rules of the games we play by?
4. I speak up and speak out for what I stand for. By speaking up and
speaking out based on what we stand for, we can shift the norms and
systems that give rise to problems and create new contexts for cultures
to thrive. It is possible for the vast majority of human beings to
reorganize their lives so that all of humanity can thrive through our
conversations at home, at work, and in society.
Two inquiries relate to the outer circle of CFSR and are related to how
we can generate actions for equitable and enduring results and radically
shift the paradigm.
5. I am the change I wish to see—I am the shift. This is a personal
inquiry and reflection. I notice when I am BEING the change I wish to
see. I am on a lifelong journey of enhancing my inner capacities. When
I share the insights I generate from my personal practice it creates a
space for authentic and inspiring communication. Knowing the
inherent propensity in humans to evolve to wholeness and integrity, my
inquiry is to discern and see things as they are with an open heart. I
must still the chatter in my mind.
6. I have the courage to create. I inquire and notice when my
compassionate heart does not allow me to simply sit apathetically on
the fence and watch the pain of other people and our planet without
responding. I notice and share with others, even when there are
objections or in the face of no agreement.
Here are some examples of BEING and manifesting integrity, where
there is a synthesis of our reflections based all the six inquiries.
Mike, the chief of human resources in a multinational company, was a
participant in the leadership program at the University of Notre Dame’s
Mendoza College of Business. He was expected to downsize the company
—that was the project he worked on during the learning-in-action program.
He inquired and reflected: “I stand for compassion and dignity; how will I
accomplish this task?” Letting several thousand people go was notcongruent with his values. He noticed this gap, and standing in the ground
of his BEING with compassion and courage, he opened new avenues for
action and created an alternative. He worked relentlessly with different
managers in the company to find out what they needed; he retooled the
jobs. He decided to shift the norms and knew how to navigate the system.
He constantly spoke up and inspired others to think differently. He created a
win-win-win situation—instead of losing their jobs, employees moved on to
other responsibilities; the company’s needs were met; and Mike was in
integrity and at peace with himself.
Fallacy: Integrity is for idealists. We must be practical and do
what needs to be done; we have no choice because the systems
are so big and society’s pressures are so formidable. Therefore,
we have to compromise on universal values if we want to
produce results.
New Learning: Integrity is based on our inherent evolutionary
call to be whole. When we take a stand because of who we are,
new solutions and pathways open up aligned with our universal
values. As we practice, we cultivate this aspect of ourselves, and
we are able to unleash our full potential as we respond.
There is something very powerful in moving beyond the right-wrong
judgments and beyond the blame games we play. It is liberating to
transcend sending ourselves or others on guilt trips because something is
not working; transcend our tendencies to blindly defend cultural identities
that are divisive and promoting harmful norms; and question norms in
variance with universal values.
We can all use the integrity lens with the six inquiries in our work, at
home, and in society.
Did I do what I said?
Did I deliver on what I planned to do?
Are my actions congruent with my universal values?
Did I speak up and speak out based on what I stand for?
Am I the change I wish to see? In other words, do I walk my talk?
Did I have the courage to create?
We can try this—it is very liberating to release defensiveness, notice
gaps, and create openings for new realities to emerge.
Communicating with Awareness
When I am self-aware, my communication generates transformational
spaces and unfolds new possibilities and ways of BEING through
mindfulness and action. There are distinctive communication and personal
development methodologies, and most management and leadership
programs have a set of communication tools in their repertoire. Here are a
few of the communication praxes we use in our work. All these tools can be
used to manage our day-to-day engagement as we are leading change and
stewarding our resources.
Cultivating Personal Resilience for Self-Aware
Communication
Self-awareness is critical for effective and transformational communication.
Some of us choose to embark on a lifelong journey to realize our full
potential, strive to notice our triggers, lighten our psychological “hang-
ups,” and “clean up” some of the mess we are in.
Three particular concerns surface repeatedly when we move forward as
principled game changers: we face ridicule, insults, and obstruction. Other
people feel threatened and become defensive. The key questions are, Howdo we build resilience without harboring resentments or feeling diminished?
How do we continue to be open to learning from others without being “shut
up and shut down”?
When someone ridicules, sneers, insults, or intentionally marginalizes
me, I use a few praxes that work for me, and I have noticed they work for
other practitioners too:
Don’t take what is being said or written personally: it is not about you
or me; it is about the other person’s judgmental way. Don’t take ridicule,
insults, and criticism personally.
Be assertive, not submissive or aggressive, speaking from what you
stand for. In most cultures, when women are assertive, they are labeled
as aggressive; when men are assertive, and not aggressive, they are
labeled as weak, and called names like “sissy.”
Be prepared to be “put down” by others, but never “get down” or “feel
down.”
Expect to face resistance—most people are afraid of change even when
they hate the circumstances they are in.
Listen. Be willing to change and express your point of view based on
universal values.
We must stand firmly in the ground of our universal values even in the
face of no agreement. This is not easy, but it is vital. In spite of how tactical
we may be, whenever we stretch to create changes, there are always people
in the organization, bureaucracy, community, society, or family who want to
put us down. Resistance is, in fact, an indicator that we are pushing for
change, and making a difference.
We must cultivate the ability not to take things personally. During
meditation retreats, Vipassana meditation teacher S. N. Goenka shares a
story about the Buddha. The Buddha was teaching at a gathering of
thousands of people; the priests were upset because their hold on people
was becoming weak—people were rediscovering who they were and
sourcing their inner capacities for action. The priests sent a woman to the
gathering, who pretended to be pregnant with Buddha’s child, shouting and
hurling insults at him. The Buddha responded, “You know the truth; I do
not wish to accept this gift of insults from you.” This example has helped
me tremendously. You and I never have to accept such “gifts” either!
Fallacy: In every culture and setting, we need to have ways of
forcing “boundaries.” When people don’t listen to reason, the only
way to manage them is through fear, or control through
appropriate ridicule and insults. Only then will they comply, and
this is normal in every culture. And since I am not an important
person, I have to comply.
New Learning: Irrespective of my social status, my material
resources, or my place in an organizational hierarchy, I am equal
as a human BEING. When I am resilient and I do not diminish
myself, I unfold my full potential. Others cannot diminish me,
even if their intention is to do so.
Eleanor Roosevelt said, “No one can make you feel inferior without your
consent.”
Ridicule, insult, and obstruction are inevitable because of the way we
humans currently operate with a mindset of domination, scarcity, and
competition. When we don’t take these things personally, it frees up space
for new possibilities to emerge.
My first professional presentation was at an international conference onhealth financing in the late 1970s; it was held in New Delhi at my alma
mater, the All India Institute of Medical Sciences. I had done my research
and interviewed people in villages and urban slums, and my presentation
was an argument for health care without a fee for those who could not
afford to pay. Even before I could finish, a senior expert from one of the
Bretton Woods institutions in Washington, DC, came up, took the
microphone away from me, and said, “You are a really beautiful Indian
woman, and emotional too!” I felt dressed down and insulted! I have since
learned that insult has nothing to do with me—it is about the mindset of the
other person. Time and again, I am in similar situations, but my internal
state and external responses are very different.
Khadija Moalla, the Tunisian lawyer and a mindful pro-activist whom
you met in previous chapters, has also cultivated this capacity. Recently, in
a meeting on women’s rights in Dakar, when some executive heads of
government departments from West Africa insulted her in public and made
disparaging personal remarks because she spoke up against physical
violence inflicted on women in the region, Khadija was able to remain
unruffled and continue in the meeting.
Practice this—it works! When colleagues, friends, or family members
ridicule you, make fun of you in a diminishing way, insult you, or ignore
you, notice how you feel. Decide not to accept this “gift of words.” And,
maybe, express your decision not to accept their gift!
Distinctions and Praxes Enhancing Aware
Communication
Based on the CFSR template, here are some mindful conversations we have
as we implement programs, at our workplace and at home.
BEING “unmessable with,” distinguished from a defensive position of
“don’t mess with me.” In other words, BEING steady in wisdom, and
sourcing our inner capacities for action.
Courageous heart responses, distinguished from emotional reactions—
in order to transcend social, cultural, and institutional dichotomies.
Courage, distinguished from bravado. Courage is action born out of a
compassionate and caring heart, not BEING able to walk past pain with
indifference; bravado is related to daring acts, and is most often based
on social constructs and expectations.
Worldviews emanating from universal values, distinguished from points
of view based on ideology or one’s social identity.
Here are a few other distinctions that further transformative
communication for action and results.
Principled outrage is distinguished from destructive anger. Principled
outrage and destructive anger often sound the same. Principled outrage
might look like anger externally, but it is not anger.
Destructive anger is a reaction that damages and diminishes self and
other. Our ability to regulate our anger—not suppress it—is necessary for
mindful implementation. When we fly into a rage, are triggered, or need to
be right in order to make the other person wrong, it is our emotion that is in
charge. As a result, we are unable to source our full potential; there is no
space to generate transformation. To be able to transcend destructive anger,
we must first experience and notice our anger.
Megan, whom we met in Chapters Five and Six, shares her example of
principled outrage. “Recently, I learned that several politicians in a
predominantly Latino community had undermined work we were doing on
youth violence prevention there, using the fact that we were white as a
reason to create a barrier. I was then in a meeting where the barrier arose,yet the undercurrent of race was swept under the carpet. I certainly felt
emotion and reaction arise, but I took a breath, actually several, and waited
until I could formulate a response that came from my stand of dignity and
justice for all, that could recognize and name our shared commitment to
equity and peace, and that could name and make visible what was not okay.
I did not take their comments personally. I spoke up and spoke out, and it
completely shifted the meeting and the interactions I had with partners
afterward. Getting angry and reactive would not have done that—
destructive anger does not give us space to create change, to do things
differently. It makes others wrong, us right, and only honors our own
perspectives on what is happening. It comes from our fears and triggers,
and sends others straight to theirs.”
Most of us can recall incidents where we experienced or expressed
principled outrage in our lives; and also recall incidents of destructive
anger. These are two very different states within us.
Discernment is distinguished from judgment. Discernment is our ability
to think critically, informed by universal values. For universal values such
as compassion, dignity, and fairness to be the basis of strategic action, we
need to cultivate discernment. We tend to look at what is happening through
the lens of right or wrong, us or them, blame or shame. As a result, we tend
to polarize opinions and behave in a self-righteous manner—judging
ourselves and judging others and continuously badgering. I am not using
the word judgment in the legal sense or in the way it is defined in some
religious scriptures.
Distinguishing discernment from judgment is freeing and practical.
Discernment is seeing things as they are, not as we want them to be, with an
open heart and stilling our chattering mind. Discernment is noticing what is
present in a situation and then choosing to act based on universal values.
Judgment is placing a value of right or wrong, better than or less than, on a
situation and then choosing to act from that space. Recently, a popular guru
from India made a public statement judging farmers who committed suicide
as “people who are not spiritual.” Farmers have to take loans, and when the
crops fail because of climate change and drought or floods, there is no
support. Such judgments create disempowering societal conversations and
ignite indifference. With judgment there are limited choices for action—
with discernment there are many important nuances and more options for
action.
Honor based on dignity for all is distinguished from honor for social
compliance. Dignity and honor are used synonymously in common
parlance, but we distinguish them in our work in order to shift
disempowering norms. We relate the word dignity to one’s self-esteem, self-
worth, self-respect. Dignity is a universal value underpinning all our work
and applies to everyone everywhere.
Honor upholds dignity between people and groups and creates an
interactive space for the expression of wholesome relationships.
Unfortunately, in large parts of the world, the word honor has been hijacked
by divisive forces to create a culture of fear and control.
Table 7-1. Aware Conversations for Radical Transformation.
Conversations sourcing inner Uncreative, unproductive
capacities and universal values societal conversations
BEING unmessable with Don’t mess with me
Courageous heart responses Emotional reactions
Courage Bravado
Worldviews based on universal values Views based on ideology or
social identitiesDiscernment Judgment
Principled outrage Destructive anger
