compliance
Embodying values—living what I stand Values as rhetoric
for
Sourcing universal values to shift Offering superficial “fixes” for
unworkable systems and norms problems
We distinguish (1) dignity, an inner capacity and a universal value, (2)
honor based on the supportive universal values of dignity, fairness, and
compassion, and (3) dishonorable so-called honor, such as honor killing or
“honor of the gang.” These stark examples may seem self-evident, but
during the learning-in-action sessions, we see how difficult it is initially for
people to notice the subtle or the blatant mechanisms of social control in
their families and workplaces and in society. Practitioners start to recognize
the ways in which insults, shaming, and guilt are routinely used for control
and domination, and they practice how to build resilience. They notice
when the word honor is abused to reinforce otherness and separating
actions, domination, and even exploitation of others. They speak up and
speak out to generate new conversations.
Fallacy: We are one big family—our different castes, tribes,
religious sects, and races. These ways of organizing ourselves
have worked for thousands of years, continue to work now, and
will work into the future. The problem is that we are not abiding
by these cultural divisions that serve us, each in their rightful
place.
New Learning: Diversity is a space of celebration and inclusion.
As human beings, we have much more in common than we have
differences—we all experience love, compassion, sorrow, joy,
grief, and the need to belong, to dance and sing, to revere nature
and life. We are interdependent.
Deep Listening, Responsible Speaking
It is a choice to develop the ability to listen deeply. There are many books
on listening; different types of listening exercises are used to improve
communication, each having its own purpose and utility. Researchers
conducting surveys are asked to listen carefully and record verbatim;
negotiators are trained to listen for what is not said in the midst of conflict.
In our work, we cultivate the ability to listen proactively and deeply. I
learn to still my mind and open my heart. We mentally filter what people
say and do; hence, noticing my conversations, knowing the assumptions I
make, is the beginning of releasing my biases and even my unintentional
discrimination. Human beings listen to what others say in a particular way
subconsciously—almost in an automatic way—without being aware of it.
Instead of listening deeply, we tend to judge what is being said as right or
wrong, find fault, build the argument while someone else is speaking in
order to put forth our point of view.
Deep listening develops one’s own leadership capacities and fosters
leadership in others. Practitioners are amazed at their own ability to see
others unfold as they listen deeply, without even saying a word. To be able
to listen deeply, stilling the chatter in our mind, suspending judgment and
opinions for a while, appreciating the other person’s reality the way it is for
them, opens up new learning, new possibilities.
Fallacy: There is no point in listening carefully to everyone; we
need to be practical and listen to the opinions of those who haveknowledge and expertise or those who have influence and are in
power.
New Learning: Listening deeply creates new openings for
action. Everyone can make a difference. It is not about expertise
or opinions. Listening deeply helps unfold the full potential of
another human BEING.
Conversations at home, at work, and in society create the culture of
thriving. In other words, society, organizations, and groups are networks of
conversations that can create new futures. By the time I led UNICEF’s India
team on universal immunization, I had been exposed to several
management trainings within the organization and powerful advocacy
programs. This training, combined with my earlier formal learning and my
father’s perspective that everyone can contribute provided they have the
opportunity to do so, helped me establish spaces for meaningful
conversations to take place.47 Through these conversations with
stakeholders from every sector and every level, we built a stronger
foundation for action and generated measurable results—immunization
coverage increased from 30 to 80 percent in infants and pregnant women in
three years.
Heba, a young professional pharmacist from Egypt, stands for dignity,
justice, and caring. She was part of the team addressing HIV/AIDS in the
Arab States. What Heba learned and practiced to reverse the HIV/AIDS
epidemic she now applies in her work to make medicine accessible in
developing countries. She relentlessly advocates for this accessibility by
addressing intellectual property protection, public health and pricing
policies, pharmaceutical manufacturing, and generic competition—all
major international and national factors influencing access to affordable
medicines in developing countries.
Heba is a mindful pro-activist, challenging systems for sustainable
change, working with people in the system rather than against them; but she
is steadfast in calling out what is not working. Others have taken an
oppositional stance. She says, “I have to continuously draw attention to
issues which are overlooked. I am working to shift the focus to promoting
local generic production and investing in the national pharmaceutical
industry. I critique medicine pricing policies of the government and frame
them as barriers to access to affordable medicines in light of a limited
health care budget. I have more courage to discuss ‘systems’ with those
who, in theory, design them: the government. I am able to critique systemic
issues, based on research and evidence, and propose alternatives.
“It is the simple and basic practices that helped me realize results—
expressing my BEING in terms of the values I stand for and believe in, my
life’s purpose, choosing to live out these values on a day-to-day basis.
Often, I needed to inquire and restore congruence between what I stand for,
deeply believe in, and what I wish to see happening in my professional life.
This work helped me very much to realize that I should not hide myself and
live in a closet. I am the same person everywhere, and this has to be
expressed, because it is my inner power and greatness, and it is valuable.
The good inside me has to come out and translate into good deeds and
thoughts which could make this world a better place.”
Heba’s speaking is transformational. Figure 7-2 illustrates the
transformational speaking frame using the example of Heba’s work on
having essential medicines for all.
Figure 7-2 . Transformational Speaking: Having Medicine for All.The transformational speaking frame is a generative template for us to
speak up and speak out, and it is congruent with the CFSR approach.
Distinguishing four ways by which we can communicate and speak gives us
choices.
Marketing is promoting or selling products or services, even ideas.
Advocacy is publicly supporting or recommending a particular cause or
policy. These are usually based on the universal values of equity, dignity,
and fairness.
Lobbying is supporting a position, usually not based on universal values
—for example, a position that universal access to health care is not
necessary—or lobbying for harmful pesticides to be sold. In common
parlance, we use advocacy and lobbying interchangeably, but I find it
practical and useful to distinguish them.
Enrollment is when someone inspires others to recognize and create new
pathways and possibilities based on universal values and commits to action.
It is not a typical sales or recruitment conversation. It is not about
manipulating people to fulfill an agenda. Enrollment requires us to listen
deeply from our oneness with respect, speaking in an honest and
straightforward manner, generating spontaneous conversations and action.
Fallacy: We need to speak to what the audience will understand
and respond to. We must be practical and do what is needed.
There is no point talking about lofty values—that is just
philosophy. People want jobs so they can earn more money, buy
new cars, homes, and gadgets, take care of their family, and
move up the social ladder.
New Learning: People care deeply about their own happiness
and the happiness of their loved ones. People care about others
too. They care about the opportunity to work and contribute to
others’ quality of life and well-being. Speaking responsibly about
our universal values inspires people to engage in creating new
futures.
Speaking responsibly fosters leadership in oneself and others, just as
deep listening does. These are very important leadership skills. When we
explore and enhance our listening and speaking abilities and the way we
generate aware conversations, new possibilities emerge for a paradigm
shift.
BEING a Mindful Implementer
Leading large-scale programs globally and nationally gave me the
opportunity to serve and learn over thirty years. Even though I was never
the head of any United Nations agency or CEO of an organization, I learned
it is possible to generate significant results using transformational
approaches when I lead specific programs, divisions, or offices within a
large organization. As long as I was cognizant of a few principles and
delivered results on the ground, I had the freedom to use various approaches
and methods. Fortunately, I had committed leadership that supported my
work in 95 percent of my jobs; and when I did not, I was proactive, found
another position, and moved within six months. In my view, life is too short
and the working hours too long to stay in places that do not allow full
potential to manifest.
Fallacy: There is not much I can do to create the changes I think
are necessary in an organization. I am not the CEO, so I do not
have much say or influence. Besides, what’s the point of trying to
generate change within a unit when the organization is so big!New Learning: There are many opportunities for significant
change in most organizations within the programs and
departments we work in. As results ensue, they have a powerful
influence on other programs and units, and new policies and
projects are shaped. In fact, it is often easier to start change with
high-performing individuals and teams and within efficient
departments than in a large organization as a whole.
Leadership is not reserved for chairpersons and CEOs. Because
we focus on shifting routine activities into spaces for enduring
change, every person can lead and generate transformational
results within their sphere of influence and accountability, no
matter how large or small.
Practical Suggestions for Mindful Implementers—Doing
the Same Thing Differently!
This work is not about having a new to-do list because we think that the
previous list of activities did not generate the change we wish to see. It is
about doing the same things differently to make a difference—it is about
wise, ethical, and mindful implementation.
Transformational managers, leaders, and stewards must be clear about
the universal values that underpin their work, what results they wish to
generate, the shifts required in systems and culture, and the transformative
processes they plan to use. As expected, I have experienced managerial and
leadership challenges; I am not systematically describing how I faced them.
Here, I am sharing some practical tips that have enabled us to generate
sustainable results.
Connect to Universal Values of the Organization and Core Policies
Identifying and working with the core values, policies, and goals of the
organization is sensible and transformational. For example, the Declaration
of Commitment on HIV/AIDS at the United Nations in 2001 stated, “Strong
leadership at all levels of society is essential for an effective response to the
epidemic.… Leadership involves personal commitment and concrete
actions.”48 This was one of more than two hundred policy statements. I
focused on the leadership statement, which formed the basis of our strategy
to address HIV/AIDS using transformational approaches and praxes
through UNDP.
The UN Charter states, “We the peoples of the United Nations
determined … to reaffirm faith in fundamental human rights, in the dignity
and worth of the human person, in the equal rights of men and women and
of nations large and small.”49 Dignity, nondiscrimination, equity, and
freedom for everyone are the foundation of human rights. In every program
I directed, we articulated the policies derived from these universal values,
formulated strategies, implemented actions toward an equitable and new
thriving future. Aligning value-based projects with program goals, in the
context of policies, is pragmatic and essential.
Fulfill Purpose—Generate Results
Generating measurable results in priority areas of the organization, results
in programs where I was accountable, created space for other creative
initiatives. Working with our teams, I was clear about my purpose in life—
to manifest my full potential as I serve to make a difference, and create
opportunities for others to unfold as they generate results in whatever topic
or area they choose. Improving immunization coverage and other measures
to reduce childhood mortality, reducing maternal deaths, reversing the
HIV/AIDS epidemic were all organizational goals and programs that Idirected with UN teams and partners; measurable results were generated in
the different assignments. When we are clear about the results we wish to
generate, and are unambiguous about sourcing our inner capacities—not
simply “fixing it”—we are in tune with universal values, and we generate
measurable results.
Inculcate Learning through Action: It Is a Time-Saver
In every institution and program I worked in, learning and practice were an
integral component of our work. We were clear that unless we transcended
our own differences in new ways and created a culture of authentic
speaking and straight talk, we would be catalysts wanting everyone else to
change, without ever changing ourselves. Continued practice is essential,
and it is hard to create a culture of learning in most institutions.
It is essential to find “time-saving time” to synthesize learning, process
and share learning, and integrate it into practice. In every organization,
people say there is no time—they are too busy and have to get the job done.
During one of my assignments, I was told that the time we spent on
enhancing our inner capacities and leadership competencies was excessive;
we should focus on our technical skills and have some eminent leadership
experts come over and deliver lectures on leadership. I did a rough back-of-
the-envelope calculation based on several years’ experience and showed
them that for every hour we spent on self-awareness, strengthening our
inner capacities and shifting our behaviors, we actually saved about twenty
hours. We saved time spent on interpersonal disagreements and conflict at
work; saved time by not procrastinating and delaying decisions; saved
unnecessary discussion time on the same topics in meetings.
I also created time and space for ongoing coaching for our teams. Ravi
Pradhan, whom you met in Chapter Five, coached us at UNICEF; Allan
Henderson and Sharon Knoll at UNDP. Our coaches practiced themselves,
too, which inspired our learning.
Reflecting on his experience, Ravi said, “At first, this may not seem so
unusual. However, given how development agencies have worked in the
past, a whole system or multistakeholder approach to generating
breakthrough results was a major innovation. A key contribution I made,
invisible as it may seem, was the application of a learning methodology that
was consistent with the findings of neuroscience—‘embodied cognition.’ In
other words, real genuine learning and skill development involves the
whole body, mind, emotions, and action and behavior.”
Sharon generously shared all the methods and materials she had
developed in service of people and the program to reverse the HIV/AIDS
epidemic. Sharon said, “I do not view my work in terms of ‘me’ making a
contribution, significant or otherwise. I learned I am part of a global family,
and wherever I travel there are committed human beings on a team to have
things work. We left about three hundred people in the countries I was in
that are still leading and making a difference in the areas most important to
them. The common principle in both the world of business, where I have
worked extensively, and that of human development is we are all human
beings and when we build programs based on humanity and valuing life,
sustainability is available. When we interact with each other as things, units,
or pieces to be fixed, we have the world we are in now. In both cases,
people are the solution.”
Allan Henderson said he learned from participants: a woman who had
been trafficked from Nepal to Mumbai told him how she organized an
NGO, and when Allan was visibly moved, she said, “Please do not think I
have a tragedy—you have it wrong; I use the source of my ‘inner power,’
and I have learned from my adversity.” Another participant shared, “Irealize that I have the right to step up and take leadership—I have the
permission to step up and act.” During the interviews on what people
wanted to see changed, participants said it was the first time people in the
UN had asked about what they thought and were not manipulating them
with an agenda.
Learning is a lifelong process. In what ways do you proactively engage to
learn?
Care for Each Other
Caring for each other may seem too simple to be mentioned here. We say
that caring should be present in our family, community, workplace, schools,
or health facilities—but we know it is not always the case. Families are a
place of nurture; they are also a place of abuse. Services and the facilities
through which we serve are meant to be places to heal or grow and to
nurture well-being; and for that to happen, we must care for each other and
experience being cared for. Businesses are not just about profit—they are
also about caring for the well-being of people and sustaining a thriving
planet. Caring for each other creates an environment in which each of us
can step up and act beyond personal interests and agendas. Caring requires
us to be mindful.
As we engaged worldwide to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic through
UNDP, we designed and implemented a program for our staff—We Care.
The stigma and biases that exist in society also creep into workplaces. The
purpose of the We Care initiative was to create a work environment where
people living with HIV or AIDS felt free, valued, and accepted, while
respecting confidentiality and privacy. We created education and preventive
measures for all staff, caring for each other in everyday actions with space
to bring up concerns. Program reviews showed that We Care made a
difference to staff within UNDP offices; subsequently, UN Cares was
established in different countries for all UN offices.
Another example, this time from my family: Mudit is a pediatric
intensive care specialist who loves his work and is trusted because of his
clinical competence. He is married to Aeshna, our daughter. His caring
ways encompass his patients and his staff. Mudit showed us a drawing of
him made by a critically ill ten-year-old child in the hospital. The child
drew himself and Mudit holding hands and playing in the garden—it
reflected Mudit’s love, his listening and conversations, and the time and
attention he gives to every child. Mindful of the exhaustion of nurses at the
end of a particularly difficult night in the hospital because of critically ill
patients, Mudit acknowledges them for their work and makes the time to
buy them breakfast. And despite his extremely busy schedule, he volunteers
regularly to teach clinicians in pediatric intensive care units in developing
countries such as Armenia, China, Egypt, and India.
Partner with Others to Shift the Paradigm
There is a difference between working with like-minded people and finding
potential partners who are like-spirited or attuned. I use the word spirit to
mean our nonphysical self, the expression of our character, energy,
enthusiasm, and determination emanating from universal values. We seek
like-minded partners while addressing violence or reducing poverty or
creating green jobs. Our efforts are then synergistic—they are more than the
sum of each person’s contribution. When we work in unison with like-
minded and like-spirited people, the change we create is amplified greatly.
We are able to generate a new field, a paradigm shift, anchored firmly in
what we both stand for, our universal values, our humanity. This work is
sheer joy, even if we disagree on tactics or have differing opinions andpositions.
When I consider partnering, I think of individuals with whom I am
attuned. How do I find these people, or how do they find me? Here is the
good news, based on my work worldwide: they are everywhere, in every
country, in every sector. Sometimes our first impressions may not be
accurate, or our chemistry may not work; the social or work environment
may be repressive and may not allow them to come forth. But through the
years we have found each other. I use five simple, practical criteria:
Do they value the inner space, wisdom, for sourcing action? The
contemporary pioneer.
Do they burn for fairness? Do injustices bother them? The unifying
architect.
Do they have a track record of producing results? The mindful pro-
activist.
Do they foster other people’s leadership? The radical transformer.
Do they renew themselves and invest in their own growth? All of the
above.
I have found it very useful to distinguish three modalities of engagement
with partners once we have agreed to work together with shared values.
First, coordination—the usual processes of defining who does what. With
whom do I need to partner to coordinate my work? The finance department?
Which organizations run the same campaign; with whom do I need to
coordinate to avoid unnecessary duplication? When we coordinate with
openness, trust, and accountability, we create spaces for new cooperative
norms and systems to emerge.
Second, collaboration to achieve results—working together to achieve
outcomes, goals, outputs related to the mission or purpose requires us to
move beyond individual leadership to shared leadership; to give prime
importance to the mission and its goals (for example, ending poverty and
establishing prosperity, reversing the HIV/AIDS epidemic) rather than
promoting one’s organization.
Third, synergy and resonance for greater impact—working together
based on universal values means that spaces of competition dissolve into
spaces of joyful acknowledgment of each other’s success. We recognize that
when the other succeeds, we are closer to the mission, purpose, and goals;
this reflects success within all organizations involved; and the work through
my organization is closer to success too.
Distinguishing coordination, collaboration, and synergy clarifies why we
are engaging and what to expect from others. Too often, we build
expectations, and when they are thwarted, we resent the partner. For
example, in one of our UNICEF country offices, because of the vibrant
partnership we had with Rotarians in the immunization program, some staff
members expected Rotarians to also collaborate with us in the program to
address child labor. When the Rotarians were not enthusiastic, the staff
members were disappointed. Rotarians had given importance to child
survival, and we assumed that this meant we would partner together on
child protection; but our expectations were thwarted.
Synergistic and resonant partnerships are cornerstones of large-scale
transformation when their strategic actions span the entire range of the
conscious full-spectrum response, based on universal values.
The key abilities of a mindful pro-activist include:
Transforming routine and daily activities in order to manifest each
person’s full potential.Enhancing management, leadership, stewardship: holding multiple
perspectives without compromising values; making ethical decisions
even when they are difficult; taking action in order to generate results
that further dignity, equity; ensuring clarity, transparency, accountability
in our different roles and responsibilities.
Creating a culture of integrity by BEING our word; ensuring
congruence between universal values and strategic action; speaking up;
embodying universal values instead of only talking about them.
Communicating as an impetus for universal values to manifest;
cultivating resilience by being assertive; expecting resistance when
challenging the status quo; managing the inevitable insult and ridicule.
Speaking about inner capacities to manifest a paradigm shift; expressing
principled outrage but not destructive anger; discerning with an open
heart and mind; articulating what is present in order to make wise
decisions; not judging people; upholding dignity and at the same time
speaking up and acting against exploitative and exclusionary “honor
codes” and “honor killing.”
Enrolling others to see new possibilities, commit to action, generate
equitable and sustainable results; creating synergistic partnerships.
Holding governments, corporations, institutions, and communities
accountable for dignity and equity for all people and for establishing a
healthy environment and planet.
Using free time to learn, gain new knowledge, understand systemic and
cultural issues in order to support wise policies and decisions locally
and nationally.
Redefining the current paradigm of recreation in order for it to be life-
enhancing; seeking inspiration, adventure, joy in nature; shifting away
from numbing sources of recreation that sap our creativity, vitality, and
self-expression.
BEING a mindful implementer is joyful and requires discipline of
practice.
Chapter 8
Manifesting My Full Potential: A Choice
BEING the Contemporary Pioneer,
Unifying Architect, Mindful Pro-Activist,
and Radical Transformer—All at Once
An outer activity as well as an inner change is needed and it must be at
once a spiritual, cultural, educational, social and economic action.
SRI AUROBINDO
We are more than our social characteristics. We are profound conscious
human BEINGS. It is each person’s choice to include and transcend our
social identities—nationality, race, religion, gender, class—and choose to
anchor ourselves in our inner capacities as we take action to manifest our
greatness.
We Are All—and at Once
The three intertwined attributes—our universal heart of compassion,
empathetic burning for equity and fairness, our discerning eye seeing both
invisible and visible patterns with light and pristine clarity—exist within
each one of us. These three life-enhancing attributes are innate in human
beings, though they may be clouded by our socialization process and by
culture. They are not determined by the level of formal education, expertise,
social status, or poverty or wealth—they are simply present. They are not
based on aptitude, our personality, or our preference.
The three universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion thatunderpin our work are based on inherent aspects of human beings
everywhere.
Figure 8-1 . We Are All at Once.
BEING the Contemporary Pioneer, Unifying
Architect, Mindful Pro-Activist, and Radical
Transformer
As I engage worldwide, people everywhere ask me similar questions for
clarity. “In your experience, do you find that some people have a greater
aptitude to be one of the three ‘types’ of people, instead of all three at once
—the contemporary pioneer, unifying architect, and mindful pro-activist? In
this era of specialization, should we focus on cultivating one of the three
aspects?”
It is our choice to embark on the adventure of discovering and
manifesting our full potential. To manifest our full potential, we need to
explore and cultivate all three of these intertwined dimensions of ourselves.
In this era of technology, of the “knowledge economy” and specialization, it
is even more important to synthesize in order to create value-based
alternatives for enduring and equitable results.
At first, it may seem complex to BE all three simultaneously—the
contemporary pioneer, unifying architect, and mindful pro-activist. Yet, as
we choose to engage and as we practice, we see how powerful, fulfilling,
and simple it actually is!
All of us have the potential to make a significant difference in the world.
It is a choice. Once we make the choice to embark on life’s discovery and
adventure to realize our full potential, the rest follows through inquiry,
insight, and practice.
In Part Three, we explore what it means to go to scale. We manifest the
contemporary pioneer, unifying architect, and mindful pro-activist within us
to be the radical systems and cultural transformer.
PART 3
GENERATING RESULTS AT SCALE
Chapter 9
The Radical Systems and Cultural
Transformer
Everyone’s Contribution
I don’t know Who—or what—put the question. I don’t know when it was
put. I don’t even remember answering. But at some moment I did answer
Yes to Someone—or Something—and from that hour I was certain that
existence is meaningful and that, therefore, my life, in self-surrender, had
a goal.
From that moment I have known what it means “not to look back” …
DAG HAMMARSKJÖLD, MARKINGS
In today’s globalized world, people who care and have the courage to
engage in tackling the major problems we face, such as climate change or
dire poverty in a world of abundance, look for solutions that are equitable,
enduring, and at scale.
The conscious full-spectrum response, when applied, contributes to
generating a paradigm shift—a shift in which humanity and our planet
thrive based on the universal values of dignity, fairness, compassion. In this
chapter, we explore how everyone can contribute to the paradigm shift, and
everyone can choose to generate results and bring about the necessary
transformation at scale through their capabilities and actions. It is no longer
about how big or small anyone’s idea or initiative is; or whether we work
outside or nurture a family at home; or whether or not we have the financial
resources to design and implement a project. It is about one’s commitment,not just as intention, but commitment-in-action; and a yearning to presence
and nurture a thriving humanity and planet!
I have been blessed with opportunities to serve at scale in my work life,
particularly in my work with the United Nations. The design templates I am
sharing are the ones I used and refined to generate results around the world.
With every new assignment, I added what was missing and integrated new
elements from my insights and continued learning—always in the context
of achieving goals and accomplishing outcomes. For example, I was part of
a program that organized immunization services for twenty-five million
infants and twenty-five million pregnant women every year in over six
hundred thousand villages and numerous urban locations in India. In the
year 2000, I was working to reduce maternal deaths in South Asia, a place
that at the time was home to 22 percent of the world’s women and
accounted for 50 percent of maternal deaths worldwide.50 I have worked
globally in 170 countries to increase child survival and to address stigma
and discrimination against people living with HIV/AIDS and increase their
access to treatment. These were all highly demanding challenges that
resulted in rewarding accomplishments.
CFSR: The Fractal of Large-Scale
Transformation
The pattern of the conscious full-spectrum response is a fractal, and when
repeated in its numerous applications irrespective of the theme or topic, it
generates paradigm shifts at scale.
A fractal may be defined as “a pattern that repeats itself.” The Oxford
English Dictionary defines fractal as “a curve or geometric figure, each part
of which has the same statistical character as the whole.”
CFSR—the Pattern for Scale
A conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR) to any specific situation is a
fractal of the whole paradigm shift; in other words, each idea, initiative, or
endeavor designed as a CFSR response has the same characteristics as the
whole. What are these characteristics of the whole? There are three essential
components of CFSR: (1) sourcing inner capacities and universal values for
action—acting from our oneness; (2) shifting systems and cultural norms,
creating new patterns, BEING a principled game changer; and (3) solving
problems.
And they apply no matter what we do and where we work—at home, in
our community, or in an organization. Imagine what would be possible and
present if every political party, business, educational institution, health
facility, and environmental or legal institution designed their organization,
their work, their initiatives in a conscious full-spectrum way. Sourcing
universal values for strategic action, using technological advances to serve
humanity as a whole, creating new patterns that presence equity and
sustainability would become the norm—the way things are always done. It
would usher in the next stage of our civilization.
Figure 9-1 . The CFSR Fractal: A New Field at Scale.
Figure 9-2 . The Fern Leaf: A Fractal.
People may consider the conscious full-spectrum response to be a new
meme for generating a new future. A meme is an element of a culture or
system related to behaviors that may be passed from one individual to
another by nongenetic means, especially imitation. But I consider the CFSR
to be a much deeper process because its design elements and mindful
implementation generate transformational results based on our innate
human capacities.Fractals occur everywhere in nature. For example, in snowflakes, in
succulent plants like the Aloe polyphylla, and in ferns, the same pattern
recurs at progressively smaller scales. In this fern (Figure 9-2), every
progressively smaller part has the same pattern as the whole.
Patterns for Working at Scale
Scale requires organization as well as partnering and connecting with many
different people. Often, concerned citizens, policy-makers, business people,
and authentic media professionals feel that the problems are too big, too
entrenched, and that “this is the way it is and nothing much can be done.”
However, there are groundbreaking ways to act at scale and make paradigm
shifts. We must understand the conventional ways of scaling up and
differentiate those from acting at scale for generating a new future. I will
describe three conventional ways of scaling up for specific results
(illustrated in Figure 9-3), and there are other combinations and
permutations of these examples.
One way to scale up is to replicate a uniform and constant entity with the
same standards. For example, at Starbucks or McDonald’s, customers
expect to find the same products—the same coffee or the same hamburger
—at every location.
Another way to scale up is to define the entities involved according to the
level of technology commensurate with what is needed. For example, the
number of high schools in a city is likely to be larger than the number of
colleges, since not all children graduating from high school go to college;
and, for the same reason, the number of colleges is likely to be higher than
universities with master’s programs. Similarly, we require a larger number
of health clinics to take care of our frequent and everyday health needs and
fewer referral hospitals with specialists to manage more complex health
problems; and we need even fewer tertiary hospitals where super-specialties
are available for treating far more complex health problems and illnesses
(Figure 9-3).
Figure 9-3 . Conventional Ways of Going to Scale.
Networks and meshworks are yet another way of taking an idea and
related actions to scale, as shown in Figure 9-3. People with similar
interests connect to move their projects or ideas forward, sharing
knowledge and best practices. The internet and social media enable users to
create and share content or to participate in social networking, far beyond
our geographical locations, thereby opening vast new connections.
The conscious full-spectrum response is different from all the patterns of
going to scale described above. The design and implementation of
conscious full-spectrum responses with commensurate results can be
accomplished by anyone. Everyone can fully participate in contributing to
action at scale. The distinct features of the CFSR are described in Chapter
Six. Every entity is based on the universal values of dignity, equity,
compassion for all; all actions or solutions are explicitly and simultaneously
integrated with strategies to shift unworkable cultural norms and systems.
The CFSR template invites us to formulate broad directions sourced from
universal values and is based on inquiry, insights, and application. It is not a
cookie-cutter approach. A new field is generated, making universal values
the foundation of systems and normative shifts; it presences a thriving
humanity and planet. Radical, ethical leadership is realism, not idealism. It
must and will become the norm in society.
Respond and Realize to Shift Paradigms
To generate equitable and sustainable results, we must realize our inherent
oneness and manifest our full potential in order to create alternatives that
generate prosperity for a thriving humanity and planet. At the same time,we must authentically call out what is not working and respond effectively
to inequities, injustices, and the basic survival needs of humanity and our
planet.
Too often we focus on one or the other—either addressing problems and
what is not working or creating alternatives and hoping that problems will
resolve on their own. Radical transformation necessitates responding to the
problems of today and simultaneously realizing our full potential to
generate alternatives for tomorrow, both sourced from universal values.
Fallacy: I want to see a better world, but there is nothing much I
can do, because the problems are so big. Small initiatives are
like a drop in the ocean and will not make any difference. To
make a difference, we need to work at scale, and I do not have
the money nor the influence or connection to do so.
New Learning: Everyone can directly make a difference to scale
action and results by designing and implementing CFS
responses at home or at work or in communities. It is strategic to
BE in action by sourcing universal values and shifting unworkable
norms and systems.
The strategies for responding to problems are different from strategies to
realize our full potential to generate alternatives. Most people design
projects and initiatives thinking that if we succeed in responding to
problems (e.g., survival needs), the rest will follow. Others look at
responding to problems and realizing full potential for generating
alternatives as a sequential continuum, often describing it as an
evolutionary process. The radical transformer has a whole-systems
perspective and must have the ability to respond to problems effectively and
at the same time, put in place strategies to realize the full potential of
people to generate a paradigm shift. This is illustrated in Figure 9-4. The
radical transformer has the capabilities to design and implement initiatives
that are dynamic and meteoric, instead of slower incremental change that
does not address systemic issues.
We must respond to the basic needs of people, but we must not presume
that they do not have the inner capacities to actualize happiness. All of us
who have worked at the grass roots have experienced the beauty and power
of the inner capacities of people who are poor and marginalized because of
the systems and cultural norms we have created in our world of abundance.
Hemlata and her colleagues, whom you met in Chapters Four and Six, are
testimony to this.
Figure 9-4 . Responding to Challenges and Realizing Full Potential.
Let’s explore three issues that we grapple with in present times—
survival, poverty, and violence (see Figure 9-4). The same principles apply
to other challenges.
The mindset and strategies of survival are different from the mindset and
strategies for thriving. At home and in our families, our basic needs—
health, nutrition, education, income, energy, housing, water, sanitation, and
digital connection—must be met. But at the same time, we want health not
only to survive and reduce death and disease, but to thrive; education not
just to read and write, but to unleash our creativity and contribute to
society; we want jobs not just to earn money and fuel materialism, but to
make a difference in our workplace and society.
The mining industry has rules that stipulate that “they do no harm to
people or the planet,” which comes from a survival mindset. What if weestablish rules, strategies, and technologies that “do no harm” and also have
rules, strategies, and technologies that create prosperity for workers and
contribute to a thriving environment?
Rich countries are not rich in terms of equity, and they are actually poor
in terms of their courage to act for prosperity for all. For example, in India,
the insatiable demand for dowry and the atrocious consequences of
noncompliance on the bride or wife—in many rich, middle-class, as well as
low-income families—and opulent weddings in income-rich families in the
midst of dire poverty, are evidence that meeting the basic needs of people
without the foundation of universal values does not result in prosperity.
Poverty reduction strategies have created development conditions where
a few people have been pulled out of dire circumstances and survive; but
people are not thriving. Prosperity is about synergistic strategies that
emanate from the mindset of abundance, contentment, and thriving; it is
about meeting basic needs as well as the unleashing of full potential. It is a
realistic knowing about the bountiful resources we have, a deep
understanding of the beauty of sophisticated simplicity, and the wisdom of
mindful living “in the now.”
We generally agree, at least to be politically correct, that violence at
home, at work, and between different social groups or countries must be
stopped. The global economic impact of violence is estimated at $13.6
trillion—$2.5 trillion from crime and interpersonal violence, $6.2 trillion
through military spending and action, and $0.76 trillion from losses due to
conflict.51 We must respond to this crisis and establish cultures and societies
where violence is absent and where the fear of violence is absent. We must
also cultivate peaceful societies where we nurture human BEINGS to
manifest their full potential. The systems and cultural norms—the attitudes,
structures, and institutions—must create and sustain peaceful societies.
Another example: Papua New Guinea has an exceptionally high level of
sexual violence, attributed to a male-dominated culture along with cultural
acceptance of rape and everyday violence. It has been estimated that 70
percent of Papua New Guinean women experience rape or assault.52 When
we were engaging to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic using a conscious
full-spectrum approach, four journalists decided to address rape and
generate a more peaceful society. This campaign in the media emerged as a
courageous response by committed citizens, shifting cultural norms—their
strong compassionate hearts simply could no longer sit back and watch
what was going on—individuals made choices for collective action.
To respond to the challenge, participants took steps to enforce laws;
hotlines for women to seek help and support structures were put in place,
and educational programs on their human rights were designed and
implemented. In addition, a campaign was designed and implemented to
realize the full potential of people, “amplify the good,” and make visible the
nurturing Papua New Guinean men who were generating new icons and
cultural norms for a peaceful society.
Fallacy: We must first focus on solving the problems in society.
Only after that can we provide opportunities to the poor and
marginalized to unleash their full potential. Some people talk
about universal values—they are idealists. We must respond to
people’s demands and be realistic. Change happens slowly—
past experience shows that.
New Learning: Prosperous and thriving societies are established
when we source universal values, align our strategies and action
to respond and solve problems; and simultaneously put in place
strategies to realize our full potential to generate alternatives.With our tremendous technological advances, we can disrupt
unworkable systems and accomplish tremendous meteoric
transformation in a short span of time, not merely incremental
change.
We continue to muddle through, from crisis to crisis, never addressing
the underlying factors and systems causes of problems of humankind in a
definitive and sustained way. Yesterday, we were engaged in resolving a
crisis: HIV/AIDS. Today, we are focusing on global warming and climate
change. Tomorrow, we may focus on nuclear waste and the consequences of
the dire imbalances of our ecological systems. Sourcing universal values for
generating equitable and sustainable systems and cultural norms is the key
to responding to challenges and realizing full potential. In this way, we have
enduring results.
Basis of Equity and Sustainability
After millions of years of evolution, for the first time, all the major threats
to our survival are human-caused. The state of the world is therefore a
consequence and expression of our own minds. In explaining our global
crises we generally focus on the importance of economic, social, and
political forces and factors as causes for the persistence of the world’s
problems. And therefore, the response focuses on technological, economic,
and political approaches. And these are indeed often necessary. But it is not
until we see the global problems as symptoms of one fundamental, deeper-
rooted crisis—the symptoms of our individual and shared mindset, where
psychological and cultural factors and forces reign—that we can begin to
mount a more profound response.
The key to a thriving future lies in transformative approaches based on
universal values that transcend our scarcity mindset on our abundant planet
and our attachment to our own rigid ideologies and egos.
Interrupting Exclusionary Isms
Human beings express their inner capacities through worldviews and
mindsets that manifest dignity, equity, and compassion. And human beings
also choose to limit their full potential and contribution by dominating,
exploiting, and excluding others. There was a time when slavery and
exploitative labor were accepted almost everywhere; today in many parts of
the world they are unacceptable even though they exist. Concerned scholars
who study oppressive beliefs such as racism, casteism, classism, sexism,
tribalism, and religious hatred describe the detrimental effects of these isms
and prejudices on people and societies. In Part One of this book, we noted
some of them.
People are aware that change lies in influencing mindsets. Market
research and its application has led to skillfully shaping unending
materialism instead of responsible consumption. Political campaigns around
the world are sometimes designed to manipulate people’s preferences,
biases, and fears, instead of shaping democratic action for individual and
societal well-being for everyone. In radical transformation we are talking
about transforming our mindset based on universal values that include
everyone, everywhere, with the explicit agenda of manifesting our full
potential and innate attributes; this transformation will lead to equity,
sustainability, and prosperity for all.
Fallacy: If we all start sourcing universal values, we will not have
diversity and pluralism. We will lose our independence. And we
will be forced to accept universal values of dignity, fairness, andcompassion like a rigid formula. It should be up to us to decide
what to live by.
New Learning: When we source universal values, and express
them through strategic action, multitudes of initiatives come alive,
and a vast array of ideas find expression based on our
aspirations, interests, and talents. Our independence is
wholesome through our interdependence.
Current understanding of human beings shows that everyone has
an innate sense of self-worth and dignity in all cultures; we have
an innate “equity impulse”; and when our heart breaks open we
are called to respond with compassionate action.
Dignity, fairness, and compassion are simply inherent in us, and
are stifled through socialization, rigid systems, and cultural
norms.
Concerned citizens, courageous scholars, and thoughtful policy-makers
in every country in the world are asking a fundamental question: how can
we break the cycles of classism, racism, casteism, sexism, tribalism, hateful
religiosity, our never-ending wants and greed?
When we are committed to finding equitable and sustainable patterns that
work for humanity as a whole, we must look deeper. Finding inspiration in
the Constitution of UNESCO, which states that “since wars begin in the
minds of men, it is in the minds of men that the defences of peace must be
constructed,” we can start exploring whole-systems transformation. With
the complex political, economic, and social forces at play and the
numerous, ever-increasing technologies available to us, a radical departure
from the way we addressed problems in the past is imminent. Although
individuals, organizations, and communities realize the interconnectedness
at the root of many of our global challenges, most of our responses remain
at the level of technological interventions, tinkering at the margins and
changing a few “rules of the game.” Working at this level, we are unable to
understand and respond to the root factors of unsustainable production and
consumption patterns, proliferating conflicts, and new climatic patterns.
Now, we are called to source our inner capacities and universal values in
order to think and act locally and globally to ensure intergenerational equity
and dignity as well as sustainability of our ecosystems.
The patterns of otherness and exclusion can be interrupted by embodying
universal values for strategic action and results. Figure 9-5 illustrates this
paradigm shift. We must learn to transcend our scarcity mindset fueled by
competition and fear, and move beyond token representation, superficial
gestures of inclusion, inadequate support for people who are marginalized
by cultures or systems, and the hollow politically correct statements we
make.
Concerned citizens focus on the rights of a particular group, for example,
women’s equity, inclusion of all genders and LGBT people, racial equity,
labor rights. They often do not recognize and realize that these are all
expressions of the same exploitative and exclusionary forces in us, our
cultures and systems. For example, there are women in India who work for
“women’s rights” but will not sit and eat with a Dalit woman or allow their
son or daughter to marry someone from another caste. In the United States,
there are groups working to have racial equity, but some of them do not see
the presence of gender-based or class inequities. Equity and dignity are a
way of BEING.
I shared examples of interrupting the patterns of dominance, exploitation,
and exclusion in preceding chapters: Influential Muslim and Christian
religious leaders from the Arab States sourced their inner capacities anduniversal values to interrupt the patriarchal and exploitative promotion of
female genital mutilation in their society. Hemlata, Sudarshan, Srilatha, and
other members of the TISS team work with people from all castes to
interrupt caste-based patterns of exploitative labor in India. Megan and her
colleagues are working to break the cycles of violence in the United States.
In all these instances, the common feature is sourcing universal values of
dignity, equity, compassion for strategic action.
Figure 9-5 . Decide to Interrupt Isms.
In the leadership and stewardship programs, depending on where we are
—the country or region in the world—issues related to racism, casteism,
sexism, tribalism, and inequity inevitably surface. In the United States, in
several instances, the issue of racism arose. Policies that ensure everyone
has equal opportunity to manifest their full potential, historical knowledge
of exploitation and suffering, understanding the social and political
dynamics of racism (or any other ism), organizing to realize human rights
and responsibilities are all necessary.
I recall a profound moment when a participant in a learning-in-action
program and I worked together—she on race and I on caste—from a
resonant space of universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion. And
from this universal value base, we mapped the interventions on a conscious
full-spectrum sheet. We also connected what keeps such otherness and
exclusion prevalent and alive. And in that profound moment, she could see
how to interrupt the pattern of racism (and casteism); and then almost all of
us in a room with nearly one hundred people could see that the only sure
way of interrupting centuries of human domination and exploitation is to
transcend our egos and source our strategic actions from our inner
capacities.
There was a contemplative silence in the room—the deep silence that
leads to new emergence—spaces you and I have experienced. After some
time, another participant came up with her learning and her insight: she said
she was working to end racism and struggled with what she had been told
about her white privilege, which she held with a sense of guilt because of
the exploitation and suffering of many people. The guilt of white privilege
had made her feel that she should play small, which actually didn’t benefit
anyone. She shared that “in this moment, I embrace my inner capacities and
greatness, our oneness, like everyone else, without any guilt, and will act
from my greatness to disrupt racism and other inequities.”
When injustice is present through socially constructed identities of race,
caste, class, gender, tribe, or nationality, people who touch their universal
heart of compassion and alter their mindset are called to act and address the
inequities. And when society and individuals stereotype these
compassionate people in ways that do not honor their inner capacity, we
make it harder for all of us to achieve the goals and outcomes of
interrupting these isms.
We will unleash yet another version of the Jim Crow laws unless we look
into the root factors of otherness; the only way we can interrupt isms is to
source our oneness, our inner capacities. The Jim Crow laws, which were
formally in effect until 1965 in the United States, enforced “separate but
equal” conditions in public facilities and services, schools, and other
communal spaces. Where there were facilities for white people, there may
not have been services and facilities at all that African Americans were
allowed to use. This pattern of segregation became normalized in society,
showing up in housing, employment, and other basic tenants of community.
Michelle Alexander’s eye-opening book, The New Jim Crow, points out
that “no other country in the world imprisons so many of its racial or ethnicminorities. The United States imprisons a larger percentage of its black
population than South Africa did at the height of apartheid.”53 In fact, one
in four black men will experience incarceration in their lifetime. The
epidemic and normalization of mass incarceration of black men, women,
and children has in fact become a new iteration of the segregation of the
Jim Crow era. With a criminal record, even if never incarcerated, these
“marked” individuals face isolation and alienation from civic life,
employment opportunities, and other basic prerequisites for self-sufficiency
and a decent quality of life.
While on the surface, systems that lead to the segregation of certain
populations might not seem to be biased, they are in fact designed in such a
way that they have become what Alexander describes as a contemporary
system of control. These systems are designed by humans and for humans.
And therefore to reverse the emergence of a new Jim Crow era, to create
communities where all are welcomed and treated fairly, we must both shift
the mindset of inequality that gave rise to these systems and create
pathbreaking alternatives that will lead to equitable outcomes.
All isms are held in the relationship of one group to another. We can
interrupt the disempowering social, political, and economic isms in our
cultures, systems, structures, and societies when we choose to commit to an
equitable and sustainable world and manifest universal values for strategic
action, altering the mindset that gave rise to the ism. If we choose not to
interrupt the patterns of isms by not sourcing our universal values and retain
the same mindset and ideology, we will face another version of the same
ism even though we have strategies to address the present problems. The
New Jim Crow describes the new expression of racism in the United States.
Similar patterns of exclusion hold true elsewhere in the world for the
different expressions of various isms that create suffering in humanity.
We can interrupt classism or inequity too. The financial and economic
policies that concentrate wealth and power in the hands of a few at the
expense of many were formulated by people. Citizens, farsighted corporate
leaders, and responsible politicians are people too, and can see the impact
of massive inequities and climate change on humanity and generations to
come. Standing in universal values, they can interrupt classism and take
policy measures to break the patterns. For example, there is no reason why
universal free education from elementary school through college and
universal health care cannot be provided. Education and fostering
capabilities are the foundation for equity and prosperity. We could simply
stop lobbying, and being a lobbyist becomes a profession of the past. We
could have a fair taxation system and proportionately tax wealth,
corporations, and dividends and have less tax on wages and consumption.
We could stop tax havens. And these steps will leave all people and society
prosperous. The wealthy do not need more wealth—they too want well-
being and happiness.
Fallacy: People—policy-makers and citizens alike—must
understand all the systems, structures, dynamics, and history of
the different disempowering isms because they are so different.
We need a thorough understanding before taking action.
New Learning: The key to interrupting entrenched isms is to
source the universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion,
transcending our ego and loosening the grip of our exclusionary
social identities. The root factors of all isms are the same, though
their expressions may be different.
We need to act based on what we know—understand the
systems, structures, dynamics, and history of the differentdisempowering isms in order to take appropriate measures—and
these measures will change as contexts change. Understanding
is a necessary but not sufficient condition to interrupt isms.
BEING a Radical Transformer
Radical transformers manifest the triple helix of our innate intertwined
attributes—universal heart of compassion, equity impulse, discerning eye
seeing patterns—in order to BE the contemporary pioneer, unifying
architect, and mindful pro-activist simultaneously. They act at scale.
When we touch the deeper side of ourselves, something opens up to the
universe and we give unstintingly, accessing our compassion and our
humanity. We hold self-awareness, our inner power, our courage and
compassion, our spirituality as an essential foundation for a thriving
humanity. We give of ourselves because we care and because we realize we
are interconnected, part of a larger interdependent whole. Caring and
willingness to give are essential, but they are not sufficient for shifting
cultural norms and systems that lead to inequity and rectify the injustices of
the world—we need to act.
Radical transformers care deeply and source universal values for action
at scale in order to generate a paradigm shift. They address the three key
issues underpinning many of the crises in the world today—inequity,
exclusion based on our social and other identities, and the current economic
and financial systems, which we noted in Part One of this book. Scale
requires us to design programs to transform these three underpinning
factors with new policies, norms, systems, and structures and with policy-
makers, businesses, and communities.
A Radical Transformer Disrupts Social Norms That
Maintain Inequity, Exploitation, and Exclusion
B. R. Ambedkar said, “Political tyranny is nothing compared to the social
tyranny and a reformer who defies society is a more courageous man than a
politician who defies Government.” Ambedkar was an Indian jurist,
economist, politician, and social reformer who campaigned against social
discrimination of Dalits. He supported the rights of women and labor rights,
and said decades ago, “I measure the progress of a community by the
degree of progress which women have achieved.”
Ambedkar was the principal architect of the Constitution of India and
was independent India’s first law minister. He was the first Indian to earn a
doctorate overseas. His doctorates were in economics from both Columbia
University and the London School of Economics. His legacy as a social and
political reformer continues to influence India’s social and economic
policies.
Bhimrao Ramji Ambedkar was born into a poor Dalit family in 1891.
Dalits were treated as “untouchables,” and children had to sit outside the
classroom. A century ago—and even today—Dalits were not allowed to eat
or drink water with people from so-called “higher” castes, or even touch the
water or food, or the containers for water or food. When children in school
needed to drink water, someone from a higher caste had to pour that water
from a height; if the attendant was not available, then children had to go
without water. Ambedkar wrote about this in his writing “No Peon, No
Water” (a peon is an office or school attendent).
This was more than a one hundred years ago, and although India today
has made progress, such discrimination still exists in many parts of the
country. You met Raju in Chapter Six, who still faced discrimination in his
school in the twenty-first century. He and other Dalit children could not
drink water from the same water fountain as children from the so-called
upper castes, and often had to be in school without water. Raju had the
courage to take steps to transform the discrimination at his school.Like any other ism, the way to interrupt casteism is by authentically
embracing everyone irrespective of their caste, sourcing our oneness and
embodying universal values for action.
Ambedkar, often referred to as the father of India’s Constitution, was
voted as “The Greatest Indian” in 2012 with 20 million votes in a poll
organized by History TV18 and CNN-IBN.54 Ambedkar said, “Political
democracy cannot last unless there lies at the base of it social democracy.
What does social democracy mean? It means a way of life which recognizes
liberty, equality and fraternity as the principles of life.… What is required is
a profound and thorough conviction of the justice, necessity and importance
of political and social rights.” He believed strongly in individual freedom.
Although many reading this book may not be active politicians, social
transformation that presences universal dignity and equity is vitally
important for each one of us, our children, and our grandchildren; it is at the
core of a vibrant democracy. Ambedkar is a profound inspiration for radical
transformers everywhere. He transcended exclusionary social norms, not
with hatred, but with a deep knowing of the yearning of the human spirit to
be free and live with dignity. He discerned with pristine clarity root factors
in our social institutions, culture, and people that created disempowering
isms and invited everyone to join hands to address equality and dignity for
women, disenfranchised groups, and those who lived in poverty. He
navigated complex social and political systems and power dynamics
without petty, superficial rhetoric and tactics. He prepared the Constitution
of India based on universal values of social, economic, and political justice;
liberty of thought, expression, belief, faith, and worship; equality of status
and of opportunity; and fraternity, assuring the dignity of the individual.
It is up to each one of us living in a democracy to BE deeply self-aware,
embody these values instead of simply talking about them, manifest them in
action for equitable and sustainable results. This applies to other
democracies in the world, and to people who are working for universal
dignity and equity.
A Radical Transformer Faces the Resistance
Our transformative personal practices and who we are BEING anchor
societal and planetary transformation. And when we are committed to
transforming inequitable, exploitative, and exclusionary systems and
cultural norms that maintain the status quo, we should expect pushback. A
few people with vested interests will resist change. Although most people
want change, they are often very uncomfortable because they are not sure
what will emerge and find it difficult to engage in the adventure of
uncertainty. Uncertainty conjures up fears in our minds; we feel “out of
control” because many of us have an assumption that we can control what
happens to us and our children; in reality, we cannot control life’s events.
Stakeholders and participants in learning-in-action programs consistently
come up with an observation: people in their organizations or their partners
do not understand nor appreciate the breakthroughs they generate. There are
at least two aspects to this seeming indifference. First, our own vision limits
what we can see. When human beings do not engage in their own unfolding
based on their inner capacities and the universal values they stand for, they
do not distinguish business as usual from transformative action—they do
not have the lens to see, perceive, and appreciate the inclusiveness and
depth of what is happening.
Second, in our competitive world, we should not expect people to clap
when we are successful. Joyfully celebrating another’s success requires a
healthy sense of self, profound humility, and selfless generosity. We are the
ones who gain most by our contribution, because we give the fullest
expression to our whole selves and experience the joy of BEING in actionwith results.
As long as we make incremental changes on the margin without rocking
the boats heavy with vested interests and give as charity only (instead of
giving as mutual empowerment), we are unlikely to face resistance. But the
moment we stand in universal values such as dignity, compassion, and
equity, and work with the inner capacities with everyone to create new
patterns and new rules of the game, some people are threatened.
Those who are threatened realize that when people engage from this
powerful inner space of universal values, people will not allow themselves
to be manipulated. Leaders in business, politics, media, academia,
government and nongovernmental organizations know that the missing
piece is the opportunity to source our inner capacities for strategic action
and results. However, they generally do not invest in creating this
opportunity, carrying on business as usual but expecting different results.
People in leadership positions are often afraid they will lose control—this is
also the case in families.
We expect resistance—resistance is an indicator that we are pushing the
envelope and rattling the status quo. New ideas and approaches take time to
seed—but that is no reason for anyone to give up. And it is vital to establish
strategies to openly support principled risk-takers—we noted this in
Chapter Six, when we reviewed synergistic operational strategies.
A Radical Transformer Transcends Burnout
In institutions with high expectations and deadlines, people experience
fatigue and even physical and mental collapse due to overwork and stress.
We often refer to this as burnout, but in this transformative work, we
differentiate being tired, overworked, and stressed from being burned out.
Burnout is the frustration of having to face resistance. When we commit
and act to generate equitable and sustainable results and face constant
pushback and resistance, it may be hard to keep going.
Active burnout shows up as frustration and anger. Passive burnout shows
up as withdrawal and resignation. How can we prevent burnout? When we
constantly source our stand and inner capacities for action, we are able to
transcend our reactivity and frustration. We relate to resistance not as
something wrong or bad but as an inevitable part of change. Generating and
sustaining community engagement in the face of resistance is vital. We
work with partners in a way that they are able to initiate and sustain action,
transcend positional differences, and recommit to their stand and inner
capacity as the source of strategic action. BEING courageous in spite of
fears and knowing that we are bigger than any fear keeps us engaged.
I learned to internalize the tenet of impermanence through the discourses
of S. N. Goenka during Vipassana meditation retreats. Nothing is permanent
in life. It is a relief to realize that the resistances we face shall pass too—
like everything else. Knowing impermanence deep within ourselves
cultivates endurance, enhances our ability to be “unmessable with,” and
makes us unstoppable.
During a stewardship program, participants working for social justice
expressed their deep frustration about multiple obstacles, including inertia
and apathy. In one instance, a participant had spent years in a city in India
bringing Hindus and Muslims together who worked harmoniously on social
issues, and then a donor suddenly decided to give money to one group,
driving a separative wedge into the community. She was devastated.
We spent time “rebooting” ourselves. Change agents inevitably face
setbacks in their work on social justice and human rights. In order to go
beyond their frustration, it is essential to reconnect to our larger purpose
and notice changes that we have made even when it seems hopeless and we
think we failed! We reconnected with what we stood for—the universalvalues that guided our actions. Participants articulated their purpose and
contribution: How will I live my life, knowing I will die? We spoke of what
had been accomplished even when it felt like nothing much had been
achieved: the widespread awareness citizens gained because of campaigns
related to social justice, financing, ending homelessness; Muslim and Hindu
women taking the initiative to establish social networks and support groups
without donor funds.
We identified seeming dichotomies and worked together to deepen the
conversation, anchoring ourselves in universal values in order to transcend
them: How do we work collectively versus the individual need to
contribute? How do we foster openness without the work getting derailed
by people who wish to maintain status quo? We must be able to transcend
social identity constructs, and simultaneously actively engage in anti-
oppression work dealing with social reality. When we notice personal
contradictions, we continue with our commitment, knowing we are human
and not perfect. How can we transcend these dichotomies and navigate into
a vibrant future without compromising universal values of dignity, fairness,
and compassion?
Taking into account the new principles and contexts that emerged
through our exploration, participants redesigned projects or formulated new
initiatives using transformative design templates (CFSR, TRC, SOS). It was
renewing for everyone to articulate what participants were going to offer
and the results they would generate in the future. They articulated steps to
nurture themselves and thought about options and places where they could
manifest their full potential.
As we will see in Chapter Ten, radical transformers are contemporary
pioneers, unifying architects, principled game changers, and mindful pro-
activists who establish the strategic tapestry of action at scale to shift the
paradigm. In Chapter Eleven, we will look at how radical transformers
skillfully align and attune with partners and institutions to generate
equitable and sustainable results, overcoming silos and fragmentation and
leveraging transformation.
Chapter 10
Responding at Scale
The Strategic Tapestry of Scale
The future is not some place we are going to, but one we are creating.
The paths to it are not found, but made; and the activity of making them
changes both the maker and the destination.
JOHN SCHAAR
For responses to be effective at scale, it is necessary to use approaches and
methods that generate synergy and coherence and are mutually reinforcing.
In Chapter Six, we worked with three templates for designing
transformational projects, initiatives, or ideas for action and results: the
conscious full-spectrum response (CFSR), synergistic operational strategies
(SOS), and the transformational results chain (TRC). In this chapter, we
will take these templates to the next stage of design and implementation.
We will also explore the ways that interdependent entities are connected in
a constellation that manifests universal values for a thriving humanity and
planet.
When our projects take root and start generating some results, we add the
next four synergistic operational strategies, as we continue the first four.
These stage-two strategies are listed in Table 10-1.

Figure 10-1 . Synergistic Operational Strategies—Stage 2.
Capacity Development for Transformative
Results
Our quality of life and well-being depend on much more than having
material wealth and comfort. When our universal values are attuned with
how we think and what we do, a deep subjective sense of well-being is
established, anchored in contentment and compassionate action, unleashing
our full potential. In the broadest picture, our quality of life includes the
notion of capabilities—our health, our literacy, and our capacity to actively
participate in social and political spheres and pursue what we deeply care
about. This rejects the view held by some that human beings act primarily
out of self-interest. But opportunities must be made available for people to
express these literacies and capacities within society.
Capacity development is the process by which people, organizations, and
society as a whole unleash, strengthen, create, adapt, and maintain capacity
over time. Capacity development addresses three dimensions: human,
organizational, and broader institutional capacity.
To have people-centered capacity development, we need to be clear: what
do we develop capacity for? We need development of technological
capacity to solve problems, along with competencies for management,
formulating policies, and making decisions that are equitable and
sustainable. Capacity needs to be developed for leaders and stewards to take
action for systems and cultural shifts based on universal values.
A conscious full-spectrum approach is useful for building capacities of
institutions as well as individuals. It can unleash entrepreneurship that
builds on deeply embedded traditions of voluntary and mutual aid and self-
help forms of social capital without generating more debt. And applying
this deeper understanding of capacity development can generate entirely
new perspectives and responses to governance and current financial and
economic systems. For example, in the area of trade, the CFSR approach
assists people in developing human and institutional capacities based on
universal values for formulating new policies, effective negotiations, and
informed participation based on equity and sustainability. Developing
countries can then also reap maximum benefits, instead of having to cope
on the uneven playing field of today’s multilateral trading system.
Using the conscious full-spectrum approach we relate capacity
development to human potential and inner capabilities, whereby self-
awareness, self-expression, self-management, responsibility, and
accountability are enhanced. Figure 10-2 shows what capabilities and
competencies we foster for equitable and sustainable change.
At home, we can choose to follow a similar pattern for ourselves and our
children. We can develop skills and mastery in a professional craft; thenecessary competencies to effectively navigate the many family, societal,
and workplace pressures; and the capacity to live life fully and mindfully,
cultivating self-awareness along with the joy of being creative.
In order to take work to scale, a core strategy for continued and sustained
changes and results is to develop capacity. Equally important are strategies
for utilizing capacity. Too often people are trained, but it is others who get
the jobs—a particular group of people, or familiar people we want to
promote. Capacity development and creating opportunities to use this
capacity are core components of all programs we design and implement
Figure 10-2 . Capacity Development for Results.
Fallacy: Developing leadership competencies is very important
for change, particularly for those who hold senior management
and leadership positions in their organizations.
New Learning: Everyone can, and must, develop leadership and
stewardship capacities, and apply them at home, in communities,
and at work, in order to generate systems and cultural shifts for
people and our planet to thrive.
My own learning journeys have combined technical expertise and
personal development with social transformation. I feel privileged that
chance created opportunities for me to work with and learn from eminent
leaders in the health sector who cared deeply for humanity—Carl Taylor,
Vulimiri Ramalingaswami, Halfdan Mahler. Working with James Grant, the
former executive director of UNICEF, was a tremendous learning
experience. When I worked as the global child health advisor at UNICEF,
his results-oriented, strategic directions and expectations from UNICEF
staff stretched me to my fullest potential.
I was recruited to UNICEF in 1988. Within two weeks, when my
supervisor, Rolf Carriere, asked me what I needed to do my job well, I
replied, “Space to innovate and trust.” His response intrigued me: “If that is
what you need, you have to find it yourself. You have been recruited to lead
the immunization program, and you are expected to deliver results.” I
embarked on yet another dimension of learning, systematically participating
in various personal development programs and integrating that learning into
my life at work and at home.
Rolf Carriere created the milieu for team learning and development. He
believed that personal development combined with technical expertise
would foster initiative and generate results. The results were significant:
innovations to reduce childhood mortality, address child labor, improve the
quality of education in schools in Bangladesh. We also created a culture of
growth, learning, and responsibility in our workplace.
Development of competence and mastery is not a final end state but a
continuous process of learning, discovering, and applying that to life and
work.
Developing and Utilizing Capacity: Examples
Participants in leadership programs are equipped to use a combination of
cutting-edge methodologies to inspire action, design for action at scale,
produce breakthrough results, and coach others and engender
transformation in order to generate results in different programs—reducing
maternal and child mortality, halting and reversing HIV/AIDS, stopping
female genital mutilation, preventing violence, and conserving our habitats.
