For example, Chika Saito, chief advisor of a JICA (Japan International
Cooperation Agency) pro-poor project in Lagos, Nigeria, was part of our
team at the United Nations to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic. Herperseverance and commitment are inspiring. She is on a journey of
continuous learning and application. She deepened her learning of
leadership competencies, is now a certified master practitioner and coach in
neuro-linguistic programming, and trained further as a professional
psychological counselor accredited by the Japanese Organization of Mental
Health and Educational Agencies. In the different programs that Chika leads
to mitigate poverty and enhance health, she applies her expertise for
realizing human rights, building strategic alliances, and creating a work
environment where each staff member is respected and stimulated to be
creative.
Sanjeev Ranganathan designed and leads Aura Auro Design and STEM
Land for education in rural India. STEM is the acronym for science,
technology, engineering, and mathematics. Sanjeev participated in one of
our learning-in-action programs. He says, “My learning from the
stewardship workshops was significant in the creation of Aura Auro. A new
kind of institution, based on the universal values I stand for, came to me as
an insight and a breakthrough initiative; and thanks to the stewardship
workshop, I put it into action.”
Sanjeev designed Aura Auro as a socially conscious business. Their
partner, Aura Semiconductor, is a fabless semiconductor company focused
on using its product development expertise to evolve as a leading
semiconductor company in India. This provides Sanjeev and the engineers
on his team interaction and benchmarks with technology leaders and
increases the need for their skills to be on par with any design group in the
world.
Sanjeev knows the importance of developing the capacity of his team,
creating opportunities for them to utilize their learning and nurture their
growth. The STEM Land and Aura Auro team consists of five committed,
talented, action- and results-oriented, practice-based, fun-loving engineers
—Arun Surya, Bala Anand, Naveen Kumar, Sundranandhan
Kothandaraman, and Vaidegi Gunasekar. Everyone on his team participated
in the Stewardship for New Emergence programs designed and delivered by
me in Auroville. These learning-in-action programs enhance skills,
competencies, and inner capacities to design and deliver conscious full-
spectrum projects. Subsequently, the STEM Land team generated ideas for
transformation and embarked on new initiatives. For example, they design
and implement youth leadership programs for middle and high schoolers;
support women from neighboring villages to design and implement
breakthroughs in their lives; and engage with youth in gangs to change their
current ways.
In villages, schools often have limited access to competent teachers,
STEM learning materials, and inadequate infrastructure—for example, they
often have only intermittent internet access. STEM Land is a tremendous
breakthrough in these circumstances. For children in rural India, English is
the second language. Sanjeev chose a popular visual programming
environment—Scratch, developed by the MIT Media Lab. The Scratch 2.0
offline editor is used so that children can program even when there is no
internet connection. All work is intentionally carried out using free, open-
source software, so that anyone can access and use their work.
Davaselvy, the principal of the school Udavi in Edayanchavadi village,
Tamil Nadu, India, encouraged STEM Land. It is rewarding to see that it is
possible to create spaces in rural India where children can learn through
inspiration rather than through obedience. Children build and program
robots and create their own games. They develop the ability to create
software programs that animate stories they write, create mathematical
games reiterating concepts they have learned, and physically buildsomething—oscillators, counters, wind chimes, speedometers, cameras!
In tandem with their work on STEM, children’s leadership programs are
implemented systematically. The STEM Land team members say that the
purpose of education “is beyond fitting in and standing out. Education
should help us learn how to live, to know who we are, to be self-directed
and creative.” They emphasize excellence and encourage students to move
from studying out of fear of examinations to learning for the joy of
achieving their full potential.
The work at STEM Land is relevant for children throughout the country.
The new Atal Innovation Mission of the Government of India has the vision
of cultivating one million children in STEM education. STEM Land and the
Aura Auro team have much to offer to both teachers and children.
Integrating STEM education along with building the leadership and inner
capacities of children is the key to success.
Sanjeev is an electronics engineer who received his PhD from Columbia
University. He reflects: “I want to be an instrument of divine consciousness.
In simpler terms, I stand for happiness that comes with self-awareness, of
being one with the universe. People can count on me for focus and to
support their growth.
“I was involved with various activist movements—these shook my
previous worldview that things are the way they should be; but I saw that
not all was well with the world. I realized that my assumption of being
‘self-made’ had not taken into account the privilege I had growing up and
having opportunities. I committed to use the privilege I had for others.
Supported by my wife, Anita, I questioned many symbols I had held dear
including the Poonal [a cotton thread symbolizing rites of passage for
‘upper castes’ mainly] that I gave up in order to explore who I am beyond
what religion told me. Anita and I continued to make many conscious
choices including moving to India, going vegan, and adopting a child.
“During this time, I attended a couple of Vipassana courses. These
courses released much of the anger I held from injustices I had seen thrust
on people. I found my actions were no longer governed by self-righteous
anger. I still work for justice and equity to prevail for all, and stand up and
speak out when I see unfairness; but now, I am guided by a higher purpose
that I am meant to manifest.”
Another example of developing and utilizing capacity: Jerry White,
whom you met in Chapter Seven, summarized his learning experience
through this work, which enhanced his leadership, mentoring, teaching, and
coaching capacities. He notes, “I developed a teachable point of view, with
methodologies to help next-generation leaders to do campaigning for
change. I learned a new vocabulary for systemic change, including the three
elements of a conscious full-spectrum response: wisdom and inner capacity;
understanding and our pattern mind; and knowledge for action.” Alignment
and BEING attuned was important. He revised training protocols and could
see how to teach larger groups, achieving scale faster. Jerry says, “I have
come to appreciate the special type of wise leadership needed for the global
challenges ahead. There are a growing number of people who are learning
how to summon the courage to take on some of society’s most rigidly
embedded institutions in pursuit of life-saving, economy-enhancing
change.”
Fallacy: Working with science and technology requires us to
focus and precludes us from dwelling on “soft topics” like respect
for all. Excellence is what we need, so we should work with those
who are smart and can excel.
New Learning: Excellence in science (or any topic) is about
mastery—building competence and unending, lifelong learning.Excellence is about learning and applying that knowledge
intelligently for the progress of humanity. Discovering and
sourcing what we stand for and our inner capacities generates
the alchemy of transformation—the courage to shift the paradigm
through creating new patterns sourced in universal values of
dignity, equity, compassion.
Measurement to Move the Paradigm
What we measure is what we move. Measurement is a core strategy for a
paradigm shift. Measurement is also a key component of going to scale; and
we need new metrics to draw us into manifesting a new reality. Appropriate
indicators, indices, and tools are an integral part of policy, strategy, and
design that leverages human, organizational, and institutional assets. The
measures we hide or ignore as experts are spaces where a few people may
be manipulating systems and culture for their vested interests.
In our personal lives, we constantly quantify the things we are concerned
about: the amount of money we have in our bank account, our weight if we
want to reduce it, or our blood sugar levels if we have diabetes. We measure
learning and progress—and the measurements we use depend upon our
definition of success. Is learning about scoring high marks and being smart,
or is it also about being emotionally intelligent and responsible? Is progress
about climbing the economic, social, and material ladder, or is it being
prosperous through living our full potential with sophisticated simplicity?
Measuring the Big Picture That Affects Our Daily Lives
Countries and institutions have committed to impacts, outcomes, and goals
for human well-being and equity and sustainability to achieve the
Sustainable Development Goals. Most conventional measurement
instruments and indicators of social and economic progress do not measure
these commitments and goals adequately, and they do not measure the root
factors that give rise to these problems in the first place, such as the greed
that stops equity from being present in our world of abundance; the
diminished self-worth that inflicts exploitation and domination over others;
the unwarranted fears that stop us from having the courage for
compassionate action. We measure production of goods and services,
investments in infrastructure, and selected social indicators related to
income, health, education, food, and nutrition. These are important, but they
do not provide the true measures of the quality of our lives and our human
potential and capacity—the most important asset of any person, family,
group, or nation.
Simultaneously, we are inspired by stories of extraordinary acts of
courage from every corner of the world, illustrating alternatives being put in
place by committed and creative people. In order to see this emerging
reality unfold, in which equity, dignity, and prosperity are present, new
metrics and frameworks are needed that will track progress and change well
beyond what exists today.
Many economists recommend going beyond GDP (gross domestic
product) to measure how well an economy is doing.55 GDP measures the
production of goods and services in a country and counts the ones that
benefit people in the same way as those that are not beneficial and can even
be harmful for people—for example, the production of pesticides that are
harmful for human health and diminish biodiversity are counted in the same
way as the production of healthy organic food.
Wealth is defined as having assets and savings, instead of having debt
and liabilities.56 Policies and strategies that foster wealth instead of debt
and liabilities result in household sustainability. We could have savings andassets resulting in sufficiency and sustainability, if the financial engines of
today did not drive growth through debt and liabilities at the macro and
household levels.
The underlying factors that generate an equitable and thriving humanity
and planet are in an acausal domain. These aspects, such as the universal
values we embody and who we are BEING, are beyond conventional
measurable cause-and-effect relationships or correlations. New metrics,
proxy indicators, and measures are necessary to capture these aspects.
Bhutan’s initiative to measure inner capacities is inspiring. In the 1990s
Bhutan embarked on measuring “gross national happiness” (GNH). They
defined happiness as “a state of well-being and contentment.”57 Well-being
included health, nutrition, having information and education, thoughtful
consumption, decent work, care, housing near one’s workplace, security,
and healthful environments. Their understanding of contentment is earnest
and deep, and includes: being able to express creativity, being respected and
respecting others, being able to express one’s feelings and thoughts freely,
having a personal ethical code, and being able to cooperate and share with
others.
Contentment is the key to shifting our scarcity mindset to a perception of
abundance and prosperity. Bhutan’s knowledge and wisdom are reflected in
their insightful and intelligent understanding that gross national happiness is
more important than gross national product. As such, gross national
happiness fosters a human society where material and spiritual development
occur side by side to complement and reinforce each other. This is an
immediate challenge to conventional development models that focus only
on economic growth as the ultimate objective.
New measurements across the conscious full-spectrum response template
stimulate us to ask a different set of questions. These are given in Figure
10-3, Measurement for Equity and Prosperity. Here are some questions we
can ask for transforming today’s challenges with existing and new metrics.
Do we have proxy measures to see when universal values form the basis
of policies, decisions, and strategies? For example, measures of
contentment?
How can we track the presence of ethical leadership and stewardship?
How do we measure our well-being, contentment, and sufficiency?
What resources—human, social, natural, and physical—can we pass on
to future generations?
This is not about expecting people to be satisfied with whatever little
they have, nor is it a romanticized view of happy poor people. It is about
authentic happiness, a state of BEING in which we are anchored in
universal values and manifest our full potential and capabilities.
We ask about the systems and cultural norms that indicate what we need
to shift on policies, programs, and actions:
Will the data and indicators measure the establishment of inclusive
systems and cultural norms that foster well-being of people and the
planet?
Have we created mechanisms to enhance our capabilities and the
opportunities for us to utilize them? What measures do we have?
Do we have political and social voice, and are we able to dissent
without fear, based on universal values?
What indices are we using to ensure equity, good governance, and a
thriving planet?
What are the measures to make mid-course corrections?Are we investing in a socially and environmentally sustainable way?
Have we started authentic accounting systems, in which we internalize
the true cost of products, instead of obfuscating the true cost?
Do we value and account for household services and costs—or is only
what we obtain in the market “valuable” and therefore accounted for?
Are we redirecting the course of true economic well-being from the
growth-at-any-cost paradigm to prosperity as the basis of thriving?
We must also ask questions about our everyday life, our basic needs,
about thriving in an equitable society:
Do we have data on access to and utilization of services for basic needs
—health, nutrition, and healthy food; education; energy; housing;
internet and communication, life-enhancing leisure; cultural, natural,
and recreational activities; and fair financing?
Do we highlight the distribution of these services to ensure equity and
sustainability?
What income, consumption, and wealth indices do we use? Are they
based on debt or savings, because debt does not lead to stability?
What are our profits and who benefits?
As citizens, business leaders, scholars and experts, and providers of
social services, we need to understand the measures now being used to
promote human progress and advocate for change based on informed and
universal value–based perspectives. We should not conveniently side with
opinions that emanate from limited understanding or vested interests.
Figure 10-3 . Measurement for Equity and Prosperity.
Fallacy: The measures that indicate how well the economy is
doing, such as GDP, are very complicated. We should leave it up
to experts and economists to tell us what is happening. It is
confusing because different economists have different opinions,
so we may as well leave it to them to sort it out. All I really care
about is how well my family is doing.
New Learning: As citizens, we need to inform ourselves about
how well the economy is doing, whether there is equity or not,
and how these are being measured. Expert opinions are useful
when they uphold equity and sustainability for people. The well-
being of my family depends upon the quality of our lives, equity
for all, and how wealth is created and distributed. I can make a
difference by understanding the situation and speaking up for the
changes we need for my family and community.
Measurement for a Paradigm Shift—Our Work in
Progress
Measuring equity and sustainability is work in progress—we must consider
what we measure, how we measure, who measures for whom, and the
decisions we make based on our measurement of data and information.
To Be Measured Meaningfully, Stability Must Be Anchored in
Universal Values
The essential stability of human endeavors and the futures we create are
grounded in universal values and our capability to source our inner power
and wisdom. Universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion do not
change over time. Technology and systems and cultural norms change.
When I worked on the project Saving Women’s Lives to reduce maternalmortality in eight countries of South Asia through UNICEF, we were clear
that the foundation of our work explicitly rested on “the self-determination
and dignity of women, and that this value reflected in each woman’s
realization of her right to a safe, life-enhancing pregnancy and birth.”58 We
had indicators to measure interim outcomes that resulted in saving women’s
lives, such as availability and use of effective emergency hospital services.
We also had culturally appropriate measures of a woman’s self-
determination and dignity as well as her fundamental equity. For example,
in a culture where women have almost no say about financial resources,
could a woman decide to spend even a small amount of money without
asking permission from her husband or mother-in-law?
As we engaged to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic, governments and
United Nations agencies measured wide-ranging aspects: new infections in
people, affordable and equitable access to testing facilities and treatment,
treatment rates and case fatality rates. New infections are often related to
unsafe sex as well as gender-based domination. We tracked whether the
universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion were the basis of
people’s actions. Were people living with HIV/AIDS treated with respect,
and were their voices heard without stigma and discrimination in families,
communities, and health facilities?
Cynda Rushton, whom we met in Chapter Five, is developing alternative
approaches for cultivating moral resilience in clinicians, since moral
distress is a common experience in their work, and consequently, in their
lives. Cynda has pioneered work regarding embodying universal values in
the context of compassionate care. She reports that using the conscious full-
spectrum process invites a more robust inquiry into the elements necessary
to cultivate and sustain a culture that supports ethical practice as well as
individual, team, and organizational integrity. The CFSR provides a
roadmap for engaging clinicians to leverage their shared values. In each
sphere—individual, team, and organization—it is a process for exploring
the underlying values and inner capacities—the shifts necessary to create
new patterns and the results that are desired.
The stable foundation of resilience is our moral compass, based on the
universal values of each person. A variety of specific tools and techniques
using the conscious full-spectrum process reduces the intensity of moral
distress, decreases the depth of moral injury, and contributes to healing and
moral repair and ultimately moral resilience. Systematic quality
assessments are undertaken related to policies and practices such as
structures for ethics education and clinical ethics consultation, and policies
governing areas such as conscientious objection and end-of-life care.
Barbara Torggler has been working at the United Nations as well as
several multicounty organizations for two decades and develops measures
to monitor and evaluate progress toward the overall goal of enhancing
people’s well-being. Barbara says, “I learned to anchor myself in spirit as
the deepest level where we are all united; to take the risk and engage in
large-scale, global, high-level activities that potentially have wide outreach
and large impact. Recent examples of this are the evaluations of standard-
setting work (conventions) of the organization I worked for at the United
Nations, which are being implemented by member states at a global level.”
The same principle applies to our lives in society and in families—if we
want to accomplish something different, we must design our ideas for
action in a different way, and have different measures. We are on the path of
thriving when we are in touch with who we are, what we stand for, the
universal values underpinning the decisions we make; when we define
prosperity beyond material wants, social status, and extrinsic power; and
when we articulate new measures of success for ourselves, our familymembers, and our society.
Citizens’ Capacity for Building Thriving Democracies
There is untapped energy in the realm of measurement for change. So far,
measurement has been the domain of policy-makers, experts, and scholars.
Today, citizens can shape their opinions based on the information they
receive; but this is inadequate to generate momentum for well-being. As
citizens, we need to think more deeply in order to understand what is behind
the sound bites we hear; we need to inform ourselves more accurately about
the systems and cultural issues, and go a few steps further to drive change
in the direction of a thriving humanity and planet.
With so much information being pushed through the media and
advertising, it is increasingly difficult for us to know what is reliable and
differentiate it from the power- and profit-motive propaganda. The
corporations and groups who choose not to be socially responsible—
although social responsibility is everyone’s business—support studies and
information to promote their positions. They sponsor a few willing scholars
as well as selected nonprofit organizations with seemingly laudable mission
statements—making it even more confusing and misleading for citizens.59
Only when we radically shift our mindset and choose to leave a world that
is better than we found it for our children and the following generations,
instead of responding to the shortsighted immediate gratification of our
unending wants, will we make the necessary effort to understand metrics
and measurement and use data differently.
The focus on popularity in present times is a distraction from what is
important and often rests on superficial measures, such as how many times
a song was heard or a person quoted. These outputs have little to do with
the changes we wish to see—music that is uplifting and life-enhancing or
responsible citizens and ethical leadership. Cultures and citizens in different
parts of the world need to value norms and measures that are in consonance
with a thriving humanity and planet.
The first step is to inquire into what we deeply care about and the
universal values we stand for; and then to use this as the foundation for
inquiry to see whether our strategies and measures are moving in the
direction of a thriving humanity and planet. Some of these inquiries are
listed earlier in this section. Thinking critically and understanding the
dynamics of the vested interests of particular groups who maintain the
status quo at the cost of people’s well-being is no longer an option—it is an
imperative. Developing the ability to inform ourselves from authentic
sources of data is in our own interest and essential for future generations to
thrive. Numerous indices attempt to provide us with information, and
citizens need to know and understand exactly what indicators are used to
formulate these indices. This may seem complex at first, but it becomes
clearer and comprehensible when we start informing ourselves. An index is
a composite statistic—a compound measure that aggregates multiple
indicators. An index summarizes and ranks specific observations and is
used to advocate for change. For example, the Human Development Index
ranks countries and measures enhanced human capabilities—long and
healthy life span, extent of knowledge, decent standard of life. It also
measures the conditions necessary for human development, such as
participation in political and community life, human security and rights,
environmental sustainability, equity, and justice. The Human Development
Index is formulated to promote the well-being of all people.60
The Global Peace Index comprises twenty-three indicators, which
measure such factors as violence, internal conflicts, ease of access to small
arms, military expenditures, and import and export of weapons. They alsomeasure peace—the attitudes, institutions, and structures that create and
sustain peaceful societies.61 The Green Transition Scoreboard, an ongoing
research project, in 2017 revealed an increase in green energy investments
and commitments worldwide—$8.13 trillion since 2007.62
However, there are indices that claim to benefit countries and people, but
seem to drive change in a particular direction on behalf of only a few
people. For example, an index on economic freedom is mostly about
intellectual property rights, limiting government spending, and even more
unregulated markets. This index does not consider economic opportunities,
decent work, and well-being for all people.
It is imperative that we inform ourselves of what comprises an index and
understand the trajectory of a future if we use that particular index to inform
our opinions, make demands, formulate strategies, or make decisions. It is
crucial that we question the answers we are given; and that we question
these answers from the perspective of equity and sustainability.
Our opinions and assertions matter when they are based on accurate
measurement and information as well as the universal values we stand for.
They strengthen democracy and create new societal conversations beyond
unexamined ideological positions.
We have enough studies and data that establish beyond doubt that all
human beings at our deepest core value self-worth and dignity, fairness, and
equity and are capable of compassionate action.63 Appropriate metrics and
measurement will create momentum and shift the current narratives for a
thriving humanity and planet to emerge.
New Narratives for New Systems and Norms
What we “see” is what we say.
And, what we measure is what we move.
Our lenses color what we see. To generate new narratives, we need to take
off our blinders and open up our compassionate hearts and radical minds so
that we can actually see the emerging icons who are shifting the paradigm.
We must distinguish the ways in which stories are currently told from
narratives that generate paradigm shifts. Too often, we write about what we
see directly, but without seeing the related patterns, and we miss
opportunities to generate transformative actions. Having deeper
conversations with people, inquiring about what they are doing (outputs),
and connecting their actions to outcomes and impact—the changes they are
making through their actions—stimulates people to see their contribution to
the big picture. It is exciting to see people connecting the dots and realizing
the difference they are making. Deepening the inquiry focuses on what
really matters to people—the outcomes and impacts they want to
accomplish in their lives—and is empowering.
Generating conversations that go beyond the mundane and offer the
opportunity for everyone to access and articulate their own inner power,
asking people what they care about, acknowledging them for their deeper
commitment behind what they do, moves us all toward creative action.
Jessica Underwood Varma, an actress, singer, and educator at the
Tenement Museum in New York City, was part of our leadership programs
in New York in 2009. Jessica shares, “The core of who I am, what the Sufi
poet Rumi called ‘the deep root of your being,’ has not changed, because it
was always there. I burn for dignity not just for myself but for all people
and for the planet on which we live.” Jessica noticed the difference in the
way she guided tours at the Tenement Museum. “After the learning-in-
action programs, I now have a rich environment at the museum in which to
apply everything I learned. The potential is huge: a growing institution with
an increasing visitorship. I decided to give a different kind of tour, makingthe systems and patterns that shape our lives, usually seen by a few, visible
to everyone. For example, I ask children on the tour how and where their T-
shirt or backpack was made, and by whom. It is amazing how children
understand systems and patterns! I create an experience that shifts my
visitors from a passive state to an active, socially conscious one.
“I remember one tour in particular. A representative from a foundation
was one of my visitors. At the end of the tour, his mind was made up, and
the museum got a grant of $500,000.
“I feel inspired sharing this story now, looking back on how far I have
come. My learning has completely transformed how I embark on strategic
action, how I lead and partner with people, my approach to problem
solving, and how I view the world. Nothing is the way it is because ‘that’s
just the way it is.’ People create systems and cultural norms, and systems
and cultures can always be shifted.”
We need to emphasize conscious full-spectrum approaches, instead of
narratives reflecting partial responses. Many stories that inspire us are
anchored in dignity, equity, compassion, and courage. These new and
exciting narratives challenge existing systems and norms as we solve
problems; but these connections and shifts are often implicit. Making them
explicit generates new societal conversations and influences our policies,
strategies, and politics.
Stories of acts of kindness and bravery inspire us too. In organizations,
“best practices” describe some successful aspects of projects and initiatives,
which can be interesting. These may be important, but they are incomplete
building blocks for a paradigm shift. We must be able to distinguish and
document efforts that create some results from the efforts that create a
paradigm shift. The paradigm shift in today’s context means equitable and
enduring well-being and dignity for all human beings and a thriving planet.
Artists and the Media Transforming Narratives and
Leadership
Artists, writers, and the media interpret the world for us. They are capable
of constructing new social realities and inspiring action. The reach and role
of mass media, social media, and artists—writers, filmmakers, visual and
performing artists, theater directors—in responding to any issue and setting
the tone for global and nationwide conversations is unparalleled.
In programs through the United Nations, including the maternal mortality
reduction program in South Asia and the global effort to reverse the
HIV/AIDS program—we engaged with media professionals and artists.
They were a critical part of the response. They participated in the learning-
in-action programs in different countries. Artists and media professionals
were leaders in their own right, and at the same time, they supported
emerging value-based leadership throughout the different echelons of
society.
Artists and the media in Africa, the Arab States, Asia, Eastern Europe,
and Latin America and the Caribbean created an abundance of richly
textured and insightful songs, poems, plays, music, and writing that
reflected the personal transformation through which they as participants in
learning-in-action programs had journeyed. These works revealed the new
reality from which artists were BEING, thinking, and doing. They moved
from images of blame, death, and overwhelming destruction to new images
with symbols, icons, and metaphors that portrayed strength, resilience, and
caring. Artists and media professionals understood the cultural norms that
fueled the spread of the HIV/AIDS epidemic and committed to being
proactive partners in the response.
Gulan Kripalani, a leadership practitioner, coach, and media professional,
was part of our team to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic. She had worked inadvertising and as a theater artist. She reflects: “I am self-aware so I can
align my principles and actions. I learned about how my values underpin
how I live and work. I learned that I too can inspire people.
“I believe this is the only methodology that I have experienced that is
capable of making real, sustainable change and delivering results. Without
the conscious full-spectrum response, we will always have millions of well-
meaning projects but can never create equitable and sustainable change.
This way of designing can transform individuals, communities, institutions,
and nations. This methodology can be taken to scale to change systems and
cultural norms that keep people and communities in a constant state of
being exploited and oppressed.
“I believe passionately in gender equity and social justice, in respect for
all. My work has been transformed because of my learning. I now see
societal patterns and interconnectedness much more clearly. I feel that had I
not encountered this approach, I might have become part of the millions of
‘project planning’ brigades. This work has strengthened my resolve to work
more strategically, and I feel my contribution can make a difference through
the many different ways I engage in society—as a leadership coach in
businesses, NGOs, and civil society and as an artist.”
An example from India today, beyond the United Nations: Rintu Thomas
and Sushmit Ghosh are young, talented, and committed filmmakers who
founded Black Ticket Films. Rintu and Sushmit describe their work as
follows: “We want to express the power we are. We tell stories of
transformation that highlight the good that exists in the world and challenge
those paradigms that stop people from achieving their full potential. We
stand for justice, dignity, and equity for every being, and we work toward
creating a thriving planet, where there is reverence for all life. We want to
produce films that show the world the pathways of transformation.”
Fallacy: Media professionals are trained and skilled. They should
always write facts from a neutral point of view. And in the world of
advertising, media professionals should be creative about
promoting the products and services for which they have been
hired, irrespective of the consequences on people or the planet.
They have to write what their audience wants! Leadership
development and universal values are irrelevant and have
nothing to do with media professionals.
New Learning: Media professional need to report facts. The
universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion are essential
for every human being to manifest their full potential, including
media professionals. What they see see influences how they
choose to lead their lives and what they report, write, and
document, which in turn shapes the mindsets of people and the
trajectory of the future of humanity and our planet.
Transformational stewardship development is of paramount
importance for media professionals too. Responsible media and
advertising is critical for a thriving humanity and planet.
Their fifty-two-minute film Writing with Fire profiles Meera. Born into
the low-caste community of Dalits or “untouchables,” Meera was kept
illiterate, married at puberty as tradition dictates and was put behind a veil.
Today, she is a journalist with India’s only newspaper run entirely by Dalit
women—Khabar Lahariya, which means “Waves of News.” Thirty-five-
year-old Meera travels the arid landscape of the state of Uttar Pradesh in
India with journalists she trains, reporting on stories that matter to her
community—issues that are typically ignored by the mainstream, English-
speaking media.Riding the smartphone boom in rural India, Meera and her reporters
decide to amplify their voice by making a radical choice—to go digital. The
women train themselves in the art of using smartphones to record,
WhatsApp to transfer, YouTube to publish, and Facebook to share their
unique stories. Despite slow data connections, expensive internet plans, and
resistance from all quarters, these women find creative solutions to their
daily problems in the hopes of building Khabar Lahariya into a strong
digital news brand. Within a single month of shifting to digital, viewership
jumped from under five thousand views per week on their YouTube channel
to over one hundred fifty thousand.
As a journalist, Meera experiences a rare sense of respect, and as an
individual, she discovers her own voice. Her purpose is clear—to highlight
the inequities that stop her community from achieving their full potential.
The film explores how despite growing up in a violent and oppressive past,
Meera and her reporters choose tools of nonviolence to stand up and
reclaim their identities as human beings. In the global context, the media is
used by the politically and economically powerful to further the interests of
a select few, creating divides. Meera and her intrepid journalists remind us
of the core tenets of journalism—to be fair, fearless, and independent.
Filmmakers Sushmit and Rintu talk about how the stewardship learning-
in-action program and our conversations deepened their narratives and how
they applied their learning to the heart of their story.
“We see what shifts women are making to manifest dignity for all: from
women being treated as commodities that can be exploited to women being
equal partners in every aspect of life; from centuries of violence—social,
cultural, economic, physical, emotional—being perpetuated on Dalit
women, to them achieving their potential, and using the nonviolent
approaches of reporting and distributing news. The women demand justice;
they participate in shifting a system and culture that strips people of their
basic rights toward a social and political system that values and protects
every single individual.”
Rintu Thomas and Sushmit Ghosh skillfully document the new emerging
narratives of radical transformers. They portray the cultural norms and
systems people are shifting. Through their visual language, they have
showcased development initiatives and responsible business as exciting
spaces charged with action that lead to tangible outcomes.
Fallacy: Artists are creative and gifted people—and many of
them have studied to develop their talents. We should not
interfere with their creativity. They mirror what the culture is.
Leadership and stewardship development has nothing to do with
them.
New Learning: Artists are like all other human beings with the
potential to discover and manifest our inner power and wisdom
along with our gifts and talents. When artists journey through
their own leadership and stewardship development based on
universal values they stand for, they see new invisible patterns
and they generate spectacular images, symbols, icons, and
metaphors for an equitable and thriving humanity and planet.
Filmmakers, musicians, singers, and media professionals all have such a
powerful influence on how we see the world and what decisions we make—
decisions that make the world a better or worse place to be in.
Marcus Bellringer is a music producer, musician, and activist, expressing
the power of music to heal, motivate, inspire, and entertain.
Marcus’s desire for equity for all human beings stretches into his activist
upbringing. His mother, Carolyn W. Bell, is a civil rights activist. When hewas young, Marcus participated in various movements for the African
American community in the Hampton Roads area of Virginia. In the early
1980s he was involved in a school busing court case when his family lived
in Norfolk, Virginia. Carolyn and Marcus became a part of a class action
lawsuit aimed at keeping the school system desegregated when the city
council was attempting to reverse the law and resegregate the school
system. “I was taught through the death threats we received that everyone
isn’t ‘clapping for you’ when you are aiming to make a difference. When
the Greekfest riots happened in Virginia Beach in 1989, I was there with my
mother helping to bring media attention to the police violence we
witnessed.”
When Marcus was student government president at a historically black
high school, the school was faced with being closed, and through the
collective actions of the students, alumni, and other community activists,
they were able to not only save the legacy of the school but implement a
strategy that resulted in a new, multimillion dollar school facility being
built.
Some of the issues Marcus worked on in the past, like racism and
exploitation of underserved communities, are still present in society. “And
my burning for justice gets renewed when I hear about or see any inequities
or devaluing of a person or community. Through the transformational
leadership learning-in-action programs, I have gained new ways to address
the issues in the African American community and in the world around
systemic racism.
“I learned that I didn’t have to create a new project or company in order
to shift systems and culture. Shifts can happen from right where I am in life.
I now work with a different intent every day in my profession as a music
producer and recording artist in the music industry. My activity inside of
my work, family, and community is designed for social shifts. Part of my
mission is to impact the systemic issues facing the African American
community using music as the platform for that transformation.”
Shelita Burke is a musician and technologist and uses these mediums to
reach humanity to cultivate a space of compassion. Shelita says, “One of
the issues I care about deeply is homelessness. I believe everyone should
have access to food and shelter. It is my dream to wake up in a world where
everyone has access to resources, and homelessness is obsolete. There is
inner greatness in all of us, and through the learning-in-action program with
Monica I learned tools that I can use to activate the space inside of us that
helps shift conversations around issues like homelessness.” To Shelita there
is no difference between corporations asking for money when fundraising
for their for-profit businesses and a homeless person asking for money for
food and housing. Shelita shifts conversations in society around
homelessness with integrity and leadership.
Rose Bartu is an artist, bringing her commitment to peace, equality, and
justice in her songs to the world, creating new narratives through her lyrics
on human rights and freedom. She sees the value of this deep work through
the conscious full-spectrum response for musicians, singers, and artists
alike to unfold the potential of the planet. Rose says, “Learning how to
create shifts for large-scale sustainable change gives me tools to heal my
heart, build resilience, speak up and out about issues I believe need to be
addressed in the world, and empower leaders around me. We are all
interdependent, and I see it as my responsibility as an artist to be the voice
of the oppressed, the disadvantaged, and the forgotten, to heal the world one
song at a time, and to empower and inspire people around the globe.”
Marcus, Shelita, and Rose are creating new narratives on social issues
through music and social entrepreneurship.Pervin Varma, the former CEO of Child Rights and You (CRY) is a
practitioner and coach as well as an accomplished singer. Pervin says, “In
2012, during one of our sessions, I discovered that I truly wish to see
healing and restoration from brokenness through song. And that propelled
me to volunteer at a senior citizen home in Bangalore and start a singing
session. I realize today that without the experience of the transformative
leadership framework—what we refer to as the conscious full-spectrum
response—I would have started just another weekly day of singing together.
But this work opened my world to seeing song as a medium for seniors who
seemed to have given up on life to rediscover new possibilities for
themselves.”
It was not about just singing or starting a choir; it was about enabling
seniors to get in touch with their inner power and gifts and recognize how
much they had to offer the world. The choir and performance is a way to
express themselves and inspire others. Music became the vehicle to
discover and act from the space of their power. From there was born the
idea of starting a choir called the Choir of the Loaves and Fish (a reference
to a Biblical story of Jesus using five loaves and two fish to feed five
thousand people). Each session, each song, each concert is a message of
hope and the endless possibilities of the human spirit.
Pervin reflects: “Personally coming to this work after almost thirty years
of work in the world of advertising and then child rights, I finally found a
framework to hold all the pieces of the social change puzzle together. I had
intuitively known that no real change is possible without change being an
inside-out process and had sought to introduce that into the organization I
had worked with for fourteen years. But I still didn’t have a framework to
connect that process with the meticulous work of design, strategy, and
delivering actual results on the ground. The conscious full-spectrum
response template not only brought these three realms together but said that
they had to be worked with simultaneously! It helped me see so clearly
what had been missing elements in the programs and strategies that had
failed or faltered. And why some approaches had worked so smoothly while
others had crashed despite having all the resources needed. It was like
finally finding the language to envision, design, and act in a holistic,
substantive, and sustainable way. And the more I engage with the process
and see it come alive, the more I discover about this work and myself. It is
learning-in-action at its very best!”
Social Media Changing Narratives
Khadija Moalla, whom you met in Chapters Five and Seven, shifted the
religious discourse in the Arab States from stigma and discrimination to
respect and inclusion of people living with HIV/AIDS by involving them
and by providing learning-in-action programs for thousands of religious and
faith-based leaders from the Muslim and Christian faiths.
Khadija and her team also shifted the media discourse by involving
hundreds of media leaders in learning-in-action programs. She used social
media to address the problem of HIV/AIDS in the Arab States. The young
bloggers generated new conversations on inclusion and acceptance. Sharif
Abdelaziz, a youth leader from a religious group who worked on Khadija’s
team, is creating new narratives, working with youth from different
religious backgrounds. He says, “From the leadership programs, I learned
that I am capable, I can succeed, I can change, and I can lead. I used all I
learned to create safe environments for people from various religious
backgrounds and political ideologies to hold respectful conversations in
order to reach a point of agreement to work on issues that matter to
everyone. I was successful in ending conflicts and creating new
partnerships among those who were considered historic enemies. I learnedhow to look at things differently, to look for solutions from within.”
Social media is being used to benefit society; but it is also being used to
mislead people. There are several news reports and research studies
unearthing millions of fake social media accounts and activity by internet
bots, which manipulate people and manufacture consensus.64 There are sites
that sell fake accounts and bots and provide services or sell software with
the same purpose. Some of these are underground, or the creators and users
unknown. Persons or organizations accessing or using these bots have the
ability to create exponential hyperactivity on social media, creating
artificial hypes and influencing opinions.
The key questions we must ask are: Is the regulatory framework strong
and robust? How much do social media companies spend on cracking down
on fake accounts and internet bot automation? Do they and governments
take action and make public the creators and users? How do we create the
norms and practices for a responsible and ethical social media business? We
need an ethical regulatory framework, given the influence and power of
social media today.
Fallacy: Social media is the new way of connection,
communication, and information. Whatever is there must be
correct, or at least have some grain of truth in it.
New Learning: There are millions of fake users on Facebook.
Misinformation and lies on social media can mislead and
manipulate us. As citizens, we must be aware and not be swayed
by artificial hypes and whatever appears on social media. We
need responsible and ethical social media business.
Leaders in Politics Generating New Narratives
Political leaders play a crucial role in enhancing the quality of life of
citizens, empowering people to articulate their interests and views and bring
about social transformation. It is imperative that we change the current
narrative that “leaders must be pragmatic because leadership based on
universal values is too idealistic”! A new understanding is emerging:
leadership based on universal values is essential for the well-being and
prosperity of people and the planet. Ethical leaders are radical systems and
cultural transformers.
Ehab El Kharrat, whom you met in Chapter Six, through sourcing what
he stands for, transcends narrow social identities in service of humanity and
creates opportunities for others to manifest their greatness.
In Egypt, Ehab El Kharrat continues to apply these approaches to
politics: he is committed to an alternative system where dignity and justice
prevail. He engaged in the two waves of the Egyptian revolution, and was
one of the five founders of the largest post-revolution party—the Egyptian
Social Democratic Party. He was elected its supreme council secretary. He
ran for parliament and won a seat to represent East and North Cairo; he was
chosen, even by his political rivals, to be the head of the Human Rights
Committee of the Shura Council (equivalent to the U.S. Senate); he was
also a member of the first drafting committee of the new constitution.
However, Ehab resigned as a member in protest of the committee’s
exclusionary and autocratic approach, and went on and used his personal
and partisan influence to write the current 2014 Egyptian constitution.
Ehab shares his journey as a political leader changing the social and
political discourse in Egypt: “In building up the Egyptian Social
Democratic Party (ESDP) I shared my insights, directly and indirectly, with
the other leaders. I effectively led a ‘revolutionary’ council comprising two
hundred men and women from different backgrounds. I facilitatedgenerative conversations based on universal values of dignity and equity,
using the many tools I learned during my work with the program to reverse
the HIV/AIDS epidemic. Sourcing ‘our oneness’ enabled me to build
bridges between rivals and opponents, the revolutionaries (rioters) and the
police, Islamists and secularists—all sides trusted me in many instances.
Although I am a Christian and a liberal, the extreme right-wing Muslim
fundamentalists were able to relate and communicate with me, at least most
of the time.
“For example, in the opening session of the Senate (Shura), the majority
were Islamists. Every time an idea was proposed, the extreme ones (the
Salafists) insisted on adding ‘only if it does not contradict Shar3 Allah (the
law of God),’ which was a violation of the protocol. The atmosphere was
electrified and tense. Throughout, I looked for ‘our oneness’ behind the
Salafists’ words. After a brief introduction I said, ‘We too, as liberals, are
committed to work without violating Shar3 Allah, because we understand
the law of God as the principles of justice, freedom, dignity to all, equity,
and peace.’ The whole crowd cheered, and a sense of relief replaced the
tension of the first session of parliament.
“Months later, both the Brotherhood and the Salafists were less happy
with me and my words. We as opposition leaders were threatened by
extremists. This was a reaction to some passionate youth who supported our
ideals, threatening that if anyone touches secular and liberal leaders, they
would make him pay. Hearing this I assumed I was one of the intended
leaders, and I gave a public call in the parliament, declaring that I am ready
to be killed but not ready for anyone to use violence to protect me. I called
for the liberal and secular youth to stick to nonviolence. Then I invited my
Islamist colleagues to give a similar call. Shouts of protest came out of the
majority seats, some denying that they ever use violence and others
threatening, ‘You have not seen our violence yet.’ I was prevented from
finishing my talk!
“This does not deter me. Bringing up these issues is vital to change
societal conversations.
“The Islamists were not happy with me when I suggested that Buddhists,
Hindus, and all people from all religions should be allowed, by constitution,
to build their temples and places of worship in Egypt. This move was
rejected, and the constitution still forbids that, but a video of me on
YouTube defending this position against a veiled lady defending the
opposite point of view had twelve thousand viewers; 95 percent of those
supported my view. The culture, at least on Facebook and YouTube, is
shifting, but in public, maybe most people are afraid to speak.
“The way to democracy is here, but there is still a long way to go.
Hundreds of thousands are shifting to a higher level of commitment to
human rights. The current constitution articulates social and economic
rights, but the road to transforming those constitutional articles to laws and
policies which are applied is still a narrow and hard path. We are currently
working with an NGO and a number of innovative campaigns to continue
the peaceful journey toward equal rights for all.”
Ehab strives to generate a world that works for everyone, beyond social
divisions and the quest for external power and control.
Another inspiring example from the political sphere: Mel Wymore,
executive director of TransPAC, an organization that is committed to full
and equal rights for transgender people. Mel stands for empowerment and
inclusion for everyone, and he participated in a series of learning-in-action
programs.
Mel ran for office as a natural progression of his work in his community,
which has always been a central part of his life. Mel designed his campaignfor New York City Council as a conscious full-spectrum project. He
identified what he stood for, the systems and cultural norms he wanted to
shift, and the measurable results he wanted to achieve. Mel and his team
developed core principles that were reflected in all of their messaging and
campaign activities. Running a principle-based campaign started to shift the
narrative around local politics.
“I feel that was a major contribution,” he says. “First, the simple shift
from running a political campaign by design rather than by reaction,
identifying the systems involved and how we might change those systems
to create fairer and more transparent representation, generated new
conversations locally. Secondly, the norms in local politics involve
patronage and political trade-offs. We broke out of those norms by focusing
our work and messaging on principles.”
The principles were inclusion and empowering of all people; an
invitation to participate; campaign money raised from committed
individuals only—no corporate funds; no quid pro quo compromises;
ongoing contribution, adding value to the community during the campaign;
democratic engagement. One of their core principles was inclusivity. “We
had volunteers from six to ninety years old, people of all abilities, races,
and economic backgrounds. We had three homeless people on staff.”
In their reporting, the media referred to the core principles and systems
Mel and his team were shifting. Community contributed during the
campaign: they created awareness and raised funds for a local park; created
environmental action forums; had housing placement for homeless
constituents; reached out to food and health programs for their community;
and advocated for traffic safety and vehicle-free zones.
Mel says, “I learned that it’s possible to ‘jump into the void’ every day, to
take on new challenges without knowing all the answers first. I learned that
I have the power to inspire people, especially when I speak from my heart. I
learned that I can rely on people to take responsibility, but that I need to
communicate clearly about expectations and accountability.
“I came to understand that everything I do or say is an opportunity to
bring awareness and shift the systems and cultural norms that adversely
affect our society and its people. The trick is to identify the leverage points
at which individual actions affect larger forces. For example, whenever I
got a chance to speak to the media, I talked about principles and system
shifts. Whenever an issue came up in the community, I spoke about it in a
larger context, to bring light to underlying factors rather than immediate
reactions. I also did this whenever I spoke to campaign volunteers and staff.
I wanted to expand consciousness about systems and cultural norms in
every dimension of the campaign.
Fallacy: Politicians have to do what is pragmatic. People are
interested in their immediate needs being met—they are not
interested is what is causing problems. They are too busy coping
with their everyday lives. To win the votes, politicians have to
“play to the gallery.”
New Learning: Politicians can be leaders and stewards who are
pragmatic and responsible and operate from universal values to
create a thriving future. They understand that the primary
purpose of a political campaign, at its best, is to transform the
political discourse for citizens to generate a prosperous,
equitable, and enduring future and mature the democratic
process.
Citizens want to know, and are fully capable of understanding,
the systems and cultural norms that give rise to problems ineveryday living as well as those that erode our dignity and
capabilities from manifesting. Citizens want to engage in making
their lives, and the lives of the next generation, better.
“I always think of context, and I always consider ways to design rather
than react. It’s not always easy to see hidden systems or identify appropriate
points of leverage, but it is usually possible to bring this consciousness to
almost any endeavor.
“We must develop our ability to create and care about our larger selves.
The tools you offer—connecting to my inner capacities and universal
values, identifying and shifting systems, articulating measurable results—
have been invaluable to me in pursuing that goal.
“After years of confusion and turmoil, I finally uncovered my desire to
transition to a more masculine expression of gender. At that time, I was
ready to dedicate my life’s work to changing the world’s notion of gender,
to ‘save’ all those oppressed by gender norms. In conversations with you,
however, I came to understand how focusing my attention solely on gender
would limit my capacity to create change. Gender is but one system that
boxes us up and creates us as ‘other.’ I have grown beyond my own
circumstances to serve a greater good.”
Mel’s campaign changed the discourse—people talked about the systems
and cultural norms they wanted to shift, and they simultaneously worked
during the campaign for their community.
Another example is Moustapha Gueye, who was a member of our team to
address the global HIV/AIDS epidemic at the United Nations Development
Programme. After Moustapha left the United Nations, he applied some of
the same praxis to politics and human development. Moustapha was elected
as the mayor of Sokone, Senegal, where he is changing the public
discourses and narratives through the media, emphasizing the need for
political engagement of both politicians and citizens, based on universal
values of dignity and equity.
Changing the narratives around politics is crucial. To generate a thriving
and prosperous future, people must understand that temporary Band-Aid
solutions will not work; it is imperative for people to make the effort to
understand the systems and cultural norms giving rise to today’s problems
and the problems the next generation will face, and ensure that universal
values underpin the policies and strategies we formulate.
Making the invisible rules of the game and their consequences visible is a
concrete step toward changing the narratives. Demystifying technical jargon
and the web of rules and regulations that lead to disparity in a world of
abundance is essential for people to participate fully, instead of engaging as
token participants.
As citizens, we have a crucial role in transforming the narratives that
maintain the disempowering status quo.
As citizens, we know the difference between information that promotes
consumerism and information that promotes our well-being and the
well-being of our family, society, and earth; we make informed
decisions, based on universal values of dignity, equity, compassion.
We have the responsibility to learn to differentiate between political
communication that is built on rhetoric and false statements “to protect
people” that lead to short-term financial benefits, violence, exclusion,
hatred, and otherness; and authentic communication that is built on
universal values for the prosperity and well-being of all people.
We consistently make the effort to check the reliability of the different
sources of information, their affiliations, and their possible vestedinterests; we use information with discernment.
We always consider the consequences of our words, our decisions, and
our actions on the well-being of the next generations.
We create new narratives for a thriving future by sharing what inspires
us. We are explicit about the universal values that underpin the story as well
as the systems and cultural norms that are being shifted. We discuss the
equitable and enduring results being generated through new narratives.
Constellations: Organizing for a Paradigm
Shift
Action at scale for a paradigm shift is inextricably linked with the way we
connect, operate, and organize institutions, projects, initiatives, and ideas.
How we function at home, at work, and in society depends upon the norms,
mechanisms, processes, relationships, and institutions we choose to put in
place. As family members, citizens, or staff in an organization, we must be
able to express our feelings, articulate our interests, exercise our rights,
mediate our differences, and solve our problems.
One of the four synergistic operational strategies of stage two is the
establishment of constellations—entities that operate from the universal
values.
When we establish entities—families, organizations, institutions,
coalitions—we have the choice to develop them as spaces and places that
nurture the full potential of people who are part of that family or entity. We
have the choice to use transformative processes in everyday life and in all
our functions, which are elaborated in Parts Two and Three of this book.
Then, we can proactively generate equitable and sustainable results based
on universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion that shift the
paradigm. These types of families and entities are the “stars” of a
constellation.
What do we mean by constellation? The Oxford English Dictionary
defines constellation as “a group of stars forming a recognizable pattern
that is traditionally named after its apparent form or identified with a
mythological figure” and as “a group of associated or similar people or
things.” In our work, constellation means entities or people that connect,
organize themselves, and operate in a particular recognizable way, based on
universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion, and establish equitable
and sustainable systems and cultural norms and proactively respond to
solve the problems facing them at any given time, which in turn impacts
humanity and the planet.
There are several ways of connecting. For example, we may wish to link
up with people, groups, or projects for particular actions, such as watching a
football game or serving in a soup kitchen. Or, we may wish to network
with a group that has similar interests, such as animal rights or pollution
free environments, where we may advocate for policy change or share best
practices on the topic. In a constellation we connect through resonance,
through an entity or family, simply because we are attuned to each other,
operating from universal values of dignity, fairness, compassion.
Creative ideas are being put forth to organize and operate as a
constellation based on universal values, for example, the transition
movement.65 This is a nascent, exciting new field, in which we live and
work in a manner congruent with who we are, what we stand for, with
systems and norms that are life-enhancing, individually and collectively.
A constellation, which is the way we connect, operate, and organize, has
some unique features.
Every entity and person in the constellation is the center of theconstellation, and, at the same time, is the periphery as well as the field.
Each entity and person is unique and operates independently, and, at the
same time, is resonant and interdependent with other entities, people,
and the planet. They are all committed to generating a thriving humanity
and planet, shifting dysfunctional systems and cultural norms through
an architecture that sources the universal values of dignity, fairness, and
compassion for action. They are part of the same “thrive-system” of
life!
Each entity and person is a source of inspiration for others to commit to
action. They are self-inspired, experience the joy of BEING in action,
enjoy prosperity with equitable and sustainable results, and are able to
persist and keep up the momentum!
The entities and people in the constellation are awake and aware of our
inherent oneness. They are aware that the mindset that emanates from
our oneness generates worldviews that transcend otherness and
conventional us-them dichotomies, and the isms we have created.
Entities and people in a constellation self-organize, without external
pressures. They establish ways to notice the integrity of their strategies
and actions and have self-correcting mechanisms.
The pattern of the each entity or person in the constellation is a fractal
that repeats itself. When the pattern is repeated in its unique and
numerous applications, it generates a new field for a paradigm shift.
A constellation operates in a distinct way. We have explored these
through real-life examples in this book. Here are some features of how a
constellation operates:
Transformational stewards, leaders, and managers operate the entities in
a constellation. They are individually and collectively paradigm shifters
who establish equity and dignity for the well-being of humanity and a
thriving planet, making this normative through results.
In the everyday work of the entities and people, they use radical
transformative processes based on universal values. Every action at
home and at work is an opportunity to manifest one’s inner capacities
for self-expression.
Entities and people engage in learning with the mindset and philosophy
of enhancing one’s inner and outer development, growth, and evolution
through life’s journey by BEING in action. Ways to notice growth,
development, and contribution and systematically process one’s own
learning with peers creates a culture of continuous lifelong learning,
openness, and receptivity.
Each entity and person operates as a radically transformative unit. When
we discussed mindful implementation in Chapter Seven, one of the
frameworks for developing and noticing our stages of leadership was
based on the work of Likert-Emberling. The same principles apply to
developing radically transformative entities or organizations. In each of
the five stages of this inseparable continuum, an entity can be organized
to develop as a paradigm-shifting venture and operate in a wholesome
and healthy way based on universal values of dignity, equity,
compassion.
Radical evolutionary design is a core component of the way entities and
people operate in a constellation. In Chapter Six, we discussed the
unique capabilities of a unifying architect and the design templates that
forge the schema for transformational results.Monica Martinez, executive director of Encompass in Santa Cruz
County, California, shares her learning: “I have become increasingly aware
of the way that many well-intended efforts are frequently misaligned with
each other, wasting energy and competing for resources. We all want many
of the same things for our communities; we want equity, dignity, and well-
being for ourselves and others. I now source my efforts from this place,
rather than the bureaucratic systems that I have been trained to navigate.
This motivates me to create new rules to the old game and to do things
differently. I am now working to create alignment among the partners in my
constellation, and I am seeing the transformative results.”
Tremendous energy and commitment for action is generated by inside-
out leadership, working from who we are and what we deeply care about.
When we combine this energy and commitment for action with
decentralized entities and peer support in a constellation, instead of top-
down leadership and rigid hierarchies, the transformation is unstoppable.
Here is an example of a constellation.
Auroville is located in Tamil Nadu, India, and is a universal township
with residents from fifty-five countries. The architecture of Auroville was
designed as a constellation of different entities and people based on the
same purpose and values—residential communities, businesses, services,
farms, and forests, which are largely self-organized. There is no
institutional head or CEO. Stewardship is valued.
The philosophies of Sri Aurobindo and The Mother (Mirra Alfassa) guide
Auroville. The purpose of Auroville is to realize human unity; the Dream is
inspiring: “There should be somewhere on earth a place which no nation
could claim as its own, where all human beings of goodwill who have a
sincere aspiration could live freely as citizens of the world and obey one
single authority, that of the supreme Truth; a place of peace, concord and
harmony, where all the fighting instincts of man would be used exclusively
to conquer the causes of his suffering and miseries … a place where the
needs of the spirit and the concern for progress would take precedence over
the satisfaction of desires and passions, the search for material enjoyment.”
Fallacy: If we encourage and establish
independent/interdependent entities and allow people to self-
organize, there will be chaos, and people will not know what to
do. There will be no order; everything will be out of control.
People always act out of self-interest, so it will be harmful for
society.
New Learning: When people embody and act from the universal
values of dignity, fairness, and compassion, they engage in what
they care deeply about, self-organize, self-correct, and operate
as independent yet interdependent entities. Diversity and
pluralism thrive for the enhancement and joy of all in society.
Auroville was designed by The Mother as a constellation. Aurovilians
live and work independently and at the same time within the interdependent
fabric of values and a few norms and participation in community matters.
People from different cultures, professions, and fifty-five nationalities are in
Auroville.66
Sanjeev Aggarwal sees the power of spaces that foster progress
emanating from our inherent oneness, where sourcing one’s inner capacity
and wisdom for action in the world is valued. Self-organization is key.
Sanjeev reflects: “A first requirement for being in here is to find one’s inner
BEING and wisdom and being in contact with it and learning to hear its
voice and follow its guidance. The specific guidance for building Auroville
is very minimal. We carve a path for ourselves in our work, mostly workingthis out alone, without being influenced by others, without discussion,
without any advice of more experienced colleagues. We work on projects
that call us to act, sometimes as individuals while others work as
collaborators of different initiatives. The principal aim in the doing of these
works is to raise oneself to the highest consciousness possible and also to
infuse the work one does with a higher consciousness.”
Sanjeev continues to be an encouraging presence. He sees the value of
practice and change of consciousness as distinct from a purely theoretical
understanding of concepts. He says, “In the stewardship learning-in-action
programs, through peer learning groups, we periodically review one’s
practice as well as the change in oneself, with others. For many of us it was
the first time that we shared with others our aspirations and the difficulties
that we faced in manifesting them.”
Over four hundred people from Auroville and the villages in the
bioregion chose to participate in the stewardship learning-in-action program
over four years, generating numerous initiatives. I am sharing just a few
examples to illustrate some features of a constellation, and citing what this
work means to people. All of them source oneness for strategic action, are
independent and interdependent at the same time, manifest results, and self-
organize. They operate with freedom, based on universal values, and engage
in different areas depending on their interest—such as socially conscious
business, services, governance, measurement and tracking, generating and
documenting new narratives, and enhancing capabilities in people, teams,
and entities.
Socially and Environmentally Conscious Businesses and Initiatives: You
met Martin Scherfler, who stewards the socially conscious Auroville
Collaborative, a consulting group, and the Auroville Green Practices
Summer School; Sanjeev Ranganathan, from Aura Auro and STEM Land;
Helena Becker, working in Sante, a health care facility, and other entities, to
manifest joy at work; and Uma Haimavati, from Upasana.
Let me introduce you to Margarita Correa, the cofounder of a socially
and environmentally conscious business, MGEcoduties, with the vision of
offering a new concept of environmentally friendly products that are made
with natural ingredients, using probiotics. Their work is game-changing,
what the team calls “An Earth-Saving Revolution.” They are changing the
mindset of consumers whose uninformed actions fill our planet with trash
and pollution. “With the help of probiotics, we have taken the challenge to
change that mindset,” says Margarita.
Margarita was born in Colombia and trained as a chemical engineer. She
was subjected to torture and extreme hardship in captivity at the hands of
guerrilla forces in Colombia. Later, after her rescue, she worked as a senior
executive in a multinational company, and then with microorganisms.
Margarita shares, “Through the stewardship learning-in-action programs,
I design differently, using a conscious full-spectrum approach; I transform
breakdowns into breakthroughs in my work; I foster stewardship
competencies and inner capacities for sustainable and equitable
transformational change, and I am BEING a paradigm shifter/maker.
Recently, when my country, Colombia, signed a peace deal with the
guerrillas, Colombians were asked to vote in a referendum for a yes or no to
that peace deal. If it was not for my learning, insights, and practice in the
stewardship programs, I would have voted for the no, as my scars would not
have allowed me to forgive and move on! The fact that I voted for the yes is
because I have been touched with compassion and endless love.”
Geert Tomassen (Tomas) is the steward of Annapurna Farms, the largest
certified organic farm in the region near Auroville. Tomas reflects:
“Through my inner journey, I always looked at people as souls on a journey,so not so much as someone as a good/bad character but looking beyond
that. Through the work in the stewardship program, I feel I have been given
a handle to actually practice this.”
Deoyani Sarkhot, an environmental scientist, is changing conversations
through her work on bringing affordable technologies to schools and school
children, women, and farmers. She reflects: “My journey is aligning
everything I do with my core values. The same space of possibility lies in
everyone, and I am learning how to invoke that space.”
Changing Culture by Generating New Conversations: Jaya Berggreen-
Clausen opens doors for partnering, and the spaces she opens are beyond
exclusionary culture, nationality, creed, or caste. “They are about who we
are and deeply care about and where we want to go as a species. It is about
our highest dreams as reality. It is about transcending the fears which hold
us back, not just as individuals, but in the interactions with each other at all
levels, and the divisive forces based on religion, race, nationality, etc. It is
about BEING and living as global citizens.” For example, she designed and
implemented a workshop on Sourcing our Oneness for university students
from Rwanda who study in Chidambaram in India. It was a step toward
building the resilience of students who had lived through dark times in their
lives during the genocide. Jaya reflects: “The Stewardship program and the
approach, tools, and template are related and part of the same weave of
manifesting our higher consciousness in action. No wonder it is so exiting,
no wonder things fall in place!”
Minhaj Ameen engages to transform governance through new
conversations, and is clear that a critical aspect is to foster integrity, where
connecting with people on universal values and understanding other
perspectives is crucial. Min says, “Failure while experimenting is nothing to
be ashamed of—it is simply part of engaging and trying out new ideas. This
understanding is very empowering.” Mita Dasgupta uses transformative
practices in working groups to generate the cultural shifts that people in
Auroville wish to see.
Bindu Mohanty sees, shares, and often writes about emerging,
empowering narratives that shift culture toward wholeness. Bindu reflects:
“It is about fostering meditation in action so that we can listen deeply to
others, speak effectively, and most importantly, generate new conversations
for action. It will not be easy, but finally, after many, many years, I am
encouraged to dream again. What is demanded of us is an unwavering
commitment to always act from our stand—from our values—and not just
mechanically apply the tools we learn or mime a newly developed
vocabulary.”
Exploring New Futures with Neighboring Entities: Bridget Horkan works
in villages in Tamil Nadu, India, focusing on health, healing, and education.
Bridget and the Thamarai team address alcoholism, which is increasingly a
health and social issue in India and in the villages in Tamil Nadu. Bridget
