used the transformational design templates, sourcing universal values to
shift cultural norms and systems. The entire program is conducted in Tamil,
with handouts in Tamil. Those affected negatively by alcoholism—families
and substance abusers—access their inner wisdom, what they stand for, to
challenge substance abuse, stigma, and cultural norms associated with
alcoholism. It was not easy in the beginning, and members of the
community did not want to break the denial and silence. Bridget and her
colleagues skilfully enrolled elders and youth and partnered with experts, as
well as Yatra, a Tamil theater group.
Yatra scripted and put on plays that opened spaces for reflection in the
audience, based on real-life situations. For example, a boy in one play
talked about how he missed his father’s presence at parent-teacher meetingsand school events—his father never showed up because he was drunk.
Seeing and hearing this, men in the audience ruminated about the
consequences of alcoholism in their families.
Yatra Srinivasan stewards the Tamil theater group Yatra (which means
“journey”). He and his team members participated in the stewardship
learning in-action sessions. He creates plays that communicate effectively
to people in the village and brilliantly integrates the inner spaces of wisdom
and universal values, simultaneously inviting people to have the courage to
change cultural norms, including violence against women and girls, and to
BE in action.
Bridget grew up on the west coast of Ireland. A formative turning point in
her life was an interview she heard on the radio about the deaths and mass
rapes of up to one million people in the hundred-day Rwandan genocide.
She subsequently went to Rwanda as an aid worker, and this experience
shook her to the core and cracked open an intense period of questioning
both world order and her own internal conditioned patterning and reactions.
Bridget says, “I have been deeply touched—Rwandan Peter’s sharing
when he realized the potential of this work for transforming the memory of
the genocide buried deep in his body. It made me reflect on the conditioning
I also carry, to forgive without condoning unacceptable actions, and to let
go of what does not serve me anymore. I love the way this work is
inclusive; it cuts hierarchy, and it is embracing. Auroville and the
surrounding villages can hold very different perspectives. During
Stewardship for New Emergence programs, I experience unity and a
collective learning field that feels precious and full of possibility. I feel it is
a very important forum for collective planning the future together and loved
to hear the sharing of our village neighbors who joined us for the learning-
in-action trainings.”
During one of the learning-in-action sessions, exploring “wild, out-of-
the-box ideas,” Kyoung Hyoun came up with the idea of designing and
implementing a learning-in-action program for the workers in Auroville,
most of whom live in neighboring villages. It blossomed into a powerful
initiative, Developing Stewardship Capacity for Workers in Auroville.
The transformation is inspiring. Women and men, many with little formal
education, learning to manifest their greatness with the same tools and
techniques as faculty in world-renowned universities! This is a testament to
the beauty and power of the human spirit. The participants immediately put
into practice what they learned right away.
A woman in the program said that it was the first time in her life anyone
asked her what she cared about and valued what she said. Another woman
said that she had not spoken to her sister-in-law for five years; she was able
to see that her interpretation of what happened could be different from what
actually happened; she could see the incongruence between what she stands
for—equity and dignity—and she decided to break the silence. Several
participants said they learned to overcome their fear of speaking in public.
These entities and people in the magnificent architecture of constellations
all self-organize and operate in an interdependent way—they are all diverse
but interconnected, working for human unity in different ways based on our
innate oneness. And all are on journeys in which there is more to learn,
design, implement, and transform. This is work in progress.
In Chapter Eleven, we will see how to generate resonance, alignment,
and synergy between the profusion of projects, initiatives, entities, and
organizations for equitable and sustainable outcomes and impacts.
Chapter 11
Transformative Opportunities
Attuning and Aligning for RadicalTransformation
If you have come here to help me, you are wasting your time. But if you
have come because your liberation is bound up with mine, then let us
work together.
LILLA WATSON AND INDIGENOUS AUSTRALIANS
Radical transformation—working with the root factors to transform current
realities that do not work for everyone—requires us to source the universal
values of dignity, fairness, and compassion for strategic design, action, and
results. We work in partnership at home, in society, and in our workplace.
Aligning with partners generates synergy, in which the result generated by
each person or group is far more than the sum of the individuals’ or groups’
contribution. BEING attuned with like-spirited partners generates
resonance. Partners design and act from the same value-based foundation,
though the strategies and tactics may differ.
New pathways for transformative results show up when we nuance the
different expressions of alignment and attunement. In this chapter, we
explore these expressions and the results generated through
transdisciplinary initiatives—whole-systems transformation that shifts
unworkable systems and cultural norms. We overcome workplace silos and
fragmentation, leverage radical transformation, and review unprecedented
openings.
Transdisciplinary Initiatives Transforming
Learning
To address complex problems we need to partner with people who are from
different disciplines. Let’s take climate change—we need experts, leaders,
policy-makers, and program managers in finance, agriculture, industry,
transportation, urban planning, forestry, energy, and media. Civil society,
government, business, and human development specialists are needed
around the table.
For clarity and ease in application, I differentiate three approaches in
working together with different disciplines.
First, in interdisciplinary work, we interact to share knowledge through
the frameworks and theories of different branches of knowledge, adding
information, but not necessarily working together to solve a problem. For
example, professionals who are working independently on climate change
may meet in order to learn about what steps others are taking.
Second, multidisciplinary work implies working on a common goal, such
as reducing carbon emissions, using knowledge and techniques from
different disciplines in a coherent manner.
Third, the most exciting, transdisciplinary work crosses discipline
boundaries to take a holistic approach, in which different experts from
different disciplines and professionals create new knowledge and
understanding, always informed by universal values of dignity, equity, and
compassion—our resonant space of oneness.
The definitions I am using for interdisciplinary, multidisciplinary, and
transdisciplinary work are used to generate value-based alternatives; this
differs from conventional definitions in research. As the world’s knowledge
economy expands, transdisciplinary work is an important area for us to
explore, whether in community circles, study groups, or academia, or as we
work on projects and programs.
Here are examples of noteworthy transdisciplinary initiatives—the
Bailey Bridge Initiative (BBI) and a similar initiative at the Centre for
Disability Studies and Action (CDSA) at the Tata Institute of Social
Sciences in Mumbai (TISS). These are transformative learning-in-action
programs, synthesizing science and technology and social work, as well as
leadership skills, competencies, and inner capacities.The purpose of BBI is to foster agency in interested individuals and the
communities they engage with. The participants were trained and equipped
with relevant science and technological tools and techniques for informed
decision-making in social interventions.
The design team—Muthu Kumaran, Adya Siddharth, and Vivek Coelho
—identified the skills, competencies, and inner capacities for learning and
skillfully integrated exercises to foster the agency of each participant and
the community members they serve. They demystified science and
technological concepts. Participants applied the mapping tools and
techniques—cartography, geographical information systems, spatial
visualization, and reasoning—in field interventions. They used the CFSR
approach, and named their template the Spectral Framework for Agency
Building—SFAB. I encourage people who aim to generate equitable and
sustainable results to use the CFSR approach in the specific context of their
work, and name it accordingly.
Participants changed the way they work in communities. They saw how
these mapping tools and techniques significantly enhanced their work in the
field. Students formulated their projects on the Spectral Framework for
Agency Building worksheet, and said that this provided clarity and made
visible the systems and cultural shifts needed. Participants learned and
integrated the agency building components in the workshops they designed
for people in the village. They fostered agency in people in the community,
and rather than doing work for them, they encouraged individuals to source
their inner capacities for action. Men and women from the village took the
initiative and worked with government officials to resolve their issues.
Let me introduce the main architects of the Bailey Bridge Initiative:
Muthu Kumaran, Adya Siddarth, and Vivek Coelho.
Muthu Kumaran, an engineer, enhances students’ ability to design and
implement their own breakthrough initiatives as well as foster the agency of
people in the community. Muthu fosters their growth and notices how
students shift from being knowledge-centric to using knowledge-based
action.
Muthu is on a continuous learning journey himself. After the Bailey
Bridge summer school experience he reflected: “From the days of
conceptualizing, designing, and conducting the workshop I stood by my
values of agency, dignity, and empathy all throughout. Sometimes in the
tightness of time and work I was frustrated, but I got back into the flow by
continuously reminding myself of my stand—it really helped me to deliver
what I intended to. This was evident from the interest shown by participants
who worked late after the sessions and designed their breakthrough
initiatives for larger outcomes and impact. And most importantly, they were
able to see that technology is just a tool which is value-neutral; and that it is
the person using it, based on their values and purpose, who can make the
technology generate its full potential for social transformation. Some
participants said it was one of the best workshops they ever attended
because it was unique in integrating agency-building praxes along with
technological mapping skills and practical work in social sciences.”
Adya Siddarth made a conscious career shift from information
technology to social sciences, often wondering if it was a big mistake. Adya
says, “A key difference in my life is the confidence with which I stand
steady and walk the path of the road less traveled. There was always a voice
in me that said, ‘I care for the society and the environment … and I want to
do something about it.’ This work has allowed me to articulate what I stand
for, what my purpose in life is, and what I can contribute to society. That by
itself has been a huge shift. I no longer need assurance from another person
that I am doing the ‘right’ thing. Now, I know who I am and how I canmake a difference in this world.”
At the end of the Bailey Bridge summer school, Adya noted, “I stand for
compassion and justice. I was able to embody what I stand for during the
sessions by being fully present, observing, feeling the pulse of the
participants. I learned that sourcing inner power for myself and that of
others lies in my BEING. When I am grounded in the space of my wisdom
and work from compassion, people can see who I am BEING, and they
open up like flowers blossoming. I learned the importance of rigor and
attention to detail even in the smallest of tasks.”
Vivek Coelho stands for integrity, justice, and equity. He develops
community beach monitoring and restoration systems and is establishing
community disaster risk reduction cells. After the Bailey Bridge summer
school, Vivek shared, “I learned that when I live my stand, I am open to
learn and create an enabling environment for others to learn. Participants
were able to notice their own shift from output-driven initiatives to
outcome- and impact-driven initiatives. Templates such as the conscious
full-spectrum response and the transformational results chain were useful to
design and implement with clarity. They noticed the difference between
partial and full-spectrum responses and crafted well-informed strategies
based on universal values.”
The Bailey Bridge Initiative is an excellent example of professionals
designing a transdisciplinary initiative. One participant said although she
belonged to a “tech” discipline, she did not realize that these skill sets can
be used in a value-based, constructive manner for addressing social issues.
The Spectral Framework for Agency Building (SFAB) was instrumental in
bridging the technical and discipline-based skills of engineering,
information technology, social sciences, and leadership development, where
universal values and agency building transcend the boundaries of each
discipline and the social interventions carry the shifts needed in systems and
cultural norms. This is what the Bailey Bridge Initiative envisioned and
what it stands for.
Vivek Coelho organized the event “A Tide Turns” in 2017 with Legal Aid
to Women (LAW) Trust and Social Need Education and Human Awareness
(SNEHA). Members of the fishing communities from Tamil Nadu and
Puducherry spoke at the event. They shared what they cared deeply about
and the values they stood for, described beach profiling in the context of
restoring the ecosystem, and inspired the audience with their commitment
and confidence. In this transdisciplinary program at the community level,
people from fishing communities learn how to enhance their leadership
through transformative learning-in-action programs in Tamil.
Whole-Systems Transformation
Shifting systems and norms to generate results leads to whole-systems
transformation.
Fallacy: Each discipline, such as engineering, information
technology, social sciences, and medicine, requires that we
specialize and advance so that we can create more innovations.
It is very difficult to work in a multidisciplinary way because our
frameworks are so different.
New Learning: The conscious full-spectrum response template
provides a transdisciplinary framework for multiple disciplines to
use their technical expertise to solve problems; synergize and
contribute by shifting systems and norms together; and transcend
differences by sourcing actions and strategies from universal
values.In this rapidly changing world, we can generate a paradigm shift through
whole-systems change, building on the inner power of human wisdom and
personal transformation. We attune and align for whole-systems
transformation at home, in our workplace, and in society. In the broadest
sense, BEING aligned and attuned within also applies to us as individuals.
Our daughter-in-law, Anindita, a talented business executive, focuses on
developing the full potential of managers and staff. Her respect for diversity
expresses itself in different spheres and is aligned with her inclusive
worldview. She cares for people, the world of animals, and our earth.
Anindita organized a family vacation in Thailand, and I was inspired by the
stories our grandchildren shared about going to an elephant rescue
sanctuary. Anindita’s exploration of cultural diversity, food, art, and music
informs her creativity. Her professionalism and global perspectives
combined with local sensitivity are valued in her workplace. Both her
personal and professional life are aligned and based on the same core
values.
Let me return to the work setting. In the different programs we designed
and implemented, we worked with partners from all walks of life. We
designed processes to align our efforts in order to establish synergy; and we
worked in attunement from universal values of dignity and respect, fairness
and equity. The leadership and stewardship learning-in-action programs
foster alignment and connect through attunement grounded in human
dignity, equity, and compassion.
To reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic, we had projects to increase treatment,
promote prevention, foster leadership at national and local levels, involve
the media, expand education, and monitor and evaluate the epidemic. Based
on the universal values of dignity and respect, fairness and equity, we
articulated the systems and cultural shifts that aligned the projects and
generated synergy. We agreed on changing the cultural norms that
stigmatized people living with HIV/AIDS in families and communities to
norms that are inclusive and respectful, and altering cultural norms that did
not uphold the responsibility of individuals to have safe sex. We agreed to
change the way systems functioned in order to provide services without
discrimination, and to shift the system for equitable access to medication.
This involved shifting from the profit-driven market determining access to
treatment by unaffordable pricing to more equitable pricing and profits. The
results described earlier in this book are noteworthy.
An example of alignment and attunement: to address climate change in
Egypt through the United Nations, a transformational leadership program
was designed to develop the capacity of people and experts to mobilize
sustained and effective action. Decision-makers from different sectoral
ministries, scientists, experts, and representatives of civil society
participated in this results-oriented program. The three most important
climate change issues in Egypt were related to water resources, coastal
zones, and agriculture. They were concerned about energy efficiency,
renewable energy, and clean development without pollution.
The approach and practices of the leadership learning-in-action programs
overcomes fragmentation and leads to synthesis and synergy. In Egypt,
seventy-five participants were engaged in over 125 projects from different
sectors. Each participant mapped every project on the conscious full-
spectrum framework (see Figure 6-3). They mapped the following:
The inner universal values—what each participant stands for. The very
foundation of all strategies and actions that create equitable and
sustainable change is the sourcing of one’s wisdom, one’s inner
capacity. The participants recognized each other’s potential contribution
in a different and deeper way, and this was a new foundation in theirrelationships.
The core values of the project—equity and sustainability. The National
Communication Reports in Egypt documented the climate change issues
and stated that equity and sustainability are the foundation of strategic
action.
The measurable deliverables—what activities were required to solve the
problem? As expected, there were hundreds of activities. To name a few,
participants from civil society were working on educating communities
and water conservation; the transport sector was looking at ways to
increase the energy efficiency of transport; the agriculture department
looked at cropping under conditions of drought as well as sourcing
renewable energy.
The systems and norms that needed to shift to make the solution
sustainable and equitable. These were shared first within smaller
groups, and then with the entire group. The result was alignment—and
all the participants could see that.
Eighty percent of the ideas for shifting systems and norms were about six
similar issues! These ideas included removing import taxes for equipment,
as well as the parts for equipment that were to be used for renewable energy
and clean energy; stopping indirect subsidies to fossil fuel–based goods and
equipment; levying import taxes on goods and equipment that use fossil
fuel directly or indirectly; providing incentives for local initiatives that
promote or use renewable energy as well as energy-efficient and clean
development; revising community education programs by demystifying
science and technology, stimulating local action, and expanding the reach
and making information and education relevant to local situations; and
revamping water distribution and conservation to make it equitable.
People know what needs to shift—and when we provide safe spaces for
critical thinking, they come up with informed and effective ideas. And when
the participants in Egypt’s climate change discussions became aware of the
alignment in the shifts needed in systems and cultural norms for their
different projects to be sustainable, they could see the interdependence.
They could see that when each project was successful, it created the
conditions for others to succeed. Partnering for synergistic action and
results is both exciting and game-changing. Through their learning in the
leadership program, the participants created a new foundation for equitable
and sustainable change.
When we initiated the first projects at TISS, the team I worked with,
including Gomathy Balasubramanian, an assistant professor at TISS, chose
three themes: pluralism and democratic decision-making, sustainable
commons, and economic sufficiency. We articulated the systems and
cultural shifts, which became the aligning context for all initiatives.
For pluralism and democratic decision-making, the focus was on shifting
individuals and societies from inequity because of their identities to
individuals and democratic societies that celebrate pluralism and foster self-
determination, participation, and results-based action.
Sustainable commons requires a shift from the privatization and
government control of knowledge and the environment to enabling open-
source knowledge repositories, community resource banks, and
mechanisms for sustainable community stewardship of commons and
regeneration of the environment.
Economic sufficiency requires a shift from the commodification of
human beings and depriving them access to resources essential for life and
livelihoods to honoring the human spirit and life, valuing labor, ensuring
fair wages, and providing social security and access to capital and markets.Gomathy shares, “The first thing I do differently in any engagement now
is to work from all three areas: inner values, critical systems thinking, and
results-based conversations. In the past, these were often held in a broken
way, with one becoming more important than the other. Now I habitually
examine all my current engagements from this holistic lens.
“I have been part of the radical feminist movement for many years
because of my firm commitment to justice. I also explored the nature of
self, communities, and organizations, through various philosophies and
theories, such as management, organizational behavior. In these spaces, I
found that there was no reflexivity on structural inequalities such as gender,
caste, class, and how they determine human behavior. In the last five years
of work in TISS using these transformative methods, I have been able to
bring together these various experiences into a larger whole, to articulate
my sense of justice not as per what a given ideology says, but as an
embodied self, confronting complex realities. Also, this embodiment is no
longer limited to conversations but in concrete action and engagements. In
that sense, being just for me has become a lived adventure rather than a set
of critical internal and external norms.”
The Importance of Language
The tools, techniques, and approaches of the stewardship and leadership
programs are profound and often easier to grasp in one’s mother tongue.
The TISS team generated results in the town of Nagapattinam with
community members because we translated all the materials of the
stewardship programs into Tamil. In Auroville, all the stewardship
programs were bilingual—English and Tamil. The translation team was
such fun to work with—Srilatha, Suriyaprakash, Muthu, and Adya! We
worked many hours every evening after the days’ sessions, and their
continuous humor, laughter, and banter kept the energy flowing. In this
work, translation cannot be literal—it must be rigorous and convey the
meaning of the English words and phrases. Translating it back into English
and checking that with me, pretesting it in the field, using new words to
communicate new insights, finding or creating new words to move beyond
religious interpretations, were all part of the process.
Suriyaprakash C. is a professor at the Jansons School of Business. He
says, “The stewardship tools provide me a new paradigm to approach my
work as a teacher, trainer, facilitator. The centrality of value-based design of
projects has propelled me to design my organization development programs
to create tangible outcomes. I extensively use the conscious full-spectrum
design framework, which I call the Integrated OD Framework, and the
transformational results chain with my organizational trainees. In turn they
design their own breakthrough initiatives using the templates. This is a
practical and effective full-spectrum approach to organizational change.”
Because we translated our work into Tamil, it was possible to work along
the Nagapattinam coast in Tamil Nadu, India, with Legal Aid to Women
(LAW) Trust and Social Need Education and Human Awareness (SNEHA).
They have been instrumental in mobilizing communities on livelihoods,
disaster response, human rights of women, children, and marginalized
communities, health, and strengthening of community-based organizations.
One of the biggest struggles has been their campaign toward securing the
rights of traditional fishing communities over coastal resources. Dignity,
human rights, and living in harmony with nature are their core values. They
work to establish a just society that is free of discrimination based on caste,
class, religion, gender, language, and age through social transformation with
people’s participation.
A. Gandhimathi, director of LAW Trust, is unstoppable. She stands for
inner freedom for herself and others and manifests this through her work byreaching out to the most marginalized communities in the coastal districts
of Tamil Nadu. She is committed, burns for justice, and acts. Resources
may be minimal, and it may not seem possible, but that does not stop her
from persevering and finding other solutions for justice and well-being in
communities. Gandhimathi says, “My freedom lies in the freedom of
others.”
Jesu Rethinam, director of SNEHA, is an embodiment of humility and
courage working with and for marginalized and affected communities along
the coast of Tamil Nadu. She is a trusted and respected person in the
community, based on her commitment, results, and passion. She is always
ready to do more, learn more, and keep up to date with what is needed for
herself and her team to deliver results.
In many countries where we worked, we painstakingly translated this
work—for example, we translated it into Arabic with Ehab and Heba.
Pinaki Roy says, “The most important opportunity that emerged since I
joined the Stewardship for New Emergence journey is translating the tools
into Bengali. This is an extremely important step because it becomes
accessible to members of marginalized and rural communities in Bengal;
gives them tools and processes to consciously participate and decide on
transformational development priorities for themselves; and allows them to
be active agents of change.” Pinaki was working in villages in the
Sundarbans in West Bengal—which has the world’s largest mangrove
forest.
Fallacy: Whole-systems transformation is a complex process
that experts and systems analysts have to work on.
New Learning: When we articulate the systems and cultural
shifts needed for problems to be solved in an equitable and
sustainable way, and align our work toward accomplishing these
shifts based on universal values, we are putting the pieces of the
puzzle together for whole-systems transformation and results.
Overcoming Silos and Fragmentation in the
Workplace
Most managers, leaders, and CEOs want to break down silos and foster
teamwork and synergy. To overcome silos and fragmentation in the
workplace, staff must be able to see how their work contributes to the
organization and how their activities can shape the systems and culture of
their work environment. Using the conscious full-spectrum template is a
powerful way of breaking down silos and fragmentation in the workplace.
Within and between organizations, coalitions, or any entity, we need
internal and external alignment and attunement.
Aligning my projects for synergistic outcomes and impact. Most of us
work on more than one project in our workplace. When I revisit project
designs and clearly articulate the systems and cultural shifts based on
universal values, I see the alignment, synergy, interdependence; how
progress in one project affects the others.
Aligning initiatives within units of a department or entity. In large
organizations, there are departments that employ many people, with each
person designing and implementing several projects. People and their
projects are often organized in units. We can create synergies and greater
impacts and outcomes between the projects by designing and working on
the systems and cultural shifts related to the different specific outputs and
activities of each project. And equally important, we foster teamwork and
effective communication between people in each department because they
explicitly see the interdependence between their projects. This is always
exciting for staff—they can see that they are part of the whole interrelatedsystem.
Aligning between departments or entities. Most senior leaders and CEOs
endeavor to break down silos between departments. These silos surface
because the functions of the department and accountabilities of staff are
often perceived as exclusive and based on unique expertise. Funding
mechanisms reinforce the silos and set up competition, particularly where
financial resources are limited. When staff, managers, and leaders in an
entity connect with who they are BEING and what they stand for, they see
the mission and core universal values of the organization beyond the blind
compliance and intellectual understanding of a mission statement—they are
attuned to the universal values and act from that space. And when staff from
different departments work together through a process where they articulate
the systems and cultural shifts they wish to see, they align, create synergies
and far greater impacts and outcomes. Through their activities and
initiatives, they manifest wonderful, creative moments that shift the culture
of silos in real time.
Aligning between programs and operations. In almost all organizations
and entities, the staff that work on programs (such as livelihoods, health,
education) operate from departments that are different from staff who work
in operations (such as accounting, funding, contracting, human resources).
There is often a sense of “us versus them” resulting in chatter and judgment
about “the other.” Program staff may say, “Senior management, accounting,
human resources, and administration sit in air-conditioned offices and have
no idea of the ground realities and what we do. We are the ones working
with people and have a better idea.” The operations groups may say,
“Program staff and managers get defensive when people in accounting,
human resources, and administration ask questions for clarity and think that
their integrity is being questioned.”
When program and operations staff, managers, and leaders in an entity
work together in the learning-in-action programs, they discover themselves
and each other in a deeper way, building a new foundation of respect and
appreciation, attuned through resonance with universal values. And when
they articulate the shifts in the systems and culture of the organization
together, they align and create synergies through their actions and
initiatives. The communication between people in operations and programs
is more authentic, a new acknowledgment of the others’ work emerges, and
day-to-day problems are easily resolved.
Participating in learning-in-action programs and BEING in practice saves
time in the management of an organization because of alignment and
attunement.
Here is an example of practice leading to alignment and attunement.
Leigh Guerrero is a leader and manager in an organization. She says, “I
inherited staff that were not working together and arguing about the ‘right’
way to accomplish serving our clients. Through individual and team
conversations providing feedback on how to deeply listen to each other, and
how to begin team discussions with the shared universal values of dignity
and equity that underpin the service to our clients and community, staff
were able to see the commitment in their fellow team members and began
to work together to find solutions.”
Aligning with partners, citizens, and grassroots organizations. No matter
what the theme—ending homelessness, reducing HIV infections, reducing
youth violence, conserving the environment, reducing crime—it is
articulating and acting upon the systems and cultural shifts that provides the
direction and results for equitable and sustainable change. The strategies
and activities may differ with different citizen groups and grassroots
organizations. Even though groups might work in the same community withthe universal values of dignity and equity, their actions can seem disparate.
Too often we say that there is fragmentation because we see only the
trees and not the forest! Or we say that too many entities or projects are
working on the same issue, duplicating efforts; yet the services and support
mechanisms for those in need remain inadequate. Fragmentation and
duplication are not the problem—the issue is that we often do not know
how to create synergy and resonance. When the public, citizens groups, and
grassroots organizations work together with government, businesses, the
media, and nongovernmental entities through a process in which they
articulate the systems and cultural shifts they wish to see, they align and
create synergies, and greater impacts and outcomes.
In the different programs through the United Nations—humanitarian
work in conflict areas, environmental issues, addressing HIV/AIDS,
reducing maternal mortality, increasing child survival, realizing human
rights—the role of each agency is different, based on the mandate.
Articulating and acting upon the systems and cultural shifts required with
partners and stakeholders provides the necessary direction for achieving
equitable and sustainable results.
Robyn McKeen is a manager at the Homeless Services Center in Santa
Cruz County, California. She says, “One of the most significant results of
the three hundred members of our community participating in this program
has been the synergy and relationships that deepened and made our work
more effective.
“The way we approach formal systems and social norms in the leadership
program is accessible and empowering. Before the program, I felt generally
overwhelmed and powerless in the face of what seemed to be
insurmountable rigid systems perpetuating poverty, inequity, and other
social problems. I have learned to see my project as one of many entry
points into the systems that give rise to the problems we are working to
solve. Now when we participate in the design and implementation of our
projects, leadership program participants look for ways to shift the formal
systems and social norms toward dignity and equity throughout the
process.”
Kymberly Lacrosse worked on obesity prevention by promoting healthy
eating and active living in Watsonville, California. She spoke up at the
Watsonville City Council meeting when a fast-food company was going to
open a restaurant where new walkways were planned. This was inconsistent
with promoting healthy eating and active living. The residents won the
appeal. And something shifted in the representatives of the fast-food
company—they aligned with what the residents wanted. They decided not
to pursue a lawsuit because they did not want to take resources away from
the community—they saw that the residents were creating a thriving
community.
In the transformational leadership and stewardship programs, participants
see the alignment and synergy of their work with those of others. It is about
aligning and attuning for results that have the potential to make a paradigm
shift. When we see ourselves as principled game changers, we must be clear
about what we are shifting and must connect every action to what we said
we would shift. This may sound complex, but we align our actions with
what we want to shift and the results we want in our everyday lives—when
I decide to reduce my weight, I eat particular foods and exercise.
Do we notice and act when societal and political actions are congruent
with universal values and the shifts we want in our families, our
communities, our earth, our well-being? And do we notice and take action
when they are incongruent?Fallacy: Once we agree and align with common goals, we will
automatically transform the whole system and solve problems.
New Learning: Agreeing to common goals, outcomes, and
impacts is necessary and aligns us around the “end point.”
However, unless we shift the systems, structures, and cultural
norms that give rise to problems, based on universal values, we
will not achieve the goals in a sustainable manner, and we will
not accomplish whole-systems transformation.
Leveraging Opportunities for Radical
Transformation
Throughout this book, you have met people from different walks of life
who are leveraging the opportunities and entry points to presence an
equitable and sustainable world. They are citizens, experts, social services
providers, politicians; people from government, academia, businesses,
nonprofits, the media. By leverage, I mean the ability to use one’s influence
and entry points to generate effective and powerful results. Our
commitment and courage to create value-based alternatives is foundational.
In her erudite systems work, Donella Meadows states that the power to
transcend paradigms is the most effective intervention for systems
transformation; and it is the mindsets or paradigm of people who create
these systems—its goals, structure, rules, parameters—which must shift.67
Leveraging to presence radical transformation in order to generate
transformative results requires us to work with partners with approaches
that generate paradigm shifts.
Invitation to Leverage
Exploring the foundation of our work together sets pathways to accomplish
results—getting in touch with our compassion, stating the universal values
that will underpin our strategies, articulating the systems and normative
shifts, seeing new entry points for leverage, opening up to the adventure of
unforeseen possibilities. We embarked on this exploration when we worked
on a program to reduce maternal mortality in eight countries of South Asia
—Afghanistan, Bangladesh, Bhutan, India, the Maldives, Nepal, Pakistan,
and Sri Lanka.
The strategies to reverse the HIV/AIDS epidemic and enhance
Leadership for Results began with an inspiring retreat with the top
leadership of the United Nations and UNDP from thirty countries. Maria
Ndlovu, a public speaker, invited everyone in the room to walk with her
through her life’s journey, bringing people to tears, urging them to step up
and act. Annie McKee and Frances Johnston, proficient consultants who
worked with our teams in UNDP, skillfully deepened the space for
participants to get in touch with their own leadership. Everyone in the room
saw new openings, and half of them committed to radical strategic action.
Annie and Fran brought their personal values of dignity and equity and their
commitment to reversing the HIV/AIDS epidemic and to social change and
created spaces for value-based actions at this meeting and later at the
national level.
Probir Banerjee, founder of a nonprofit, PondyCAN, opened the door for
me to work in Auroville, because he values this work and share it with
others. He says that the radical transformative work created a huge shift in
him and made his path clear and journey in life enjoyable. He says that by
practicing the processes, “the clutter in my mind seems to have disappeared,
my weaknesses do not bother me anymore, and I see problems as
opportunities for growth, which is helping me toward achieving my full
potential. Professionally I am able to inspire others to committed action and
build a synergy for making systems shift in all the projects. I am seeing
results as never before.”The way we begin our conversations to effect radical transformation sets
the stage: Will we source universal values and people’s inner potential and
capacity for equitable and sustainable change? Or will we design our
strategies based only on technical and management solutions?
Transforming Philanthropy, Generating Prosperity
Philanthropists respond to human suffering by providing support. The next
generation of philanthropy and charity is evolving—moving from
responding only to basic survival needs of people and the planet toward
solving problems and generating new paradigms, new social forms,
outcomes, and impacts that presence equity, sustainability, and thriving. I
refer to this as conscious reciprocity to distinguish this next stage from the
conventional forms of charity and philanthropy.
The charity and philanthropy mindset is built on generosity and giving,
usually based on the concerns of the philanthropist. Unintended or intended
consequences may include the donor acting as hero by rescuing those in
need. Compliance may be expected from beneficiaries because they are
receiving money; nonprofits may receive money from a donor or
corporation that expects name recognition or sponsorship in return. Donors
feel good about giving money to the needy, even though they continue to
maintain the status quo and perpetuate the systems and cultural norms that
give rise to the very problems they are solving.
Conscious reciprocity, however, sources the inner capacities and the
ability to transform systems and cultural norms of both donor and recipient
as they solve problems. It is based on the principle that we are
interdependent, that all of us have a stake in creating the future. Both
recipients and donors participate in processes in which all members are
invited to discover their deeper aspirations, what they stand for, and
generate transformative results.
Therese Adams is the development director for a nonprofit serving
families experiencing homelessness. In the fall of 2015, she coordinated an
appreciation party for the donors. Therese says, “This event was different
than any other donor relation event I had done in the past. I created a
transformational event by using the concept of conscious reciprocity.
“A traditional donor event is often held at an upscale restaurant or venue
and includes fancy decor, flowers, and a gourmet dinner with wine. Often a
token client from the nonprofit thanks the donors for their contributions and
speaks about how the nonprofit changed the client’s life. At our donor
appreciation party, we wanted to promote the sense of equity in society, and
equity with the donors and the women living in the shelter. Holding the
event where the women lived created a different dynamic than if the women
were meeting the donors at an upscale restaurant.”
They planned a simple event with tables arranged in the backyard,
offered simple but delicious appetizers. Speakers from the shelter shared
what they cared deeply about—their universal values—when they spoke of
their experiences. The clients also talked about being parents, with
challenges just like everyone else. Therese shares, “This created a space for
everyone present to open their hearts. And in that moment, when grace was
also present, there was no donor or client—there were only parents and
human beings.
“The outcome of the event was different than any other event I had
coordinated. By the end of the evening, the attendees were visibly touched.
I believe donors saw themselves in the clients at the shelter. After the event,
the donors who came that day took on a new level of investment. They
began to undertake roles such as signing up to volunteer, joining the board,
and increasing their personal donations. Many of them still speak about
how deeply moving the event was.“I designed the event differently. I stand for generosity and equity, which
I wove throughout the event. I have seen in action that if universal core
values are communicated, people can resonate with each other regardless of
class, race, gender, age, or opinions. I identified what shifts were needed to
turn a regular donor party into a transformational event. The focus was to
shift the charity mindset to conscious reciprocity and to promote generosity.
We had conversations with everyone to connect to a deeper purpose and
engagement.
“Using the conventional charity or philanthropy model creates a
separation between the donor and the people the nonprofits serve. There is a
hierarchy inherent in the interaction. By creating an event that promotes
conscious reciprocity, donors’ hearts open, and they give for their own well-
being, for their community, for the people they serve. Donors see the issues
of poverty, homelessness, and inequity differently. They feel personally
invested in solving the problem and are more likely to be co-creators of
solutions.”
Philanthropy plays an important role in global aid flows, affecting
development outcomes in different countries. The traditional model of
development across centuries by faith-based organizations was built around
a model of charity that provides goods, services, and technologies that
address the immediate causes of poverty, hunger, and war; now, many of
these organizations are working on empowerment. Similarly, we turn to
emergency relief as the immediate response to the complex emergencies
caused by human and natural disasters. As we seek to develop responses
that bridge emergency relief toward sustainable development, philanthropic
models have evolved to include institutional development and advocacy
strategies that address more systemic issues impacting complex
emergencies and human suffering.
While necessary, these models have proven insufficient in transforming
systems of exclusion and human strife. Examining underlying factors that
inform philanthropic and aid models gives us access to a new perspective.
Inquiring at this level, we start to understand philanthropy, aid, and giving
as reciprocity: a process of mutual learning and exchange that empowers
individuals and communities through service to others. And a process that
responds to the challenges; and at the same time, creates opportunities for
people to manifest their full potential.
The results we generate through conscious reciprocity empower both the
giver and the recipient of aid, creating a whole new context for global
giving. In this understanding, philanthropy becomes grounded in a mindset
of abundance, where we realize and share the oneness of the human
condition beyond material disparities.
Ashish, our son, is an accomplished business executive. He quietly
supports the education of many children whose parents are street vendors in
Mumbai, so that they are able to achieve learning levels in spite of their
disadvantages in school. The children whose parents are educated find it
easier to learn. For him, giving is a matter of expanding himself, without
expecting any credit, recognition, or return. It is what we call conscious
reciprocity.
I fondly recall a time when nine-year-old Ashish participated in
anganwadi activities in villages in India. An anganwadi is a child care
center for children below six years of age. He enjoyed playing with the
children and supporting the anganwadi worker in her daily tasks, and had
many fun stories to share at the end of the day.
Next-Generation Movements and Campaigns
There are points in history where some human beings who see social,
economic, and political fault lines rise to the surface and step up to bring anew vision of dignified and equitable humanity into existence. And
although we stand alone in our commitment and courage to act, we seldom
move forward alone … there are always others whose compassionate hearts
are wide open, and are also called to act. Previously unimaginable shifts
have happened—ending apartheid, the civil rights movement, ending
geographic colonization (although it has been replaced by economic
colonization), gender quality and equity, enhancing labor rights. But we
have not experienced the full potential of these movements, and we
continue with our othering mindset.
Movements to address social injustices where concerned people organize
and work in an active way toward particular political, economic, and social
goals and campaigns where a group of people work together in order to
advance their shared political, social, or artistic ideas are usually based on
values such as equity and social justice. They are not propelled or carried
along by force in a specified direction. However, there is a missing piece:
embodying the universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion and
BEING able to see the inner greatness and potential in everyone, where we
have the courage to call out what is not working, speak truth to power and
generate alternatives, transcending the “us-them” dichotomies. It is the
beautiful and potent integration of personal transformation with systems
transformation, where we continuously cultivate our own power and
capacity as leaders and understand and activate our roles in bringing about
broader systems and cultural shifts in our society.
I have worked with many mindful pro-activists, people whose
compassion calls them to act beyond self-centered interests. They are from
different fields in various countries—government personnel and
businessmen who care about their colleagues, society, and the planet;
scientists and experts using their talents to transform people’s lives; socially
aware philanthropists and media professionals; citizens engaged in ending
casteism, racism, tribalism, bonded labor, and classism, bringing social
justice and equity to people. As I learn from them, I offer my ability to
transcend the typical “us versus them” paradigm that keeps us from
realizing our full potential, individually and collectively. They understand
what is missing in the foundation of their work, their strategies and their
responses.
Kobi Skolnik, from the Occupy movement, approached me to develop a
program for their leaders—it was named Occupy Manifest. Soon, most
participants discovered that this work is about “the 99 percent and the 1
percent,” even as we call out what is not working and take steps to make
change. The Occupy movement morphed, people in the movement
continued in their own way. And the seeds of the conscious full-spectrum
response blossomed in different ways.
Kobi shared, “I learned that when we connect from what we stand for—
our wisdom source—with others with different social profiles, the quality of
the connection and commitment is deeper and is sustained for longer. For
instance, this summer while the war was taking place back home in Israel
and Palestine, I was able to sit with youth from Palestine and Lebanon and
work through the shared pain. Connecting to the inner self and our values
helped us to stay together despite what the social norms or the environment
think of such interactions. Words are hard to use here because all of us lost
friends to the conflict and were able to connect to each other while the
waves of hatred and bloodbath surrounded us.”
Willow Brugh, from the MIT Media Lab, said, “I learned that equity and
justice are the two things that drive what I do, and when I source from
there, all the complications and dramas of working with various
organizations and individuals just sort of melt away. It’s easier to see whatneeds doing, and how to get there. I listen to people, instead of plowing
over them. I consider the whole space, and how to contribute back to that.
We design our programs differently. I spend more time on things that are
strategic and less time on urgent unimportant things.
“It’s such a subtle thing and it’s difficult to pull out specific instances.
The rage I once felt a large amount of the time bubbling under the surface is
now replaced by a desire to understand and act. It’s opened me up to
conversations I wouldn’t have otherwise been a part of.”
Radical transformation connects our very personal BEING, inner
capacity, and knowing to structured analysis and project planning. Together,
we dig deep into our personal values and principles to use them not just as
the foundation of our projects but the entire blueprint.
Fallacy: All activists are basically angry, have a chip on their
shoulder, and simply put up resistance. They benefit from the
systems and structures that have been set up. Corporations
provide jobs.
New Learning: Activists care; and so do other people who may
not identify themselves as activists. They see the systems,
structures, and cultural norms that are not working. Most of us
have derived benefit from current systems.
Corporations have benefited tremendously from public sector
investments and efforts such as infrastructure development,
expertise, and education. There are mindful pro-activists in every
sector, and the new generation of movements is not about “us
versus them.” It is about calling out the unworkable systems and
cultural norms and generating alternatives based on universal
values.
The next generation of movements and campaigns is emerging. It sources
everyone’s greatness and inner capacities; it transforms systems and cultural
norms to presence equity and sustainability; and it solves problems with
enduring results. This work requires great humility, but it also requires great
courage. Often, we have to work in spaces and places where people are
resistant and have vested interests. It is about creating different spaces of
deep, universal value-based grounding simultaneously with analytical,
strategic thinking, which will generate equitable and sustainable results for
a paradigm shift.
With the ever-increasing numbers of people in the world, it seems
impossible to make a difference by reaching just a few people. It is
encouraging to know that when we reach a tipping point, a critical mass is
attained, which is a threshold for a result or condition to occur. In other
words, we do not need to reach everybody—we need to have a critical
mass. According to one study, once a minority opinion of committed agents
grows beyond 10 percent of the majority population, the minority opinion
becomes the majority opinion more quickly.68
The challenge in the world today is not one of natural resources, or
money, or human capital, or technology, or legislation. It is not merely a
question of implementation of laws and distribution systems, nor can things
be “fixed” merely by providing education for all. Clearly something more
foundational and profound is missing. We need to change our mindsets; we
need to redefine development and our materialistic way of life; we need to
rethink our narrow and rigid identities.
Radical transformational leadership is emerging as a cutting-edge, new-
generation approach to transforming our mindsets—but only if we choose
to do so. This approach is based on the understanding that people have innerpotential and capacity to wisely and effectively address their challenges. It
also recognizes that the root factors of social and economic injustice are
within individuals, and these have to be addressed and transformed using
appropriate processes. It is a new kind of leadership for a transformed
future: leaders who manifest the deeper convergence of principles, purpose,
and practice to generate concrete results that benefit all of humanity and our
planet.
PART 4
MOVING AHEAD ON THIS JOURNEY
Chapter 12
Ushering in the Age of Universal
Prosperity
What Will It Take? A Call to Action
Again and again someone in the crowd wakes up, he has no ground in
the crowd, and he emerges according to much broader laws. He carries
strange customs with him and demands room for bold gestures. The
future speaks ruthlessly through him.
RAINER MARIA RILKE
Each and every one of us has a contribution to make in our family,
workplace, or community, or in the world. It does not matter where. What
matters is that we are united in our commitment to serve others through our
actions. Human beings, when they stand in their inner greatness, united in
commitment, are unstoppable. Our commitment to embody our full
potential and take action to benefit others is the way to create a world in
which we all thrive. This is when transformation occurs.
In the midst of the many benefits technology has brought about—
increased life expectancy, higher levels of education, energy in our homes,
connections through the internet, access to materials, employment—one out
of every six of us in this world lives on the very brink of survival.
The escalation of conflicts, violence, and inequities is a marker of
disastrous human-made decisions globally. United Nations Secretary-
General António Guterres opened a UN Human Rights Council session in
2017 by calling disregard for human rights “a disease that is spreading” and
urged the council to be part of the cure. “We are increasingly seeing the
perverse phenomenon of populism and extremism feeding off each other in
a frenzy of growing racism, xenophobia, anti-Semitism, anti-Muslim hatred
and other forms of intolerance,” he said. “Minorities, indigenous
communities and others face discriminations and abuse across the world,”
he added, noting abuse targeting refugees and migrants, and people who are
lesbian, gay, bisexual, trans, and/or intersex (LGBTI).69
There is a way out of this.
Radical transformational leadership is an idea whose time has come. It is
robust, but nascent, and is still in the margins of mainstream activity in
every sector. Most communities, institutions, organizations, and countries
are still unaware or unexposed to its power and potential. Vested interest
groups fight to maintain the status quo and strongly resist change. This is
the challenge. As the disparity, intolerance, and runaway greed of
individuals and nations continue to grow, how do we strategize to
mainstream this work around the world?
This radical evolutionary response has unique features that apply to all of
us. These features are more like axioms—they are established, accepted,
and self-evidently true. When we source our inner capacities for strategic
action, when we are emotionally intelligent and self-aware, and committed
to a thriving world, we manifest transformative results as we unfold in
action, and new patterns are put in place. It is our choice to engage in this
adventure of life.Axioms of Radical Transformational Leadership
Axiom 1. It’s Everyone’s Realm
This work allows us to see that our personal selves are not separate from
our professional and political selves, and that everyone can contribute to
radical transformation. Change is possible by starting where you are
standing, by designing for results with specificity, and by doing things
differently. Each of us has the power to make a difference no matter what
level of formal or informal leadership role we have, in the way we nurture
our families and the conversations we shift in society.
We all have the three innate, life-enhancing attributes that we can touch
and access: our universal heart of compassion; empathetic burning for
justice or fairness—our “equity impulse”; and our discerning eye, which
can see both invisible and visible patterns with light and pristine clarity.
When we cultivate our emotional intelligence—self-awareness, our ability
to regulate our emotions that are ever so often unruly and life-diminishing,
and transcend our rigid defense mechanisms—we manifest our innate life-
enhancing attributes in our families, work, and society and generate
transformative results.
The people you have met throughout this book are from countries around
the world, they follow different faiths, and they have different expertise and
proficiencies. They work in various sectors. They are ordinary people
making extraordinary contributions. They are each activating the three life-
enhancing attributes, and using that power to do things differently and
generate results.
Will you answer the call? Will you step into your full potential and take
action on behalf of people and our planet?
Axiom 2. The Prosperity Fractal
Let us think of prosperity as a state of BEING individually and collectively
in which we manifest our full potential, flourish, and thrive—and have our
basic needs of survival met. It is the vitality and contentment of self-
expression. Prosperity is about well-being for all—not growth, opulence,
luxury, or comfort.
Prosperity is success redefined. It creates cultural value for thriving for
all, instead of valuing unlimited opulence and money as the beacon of
success. The prosperity fractal repeats the pattern—it is the simplest of
everyday actions as well as gigantic endeavors and enterprises.
We have choices. We can continue to find “fixes” for our problems, never
addressing the factors that give rise to these problems. We can continue to
implement partial responses to complex issues—brutal violence, climate
change, crisis in the financial system, inequalities, unemployment, unmet
basic needs, to name a few—knowing that such responses rarely make
significant impact or generate the change we wish to see. Or, we can choose
to design and implement conscious full-spectrum responses to solve our
dire problems while radically transforming systems and cultural norms,
sourcing our inner capacities, thereby moving the whole, addressing not just
the immediate problems but that which gives rise to them. These are the
conditions of sustainable change. As we create opportunities for people to
manifest their full potential, at the same time we must respond to basic
needs for all.
What we must do has never been done before, and the scale at which we
must do this is unprecedented. But humans with compassion, courage, and
ingenuity have responded to crisis before. Let us assume we will do so
again!
Citizens, policy-makers, politicians, journalists, corporate leaders, areyou willing to take your family, work, and niche in the world, and dive into
the adventure of a new emergence? To dive in where you are not “in
control,” but anchored in your inner capacities? Are you willing and able to
create new patterns of prosperity as fractals of the whole?
Axiom 3. Principled Game-Changing Made Possible
This is where we see our universal values in action, when individuals and
leaders from every sphere of life step up to generate a thriving humanity.
Shifting our political economies and financial instruments to serve the
well-being of humanity, to create peace and prosperity worldwide, rather
than financing war, ill health, and environmental destruction, is imperative.
Instruments designed to concentrate wealth in the hands of a few are
unacceptable. This is being stated by millions of people all over the world.
Though many people may feel these forces are insurmountable, we know
that when people source equity and compassion, they act courageously and
create alternatives. Outrageous decisions and behind-the-scenes
negotiations with dire consequences for humanity and our planet are made
by only a handful of people. Many others follow these “rules of the game”
blindly or mindlessly; or because the rules are explained and understood in
the context of some self-serving theory; or they are given a partial view and
therefore do not understand the repercussions of what they are being asked
to do.
Shifting the all-pervasive mindsets that underpin these life-diminishing
political, economic, and financial policies and instruments seems
impossible. We are educated and socialized to believe in a social, economic,
and political order that is based on domination and does not work for the
majority of humanity. We live in a scientific, Cartesian world, where
economics, science, and technology are often used as smoke screens for the
politics of the elite. Elites have captured democratic policy-making so as to
benefit the few instead of the many. Today, the value and essence of
democracy has been lost. Principled game-changing is about reclaiming
democracy and citizenship in an entirely new way, embodying universal
values to change the game. To make the best use of the great technologies
we have at hand, we need to reclaim the wisdom of the human spirit and
learn how to ask fundamental questions.
Here are a couple of examples of smoke screens. A wealthy group in
India publicized their intention to build a hospital—an activity that appeals
to people. Their request to obtain free government land in a prime location
was approved because they committed to have 25 percent of the hospital
beds and services for patients who could not afford to pay. They even
received a grant for the same. The hospital was built, and 75 percent of the
beds and services were only for those who paid high costs—the remaining
25 percent of the beds remain “under construction.” People worldwide want
adequate infrastructure, such as roads, water, and sanitation systems.
Schemes that privatize public assets, such as roads and infrastructure,
deceive taxpayers, decrease revenue for government, and allow only a few
wealthy people to continue to accrue money.70
It is essential that citizens and leaders understand both the visible and
hidden sources of action and inaction, and the attitudes that determine these.
We must make the effort to understand the different smoke screens, and
responsible experts who care need to demystify the language used in
shaping economic policies and financial instruments.
Worldviews generated from universal principles of dignity and equity are
different from the ideological perspectives based on a theory. When we
source our inner capacities and understand the rules of the game, we are
willing to alter our points of view and can steer decisions toward dignityand equity.
Are you a citizen and leader who is open and willing to cultivate your
own value-based worldviews through inquiry and insights? Will you create
spaces for authentic value-based exploration and dialogue in order to make
principled game-changing the norm? Can we genuinely value leaders and
citizens who are principled game changers instead of mindlessly supporting
ideological stances just because our social identity is wrapped up with
them?
Axiom 4. The Ism Interrupter
Although what we all want as human beings is the same deep down—
happiness, to love and be loved, to belong—and we all experience joy,
grief, anger, and remorse, our socialization and education processes
influence us through life. Some are life-enhancing, and others are
dominating, divisive, and detrimental for humanity. We must be able to
transcend the numerous socially constructed detrimental isms—racism,
casteism, classism, tribalism, exclusionary religiosity, sexism—based on
assumptions and conditioning that we receive from our family, religion,
politics, education, and culture, which lead to unending social turmoil,
retaliation, violence, and destruction of cultures, people, and the planet.
How do we interrupt the centuries of isms? How can we rediscover who
we are as human BEINGS beyond race, gender, caste, tribe, and class, while
honoring diversity and celebrating difference?
Radical transformation sources our inner capacities and universal values
as the basis of strategic action; it is the ism interrupter. Radical
transformation moves us beyond hegemony and patriarchy and creates new
social and professional patterns of equity, recognizing our essential human
oneness. Grounding our work, projects, relations, and daily tasks in our
deepest values, we learn to nurture and listen and to give and sustain life
while at the same time being effective, efficient, and results-focused. We
see that creation is available to us in our hearts, minds, and hands—we
allow them to work together, and we start molding reality from our deepest
dreams of dignity, justice, equity, and peace.
This does not mean that we do not call out what is exploitative or
undermining. Radical transformation implies that we have the courage to
speak truth to power; and when we work on specific issues, such as racism,
casteism, or classism, or on specific themes, such as Black Lives Matter,
clean energy, living wages and decent jobs, or universal free education, we
do so beyond an “us versus them” strategy. We source universal values,
always knowing that anyone could at any time unpredictably unfold and
manifest their greatness.
As citizens and leaders, when we commit to a thriving humanity, we have
the responsibility to shift the current narratives that maintain the status quo.
For example, when anyone makes a statement on dignity and equity for all,
they are often branded as “left.” However, there is nothing “left” or “right”
about humanity’s mindless march downhill. It is about waking up to
realities and our patterns of unbridled consumption eroding our planet and
the lives of other people. It is the ability to look at a phenomenon beyond
ideologies of capitalism, socialism, and communism, to transcend the isms,
grounded in our universal values of dignity, equity, and compassion.
Are you willing to interrupt these isms in a fundamentally new way?
Otherwise, our “otherness” and exclusionary mindsets will result in another
expression of the same domination—the next version of Jim Crow! Can the
next generation count on you and me to generate the conditions for peaceful
and prosperous societies?Axiom 5. The Radical Superhero Is Here
The new superheroes are here—you met some of them in this book, and
perhaps you see yourself in them too. They are largely unnoticed—just
emerging. They are contemporary pioneers, investing in their own inner
(not necessarily religious) growth, sourcing their inner capacities to
transform the world. They proactively inform themselves about the state of
the world and about their community and society; they see patterns behind
what is happening—they see the connections in seemingly disconnected
events; and they design the unifying and radical architecture for equitable
and sustainable results.
They have the courage to take on difficult issues and solve problems;
they act from a source of wisdom, compassion, and empathy, rather than
charity and “doing good.” They do not reflect the traditional sage aloof
from the world; or the superhero characters who rush to save people in
desperate situations and are brave in the face of death. They are instead
informed sages, wise in the ways of the world, and they are here to
contribute. They are courageous architects of new futures. They do not need
to be the star of the show; rather, they midwife the ideas and aspirations of
people, bringing into existence the prosperity fractal with conscious full-
spectrum responses.
Ethical citizenship and leadership are not idealist—they are realistic and
must be normalized. They lead the way with the mindset of abundance, not
scarcity; sufficiency, not greed; peace, not war.
Today’s radical superheroes are the radical transformational leaders
throughout the world, grateful to be able to serve. How can the creativity
and commitment of eminent film producers and writers be stimulated to see
and share the excitement and unfolding of this next generation of
superheroes? Will Hollywood and Bollywood step up to the challenge of
featuring the new superheroes and changing the societal narratives? Will
concerned and visionary artists, musicians, and performers create the
thrilling new narratives of prosperity and success through the stories of
emerging radical superheroes? Will citizens and media see and highlight the
courageous, compassionate, and equitable actions and create new social
trends for responsible leadership to emerge?
Axiom 6. Untapped Potential Unfolds
There are millions of people engaged in solving social, environmental, and
business problems. It’s admirable to have a vision of new futures,
something good that we want to achieve in the world or in our own lives.
But visions fail when they are bound up in the force of a charismatic
personality and not grounded in the wisdom of universal values. Wisdom is
often missing in modern leadership. Leading from this inner capacity will
tap into the power to deliver sustainable results. To tackle intractable
problems, leaders must address the root factors and mull over the strategic
leverage points that bring the most promising shifts in societal behavior.
Technical fixes don’t work. It is about creating value-based alternatives.
You have met creative, committed, and effective leaders in this book and
elsewhere generating transformative results. This is the new potential
unfolding.
And there are millions of people in the world today who genuinely want
peace and prosperity. If only we could all decide to embark on our unique
journeys to manifest our full potential and create platforms for others to do
so too, we would be on the path to a sustainable paradigm shift.
Environmentalist and author Paul Hawken estimates that there are two
million organizations working toward social justice and ecological
sustainability.71 Clearly, these committed people care deeply enough to give
up other opportunities and have chosen to serve. What if the leaders of theseorganizations were willing to shift from partial responses to conscious full-
spectrum responses, each in their own unique way? Renew their
organizations to work as unique yet resonant entities in interdependent
constellations? Entities in which each person solves problems, creates new
patterns changing the rules of the game, sources inner capacities related to
our oneness. Our impact would be huge!
Cooperatives with democratic ownership and governance are emerging as
a powerful global economic influence. Individuals in these cooperatives
have chosen to engage in business differently, valuing the contribution of
each member, moving from a competitive, scarcity mindset to shared
leadership. In 2014, 2.6 million cooperatives had over one billion
memberships and clients. Cooperatives are low-profile. The annual revenue
generated by cooperatives around the world is $2.98 trillion. If they were
national economies, they would rank as the fifth in the world.72 What if
each one of these cooperatives invested in learning to source inner
capacities and designed for enduring and just change? The era of ethical
business would manifest!
Many leadership programs are skillful in personal development, team
building, and enhancing the performance of individuals within
organizations. However, these courses are integrated firmly in current
notions of growth, economic thinking, market forces, and corporate
policies. Rarely do leadership programs focus on the key component that
leads to equitable and sustainable results for society—shifts in unworkable
systems and norms. In the United States alone, investment in leadership
education and development approached $50 billion in 2000, and it has
grown considerably. More than 20,000 books and thousands of articles have
been written about essential aspects of leadership and its results.73 If the
thousands of leadership consultants and teachers committed to a thriving
humanity and planet, and worked on transforming unworkable systems and
norms when they deliver leadership programs, there would potentially be a
tremendous outcome and impact for people and for our planet.
For the first time ever, world leaders pledged to common action for a
universal policy agenda for our planet and people throughout the entire
world and to strengthen universal peace and in all developing and
developed countries. In September 2015, world leaders from 193 countries
announced 17 Sustainable Development Goals related to social, economic,
and environmental progress. They said, “We envisage a world free of
poverty, hunger, disease and want, where all life can thrive. We envisage a
world free of fear and violence. We envisage a world of universal respect
for human rights and human dignity. As we embark on this great collective
journey, we pledge that no one will be left behind.”74
Will you proactively advocate for the Sustainable Development Goals?
Will you capitalize on the unprecedented agreement by all countries of the
Sustainable Development Goals with a conscious full-spectrum approach
and generate a paradigm shift locally and globally? We could establish
equity, well-being, peace, progress, prosperity, and a thriving humanity and
planet. It is critical to ensure that the universal values of dignity, equity, and
inclusion form the basis of all strategic action and progress. Otherwise, new
expressions of inequalities, erosion of dignity, poverties, and
disempowering power play will emerge, and we will not be able to achieve
the goals.
How can we hold institutions and different sectors—politicians,
government, businesses, civil society—accountable for humanity’s
progress? In what way do I want to hold myself responsible and contribute
to a new future? We are poised to generate the tipping point for a new
paradigm to emerge!Axiom 7. Seeing the Missing Piece
Responsible corporate, political, government, media, academic, and
community leaders know what is required for shared prosperity and a
thriving planet and publicly share their views. They can see how corporate
power needs to transform to support governments in order to ensure the
well-being of all people. They see the importance of reducing income
inequality, ending lobbying for policies that exacerbate inequities, paying
workers living wages, sharing the tax burden where the richest companies
and individuals pay more, and investing in universal free education and
health care for societal prosperity. Most often, they do not see the missing
piece: taking into account the immense value of the inner capacity of people
to design strategies and implement change. Some may understand what is
missing intuitively, but do not see how they can systematically draw upon
transformative inner capacities and universal values of everyone—policy-
makers and people at the grass roots alike.
Social and environmental activists see the invisible forces that create
unworkable systems and the cultural norms that give rise to the many
problems in this world. In the context of the universal values of dignity and
justice, these activists see their role as stopping the injustices that affect
people and the planet. Most often, they do not see the missing piece: taking
into account the immense value of the inner capacity of people to design
strategies and implement change. When activism is anchored in everyone’s
inner capacities, it is compassionate and strong, where principled outrage
replaces destructive anger, where burnout is transformed into sustained
contribution.
The human potential movement has created deeper societal
